Q: A ball thrown horizontally from the roof of a building 7.5 m tall and lands 9.5 m from the base. What was the ball's initial speed?
A: The time of flight is:
 \[t = \sqrt{\frac{2 \times 7.5}{9.8}}\]
 The initial speed of the ball is:
 \[v = \frac{9.5}{\sqrt{\frac{2 \times 7.5}{9.8}}} = 7.7 m/s.\]

[5] Exercises
Q: A football is kicked at ground level with a speed of 18.0 m/s at an angle $31.0^\circ$ to the horizontal. How much later does it hit the ground?
A: The vertical component of the initial velocity is:
 \[v_{y0} = 18.0 \sin(31.0^\circ)\]
 To find the time it hits the ground, we solve:
 \[0 = 18.0 \sin(31.0^\circ) t - \frac{1}{2} \cdot 9.8 \cdot t^2\]
 This equation can be solved for \(t\) to find the time until the football hits the ground, giving $t = 1.4 \unit s$.

[6] Exercises
Q: A fire hose held near the ground shoots water at a speed of 6.5 m/s. At what angle(s) should the nozzle point in order that the water land 2.5 m away? Why are there two different angles? Sketch the two trajectories.
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[width=4cm]{fire_hose.png}
 \end{center}
A: To find the angle(s) for which the water lands 2.5 m away, we use the equation:
 \[ \theta = \frac{1}{2}\sin^{-1}\left(\frac{2.5 \cdot 9.8}{6.5^2}\right) \]
 There are two angles because the function \(\sin(2\theta)\) has two intersections with any given value in its domain, corresponding to a steep and a shallow trajectory. Calculating, the angles are $72.3^\circ$ and $17.7^\circ$.

[7] Exercises
Q: A grasshopper hops along a level road. On each hop, the grasshopper launches itself at angle $\theta_0 = 45^\circ$ and achieves a range $R = 0.80 \unit m$. What is the average horizontal speed of the grasshopper as it hops along the road? Assume that the time spent on the ground between hops is negligible.
A: Given \(\theta_0 = 45^\circ\) and \(R = 0.80 \text{m}\), the average horizontal speed of the grasshopper is constant,
 \[ v_x = v_0/\sqrt{2} = \sqrt{Rg}/\sqrt{2} = 2.0 m/s. \]

[8] Exercises
Q: Show that the time required for a projectile to reach
its highest point is equal to the time for it to return to its
original height if air resistance is negligible.
A: The time to reach the highest point is given by:
 \[ t_{up} = \frac{v_0 \sin(\theta)}{g} \]
 Due to the symmetric nature of projectile motion under uniform gravity, the time to ascend is equal to the time to descend, thus showing \(t_{up} = t_{down}\) if air resistance is negligible.

[9] Exercises
Q: Extreme-sports enthusiasts have been known to jump
off the top of El Capitan, a sheer granite cliff of height 910 m in Yosemite National Park. Assume a jumper runs
horizontally off the top of El Capitan with speed
and enjoys a free fall until she is 150 m above the valley
floor, at which time she opens her parachute.
\begin{center}
 \includegraphics[width=4cm]{el_capitan.png}
\end{center}
\begin{anum}
 \item How long is the jumper in free fall? Ignore air resistance.
 \item It is important to be as far away from the cliff as possible before opening the parachute. How far from the cliff is this jumper when she opens her chute?
\end{anum}
A: \begin{anum}
\item It is in free fall for a vertical distance of $760 m$, so focusing on the $y$ component,
 $$t= \sqrt{\frac{2 \cdot 760}{g}} = 12.45 m/s.$$
 \item Multiplying by 4 m/s, we get around $50$ meters.
\end{anum}

[10] Exercises
Q: A person in the passenger basket of a hot-air balloon
throws a ball horizontally outward from the basket with speed 10.0 m/s. What initial velocity (magnitude and direction) does the ball have relative to a person standing on the ground (a) if the hot-air balloon is rising
at 3.0 m/s relative to the ground during this throw, (b) if the hot-air balloon is descending at 3.0 m/s relative to the
ground?
\begin{center}
 \includegraphics[width=4cm]{hot_air.png}
\end{center}
A: \begin{anum}
\item Adding the velocity vectors, we see that the magnitude is
 $$v=\sqrt{10^2+3^2} = 10.4 m/s.$$
 And the angle is $\arctan(3/10) \approx 16.7^\circ$ above the horizontal.
 \item Adding the velocity vectors, we see that the magnitude is
 $$v=\sqrt{10^2+3^2} = 10.4 m/s.$$
 And the angle is $\arctan(3/10) \approx 16.7^\circ$ below the horizontal.
\end{anum}

[11] Exercises
Q: A boat, whose speed in still water is $2.50 m/s$ must
cross a 285-m-wide river and arrive at a point 118 m
upstream from where it starts. To do so, the
pilot must head the boat at a $45.0^\circ$ upstream angle. What
is the speed of the
river’s current?
\begin{center}
 \includegraphics[width=5cm]{boat_river.png}
\end{center}
A: The horizontal component is $2.50\cos(45)$. So, to end up at 118 m upstream, the total vertical component upstream should be $$2.50\cos(45) \frac{118}{285} = 0.73 m/s.$$
 So, the river speed is $2.5/\sqrt 2 - 0.73 = 1.0 m/s.$

[12] Problems
Q: You buy a plastic dart gun, and being a clever physics
student you decide to do a quick calculation to find its
maximum horizontal range. You shoot the gun straight up,
and it takes 4.0 s for the dart to land back at the barrel.
What is the maximum horizontal range of your gun?
A: The total time for the bullet to fall is given by $$t = 2v_0/g,$$
 so $v_0 = gt/2$. We learned that the maximum range was for launching at 45 degrees, giving us $$R=v_0^2/g = gt^2/4 = 39 \unit m.$$

[13] Problems
Q: A stunt driver wants to make his car jump over 8 cars
parked side by side below a horizontal ramp as shown below.
\begin{center}
 \includegraphics[width=6cm]{car_stunt.png}
\end{center}
\begin{anum}
 \item With what minimum speed must he drive off the horizontal ramp? The vertical height of the ramp is 1.5 m above the cars and the horizontal distance he must clear is 22 m.
 \item If the ramp is now tilted upward, so that "takeoff angle" is $7.0^\circ$ above the horizontal, what is the new minimum speed?
\end{anum}
A: \begin{anum}
\item The time for the car to fall is $\sqrt{2h/g}$, so the speed is $22\sqrt{g/2h} = 39.8 m/s$.
 \item
\end{anum}

[14] Problems
Q: A basketball is shot from an initial height of 2.40 m with an initial speed directed at an angle above the horizontal. (a) How far from the basket was the player if he made a basket? (b) At what
angle to the horizontal did the ball enter the basket?
\begin{center}
 \includegraphics[width=5cm]{basketball_shot.png}
\end{center}
A: \begin{anum}
\item Focusing on the $y$ direction the basketball has $y$ velocity
 $$v_{yf}^2 = (v_0\sin 35)^2 - 2g(3.05 - 2.40) \implies v_{yf} = 5.89 m/s,$$
 So, the time travelled is $\frac{v_{yf} + v_0\sin 35}{g} = 1.30 \unit s$, which means that it travels $$x = v_0\cos (35^\circ) t = \boxed{12.8 \unit m}.$$
 \item We found the final velocity components, so the angle is $\arctan (5.89/v_0\cos 35^\circ) = \boxed{34.3^\circ}$.
\end{anum}

[15] Problems
Q: A rock is kicked horizontally at $15 m/s$ from a hill with a $45^\circ$ slope. How long does it take for the rock to hit the ground?
A: This is a challenging problem. Here, we can use a trick where we tilt our view such that the hill is horizontal. Then, we have a horizontal gravitational acceleration of $g/\sqrt 2$ and a vertical gravitational acceleration of $g/\sqrt 2$. Isolating just the $y$ component, the time for the rock to fall is
 $$t = \frac{2v_{0,y}}{g/\sqrt 2} = \frac{2v_0/\sqrt 2}{g/\sqrt 2} = \frac{2v_0}{g} = \boxed{3.1 \unit s}.$$

[16] Problems
Q: Spymaster Chris, flying a constant 208 km/h horizontally
in a low-flying helicopter, wants to drop secret documents
into her contact’s open car which is traveling $156$ km/h on a level highway 78.0 m below. At what angle (with the horizontal) should the car be in her sights when the packet is released?
\begin{center}
 \includegraphics[width=5cm]{ball_drop.png}
\end{center}
A: The time it takes the for the package to fall is $t = \sqrt{2h/g}$. So, after falling the package travels
 $$x = vt = (208 km/h)t.$$
 This must be equal to the original difference along with the distance travelled by the car, so
 $$(208 km/h)t = (156 km/h)t + \Delta x.$$
 So, plugging in our numbers, we calculate $\Delta x = 57.6 m$. So, the angle should be $\theta = \arctan(78/57.6) = 53.6^\circ$.

[17] Problems
Q: An airplane, whose air speed is 580 km/h is supposed
to fly in a straight path $38.0^\circ$ N of E. But a steady 82 km/h wind is blowing from the north. In what direction should the plane head?
A: Thinking about the vector sum of the two, we want their components perpendicular to the $38^\circ$ line to cancel. Thus, if the plane makes an angle $\theta$ with the $38^\circ$ line, then
 $$580\sin \theta = 82\sin (52) \implies \theta = 6.4^\circ,$$
 so the plane should head at $44.4^\circ$ N of E.

[18] Challenge Problems
Q: The classic monkey problem: An apple hangs from a tree, and a bow and arrow is aimed directly at the apple in the tree. Show that if the apple drops and the bow are fired at the exact same time, the arrow will always hit the apple.
A: If we only look at the $y$ component of both objects, we see that if the line from the bow to the apple is $\theta$, then
 \begin{align*}
 y_{\text{apple}} = -\frac 12 gt^2 + y_0,\\
 y_{\text{arrow}} = v_0\sin \theta t - \frac 12 gt^2.
 \end{align*}
 And they collide when $v_0 \cos \theta t = \frac{y_0}{\tan \theta}$. Plugging this into the first temr of the second equation, we see that
 \begin{align*}
 y_{\text{apple}} = -\frac 12 gt^2 + y_0,\\
 y_{\text{arrow}} = y_0 - \frac 12 gt^2.
 \end{align*}
 which means that they will collide. Alternatively, one can view the problem in a frame that is accelerating down at $g$.

[19] Challenge Problems
Q: A ball starts at the bottom of a very narrow but very deep well. The well has a width $w$, and a depth $h\gg w$. It is initially thrown at an angle of 30$^\circ$ to the horizontal with speed $v$. What must $v$ be so that the ball escapes the well? All collisions with the walls of the well are elastic.
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[scale=0.1]{well}
 \end{center}
A: In lecture, we showed that the trajectory of the ball is just a repeated reflection of the trajectory it takes if the well was not there. Thus, since we want to find the case where the ball is able to rise up to the top of the well, the problem simply reduces to finding the minimal speed necessary for the ball to rise to a height $h$. Our vertical velocity is $v_y=v\sin30=0.5v,$ and using $v_y^2=2gh$ we get $v=\boxed{2\sqrt{2gh}}.$

LESSON: ap-03

[1] Exercises
Q: What force is needed to accelerate a sled (mass = 55 kg) at 1.4 m/s$^2$ on horizontal frictionless ice?
A: The force is $F=ma=55\cdot 1.4=77 N$.

[2] Exercises
Q: How much tension must a rope withstand if it is used to accelerate a 1210-kg car horizontally along a frictionless surface at 1.20 m/s$^2$?
A: The force is $T=ma=1210\cdot 1.2=1452 \unit N$.

[3] Exercises
Q: What average force is required to stop a 950-kg car in 8.0 s if the car is traveling at 95 km/h?
A: First, let's convert 95 km/h to m/s by dividing by 3.6: 26.4 m/s. We can find the acceleration by $a=\Delta v/\Delta t=26.4/8=3.30 m/s^2$. Thus, the force is $F=ma=950\cdot 3.30=3135 \unit N$.

[4] Exercises
Q: According to a simplified model of a mammalian heart, at each pulse approximately 20 g of blood is accelerated from 0.25 m/s to 0.35 m/s during a period of 0.10 s. What is the magnitude of the force exerted by the heart muscle?
A: The acceleration of the blood is $a=\Delta v/\Delta t=(0.35-0.25)/0.1=1 m/s^2.$ The force is then $F=ma=0.02\cdot 1=0.02 \unit N$.

[5] Exercises
Q: A woman stands on a bathroom scale in a motionless elevator. When the elevator begins to move, the scale briefly reads only 0.75 of her regular weight. Calculate the acceleration of the elevator, and find the direction of acceleration.
A: Let the woman have mass $m$. Initially, the normal force exerted upward by the scale is exactly $mg$, because the woman is not accelerating. When the elevator starts accelerating, then the normal force becomes $0.75mg$, since her apparent weight has decreased by that amount. Taking the direction downward to be positive, the net force on the woman is now $F=mg-N=0.25 mg$, so her acceleration is $a=F/m=0.25 g$ in the downward direction.

[6] Exercises
Q: The cable supporting a 2125-kg elevator has a maximum strength of 21750 N. What maximum upward acceleration can it give the elevator without breaking?
A: Let the tension be $T$. Then, the net force on the elevator is $F=T-mg=21750-2125\cdot 9.8=925 \unit N$. The acceleration is then $a=F/m=925/2125=0.44 m/s^2$.

[7] Exercises
Q: Arlene is to walk across a ``high wire" strung horizontally between two buildings 10.0 m apart. The sag in the rope when she is at the midpoint is 10.0$^\circ$, as shown in the diagram. If her mass is 50.0 kg, what is the tension in the rope at this point?
\begin{center}
 \includegraphics[scale=0.2]{rope}
\end{center}
A: The tension in the rope pulls at two points upward with a combined strength of $2T\sin(10^\circ)$. This balances her graviational force, so $$2T\sin(10^\circ)=mg\Rightarrow T=\frac{mg}{2\sin(10^\circ)}=1411 \unit N$$

[8] Exercises
Q: A 20.0-kg box rests on a table. (a) What is the weight of the box and the normal force acting on it? (b) A 10.0-kg box is placed on top of the 20.0-kg box, as shown in the figure. Determine the normal force that the table exerts on the 20.0-kg box and the normal force that the 20.0-kg box exerts on the 10.0-kg box.
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[scale=0.25]{boxes}
\end{center}
A: \begin{anum}
 \item The weight of the box is just $F_g=mg=20\cdot 9.8=196 \unit N$, and the normal force is the exact same.
 \item Think of the two boxes as one big box of mass 30 kg. Thus the normal force between the table and the two boxes is just $N=30\cdot 9.8=294 \unit N$. The normal force the 20 kg box exerts on the 10 kg box is just the force needed to support the weight of the 10 kg, which is $N=10\cdot 9.8=98 \unit N$.
 \end{anum}

[9] Exercises
Q: A 75-kg petty thief wants to escape from a third-story jail window. Unfortunately, a makeshift rope made of sheets tied together can support a mass of only 58 kg. How might the thief use this “rope” to escape? Give a quantitative answer.
A: The idea is that the thief will use the rope to support himself so that instead of falling at $g$, he falls at a smaller acceleration. That is, the thief only pulls just as hard on the rope so that it won't break. The force the rope can support is $F=58g$, and the weight of the thief is $75g$. Thus, the net force on the thief is $F=17g$, so his acceleration is $a=F/m=17g/75 = 2.22 m/s^2$.

[10] Problems
Q: At the instant a race began, a 65-kg sprinter exerted a force of 720 N on the starting block at a 22° angle with respect to the ground. (a) What was the horizontal acceleration of the sprinter? (b) If the force was exerted for 0.32 s, with what speed did the sprinter leave the starting block?
A: \begin{anum}
 \item By Newton's Third Law, the starting block exerts a force 720 N on the sprinter. The horizontal component of this force is $F_x=720\cos(22^\circ)=668 \unit N$ so the horizontal acceleration is just $a_x=F_x/m=10.3 m/s^2$. 
 \item We have $\Delta v=a\Delta t=10.3\cdot 0.32=3.30 m/s$. 
 \end{anum}

[11] Problems
Q: A person has a reasonable chance of surviving an auto- mobile crash if the deceleration is no more than 30 $g$’s. Calculate the force on a 65-kg person accelerating at this rate. What distance is traveled if brought to rest at this rate from 95 km/h?
A: The force is $F=ma=75\cdot 30\cdot 9.8=22000 \unit N$. The velocity is initially $95/3.6=26.4 m/s$. The distance traveled is then $d=v^2/2a=26.4^2/(2\cdot 30\cdot 9.8)=1.18 \unit m$.

[12] Problems
Q: A box weighing 77.0 N rests on a table. A rope tied to the box runs vertically upward over a pulley and a weight is hung from the other end. Determine the force that the table exerts on the box if the weight hanging on the other side of the pulley weighs (a) 30.0 N, (b) 60.0 N, and (c) 90.0 N.
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[scale=0.25]{boxpulley}
 \end{center}
A: \begin{anum}
 \item The tension must balance the weight of the smaller mass, so in this case it is $T=30 \unit N$. The normal force acting on the box is found via $N=mg-T=77-30=47 \unit N$.
 \item In this case, $T=60 \unit N$, and $N=mg-T=17 \unit N$.
 \item In this case, $T=90 \unit N$, and $N=mg-T=-13 \unit N$. We appear to have a problem, though, because the normal force can only ``push" and not "pull"! This means the box will lose contact with the table, so the normal force is in fact 0. This does make sense because now the weight is heavier than the box. 
 \end{anum}

[13] Problems
Q: A train locomotive is pulling two cars of the same mass behind it in the figure below. Determine the ratio of the tension in the coupling (think of it as a cord) between the locomotive and the first car ($F_{T1}$), to that between the first car and the second car ($F_{T2}$), for any nonzero acceleration of the train.
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[scale=0.3]{train}
 \end{center}
A: Let the acceleration be $a$, and the individual masses of the cars to be $m$. Note that $F_{T2}$ is the only force pulling on car 2, so $F_{T2}=ma$. Now, $F_{T1}$ pulls both cars forward at $a$, so $F_{T1}=2ma$. Thus, if we take the ratio then $F_{T1}/F_{T2}=2$.

[14] Problems
Q: Two snowcats in Antarctica are towing a housing unit north, as shown in the figure. The sum of the forces $\vec F_A$ and $\vec F_B$ exerted on the unit by the horizontal cables is north, parallel to the line L, and FA=4500N. Determine $F_B$ and the magnitude of $\vec F_A+\vec F_B$.
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[scale=0.3]{anta}
 \end{center}
A: Notice that the force in the $x$ direction (from this bird's eye view) must balance, which gives us $F_A\sin(48^\circ)=F_B\sin(32^\circ)$. Solving for $F_B$ yields $F_B=6300 \unit N$. Now, since the net force is directly north, we just add the northern components of the forces. Thus $$|\vec F_A+\vec F_B|=F_A\cos(48^\circ)+F_B\cos(32^\circ)=8360 \unit N$$

[15] Problems
Q: A 27-kg chandelier hangs from a ceiling on a vertical 4.0-m-long wire. (a) What horizontal force would be necessary to displace its position 0.15 m to one side? (b) What will be the tension in the wire?
A: \begin{anum}
 \item If the string is displaced by a distance 0.15 m horizontally, then it will make an angle $\theta = \sin^{-1}(0.15/4)=2.15^\circ$ to the vertical. Let the tension then be $T$. By balancing forces in the vertical direction, $T\cos\theta=mg$, so $T=mg/\cos\theta$. The horizontal force must balance the horizontal tension force, so $F=T\sin\theta=mg\tan\theta=9.93 \unit N$. 
 \item From the previous part we know that $T=mg/\cos\theta=265 \unit N.$
 \end{anum}

[16] Problems
Q: A 10 kg mass and a 15 kg mass are placed on the same string and strung over a pulley. (a) What is the acceleration of the masses? (b) What is the tension in the string?
A: \begin{anum}
 \item Treating the masses as one big block, their combined mass is 25 kg. The gravitational force pulls on the heavier mass in one direction and the lighter mass in the other, so the net force acting on the masses is $15g-10g=5g$. The acceleration is then $a=F/m=5g/25=g/5=1.96 m/s^2$. 
 \item Doing force balance on the 15 kg box, $15g-T=15a$ so plugging in $a$ from above yields $T=15g-15a=117.6 \unit N$.
 \end{anum}

[17] Problems
Q: A window washer pulls herself upward using the bucket–pulley apparatus shown in the figure. (a) How hard must she pull down- ward to raise herself slowly at constant speed? (b) If she increases this force by 15\%, what will her acceleration be? The mass of the person plus the bucket is 72 kg.
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[scale=0.3]{pulley}
\end{center}
A: \begin{anum}
 \item Notice that as the girl grabs the rope, the rope will now have two spots to pull upward on the girl-bucket system. Thus, if the girl-bucket mass is $m$, then $2T=mg$ for the net force to be a constant (so that her speed is constant). Note that by Newton's third law, the girl will be pulling on the rope with force $T$, so she fill pull with force $F=T=mg/2=353 \unit N$. 
 \item If she increases her pulling force, the tension increases by this proportion, so now the net force pulling upward on the girl-bucket system is $2T\cdot 1.15$. Note that $2T=mg$, so the net upward force is now $1.15 mg$. The downward force is still $mg$, so the acceleration is $a=F/m=(1.15mg-mg)/m=0.15g=1.47 m/s^2$. 
 \end{anum}

[18] Problems
Q: The figure below shows a block (mass $m_A$) on a smooth horizontal surface, connected by a thin cord that passes over a pulley to a second block $m_B$, which hangs vertically. (a) Draw a free body diagram for each block, showing the force of gravity on each, the force (tension) exerted by the cord, and any normal force. (b) Apply Newton’s second law to find formulas for the acceleration of the system and for the tension in the cord. Ignore friction and the masses of the pulley and cord.
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[scale=0.3]{bix}
 \end{center}
A: \begin{anum}
 \item The free body diagram is as drawn: \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[scale=0.3]{freebe}
 \end{center}
 \item If we consider the system as one big block, the net force acting on it is just $m_Bg$, so $a=m_Bg/(m_A+m_B)$. If we look at the force balance on $m_A$, it follows that the tension exclusively provides this acceleration, so $T=m_Aa=m_Am_Bg/(m_A+m_B)$. 
 \end{anum}

[19] Challenge Problems — $F=ma$
Q: A block of mass $m$ is attached to a massless string. The string is passed over a massless pulley
and the end of the string is fixed in place. The horizontal part of the string has length $L.$ Now a
small mass $m$ is hung from the horizontal part of the string, and the system comes to equilibrium.
(Diagram not necessarily to scale.)

\begin{center}
 \includegraphics[width=0.6\textwidth]{2020fmap9.png}
\end{center}
\begin{anum}
 \item Neglecting friction everywhere, what is the tension at the end of the string?
 \item During the process, how much has the right block been raised by?
\end{anum}
A: \begin{anum}
 \item By balancing forces on the right block, we get $mg$.
 \item Now balancing forces on the center block, if we let $\theta$ be the angle that the string makes with the horizontal, we get $2T\sin\theta = mg \implies \sin\theta = 1/2$. Thus, the new length of the string left of the pulley is $L/\cos \theta$, so the block rises by $L/\cos \theta - L \approx 0.15L$.
 \end{anum}

[20] Challenge Problems
Q: A box of mass $m$ is held up in the air by two strings, one of length $l_1$ and the other of length $l_2$. The strings make a right angle to one another at their connection point to the box, and the strings are both anchored to the ceiling. 
 \begin{anum}
 \item Find the tensions $T_1$ and $T_2$ in the strings.
 \item If the string of length $l_1$ is cut, what is the instantaneous acceleration of the box? 
 \item Find the relationship between $l_1$ and $l_2$ such that $a=0$ after the string of length $l_1$ is cut. 
 \end{anum}
A: \begin{anum}
 \item Using right triangle properties, we have the following:
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[width=7cm]{strings.png}
 \end{center}
 This tells us $\alpha = \arctan (l_1 / l_2)$. Now, since the two tensions must cancel out horizontally, $T_2/T_1 = \tan \alpha$. And the two tensions make a right triangle with gravity,
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[width=4cm]{forces_strings.png}
 \end{center}
 so $T_2 = mg \sin \alpha$ and $T_1 = mg\cos \alpha$, which in terms of $l_1,l_2$ are $T_1 = \frac{mg l_2}{\sqrt{l_1^2+l_2^2}}$, $T_2 = \frac{mg l_1}{\sqrt{l_1^2+l_2^2}}.$
 \item Using the right triangle above, this just leaves a perpendicular component to string two, so $mg \cos \alpha$ is left, which gives an acceleration of $\boxed{g\cos \alpha = g\frac{l_2}{\sqrt{l_1^2 + l_2^2}}}$.
 \item We must have $\alpha = 0$, so $l_2/l_1 \rightarrow 0$.
 \end{anum}

LESSON: ap-04
------------------------------------------------------------------------------

[1] Exercises
Q: If the coefficient of kinetic friction between a 22-kg crate and the floor is 0.30, what horizontal force is required to move the crate at a steady speed across the floor? What horizontal force is required if $\mu_k$ is zero?
A: The normal force is $N=mg=215.6 \unit N$. Then, the frictional force would be $f=N\mu_k=64.7 \unit N$. If the crate moves at a steady speed (no acceleration) then the net force must be 0, so the force needed to push on the crate is just $64.7 \unit N$. If $\mu_k$ is 0, then no force is needed.

[2] Exercises
Q: A force of 35.0 N is required to start a 6.0-kg box moving across a horizontal concrete floor. (a) What is the coefficient of static friction between the box and the floor? (b) If the 35.0-N force continues, the box accelerates at 0.60 m/s$^2$. What is the coefficient of kinetic friction?
A: \begin{anum}
 \item It holds that $F=mg\mu_s$ since we take the maximum possible value of the static friction force. Then, $\mu_s=F/mg=0.59$.
 \item The net force on the block is $F=ma=3.6 \unit N$. This means the frictional force is $35-3.6=31.4 \unit N$. This equals $mg\mu_k$, so $\mu_k=31.4/mg=0.53.$
 \end{anum}

[3] Exercises
Q: Suppose you are standing on a train accelerating at $0.20g$. What minimum coefficient of static friction must exist between your feet and the floor if you are not to slide?
A: Let's say your mass is $m$. Then, the maximum static friction force on you is $mg\mu_s$. This must provide a force $ma=0.2mg$, so $0.2mg=mg\mu_s\Rightarrow \mu_s=0.2$.

[4] Exercises
Q: The coefficient of static friction between hard rubber and normal street pavement is about 0.90. On how steep a hill (maximum angle) can you leave a car parked?
A: The normal force on an incline, as we know, is $mg\cos\theta$, and the downward gravitational force along the incline is $mg\sin\theta$. We seek the $\theta$ such that maximum static friction force $mg\mu_s\cos\theta$ balances out $mg\sin\theta$: $$mg\mu_s\cos\theta=mg\sin\theta\Rightarrow \tan\theta=\mu_s \Rightarrow \theta=\tan^{-1}\mu_s=42^\circ$$

[5] Exercises
Q: A box is given a push so that it slides across the floor. How far will it go, given that the coefficient of kinetic friction is 0.15 and the push imparts an initial speed of 3.5 m/s?
A: If the mass of the box is $m$, then the kinetic friction force is $mg\mu_k$, which leads to acceleration $a=g\mu_k$. Now, we will use $v^2=2a\Delta x$, so $$\Delta x=\frac{v^2}{2a}=\frac{v^2}{2g\mu_k}=4.17 \unit m$$

[6] Exercises
Q: Drag-race tires in contact with an asphalt surface have a very high coefficient of static friction. Assuming a constant acceleration and no slipping of tires, estimate the coefficient of static friction needed for a drag racer to cover 1.0 km in 12 s, starting from rest.
A: The maximum frictional force is $mg\mu_s$, which provides a maximum acceleration $g\mu_s$. Then, by $\Delta x=(1/2)at^2,$ (note that $v_0=0$ so we took that term out of the equation) we have $$\Delta x=\frac12g\mu_st^2\Rightarrow \mu_s=\frac{2\Delta x}{gt^2}=1.42$$

[7] Exercises
Q: A child on a sled reaches the bottom of a hill with a velocity of 10.0 m/s and travels 25.0 m along a horizontal straightaway to a stop. If the child and sled together have a mass of 60.0 kg, what is the average retarding force on the sled on the horizontal straightaway?
A: The acceleration can be found via $v^2=2a\Delta x\Rightarrow a=v^2/2\Delta x=2 m/s^2$. The retarding force is then $F=ma=120 \unit N$.

[8] Exercises
Q: A skateboarder, with an initial speed of 2.0 m/s, rolls virtually friction free down a straight incline of length 18 m in 3.3 s. At what angle $\theta$ is the incline oriented above the horizontal?
A: The acceleration down the plane is $a=g\sin\theta$. Then, we can use $$\Delta x=v_0t+\frac12g\sin\theta t^2\Rightarrow \sin\theta=\frac{2(\Delta x-v_0t)}{gt^2}=0.214$$Then, $\theta=\sin^{-1}(0.214)=12.3^\circ$.

[9] Problems
Q: A block has mass $m = 7.0$ kg and lies on a fixed smooth frictionless plane tilted at an angle $\theta = 22.0^\circ$ to the horizontal. (a) Determine the acceleration of the block as it slides down the plane. (b) If the block starts from rest 12.0 m up the plane from its base, what will be the block’s speed when it reaches the bottom of the incline? (c) The block is given an initial speed of $4.5 m/s$ up the inclined plane. How far up will it go? (d) How long will it take for the block to return to its starting point?
A: \begin{anum}
 \item The acceleration is just $a=g\sin\theta=3.67 m/s^2.$
 \item We have $v=\sqrt{2a\Delta x}=9.39 m/s$.
 \item We have $\Delta x=v^2/2a=2.79 \unit m$.
 \item We have $\Delta t=\Delta v/a=2\cdot 4.5/3.67=2.45 \unit s$.
 \end{anum}

[10] Problems
Q: Solve all parts of the previous problem but in the case where there is friction of coefficient $\mu=0.1$ between the crate and the incline.
A: \begin{anum}
 \item The friction points in the opposite direction as the acceleration is just $a_1=g\sin\theta-g\mu\cos\theta=2.76 m/s^2.$
 \item We have $v=\sqrt{2a_1\Delta x}=8.14 m/s$. 
 \item In this part the friction points in the same direction as the acceleration downward, so $a_2=g\sin\theta+g\mu\cos\theta=4.58 m/s^2$. Then we have $\Delta x=v^2/2a_2=2.21 \unit m$.
 \item In the motion of the block upward, $\Delta t = v/a_2=0.98 \unit s.$ When the block goes down, we use $\Delta x$ from the previous part and use $\Delta x = (1/2)a_1t^2\Rightarrow t=\sqrt{2(2.21)/2.76}=1.27 \unit s$, since friction now points in the opposite direction as gravity again. The total time is thus $0.98+1.27=2.25 \unit s$.
 \end{anum}

[11] Problems
Q: A car can decelerate at 3.80 m/s$^2$ without skidding when coming to rest on a level road. What would its deceleration be if the road is inclined at 9.3$^\circ$ and the car moves uphill? Assume the same static friction coefficient.
A: The maximal acceleration provided by static friction is thus $g\mu_s=3.8 m/s^2$, which implies $\mu_s=0.39$. If the car moves uphill, we can point gravity and static friction in the same direction, so the maximal acceleration is $$a=g\sin\theta+g\mu_s\cos\theta=5.36 m/s^2$$

[12] Problems
Q: On an icy day, you worry about parking your car in your driveway, which has an incline of 12$^\circ$. Your neighbor’s driveway has an incline of 9.0°, and the driveway across the street is at 6.0$^\circ$. The coefficient of static friction between tire rubber and ice is 0.15. Which driveway(s) will be safe to park in?
A: From lecture, the static friction needed to keep the car in place is $\mu_s=\tan\theta$, which implies a cutoff $\theta=\tan^{-1}\mu_s=8.53^\circ$. Any angle above this will result in the car slipping, so you can only park on the driveway across the street.

[13] Problems
Q: Two masses $m_A = 2.0$ kg and $m_B = 5.0$ kg are on inclines and are connected together by a string as shown in the figure. The coefficient of kinetic friction between each mass and its incline is $\mu_k = 0.30$. If $m_A$ moves up, and $m_B$ moves down, determine their acceleration. \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[scale=0.2]{halfincline}
 \end{center}
A: We are given that the boxes move to the right in the figure. Now we consider the two boxes as one big box, and consider the forces acting on them as a whole (so we can neglect the tension). We define the rightward direction as positive, so the net force is $$F=m_Bg\sin(21)-m_Bg\mu_k\cos(21)-m_Ag\sin(51)-m_Ag\mu\cos(51)=-15.09 \unit N$$This means the boxes are slowing down. The acceleration is then $a=F/(m_A+m_B)=-2.16 m/s^2.$

[14] Problems
Q: A child slides down a slide with a 34$^\circ$ incline, and at the bottom her speed is precisely half what it would have been if the slide had been frictionless. Calculate the coefficient of kinetic friction between the slide and the child.
A: Suppose the distance down the slide is $d$. If the slide is frictionless her acceleration is $g\sin\theta$, so her speed at the bottom would be $v_1=\sqrt{2ad}=\sqrt{2g\sin\theta d}$. If there is kinetic friction, her speed would be $v_2=\sqrt{2g(\sin\theta-\mu_k\cos\theta)d}$. We are given that $2v_2=v_1$, so $$2\sqrt{2g(\sin\theta-\mu_k\cos\theta)d}=\sqrt{2g\sin\theta d}\Rightarrow 4(\sin\theta-\mu_k\cos\theta)=\sin\theta\Rightarrow \mu_k=(3/4)\tan\theta=0.51$$

[15] Problems
Q: (a) Suppose the coefficient of kinetic friction between $m_A$ and the plane in the figure is $\mu_k = 0.15$, and that $m_A = m_B = 2.7$ kg. As $m_B$ moves down, determine the magnitude of the acceleration of $m_A$ and $m_B$, given $\theta = 34^\circ$. (b) What smallest value of $\mu_k$ will keep the system from accelerating?
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[scale=0.2]{bed}
 \end{center}
A: \begin{anum}
 \item We define the downward motion as positive, so the net force is $$F=m_Bg-m_Ag\sin\theta-m_Ag\mu_k\cos\theta=8.37 \unit N$$ so the net acceleration is $a=F/(m_A+m_B)=1.55 m/s^2.$
 \item We want the net force to equal 0 so it holds that $$\mu_k=\frac{m_Bg-m_Ag\sin\theta}{m_Ag\cos\theta}=0.53$$
 \end{anum}

[16] Challenge Problems — 200 Puzzling Physics Problems
Q: In Victor Hugo's novel les Misérables, the main character Jean Valjean, an escaped prisoner, was noted for his ability to climb up the corner formed by the intersection of two vertical perpendicular walls. Find the minimum force with which he had to push on the walls whilst climbing. What is the minimum coefficient of static friction required for him to be able to perform such a feat?
A: Drawing a proper diagram is key here, especially because the setup is a bit confusing. Here is a top-down diagram:
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[width=6cm]{les_mis.png}
 \end{center}
 Now, looking at this, we see that the horizontal components of friction exactly cancel out the normal forces. Using this, we can write that at saturation,
 $$0 = F_y = 2F_{f_y} - mg = 2\sqrt{(\mu N)^2 - N^2} - mg.$$
 We see that for this to be possible we must have $\mu > 1$ and then pushing hard enough (large enough $N$) is sufficient. For computing the minimum force on each wall, we first have
 $$N = \frac{mg}{2} \frac{1}{\sqrt{\mu^2 - 1}},$$ but for the total force we must sum the friction force and normal force which is bigger by $\sqrt{1 + \mu^2}$, so $$F_{min} = \frac{mg}{2} \sqrt{\frac{\mu^2 + 1}{\mu^2 - 1}}.$$

LESSON: ap-05

[1] Exercises
Q: A jet plane traveling $1890 km/h$ ($525 m/s$) pulls out of
a dive by moving in an arc of radius $5.20 \unit {km}$. What is the
plane’s acceleration in $g$’s?
A: The acceleration is $a_c = v^2/r = (525)^2/(5200) = 5.4$ $g$'s.

[2] Exercises
Q: What is the magnitude of the acceleration of a speck
of clay on the edge of a potter’s wheel turning at 45 rpm
(revolutions per minute) if the wheel’s diameter is 35 cm?
A: It is $v^2/R = (2\pi R / T)^2 /R = \frac{4\pi^2 R}{T^2} = 5.18$ m/$\mathrm {s^2}$.

[3] Exercises
Q: How many revolutions per minute would a 25-m-
diameter Ferris wheel need to make for the passengers to feel “weightless” at the topmost point?
A: The normal force should be 0, so we need $$\frac{v^2}{r} = g \implies v = \sqrt{rg}.$$
 So, revolutions per minute is $\frac{v}{2\pi r} = \frac{1}{2 \pi} \sqrt{\frac{g}{r}} = 0.14 rps = 8.46 rpm$.

[4] Exercises
Q: A proposed space station consists of a circular tube that
will rotate about its center
(like a tubular bicycle tire). The circle formed by
the tube has a diameter of 1.1 km. What must be the rotation speed (revolutions per day) if an effect nearly equal to gravity at the surface of the
Earth (say, 0.90 $g$) is to be felt?
\begin{center}
 \includegraphics[width=4cm]{spin_ship.png}
\end{center}
A: We repeat the calculation from the previous part, plugging in $0.90 g$ instead, giving us $0.0202 rps =1700 rpd $.

[5] Exercises
Q: Tarzan plans to cross a gorge by swinging in an arc
from a hanging vine (Fig. 5–42).
If his arms are capable of
exerting a force of 1150 N on
the vine, what is the maximum
speed he can tolerate at the
lowest point of his swing? His
mass is 78 kg and the vine is
4.7 m long.
\begin{center}
 \includegraphics[height=5cm]{tarzan.png}
\end{center}
A: We consider force balance at the bottom of the swing:
 $$T - mg = \frac{mv^2}{r},$$
 which gives us 
 $$v = \sqrt{r(T/m - g)} = 4.82 m/s.$$

[6] Exercises
Q: At what minimum speed
must a roller coaster be traveling so that passengers upside down at the top of the circle do not fall out? Assume a radius of
curvature of 8.6 m.
A: Since the normal force will be 0 at the limit,
 $$mv^2/r = mg \implies v = \sqrt{rg} = 9.18 m/s.$$

[7] Exercises
Q: A pilot performs an evasive maneuver by diving vertically at 270 m/s. If he can withstand an acceleration of 8.0 g’s without blacking out, at what altitude must he begin
to pull his plane out of the dive to avoid crashing into the sea?
A: This problem is ill-defined, ignore it.

[8] Exercises
Q: A 975-kg sports car (including driver) crosses the rounded top of a hill (radius = $88.0$ m) at $18.0 m/s$. Determine (a) the normal force exerted by the road on the car, (b) the normal force exerted by the car on the 62.0-kg driver, and (c) the car speed at which the normal force on the driver equals zero.
A: \begin{anum}
\item We have by force balance,
 $$mg - N = \frac{mv^2}{r} \implies N = mg - \frac{mv^2}{r} = 5970 \unit N.$$
 \item We have by force balance,
 $$mg - N = \frac{mv^2}{r} \implies N = mg - \frac{mv^2}{r} = 380 \unit N.$$
 \item We need $mg = mv^2/r \implies v = \sqrt{rg} = 40 m/s.$
\end{anum}

[9] Problems
Q: Two blocks, with masses $m_A$ and $m_B$ are connected
to each other and to a central post by thin rods as
shown below. The blocks revolve about the post at the
same frequency $f$ (revolutions per second) on a frictionless
horizontal surface at distances $r_A$ and $r_B$ from the post.
Derive an algebraic expression for the tension in each
rod.
\begin{center}
 \includegraphics[width=5cm]{spin_block.png}
\end{center}
A: We have that the velocity of the masses are $2\pi r_Af$ and $2\pi r_B f$, so the tension in the outer rod by considering mass $B$ is
 $$T_B = \frac{m_B (2\pi r_B f)^2}{r_B} = 4\pi^2 m_B f^2 r_B.$$
 Then, now considering force balance on $m_A$,
 $$T_A - T_B = 4 \pi^2 m_A f^2 r_A \implies T_1 = (4\pi^2f^2)(m_Br_B + m_A r_A).$$

[10] Problems
Q: If a curve with a radius of 95 m is properly banked
for a car traveling 65 km/h, how much larger must be the coefficient of
static friction for a car not to skid when traveling at 95 km/h?
A: This problem is actually quite hard! Don't worry if you didn't solve it.
 We consider a 2-D cross section of the setup. The acceleration must point horizontally, so
 $$mg \tan \theta = \frac{mv^2}{r} \implies \tan \theta = \frac{v^2}{rg}.$$
 Then, for the faster speed, we must have
 $$\vec N + \vec F_f + m \vec g = \frac{mv_2^2}{r}.$$
 Thus, the angle that $(\vec N + \vec F_f )$ makes with the vertical is $\tan^{-1}(v_2^2/rg)$. Thus, the angle that this makes with the normal is
 $$\theta' = \tan^{-1}\left(\frac{v_2^2}{rg}\right)-\tan^{-1}\left(\frac{v^2}{rg}\right).$$
 So, the ratio between the frictional and normal force is given by, plugging in the numbers
 $$\mu = \tan \theta' = 0.32.$$

[11] Problems
Q: A flat puck (mass $M$) is revolved in a circle on a frictionless
air hockey table top, and is held in this orbit by a massless cord
which is connected to a dangling mass (mass $m$) through a
central hole as shown below. Show that the speed of
the puck is given by $v = \sqrt{mgR/M}$.
\begin{center}
 \includegraphics[width=5cm]{table_rolling.png}
\end{center}
A: The tension in the cord will be $mg$, so we have $$\frac{Mv^2}{R} = mg \implies v=\sqrt{mgR/M}.$$

[12] Problems
Q: A train traveling at a constant speed rounds a curve of
radius 215 m. A lamp suspended from the ceiling swings
out to an angle of 16.5° throughout the curve. What is the
speed of the train?
A: We have by force balance, since the vertical component of tension must cancel with gravity,
 $$\frac{mv^2}{R} = mg \tan (16.5^\circ) \implies v= \sqrt{gR \tan (16.5^\circ)} = 46.2 m/s.$$

[13] Problems
Q: On an ice rink two skaters of equal mass grab hands and
spin in a mutual circle once every 2.5 s. If we assume their
arms are each 0.80 m long and their individual masses are
55.0 kg, how hard are they pulling on one another?
A: The speed of the skaters is $2\pi r / T = 2.01 m/s$, so $T = mv^2/r = 275 \unit N$.

[14] Problems
Q: A coin is placed 13.0 cm from the axis of a rotating turntable of variable speed. When the speed of the turntable is slowly increased, the coin remains fixed on the turntable until a rate of 38.0 rpm (revolutions per minute) is reached, at which point the coin slides off. What is the coefficient of static friction between the coin and the turntable?
A: Let the coefficient of friction be $\mu$. Then, by force balance
 $$\mu m g = \frac{mv^2}{r} \implies \mu = \frac{v^2}{rg}.$$
 Thus, plugging in the numbers, $\mu = \frac{v^2}{rg} = 0.21 m/s$.

[15] Problems
Q: A device for training astronauts and jet fighter pilots is
designed to move the trainee in a horizontal circle of
radius 11.0 m. If the force felt by the trainee is 7.45 times
her own weight, how fast is she revolving? Express your answer in both m/s and rev/s.
A: We have $v^2/r = 7.45g$, so $v = 28.3 m/s$. In rev per seconds $0.41 rev/s$.

[16] Challenge Problems — $F=ma$
Q: Four identical rods, each of mass $m$ and length $2d$, are joined together to form a square. The square is then spun around its center, as shown in the figure, at an angular frequency of $\omega$. What is the magnitude of the force that the joints between the rods (at the corners of the square) must bear?
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[width=5cm]{spin_square.png}
 \end{center}
A: By symmetry, the force at the joints should point tangential to the circle of rotations. Let $F$ be the force between the joints between the rods. Then, taking the perpendicular components of these forces, we get
 $$2F/\sqrt 2 = m \omega^2 d \implies F = m\omega^2 d / \sqrt 2.$$

LESSON: ap-06
