[1] Exercises
Q: Calculate the force of Earth’s gravity on a spacecraft
2.00 Earth radii above the Earth’s surface if its mass is
1850 kg.
A: Let $m$ be the mass of the spacecraft. Then,
 $$F = \frac{GM_\oplus m}{(3R_\oplus)^2} = 18,200 \unit N.$$

[2] Exercises
Q: At what distance from the Earth will a spacecraft traveling directly from the Earth to the Moon experience zero net force because the Earth and Moon pull in opposite
directions with equal force? You may search up relevant values of the Earth and Moon.
A: Let $d$ be the distance between the earth and the moon. For the force to balance, we need
 $$\frac{GM_E}{r^2} = \frac{GM_m}{(d-r)^2} \implies \frac{r}{d-r} = \sqrt{\frac{M_E}{M_m}}.$$
 So, $r = d \frac{\sqrt{M_E/M_m}}{1 + \sqrt{M_E/M_m}} = 3.46\times 10^8 \unit m.$

[3] Exercises
Q: A hypothetical planet has a radius 2.0 times that of
Earth, but has the same mass. What is the acceleration due
to gravity near its surface?
A: We have that acceleration is $g = \frac{GM}{R^2}$, so if mass stays the same but radius doubles, the acceleration goes down by a factor of 4, so $a = g/4 = 2.45 m/s$.

[4] Exercises
Q: Calculate the acceleration due to gravity on the Moon,
which has radius $1.74 \times 10^6 \unit m$ and mass $7.35 \times 10^{22} kg$. How does it compare to $g$?
A: Applying $g_m = \frac{GM_m}{R^2} = 1.6 m/s^2$. This is around 6 times smaller than acceleraiton on earth.

[5] Exercises
Q: Calculate the speed of a satellite moving in a stable
circular orbit about the Earth at a height of 4800 km.
A: We can use centripetal accleration to write
 $$\frac{GM}{(R_\oplus + h)^2} = \frac{v^2}{R_\oplus + h} \implies v = \sqrt{\frac{GM}{R_\oplus + h}} = 5970 m/s.$$

[6] Exercises
Q: Calculate the period of a satellite orbiting the Moon,
95 km above the Moon’s surface. Ignore effects of the
Earth. The radius of the Moon is 1740 km.
A: We can use Kepler's third law (as derived in class) to write
 $$T = \sqrt{\frac{4\pi^2 a^3}{GM_m}} = 7000 \unit s.$$

[7] Exercises
Q: How far above the Earth’s surface will the acceleration of
gravity be half what it is at the surface?
A: Acceleration goes as $1/r^2$, so we need $r' = \sqrt 2 R_\oplus$, which is a distance $(\sqrt 2 - 1)R_\oplus = 2.6\times 10^6 \unit m$.

[8] Problems
Q: A certain neutron star has five times the mass of our
Sun packed into a sphere about 10 km in radius. Estimate
the surface gravity on this monster.
A: We have $$a = \frac{GM}{R^2} = G\frac{5 \times 2 \times 10^{30}}{10000^2} = 6.67\times 10^{12} m/s.$$

[9] Problems
Q: During an Apollo lunar landing mission, the command
module continued to orbit the Moon at an altitude of about
100 km. How long did it take to go around the Moon once?
A: We have from Kepler's third law,
 $$T = \sqrt{\frac{4\pi^2 a^3}{GM_m}} = 7100 \unit s.$$

[10] Problems
Q: Two equal-mass stars maintain a constant distance apart
of $8.0 \times 10^{11}$ m and revolve about a point midway between
them at a rate of one revolution every 12.6 yr. (a) Why
don’t the two stars crash into one another due to the
gravitational force between them? (b) What must be the
mass of each star?
A: \begin{anum}
\item They have sufficient velocities such that as they are pulled towards each other it results in curving of their trajectories but not so much that they crash into each other.
 \item We can apply Kepler's third law, which tells us 
 $$M_{\text{tot}} = 2M = \frac{4\pi^2 a^3}{GT}.$$
 Plugging in the numbers, we get $M = 3.8\times 10^{38} kg$.
\end{anum}

[11] Problems
Q: An asteroid of mass $m$ is in a circular orbit of radius $r$
around the Sun with a speed $v$. It has an impact with another asteroid of mass $M$ and is kicked into a new circular orbit with a speed of $1.5 v$.What is the radius of the new orbit in terms of $r$?
A: We have from one of the earlier problems that $v = \sqrt{\frac{GM}{r}},$ so if $v$ increases by $1.5$, $r$ must decrease by $1.5^2$, so $r_{\text{new}} = r/2.25$.

[12] Problems
Q: A Ferris wheel 22.0 m in diameter rotates once every
12.5 s. What is the ratio of a person’s apparent
weight to her real weight at (a) the top, and (b) the bottom?
A: \begin{anum}
\item At the top, the normal force is
 $$N = mg - m\omega^2r,$$
 so the ratio is $1 - \frac{\omega^2r}{g} = 0.72$.
 \item We have that the the normal force at the bottom is $$N = mg + m\omega^2 r,$$
 so the ratio is $1 + \frac{\omega^2 r}{g} = 1.28$.
\end{anum}

[13] Problems
Q: A satellite of mass 5500 kg orbits the Earth and has a period
of 6600 s. Determine (a) the radius of its circular orbit,
(b) the magnitude of the Earth’s gravitational force on the
satellite, and (c) the altitude of the satellite.
A: For the radius, we can use Kepler's third law, giving us 
 $$r = 7.6\times 10^6 \unit m.$$
 Then, just using Newton's universal law of gravitation,
 $$F = \frac{GMm}{r^2} = 37900 \unit N.$$
 The altitude is just $r - R_\oplus = 1.2\times 10^6 \unit m$.

[14] Problems
Q: A science-fiction tale describes an artificial “planet” in the
form of a band completely encircling a sun, as shown below. The
inhabitants live on the inside surface (where it is always
noon). Imagine that this sun is exactly like our own, that
the distance to the band is the same as the Earth–Sun
distance (to make the climate livable), and that the ring
rotates quickly enough to produce an apparent gravity of $g$
as on Earth. What will be the period of revolution, this
planet’s year, in Earth days?
\begin{center}
 \includegraphics[width=5cm]{wiajd;lf.png}
\end{center}
A: For the effective gravity to be correct, we need $\omega^2 R = g$, so $$T^2 = \frac{4\pi^2 R}{g},$$
 where $R$ is 1 AU. Plugging in the numbers, we get $T = 22.5 days$.

[15] Challenge Problems — $F=ma$
Q: A satellite is following an elliptical orbit around the Earth. Its engines are capable of providing a one-time
impulse of a fixed magnitude. In order to maximize the energy of the satellite, the impulse should be
\begin{center}
\includegraphics[width = 0.5\textwidth]{oweinw}
\end{center}
\begin{enumerate}[label=(\Alph*)]
 \item directed along the satellite’s velocity and applied when the satellite is in its perigee.
 \item directed along the satellite’s velocity and applied when the satellite is in apogee.
 \item directed toward the Earth and applied when the satellite is in perigee.
 \item directed toward the Earth and applied when the satellite is in apogee.
 \item directed away from the Earth and applied when the satellite is in apogee.
 \end{enumerate}
A: Suppose the satellite is moving with velocity $v$. If we add a perpendicular boost, then by the pythagorean theorem, the new energy is $$KE = \frac 12 m (v^2 + v_\perp^2).$$
 However, if it is parallel, then $$KE = \frac 12 m (v+\Delta v)^2 = \frac 12 m (v^2 + 2v\Delta v + \Delta v^2).$$
 So, not only do we see that this is better, we see that it scales up with higher $v$. Thus, we want the satellite in perigee, giving choice \textbf A.

------------------------------------------------------------------------------
LESSON: ap-07
------------------------------------------------------------------------------

[1] Exercises
Q: You swing a mass $m$ in a circle of radius $r$ such that it moves at constant velocity $v$. How much work do you do over time $\Delta t$?
A: Notice that centripetal force is always perpendicular to the velocity so we do zero work.

[2] Exercises
Q: A 75.0-kg firefighter climbs a flight of stairs 28.0 m high. How much work does he do?
A: The firefighter must carry his own weight up the stairs, so with each step me must exert a force $F=mg$. Then, the work he does is $W=mgh=20600 \unit J.$

[3] Exercises
Q: How much work did the movers do (horizontally) pushing a 46.0-kg crate 10.3 m across a rough floor without acceleration, if the effective coefficient of friction was 0.50?
A: We need to overcome kinetic friction, so the pushing force is just $F=mg\mu$. Then the work done is $W=mg\mu d=46\cdot 9.8\cdot 0.5\cdot 10.3=2321 \unit J$.

[4] Exercises
Q: A lever such as that shown in the figure can be used to lift objects we might not otherwise be able to lift. Show that the ratio of output force, $F_O$, to input force, $F_I$, is related to the lengths $\ell_O$ and $\ell_I$ from the pivot by $F_O/F_I=\ell_I/\ell_O$. Ignore friction and the mass of the lever, and assume the work output equals the work input.
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[scale=0.25]{lever.png}
 \end{center}
A: By similar triangles, the ratio between the distance the man pushes through and the distance the rock moves is $d_I/d_O=\ell_I/\ell_O$. The work inputted by the man is $F_Id_I$, and the work outputted is $F_Od_O$. Setting these equal, $$F_Id_I=F_Od_O\Rightarrow \frac{F_O}{F_I}=\frac{d_I}{d_O}=\frac{\ell_I}{\ell_O}$$

[5] Exercises
Q: A box of mass 4.0 kg is accelerated from rest by a force across a floor at a rate of 2.0 m/s$^2$ for 7.0 s. Find the net work done on the box.
A: We can use the work-energy theorem. The velocity at the end of the acceleration is $v=at$, so the kinetic energy is $K=(1/2)m(at)^2=(1/2)\cdot 4\cdot (2\cdot 7)^2=392 \unit J$. This is precisely the net work done on the box.

[6] Exercises
Q: The force on a particle, acting along the $x$ axis, varies as shown in the figure. Determine the work done by this force to move the particle along the $x$ axis: (a) from $x = 0.0$ to $x = 10.0$ m; (b) from $x = 0.0$ to $x = 15.0$ m.
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[scale=0.25]{graf.png}
 \end{center}
A: \begin{anum}
 \item The work done is the area under the graph. The area under the graph from $x=0$ to $x=10$ is just $2800 \unit J$. 
 \item We have to subtract the area from under the $x$ axis from the area above. The area of the trapezoid below the $x$ axis is $-700 \unit J$ so the total work done from $x=0$ to $x=15$ is $2100 \unit J$. 
 \end{anum}

[7] Exercises
Q: A 17,000-kg jet takes off from an aircraft carrier via a catapult. The gases thrust out from the jet’s engines exert a constant force of 130 kN on the jet; the force exerted on the jet by the catapult is plotted in the figure. Determine the work done on the jet: (a) by the gases expelled by its engines during launch of the jet; and (b) by the catapult during launch of the jet.
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[scale=0.25]{graf2.png}
 \end{center}
A: \begin{anum}
 \item From the graph, we know that the force of 130 kN is applied for a distance 85 m, so the work done by the engines is $W=11050 \unit J$. 
 \item The catapult does work equal to the area under the graph, which is a trapezoid with area 49500 J. 
 \end{anum}

[8] Exercises
Q: (a) If the kinetic energy of a particle is tripled, by what factor has its speed increased? (b) If the speed of a particle is halved, by what factor does its kinetic energy change?
A: \begin{anum}
 \item We have $K=(1/2)mv^2$ so $v\propto \sqrt{K}$ (proportional to). Thus if $K$ is tripled $v$ goes up by a factor of $\sqrt 3.$
 \item We have $K\propto v^2$ so if $v$ is halved $K$ goes down by a factor $1/4$.
 \end{anum}

[9] Exercises
Q: How much work must be done to stop a 925-kg car traveling at 95 km!h?
A: First, converting to m/s, we have $95 km/h=95/3.6 m/s=26.4 m/s$. The kinetic energy of the car is thus $K=(1/2)mv^2=644 kJ$ and by the work energy theorem this is precisely the work needed to stop the car.

[10] Exercises
Q: An 85-g arrow is fired from a bow whose string exerts an average force of 105 N on the arrow over a distance of 75 cm. What is the speed of the arrow as it leaves the bow?
A: The work done by the string over this distance is $W=Fd=78.8 \unit J$. By the work-energy theorem this equals $K=(1/2)mv^2$ so $v=\sqrt{2W/m}=43 m/s.$

[11] Exercises
Q: One car has twice the mass of a second car, but only half as much kinetic energy. When both cars increase their speed by 8.0 m/s, they then have the same kinetic energy. What were the original speeds of the two cars?
A: Let car 1 have mass $2m$ so car 2 has mass $m$. Let the kinetic energy of car 1 be $K$ so car 2 has kinetic energy $2K$. Then the original speeds of car 1 and 2 are given by $$v_1=\sqrt{\frac{2K}{2m}}=\sqrt{\frac Km}\qquad v_2=\sqrt{\frac{2(2K)}{m}}=2\sqrt{\frac Km}=2v_1$$ The final speeds of the cars are related by $$K_1=K_2\Rightarrow \frac12 (2m)v_1'^2=\frac12 mv_2'^2\Rightarrow v_2'=v_1'\sqrt 2\Rightarrow (v_2+8)=(v_1+8)\sqrt2$$ Plugging in $v_2=2v_1$, we have $(2v_1+8)=(v_1+8)\sqrt2\Rightarrow v_1=5.66 m/s$, and so $v_2=11.31 m/s$.

[12] Exercises
Q: A spring has a spring constant $k$ of 88.0 $ N/m$. How much must this spring be compressed to store 45.0 J of potential energy?
A: We have $U=(1/2)kx^2$ so $x=\sqrt{2U/k}=1.01 \unit m$.

[13] Exercises
Q: A 1.60-m-tall person lifts a 1.65-kg book off the ground so it is 2.20 m above the ground. What is the potential energy of the book relative to (a) the ground, and (b) the top of the person’s head? (c) How is the work done by the person related to the answers in parts (a) and (b)?
A: \begin{anum}
 \item We have $U=1.65\cdot 9.8\cdot 2.2=35.6 J$. 
 \item We have $U=1.65\cdot 9.8\cdot(2.2-1.6)=9.7 \unit {J}$.
 \item The work done by the person is the answer in part (a).
 \end{anum}

[14] Problems
Q: Spiderman uses his spider webs to save a runaway train moving about 60 km/h. His web stretches a few city blocks (500 m) before the 104-kg train comes to a stop. Assuming the web acts like a spring, estimate the effective spring constant.
A: The work done by the spring is $(1/2)kx^2.$ By the work-energy theorem this equals the change in kinetic energy of the train. Thus $$\frac12kx^2=\frac12mv^2\Rightarrow k=\frac{mv^2}{x^2}=0.16 N/m$$

[15] Problems
Q: A 36.0-kg crate, starting from rest, is pulled across a floor with a constant horizontal force of 225 N. For the first 11.0 m the floor is frictionless, and for the next 10.0 m the coefficient of friction is 0.20. What is the final speed of the crate after being pulled these 21.0 m?
A: The total work done by the external force is $225\cdot 21=4725 \unit J$. The work done by friction will be $-mg\mu_k d=-36\cdot9.8\cdot0.2\cdot10=-706 \unit J$. The net work done is then $W=4725-706=4019 \unit J$. This equals $(1/2)mv^2$ by the work-energy theorem, so $v=\sqrt{2W/m}=\sqrt{2\cdot 4019/36}=14.9 m/s.$

[16] Problems
Q: How high will a 1.85-kg rock go from the point of release if thrown straight up by someone who does 80.0 J of work on it?
A: The total energy inputted equals $mgh$, so $h=U/mg=80/(1.85\cdot9.8)=4.41 \unit m$.

[17] Problems
Q: In a certain library the first shelf is 15.0 cm off the ground, and the remaining four shelves are each spaced 38.0 cm above the previous one. If the average book has a mass of 1.40 kg with a height of 22.0 cm, and an average shelf holds 28 books (standing vertically), how much work is required to fill all the shelves, assuming the books are all laying flat on the floor to start?
A: If the shelf is at a height $h$, then the center of mass of the book is at a height $h+11$. Thus the heights of the books are 26 cm, 64 cm, 102 cm, 140 cm. Since we're putting an equal number of books on each shelf, the average height of the books is $(26+64+102+140)/2=81 cm$. Then the gravitational potential energy of all the books in the end is $U=4\cdot 28mgh_{av}=4\cdot 28\cdot 1.4\cdot 9.8\cdot 81=124 kJ.$

[18] Problems
Q: A 380-kg piano slides 2.9 m down a 25$^\circ$ incline and is kept from accelerating by a man who is pushing back on it parallel to the incline. Determine: (a) the force exerted by the man, (b) the work done on the piano by the man, (c) the work done on the piano by the force of gravity, and (d) the net work done on the piano. Ignore friction.
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[scale=0.25]{pianoman.png}
 \end{center}
A: \begin{anum}
 \item The force exerted by the man is $F=mg\sin\theta=380\cdot 9.8\cdot \sin(25)=1574 \unit N$. 
 \item The work done on the piano by the man is $W=-Fd=-4564 \unit J$, which is negative since the distance is in the opposite direction as the force.
 \item It makes more sense to do part (d) before (c). Notice that the speed of the piano doesn't change, so its kinetic energy doesn't change, and thus no net work is one on it. The work done on the piano by gravity is therefore exactly opposite that of the man: $4564 \unit J$. 
 \item Zero.
 \end{anum}

[19] Problems
Q: A mass $m$ is attached to a spring which is held stretched a distance $x$ by a force $F$ and then released. The spring pulls the mass to the left, towards its natural equilibrium length. Assuming there is no friction, determine the speed of the mass $m$ when the spring returns: (a) to its normal length ($x = 0$); (b) to half its original extension ($x/2$).
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[scale=0.25]{spring.png}
 \end{center}
A: \begin{anum}
 \item The potential energy change is $(1/2)kx^2$ and this equals the kinetic energy $(1/2)mv^2$, so $v=x\sqrt{k/m}$. Notice that $k=F/x$, so plugging this in yields $v=\sqrt{Fx/m}$. 
 \item The potential energy change here is only $$\Delta U=\frac12kx^2-\frac12k\left(\frac x2\right)^2=\frac38kx^2=\frac38 Fx$$ Thus setting this equal to $(1/2)mv^2$ yields $v=\sqrt{3Fx/4m}$. 
 \end{anum}

[20] Problems
Q: If it requires 6.0 J of work to stretch a particular spring by 2.0 cm from its equilibrium length, how much more work will be required to stretch it an additional 4.0 cm?
A: The spring constant can be found as $k=2U/x^2=2\cdot 6/0.02^2=30000 N/m$. The total energy contained in the spring when stretched 6.0 cm is then $(1/2)kx^2=(1/2)\cdot 30000\cdot 0.06^2=54 \unit J$. The additional work is thus $54-6=48 \unit J$.

[21] Challenge Problems
Q: The figure below shows a force field in 2 dimensions (i.e. the force at a given point is given by the vector pointing in that direction). Is this force conservative?
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[scale=1]{vc1.png}
 \end{center}
A: The force is not conservative, since when taking a path from $(1,0)$ to $(-1,0)$ directly along the $x$ axis the force do no work (they are all perpendicular), while if we follow the path along the unit circle then the force will do work. Thus the work done depends on the path taken, which means the force is not conservative.

------------------------------------------------------------------------------
LESSON: ap-08
------------------------------------------------------------------------------

[1] Exercises
Q: A novice skier, starting from rest, slides down an icy frictionless $8.0^\circ$ incline whose vertical height is 105 m. How fast is she going when she reaches the bottom?
A: By conservation of energy, we have
 $$\frac 12 mv^2 = mgh \implies v = \sqrt{2gh}.$$
 Plugging in the numbers, we get $v = \sqrt{2 \cdot 9.8 \cdot 105} = \boxed{45.4 m/s}$.

[2] Exercises
Q: In the high jump, the kinetic energy of an athlete is
transformed into gravitational potential energy without the
aid of a pole. With what minimum speed must the athlete
leave the ground in order to lift his center of mass 2.10 m
and cross the bar with a speed of 0.50 m/s?
A: Using the conservation of energy principle, the minimum speed required for the athlete to reach a height of 2.10 m with a final speed of 0.50 m/s is found by solving the energy conservation equation $\frac{1}{2} m v_i^2 = \frac{1}{2} m v_f^2 + mgh$. This yields $v_i = \boxed{6.44 \text{m/s}}$.

[3] Exercises
Q: Two railroad cars, each of mass 66,000 kg, are traveling 85 km/h toward each other. They collide head-on and come
to rest. How much thermal energy is produced in this
collision?
A: The thermal energy produced in the collision of two railroad cars, each with mass 66,000 kg moving at 85 km/h towards each other, is calculated from their initial kinetic energies. The total thermal energy is $\boxed{36,793,981.48 \text{J}}$.

[4] Exercises
Q: A 1.60-m-tall person lifts a 1.65-kg book off the ground
so it is 2.20 m above the ground. What is the potential
energy of the book relative to (a) the ground, and (b) the
top of the person’s head? (c) How is the work done by the
person related to the answers in parts (a) and (b)?
A: \begin{anum}
\item The potential energy of the book relative to the ground is $\boxed{35.57 \text{J}}$.
 \item Relative to the top of the person's head, the potential energy is $\boxed{9.70 \text{J}}$.
 \item The work done by the person is equal to the increase in the book's gravitational potential energy, corresponding to part (a) for lifting from the ground and the difference between (a) and (b) for lifting from the person's head to its final height.
\end{anum}

[5] Exercises
Q: A 0.48-kg ball is thrown with a speed of 8.8 m/s at an
upward angle of 36°. (a) What is its speed at its highest point,
and (b) how high does it go? (Use conservation of energy.)
A: \begin{anum}
\item At the highest point, the ball's speed is solely due to its horizontal component, as the vertical component of the velocity becomes zero. The horizontal speed, $v_x$, can be found with $v_x = v \cos(\theta)$, where $v = 8.8 \text{m/s}$ is the initial speed and $\theta = 36^\circ$ is the launch angle. Calculating this gives $v_x = 8.8 \cos(36^\circ) = \boxed{7.12 \text{m/s}}$.
 \item The height reached by the ball is determined by the conservation of energy principle. The initial vertical kinetic energy, $\frac{1}{2}mv_y^2$, where $v_y = v \sin(\theta)$, is converted into gravitational potential energy at the peak, $mgh$. Solving $\frac{1}{2}mv^2\sin^2(\theta) = mgh$ for $h$ gives the maximum height as $\boxed{1.37 \text{m}}$.
\end{anum}

[6] Exercises
Q: A 1200-kg car moving on a horizontal surface has speed $v = 85$ km/h
when it strikes a horizontal coiled spring and
is brought to rest in a distance of 2.2 m. What is the spring
stiffness constant of the spring?
A: The spring stiffness constant, $k$, is determined by setting the work done by the spring, $\frac{1}{2} k x^2$, equal to the kinetic energy of the car, $\frac{1}{2} m v^2$, where $v = 85 \text{km/h}$ is converted to meters per second and $x = 2.2 \text{m}$ is the compression distance. Solving $\frac{1}{2} k (2.2)^2 = \frac{1}{2} (1200)(85/3.6)^2$ for $k$ yields $k = \boxed{138,219.31 \text{N/m}}$.

[7] Exercises
Q: What should be the spring constant $k$ of a spring
designed to bring a 1200-kg car to rest from a speed of 95 km/h so that the occupants undergo a maximum acceleration of 4.0 $g$?
A: To design a spring that allows a car to decelerate at a maximum of $4.0g$ without exceeding this limit, we use the equation $F_{\text{max}} = ma_{\text{max}} = kx$. The maximum deceleration force, given by $ma_{\text{max}}$, where $a_{\text{max}} = 4.0g$ and $m = 1200 \text{kg}$, must be equated to the spring force, $kx$. The stopping distance $x$ can be inferred from the work-energy principle, as the work done by the spring equals the car's initial kinetic energy. Solving this system shows that the spring constant needed is $k = \boxed{5295.92 \text{N/m}}$.

[8] Exercises
Q: A 66-kg skier starts from rest at the top of a 1200-m-
long trail which drops a total of 230 m from top to bottom. At the bottom, the skier is moving 11.0 m/s. How much
energy was dissipated by friction?
A: The energy dissipated by friction is found by comparing the skier's initial potential energy, $PE = mgh$, where $h = 230 \text{m}$, to the final kinetic energy, $KE = \frac{1}{2}mv^2$, where $v = 11.0 \text{m/s}$. The difference, $PE_{\text{initial}} - KE_{\text{final}}$, accounts for the energy dissipated by friction, calculated as $\boxed{144,771 \text{J}}$.

[9] Exercises
Q: A projectile is fired at an upward angle of 38.0° from
the top of a 135-m-high cliff with a speed of 165 m/s. What
will be its speed when it strikes the ground below? (Use
conservation of energy.)
A: The projectile's speed when it strikes the ground is found using conservation of energy. The initial energy, combining kinetic and potential forms, is equal to the final kinetic energy at ground level:
 $$E_{\text{initial}} = \frac{1}{2} m v_0^2 + mg h_0,$$
 where $v_0 = 165 \text{m/s}$ and $h_0 = 135 \text{m}$. Solving for the final speed $v_f$ gives
 $$v_f = \boxed{172.84 \text{m/s}}.$$
 This result assumes that the only forces doing work are conservative; hence, air resistance is neglected.

[10] Exercises
Q: A 145-g baseball is dropped from a tree 12.0 m above
the ground. (a) With what speed would it hit the ground
if air resistance could be ignored?
A: The speed of the baseball hitting the ground, ignoring air resistance, is determined by converting its initial potential energy into kinetic energy:
 $$v = \sqrt{2gh},$$
 with $h = 12.0 \text{m}$. This gives a final speed of
 $$v = \boxed{15.34 \text{m/s}}.$$

[11] Exercises
Q: Continuing from the setup of the previous problem, do not ignore air resistance. If it actually hits the ground with a speed of 8.00 m/s, what is the average force of air resistance exerted on it?
A: With air resistance, the actual speed is lower, at $8.00 \text{m/s}$. The average force of air resistance is calculated by the difference in kinetic energy divided by the distance:
 $$F_{\text{air}} = \frac{\Delta KE}{h} = \frac{\frac{1}{2} m v_{\text{without air}}^2 - \frac{1}{2} m v_{\text{with air}}^2}{h},$$
 giving an average force of
 $$F_{\text{air}} = \boxed{1.04 \text{N}}.$$

[12] Exercises
Q: A skier traveling 11.0 m/s reaches the foot of a steady
upward 19° incline and glides 15 m up along this slope
before coming to rest. What was the average coefficient of
friction?
A: The average coefficient of friction as the skier glides up the incline is found by equating the work done against friction to the change in kinetic and potential energy. Solving for $\mu$ gives
 $$\mu = \frac{\Delta KE + \Delta PE}{mg\cos(\theta)d},$$
 resulting in a coefficient of
 $$\mu = \boxed{0.779}.$$

[13] Exercises
Q: A vertical spring (ignore its mass), whose spring constant is 875 N/m is attached to a table and is compressed down by 0.160 m. (a) What upward speed can it give to a
0.380-kg ball when released? (b) How high above its original
position (spring compressed) will the ball fly?
A: \begin{anum}
\item The speed given to the ball by the spring is found from
 $$v = \sqrt{\frac{k x^2}{m}},$$
 with $v = \boxed{7.68 \text{m/s}}$.
 \item The height above its original position the ball will reach is calculated by converting all the kinetic energy back into potential energy, giving
 $$h = \frac{k x^2}{2mg},$$
 resulting in $h = \boxed{3.00 \text{m}}$ above the original position.
\end{anum}

[14] Problems
Q: Jane, looking for Tarzan, is running at top speed (5.0 m/s)
and grabs a vine hanging vertically from a tall
tree in the jungle. How high can she swing upward? Does
the length of the vine affect your answer?
A: Since Jane swings upward, her kinetic energy at the point of grabbing the vine is converted to potential energy at the highest point of the swing. At the highest point of her swing, all kinetic energy is converted into potential energy, and she momentarily stops, so her kinetic energy is zero.

The kinetic energy when she grabs the vine is:
\[ KE_{\text{initial}} = \frac{1}{2} m v^2 \]

The potential energy at the highest point of her swing is:
\[ PE_{\text{final}} = mgh \]

Setting \( KE_{\text{initial}} = PE_{\text{final}} \), we get:
\[ \frac{1}{2} v^2 = gh \]

Solving for \( h \) (height she can swing upwards),
\[ h = \frac{v^2}{2g} \]
where \( v = 5.0 \text{m/s} \) and \( g = 9.81 \text{m/s}^2 \).

This calculation gives:
\[ h = \boxed{1.27 \text{m}}. \]

The length of the vine does not directly affect this calculation, as the energy conversion depends solely on her initial kinetic energy and the gravitational potential energy at the peak of her swing, assuming no energy is lost to air resistance or the vine's flexibility.

[15] Problems
Q: A roller-coaster car shown below is pulled up to
point 1 where it is released from rest. Assuming no friction,
calculate the speed at points 2, 3, and 4.
\begin{center}
 \includegraphics[width=5cm]{roller_coast.png}
\end{center}
A: Using the conservation of mechanical energy, the speed at points 2, 3, and 4 can be calculated from the potential energy at point 1 being converted to kinetic energy at lower points. Assuming no friction, the mechanical energy at the top equals the mechanical energy at the other points:

At point 2:
\[ v_2 = \sqrt{2g(h_1 - h_2)} = \sqrt{2 \times 9.81 \times (32 - 0)} = \boxed{25.06 \text{m/s}}. \]

At point 3:
\[ v_3 = \sqrt{2g(h_1 - h_3)} = \sqrt{2 \times 9.81 \times (32 - 26)} = \boxed{10.85 \text{m/s}}. \]

At point 4:
\[ v_4 = \sqrt{2g(h_1 - h_4)} = \sqrt{2 \times 9.81 \times (32 - 14)} = \boxed{18.79 \text{m/s}}. \]

This answers the third problem with no consideration of friction.

[16] Problems
Q: Suppose the roller-coaster car in the previous problem passes point 1
with a speed of 1.30 m/s. If the average force of friction is
equal to 0.23 of its weight, with what speed will it reach
point 2? The distance traveled is 45.0 m.
A: Using work-energy principles and the given distance of \(45.0 \text{m}\), the speed at point 2 is calculated as follows:
\[ v_2 = \sqrt{2 \left( \frac{1}{2} v_1^2 + g h_1 - 0.23 \cdot g \cdot d \right)} \]
With \( v_1 = 1.30 \text{m/s} \), \( g = 9.81 \text{m/s}^2 \), \( h_1 = 32 \text{m} \), and \( d = 45.0 \text{m} \), the speed at point 2 is:
\[ v_2 = \boxed{20.65 \text{m/s}}. \]
This accounts for the energy lost to friction over the specified distance.

[17] Problems
Q: Early test flights for the space shuttle used a "glider"
(mass of 980 kg including pilot). After a horizontal launch
at 480 km/h at a height of 3500 m, the glider eventually
landed at a speed of 210 km/h. (a) What would its landing
speed have been in the absence of air resistance? (b) What
was the average force of air resistance exerted on it if it
came in at a constant glide angle of 12° to the Earth’s surface?
A: \begin{anum}
\item In the absence of air resistance, the glider would have converted its potential energy entirely into kinetic energy. The final speed \( v_{\text{final}} \) can be calculated by energy conservation:
\[
\Delta KE + \Delta PE = 0 \implies \frac{1}{2} m v_{\text{final}}^2 - \frac{1}{2} m v_{\text{initial}}^2 + mg h = 0.
\]
Solving for \( v_{\text{final}} \),
\[
v_{\text{final}} = \sqrt{v_{\text{initial}}^2 + 2gh} = \sqrt{(133.33 \text{m/s})^2 + 2 \cdot 9.81 \text{m/s}^2 \cdot 3500 \text{m}} = 294.02 \text{m/s}.
\]
\item The average force of air resistance \( F_{\text{air}} \) can be calculated as:
\[
F_{\text{air}} = \frac{\Delta KE}{d},
\]
where \( \Delta KE = KE_{\text{initial}} - KE_{\text{final}} \), and \( d \) is the distance traveled, which can be calculated from the height \( h \) and the glide angle \( \theta \):
\[
d = \frac{h}{\sin \theta} = \frac{3500 \text{m}}{\sin 12^\circ}.
\]
Thus,
\[
F_{\text{air}} = \frac{7043750.00 \text{J}}{d} \approx 1006.25 \cdot (\sqrt{3} \left( \frac{1}{4} - \frac{\sqrt{5}}{4} \right) + \sqrt{\frac{\sqrt{5}}{8} + \frac{5}{8}}).
\]
\end{anum}

[18] Problems
Q: An engineer is designing a spring to be placed at the
bottom of an elevator shaft. If the elevator cable breaks
when the elevator is at a height $h$ above the top of the
spring, calculate the value that the spring constant $k$ should
have so that passengers undergo an acceleration of no more
than 5.0 g when brought to rest. Let $M$ be the total mass
of the elevator and passengers.
A: The energy conservation principle implies that the potential energy lost by the falling elevator converts entirely into the elastic potential energy stored in the spring. The equations set up are:
\[
Mg(h+x) = \frac{1}{2} k x^2,
\]
where \( x \) is the maximum compression of the spring, which also relates to the force by \( F = kx = Ma \). Here, \( a \) is the deceleration and is limited to \( 5g \). Solving for \( x \) in terms of \( k \),
\[
x = \frac{Ma}{k},
\]
Substituting \( x \) back into the energy equation, we get:
$$Mgh = \frac 12 \frac{M^2 a^2}{k} - \frac{M^2 ag}{k}.$$
Simplifying and solving for \( k \),
$$k = \frac{M^2a^2/2 - M^2ag}{Mgh} = \frac{Ma^2/2 - Mag}{gh}.$$
Plugging in $a = 5g$, we get
$$k = \frac{25g^2/2 - 5g^2}{g}\frac{M}{h} = \boxed{\frac{15}{2}\frac{Mg}{h}}.$$
This is the required spring constant to ensure a safe deceleration of no more than \( 5g \).
A: By the inelastic collision formula (making sure to make one of the velocities negative), $$v=\frac{m_1v_2+m_2v_2}{m_1+m_2}=\frac{110\cdot 2.5-82\cdot 5}{110+80}=-0.71 m/s$$ So in fact their mutual velocity is in the direction that the halfback was initially moving in. For the purposes of the speed though, we just take the magnitude, which is $0.71 m/s$.

[4] Exercises
Q: (a) A child in a boat throws a 5.30-kg package out horizontally with a speed of 10.0 m/s relative to the ground. Calculate the velocity of the boat immediately after, assuming it was initially at rest. The mass of the child is 24.0 kg and the mass of the boat is 35.0 kg. (b) What is the answer if the package was thrown with a speed 10 m/s relative to the boat?
A: \begin{anum}
 \item The total momentum is 0, since momentum is conserved. Thus the momentum of the box must exactly cancel the momentum of the child/boat after the box is thrown. The momentum of the box is $5.3\cdot 10=53 kg\cdot m/s$. This equals $(24+35)v$ where $v$ is the velocity of the child/boat. Solving, we get $v=53/(24+35)=0.90 m/s.$
 \item Let the magnitude of the box's velocity with respect to the ground be $v$. Then by the same logic as part (a), the magnitude of the boat's velocity would be $v(5.3/59)=0.09v$. The relative speed is then $v+0.09v=1.09v=10,$ which solves as $v=9.17 m/s$. Then the boat's velocity is just $0.09v=0.09\cdot 9.17=0.83 m/s$. 
 \end{anum}

[5] Exercises
Q: Two people, one of mass 85 kg and the other of mass 55 kg, sit in a rowboat of mass 58 kg. With the boat initially at rest, the two people, who have been sitting at opposite ends of the boat, 3.0 m apart from each other, now exchange seats. How far and in what direction will the boat move?
A: Let us find the distance of the front of the boat from the COM in both cases, since the COM will always be fixed. Set the origin to be the front of the boat, where the 85 kg person originally is. Then the COM is originally a distance $$d_0=\frac{85\cdot 0 + 58\cdot 1.5+55\cdot 3}{85+58+55}=1.27 \unit m$$ from the front of the boat and in the end it is a distance $$d_f=\frac{85\cdot 3 + 58\cdot 1.5+55\cdot 0}{85+58+55}=1.73 \unit m$$
 So the boat must move 0.46 m in the direction of where the 85 kg person originally is.

[6] Exercises
Q: A huge balloon and its gondola, of mass $M$, are in the air and stationary with respect to the ground. A passenger, of mass $m$, then climbs out and slides down a rope with speed $v$, measured with respect to the balloon. With what speed and direction (relative to Earth) does the balloon then move? What happens if the passenger stops?
A: Let positive velocity be downward. This is just conservation of momentum. Since the original momentum is 0, the momentum of the passenger must equal the momentum of the balloon, so the speed $u$ of the balloon is given by $$Mu+m(v+u)=0\Rightarrow u=-\frac{mv}{M+m}$$ The balloon moves upward. If the passenger stops, by conservation of momentum the balloon must also stop.

[7] Exercises
Q: An atomic nucleus initially moving at 320 m/s emits an alpha particle in the direction of its velocity, and the remaining nucleus slows to 280 m/s. If the alpha particle has a mass of 4.0 u and the original nucleus has a mass of 222 u, what speed does the alpha particle have when it is emitted?
A: By conservation of momentum, we have $$320\cdot 222=280\cdot 218 + 4v\Rightarrow v=2500 m/s$$

[8] Exercises
Q: A 0.145-kg baseball pitched horizontally at 27.0 m!s strikes a bat and pops straight up to a height of 31.5 m. If the contact time between bat and ball is 2.5 ms, calculate the average force between the ball and bat during contact.
A: If the change in $x$ momentum is $\Delta p_x$ and the change in $y$ momentum is $\Delta p_y$, then the overall change in momentum of the baseball is $\Delta p=\sqrt{(\Delta p_x)^2+(\Delta p_y)^2}$ by the Pythagorean theorem. Notice that $\Delta p_x=0.145\cdot 27=3.915 kg\cdot m/s$ (we ignore sign because we're squaring them anyway). We can find the initial $y$ velocity after contact by kinematics, because $v_y^2=2gh\Rightarrow v_y=\sqrt{2gh}=24.85 m/s$. Then, the change in $y$ momentum is $\Delta p_y=0.145\cdot 24.85=3.6 kg\cdot m/s$. Finally, we have $\Delta p=\sqrt{3.915^2+3.6^2}=5.32 kg\cdot m/s$. By Newton's Second law, we finally have $$F=\frac{\Delta p}{\Delta t}=\frac{5.32}{0.0025}=2128 N$$

[9] Exercises
Q: A 0.145-kg baseball pitched at 31.0 m/s is hit on a horizontal line drive straight back at the pitcher at 46.0 m/s. If the contact time between bat and ball is 5 ms, calculate the force (assumed to be constant) between the ball and bat.
A: The change in momentum is $\Delta p=0.145\cdot (31-(-46))=11.17 kg\cdot m/s$. The force is then $F=\Delta p/\Delta t=11.17/0.005=2230 N.$

[10] Exercises
Q: A golf ball of mass 0.045 kg is hit off the tee at a speed of 38 m!s. The golf club was in contact with the ball for 3.5 ms. Find (a) the impulse imparted to the golf ball, and (b) the average force exerted on the ball by the golf club.
A: \begin{anum}
 \item The change in momentum of the golf ball is the impulse. The initial momentum is zero, so the change in momentum (equivalently the impulse) is just $p=0.045\cdot 38=1.71 kg\cdot m/s$. 
 \item The average force is just $F=\Delta p/\Delta t=1.71/0.0035=489 N.$
 \end{anum}

[11] Exercises
Q: A 725-kg two-stage rocket is traveling at a speed of $6.60\times 10^3$ m/s away from Earth when a predesigned explosion separates the rocket into two sections of equal mass that then move with a speed of $2.80 \times 10^3$ m/s relative to each other along the original line of motion. (a) What is the speed and direction of each section (relative to Earth) after the explosion? (b) How much energy was supplied by the explosion? Hint: What is the change in kinetic energy as a result of the explosion?
A: \begin{anum}
 \item Let the final speed of the faster section be $v$, so the final speed of the slower section is just $v-2800.$ Then by conservation of momentum, $$725\cdot 6600=\frac{725}{2}\cdot v+\frac{725}{2}\cdot (v-2800)\Rightarrow v=8000 m/s$$so the speed of the slower part will be $8000-2800=5200 m/s$. Since they are both positive, the halves travel in the same initial direction as the complete rocket. 
 \item The initial kinetic energy is $$K_0=\frac12\cdot 725\cdot 6600^2=1.58\times 10^{10} J$$ and the final kinetic energy is $$K_f=\frac12\cdot \frac{725}{2}\cdot 8000^2+\frac12\cdot \frac{725}{2}\cdot 5200^2=1.65\times 10^{10} J$$By conservation of energy, the additional energy in the final state is the energy supplied by the explosion, so $$E=K_f-K_0=7\times 10^8 J$$
 \end{anum}

[12] Problems
Q: A 52-kg woman and a 72-kg man stand 10.0 m apart on nearly frictionless ice. (a) How far from the woman is their CM? (b) If each holds one end of a rope, and the man pulls on the rope so that he moves 2.5 m, how far from the woman will he be now? (c) How far will the man have moved when he collides with the woman?
A: \begin{anum}
 \item Using the woman as the origin, the distance is $$d=\frac{52\cdot 0+72\cdot 10}{52+72}=5.81 \unit m$$
 \item The rope ensures that there is no net force on the woman-man system because of Newton's Third Law. Thus their CM won't move. Hence the distance the woman moves is just $2.5\cdot 72/52=3.46 \unit m.$
 \item The man will have moved the distance to the CM, since they collide at the CM. If the woman was 5.81 m away from the CM this means the man was 4.19 m away, so that's the distance he moves.
 \end{anum}

[13] Problems
Q: The masses of the Earth and Moon are $5.98 \times 10^{24}$ kg and $7.35 \times 10^{22}$ kg, respectively, and their centers are separated by $3.84 \times 10^8$ m. (a) Where is the CM of the Earth–Moon system located? (b) What can you say about the motion of the Earth–Moon system about the Sun, and of the Earth and Moon separately about the Sun?
A: \begin{anum}
 \item We take the distance of the CM from the Earth, so we set the Earth as the origin. In this case $$d=\frac{5.98 \times 10^{24}\cdot 0 + 7.35 \times 10^{22}\cdot 3.84 \times 10^8}{5.98 \times 10^{24}+7.35 \times 10^{22}}=4.66\times 10^6 \unit m$$
 \item The Earth-Moon CM orbits around the Sun in a perfect ellipse, and the Earth and Moon rotate about this CM, so the Earth and Moon individually do not follow perfect ellipses but rather a rippled ellipse around the sun. 
 \end{anum}

[14] Problems
Q: A 0.25-kg skeet (clay target) is fired at an angle of 28$^\circ$ to the horizontal with a speed of 25 m/s. When it reaches the maximum height, $h$, it is hit from below by a 15-g pellet traveling vertically upward at a speed of 230 m/s. The pellet is embedded in the skeet. (a) How much higher, $\Delta h$, does the skeet go up? (b) How much extra distance, $\Delta x$, does the skeet travel because of the collision?
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[scale=0.25]{pellet}
 \end{center}
A: \begin{anum}
 \item We first use conservation of momentum in the inelastic collision to find the speed immediately after being hit. This is $$v=\frac{0.25\cdot 0+0.015\cdot 230}{0.25+0.015}=13 m/s$$By kinematics, the pellet then rises an additional $$\Delta h = \frac{v^2}{2g}=\frac{13^2}{2\cdot 9.8}=8.62 \unit m$$
 \item Note that the horizontal speed of the skeet is always $25\cdot \cos(28)=22.07 m/s$. The extra distance $\Delta x$ is equal to the extra time the skeet ends up in the air, times this horizontal speed. In the initial case the skeet just falls vertically from an initial vertical speed of 0, so we have $$h=\frac 12gt_1^2\Rightarrow t_1=\sqrt{\frac{2h}{g}}$$But now we need $h$, and we can find this via $h=v_y^2/2g=25^2\sin^2(28)/(2\cdot 9.8)=7.03 \unit m.$ So in the case where the skeet doesn't get hit it would move for an extra time $$t_1=\sqrt{\frac{2h}{g}}=\sqrt{\frac{2\cdot 7.03}{9.8}}=1.2 \unit s$$In the case where it does get hit, we have an initial velocity of 13 m/s upward, so by kinematics $$7.03=-13t_2+4.9t_2^2\Rightarrow t_2=3.11 \unit s$$ by the quadratic formula (we take the positive root because time has to be positive). Then the extra distance is $\Delta x = v_x(t_2-t_1)=22.07(3.11-1.2)=42 \unit m.$
 \end{anum}

[15] Problems
Q: A tennis ball of mass $m = 0.060$ kg and speed $v = 28$ m/s strikes a wall at a 45$^\circ$ angle and rebounds with the same speed at 45$^\circ$. What is the impulse (magnitude and direction) given to the ball?
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[scale=0.2]{thing}
 \end{center}
A: The vertical component of velocity is unchanged, so there is no change in the vertical momentum. However, the horizontal velocity flips; initially, it is $28\sin(45)=19.8 m/s$, and later it is $-19.8 m/s$ (defining the positive direction as rightward). Thus the change in momentum imparted to the ball is $p=0.06(19.8-(-19.8))=2.38 kg\cdot m/s$. This is equal to the impulse given to the ball, by definition. The impulse is applied by normal force, which points to the left.

[16] Problems
Q: Rain is falling at the rate of 2.5 cm/h and accumulates in a pan. If the raindrops hit at 8.0 m/s, estimate the force on the bottom of a $1.0 m^2$ pan due to the impacting rain which we assume does not bounce. Water has a mass of $1.00 \times 10^3$ kg per $ m^3$.
A: We have $F=\Delta p/\Delta t$. In time $\Delta t$, there is some amount of water that hits the pan and stops, resulting in a change in momentum of the water. Thus the pan exerts a force on the water, and the water exerts a force on the pan by Newton's Third Law. We first convert everything to SI units (meters and seconds), so the rate at which water accumulates is $(0.025 \text{m})/(3600 s)=6.94\times 10^{-6} m/s$. In time $\Delta t$, the volume of water that hits the pan is $(1 m^2)\cdot (6.94\times 10^{-6} m/s)\Delta t$, so the mass that hits the pan is $(1\times 10^3 kg/m^3)(1 m^2)\cdot (6.94\times 10^{-6} m/s)\Delta t$. The change in momentum is then $$\Delta p=(1\times 10^3 kg/m^3)(1 m^2)\cdot (6.94\times 10^{-6} m/s)\cdot(8 m/s)\Delta t=0.0555\Delta t$$Thus the force is just $\Delta p/\Delta t=0.0555 \unit N$.

[17] Problems
Q: With what impulse does a 0.50-kg newspaper have to be thrown to give it a velocity of 3.0 m/s?
A: The impulse equals the momentum, which is $I=p=0.5\cdot 3=1.5 kg\cdot m/s$.

[18] Problems
Q: A tennis ball of mass 1 kg is moving at speed $-1$ m/s when it is hit by a racket with the following force as a function of time. Estimate the final speed of the tennis ball. 
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[scale=0.2]{thing2}
 \end{center}
A: Since $I=F\Delta t$, the impulse is effectively the area under the curve. We can estimate the area (by counting the squares) to be around $5 N\cdot s$. Then the change in speed of the ball is just $5$ m/s, so the final speed is 4 m/s.

[19] Problems
Q: An internal explosion breaks an object, initially at rest, into two pieces, one of which has 1.5 times the mass of the other. If 5500 J is released in the explosion, how much kinetic energy does each piece acquire?
A: Let the mass of the smaller piece be $m$, so the other mass is $1.5m$. Note that the explosion has to conserve momentum, so the momentum of the smaller half has to equal the momentum of the larger half. Let the velocity of the smaller piece be $v$, so by conservation of momentum the other mass has speed $u$ given by $mv=1.5mu\Rightarrow u=0.67v$. We can thus find the total kinetic energy in terms of $m$ and $v$, which is $$5500=\frac12 mv^2+\frac12(1.5m)(0.67v)^2=\frac 12mv^2+\frac 23\cdot \frac12 mv^2=\frac 53 \cdot \frac 12mv^2$$Since the kinetic energy of the smaller piece is exactly $(1/2)mv^2$, the kinetic energy of that piece is just $5500\cdot (3/5)=3300 J$. Then the larger half has $2200 J$ of energy.

[20] Problems
Q: Car A hits car B (initially at rest and of equal mass) from behind while going 38 m/s. Immediately after the collision, car B moves forward at 15 m/s and car A is at rest. What fraction of the initial kinetic energy is lost in the collision
A: Let the mass of the cars be $m$. The kinetic energy before the collision is $(1/2)m\cdot 38^2$, and the kinetic energy after is $(1/2)m\cdot 15^2$. The kinetic energy lost is $(1/2)m(38^2-15^2)$, so the fraction is $$\frac{(1/2)m(38^2-15^2)}{(1/2)m\cdot 38^2}=0.844$$

[21] Problems
Q: A pendulum consists of a block $M$ hanging at the bottom end of a massless rod of length l, which has a frictionless pivot at its top end. A mass $m$, moving at speed $v$, hits the block $M$ and gets embedded in the block. What is the minimum $v$ so that the entire system can move through a half-circle as shown, and swing back around?
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[scale=0.2]{thing3}
 \end{center}
A: We analyze the situation in pieces. First, the collision should be analyzed via momentum, and then the swinging should be analyzed via conservation of energy. Since the bullet is embedded, the collision is inelastic, so the speed of the system right after the collision is found as $$u=\frac{mv}{M+m}$$ By conservation of energy, we can also find $u$ via $$\frac 12(M+m)u^2=(M+m)g(2l)\Rightarrow u=2\sqrt{gl}$$Plugging this in, we get $$v=\frac{2(M+m)}{m}\sqrt{gl}$$

[22] Problems
Q: A bullet of mass $m = 0.0010$ kg embeds itself in a wooden block with mass $M = 0.999$ kg, which then compresses a spring ($k = 140$ N/m) by a distance $x = 0.050$ m before coming to rest. The coefficient of kinetic friction between the block and table is $\mu = 0.50$. (a) What is the initial velocity (assumed horizontal) of the bullet? (b) What fraction of the bullet’s initial kinetic energy is dissipated (in damage to the wooden block, rising temperature, etc.) in the collision between the bullet and the block?
A: \begin{anum}
 \item By conservation of energy, the initial kinetic energy of the block-bullet system can be found via $$\frac 12(M+m)v^2=\frac 12 kx^2+(M+m)g\mu x\Rightarrow v= 0.917 m/s$$This is after the collision, so the initial speed $u$ of the bullet is found as $$v=\frac{mu}{M+m}\Rightarrow u=\frac{M+m}{m}v=917 m/s$$
 \item The initial kinetic energy is $(1/2)mu^2=420 J$ and the final kinetic energy is $(1/2)(M+m)v^2=0.42 J$. The fraction of the energy lost is then $(420-0.42)/420=0.999$. 
\end{anum}

Initially, the block is at the left end of the plank as shown but it suddenly gains a velocity $v_0$ to the right. What must $v_0$ be so that after a long time, the block will be at the right end of the plank?

[1] Exercises
Q: A rolling ball moves from $x_1 = 8.4 cm$ to $x_2 = –4.2 cm$ during the time from $t_1 = 3.0 \unit s$ to $t_2 = 6.1 \unit s$. What is its average velocity over this time interval?
A: (no solution in file)

[2] Exercises
Q: A person jogs eight complete laps around a 400-m track in a total time of 14.5 min. Calculate (a) the average speed and (b) the average velocity, in m/s.
A: \begin{anum}
 \item The speed is $s=8\cdot 400/(14.5\cdot 60)=3.68 m/s.$
 \item The net displacement after eight laps is 0, so the velocity is $0 m/s$. 
 \end{anum}

[3] Exercises
Q: Two locomotives approach each other on parallel tracks. Each has a speed of 155 km/h with respect to the ground. If they are initially 8.5 km apart, how long will it be before they reach each other?\begin{center}
 \includegraphics[scale=0.2]{trains}
 \end{center}
A: The rate at which the locomotives approach each other is the sum of their velocities, which is $155+155=310 km/h$. (Another way to see this is to shift into the reference frame of one of the trains. In such a reference frame one train is at rest, and the other approaches it at speed $310 km/h$.) Thus, using $v=\Delta x/\Delta t$, we can rearrange the equation so that $\Delta t = \Delta x/v=8.5/310=0.027 h$. For an answer in minutes, we get $\Delta t=60\cdot 0.027 = 1.65 min$.

[4] Exercises
Q: At highway speeds, a particular automobile is capable of an acceleration of about 1.8 m/s$^2$. At this rate, how long does it take to accelerate from 65 km/h to 120 km/h?
A: First, we convert the speeds to units of $ m/s$. Note that $1 km/h=1000 m/3600 s=(1/3.6) m/s$ (as a general rule, therefore, we can convert from $ km/h$ to $ m/s$ by dividing by 3.6). Using the definition of acceleration, $a=\Delta v/\Delta t$. We seek $\Delta t$, and are given $a$ and $\Delta v$. We simply rearrange, so $$\Delta t= \frac{\Delta v}{a}=\frac{1}{3.6}\cdot\frac{120-65}{1.8}=8.49 s$$

[5] Exercises
Q: A light plane must reach a speed of 35 m/s for takeoff. How long a runway is needed if the (constant) acceleration is 3.0 m/s$^2$?
A: We use the equation $v_f^2-v_0^2=2a\Delta x$. $v_f=35 m/s$, $v_0=0$, and $a=3.0 m/s^2$, so we can solve for $\Delta x$ (the required length of the runway) via $$\Delta x=\frac{v_f^2-v_0^2}{2a}=\frac{35^2-0^2}{2\cdot 3}=204 m$$

[6] Exercises
Q: A car slows down uniformly from a speed of 28.0 m/s to rest in 8.00 s. How far did it travel in that time?
A: Note that the average velocity during this time is $v=14 m/s$. Then, the distance can be found as $\Delta x = v\Delta t=14\cdot 8 = 112 m.$

[7] Exercises
Q: A space vehicle accelerates uniformly from 85 m/s at $t = 0$ to 162 m/s at $t = 10.0$ s. How far did it move between $t = 2.0$ s and $t = 6.0$ s?
A: The acceleration is found as $a=\Delta v/\Delta t = (162-85)/10=7.7 m/s^2$. We can then find the distance via $\Delta x = v_0t+\frac12at^2$, where $v_0$ corresponds to the velocity at $t=2$. The velocity can first be found via $v_0=85 + 2\cdot 7.7=100.4$. Then, we just plug in values: $$\Delta x=100.4\cdot 4+\frac12\cdot 7.7\cdot 4^2=463 m$$

[8] Exercises
Q: A 75-m-long train begins uniform acceleration from rest. The front of the train has a speed of 18 m/s when it passes a railway worker who is standing 180 m from where the front of the train started. What will be the speed of the last car as it passes the worker?
A: We can find the acceleration of the train via $v_f^2-v_0^2=2a\Delta x$ given the first piece of information, so $a=v_f^2/2\Delta x=18^2/(2\cdot 180)=0.9 m/s^2.$ We use the formula again to find the speed of the last car: $$v_f=\sqrt{v_0^2+2a\Delta x}=\sqrt{18^2+2\cdot 0.9\cdot 85}=21.8 m/s$$

[9] Exercises
Q: The figure below shows the velocity of a train as a function of time. (a) At what time was its velocity greatest? (b) During what periods, if any, was the velocity constant? (c) During what periods, if any, was the acceleration constant? (d) When was the magnitude of the acceleration greatest?
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[scale=0.2]{graf}
 \end{center}
A: \begin{anum}
 \item The graph shows the velocity so at about 48 s the velocity is greatest.
 \item The velocity is constant if it doesn't change, i.e. between 90 and 110 s.
 \item The acceleration on a $v-t$ graph is the slope, so we are looking for parts of the curve with constant slope, which is approximately from 0 to 40 s.
 \item The acceleration is greatest when the slope is the greatest in magnitude, or at around 77 s. 
 \end{anum}

[10] Exercises
Q: A ball player catches a ball 3.4 s after throwing it vertically upward. With what speed did he throw it, and what height did it reach?
A: Setting positive velocity to be upward, let the initial speed be $v$. Then, the final speed is $-v$, so $\Delta v = 2v$. By the definition of acceleration, $\Delta v =2v = gt\Rightarrow v=gt/2=16.7 m/s$. The height reached by the ball is then $\Delta x=v^2/2g=14.2 m.$

[11] Exercises
Q: A stone is thrown vertically upward with a speed of 24.0 m/s. (a) How fast is it moving when it is at a height of 13.0 m? (b) How much time is required to reach this height? (c) Why are there two answers to (b)?
A: \begin{anum}
 \item The speed of the stone is $v_f=\sqrt{v_0^2-2g\Delta x}=\sqrt{24^2-2\cdot 9.8\cdot 13}=17.9 m/s$. 
 \item The stone could either be going up or down, in which case $\Delta v$ is $24-17.9=6.1 m/s$ or $\Delta v = 24-(-17.9)=41.9 m/s$. Thus the time is either $\Delta t=\Delta v/g=6.1/9.8=0.62 s$ or $41.9/9.8=4.3 s.$
 \item The reason is described above. You could have also used the equation $\Delta x = v_0t+\frac12at^2$ to solve the problem, which would have resulted in two solutions because of the quadratic. 
 \end{anum}

[12] Exercises
Q: Roger sees water balloons fall past his window. He notices that each balloon strikes the sidewalk 0.83 s after passing his window. Roger’s room is on the third floor, 15 m above the sidewalk. (a) How fast are the balloons trav- eling when they pass Roger’s window? (b) Assuming the balloons are being released from rest, from what floor are they being released? Each floor of the dorm is 5.0 m high.
A: \begin{anum}
 \item We have $$\Delta x = v_0t+\frac12gt^2\Rightarrow v_0=\frac{\Delta x -\frac12gt^2}{t}=\frac{15-(1/2)\cdot9.8\cdot 0.83^2}{0.83}=14 m/s$$
 \item We have $$v_f^2-v_0^2=2g\Delta x \Rightarrow \Delta x =\frac{v_f^2}{2g}=\frac{14^2}{2\cdot9.8}=10 m$$Since each floor is 5 m high the balloons are being released from the 5th floor. 
 \end{anum}

[13] Problems
Q: The acceleration due to gravity on the Moon is about one- sixth what it is on Earth. If an object is thrown vertically upward on the Moon, how many times higher will it go than it would on Earth, assuming the same initial velocity?
A: We have $h=v_0^2/2g\Rightarrow h\propto 1/g$ (the symbol $\propto$ indicates proportionality, i.e. if $g$ goes up by a factor of 2 then $h$ will go down by a factor of 2). Thus if $g$ is smaller by $1/6$, $h$ increases by a factor of 6.

[14] Problems
Q: Pelicans tuck their wings and free-fall straight down when diving for fish. Suppose a pelican starts its dive from a height of 14.0 m and cannot change its path once com- mitted. If it takes a fish 0.20 s to perform evasive action, at what minimum height must it spot the pelican to escape? Assume the fish is at the surface of the water.
A: Imagine running time in reverse, so that the pelican has an initial speed $v$ upward given by the equation $v^2=2gh\Rightarrow v=\sqrt{2gh}=16.6 m/s$. We seek the height traveled by the pelican in 0.2 seconds in this reversed-time universe. We simply use $$\Delta x=v_0t-\frac12gt^2=16.6\cdot 0.2-\frac12\cdot 9.8\cdot 0.2^2=3.12 m$$

[15] Problems
Q: Suppose a car manufacturer tested its cars for front-end collisions by hauling them up on a crane and dropping them from a certain height. (a) Show that the speed just before a car hits the ground, after falling from rest a vertical distance $H$, is given by $\sqrt{2gH}$. What height corresponds to a collision at (b) 35 km/h? (c) 95 km/h?
A: \begin{anum}
 \item We use $v_f^2-v_0^2=2gH$, which in using using $v_0=0$ we get $v_f=\sqrt{2gH}$. 
 \item Solving for $H$ in terms of $v_f$, we get $H=v_f^2/2g$. We first convert the speed to m/s, which is done by dividing the km/h value by 3.6: 9.72 m/s. Plugging in, we have $H=9.72^2/(2\cdot 9.8)=4.8 m.$
 \item Doing the same thing for the other speed, $H=26.4^2/(2\cdot 9.8)=35.5 m.$
 \end{anum}

[16] Problems
Q: A baseball is seen to pass upward by a window with a vertical speed of 14 m/s. If the ball was thrown by a person 18 m below on the street, (a) what was its initial speed, (b) what altitude does it reach, (c) when was it thrown, and (d) when does it reach the street again?
A: \begin{anum}
 \item We have $v_f^2-v_0^2=-2gh\Rightarrow v_0=\sqrt{v_f^2+2gh}=\sqrt{14^2+2\cdot9.8\cdot 18}=23.4 m/s$. 
 \item Setting $v_f=0$ and using $v_0$ from before, $H=v_0^2/2g=28 \unit m$. 
 \item Using the definition of acceleration, the ball was thrown $\Delta t=\Delta v/g=(23.4-14)/9.8=0.96 s$ before it passed the window. 
 \item The total time in air is $\Delta t=2v_0/g=4.78 s$. Subtracting the answer from part (c) yields the answer to be 3.82 s after the ball passes the window. 
 \end{anum}

[17] Problems
Q: A falling stone takes 0.31 s to travel past a window 2.2 m tall. From what height above the top of the window did the stone fall?
A: We have $\Delta x = v_0t+\frac12gt^2\Rightarrow v_0=(\Delta x -\frac12gt^2)/t=(2.2 -\frac12\cdot 9.8\cdot 0.31^2)/0.31=5.58 m/s.$ (This is the speed of the stone when it it passes the top of the window.) Now, we just have $H=v^2/2g=1.59 m$ above the window.

[18] Problems
Q: A rock is dropped from a sea cliff, and the sound of it striking the ocean is heard 3.4 s later. If the speed of sound is 340 m/s, how high is the cliff?
A: Let the height of the cliff be $H$ and the speed of sound $c$. Since the initial velocity is 0, the time it takes to fall is found from $H=\frac12gt_1^2\Rightarrow t_1=\sqrt{2H/g}$. After that, the time it takes for the sound to travel is $t_2=H/c$. Our equation to solve for $H$ is thus (with $t=3.4 \unit s$): $$\sqrt{\frac{2H}{g}}+\frac{H}{c}=t$$ We now solve algebraically: \begin{align*}
 \sqrt{\frac{2H}{g}}&=t-\frac Hc\\
 \frac{2H}{g}&=t^2+\frac{H^2}{c^2}-\frac{2Ht}{c}\\
 0&=\frac{H^2}{c^2}-\left(\frac {2t}c+\frac 2g\right)H+t^2\\
 0&=H^2-\left(2ct+\frac{2c^2}{g}\right)H+c^2t^2\\
 0&=H^2-25903 H+1336336\
 \end{align*}Solving the quadratic, $H=51.69 \unit m$.

[19] Challenge Problems — $F=ma$
Q: A train starts from city $A$ and stops in city $B$. The distance between the cities is $s$. The train's maximal acceleration is $a_1$ and its maximum deceleration is $a_2$ (in absolute value). What is the shortest time in which the train can travel between $A$ and $B$?
\begin{enumerate}[label=(\Alph*)]
 \item $2\sqrt{\frac{s}{a_1+a_2}}$
 \item $2\sqrt{\frac{s}{\sqrt{a_1a_2}}}$
 \item $\sqrt{2\frac{s(a_1+a_2)}{a_1a_2}}$
 \item $\sqrt{\frac{2sa_2}{a_1(a_1+a_2)}}$
 \item $\sqrt{\frac{s\sqrt{a_1a_2}}{(a_1+a_2)^2}}$
 \end{enumerate}
A: We accelerate initially at max and decelerate maximally, meaning $a_1 t_1 = v_{\text{max}} = a_2 t_2$. Thus, if the total travel time is $t$, then using ratios, it accelerates for $\frac{a_2}{a_1+a_2}t$ and decelerates for $\frac{a_1}{a_1+a_2}t$. And each segment covers a distance of $\frac 12 a_i t_i^2$, so
 $$\frac 12 a_1 \left(\frac{a_2}{a_1+a_2}t\right)^2 + \frac 12 a_2 \left(\frac{a_1}{a_1+a_2}t\right)^2 = \frac 12 \frac{a_1a_2}{a_1+a_2}t^2 = s.$$
 So, solving for $t$, we get $t = \sqrt{2 \frac{s(a_1+a_2)}{a_1a_2}}$, which corresponds to answer <strong>(C)</strong>.

[20] Challenge Problems
Q: Jerk is defined as change in acceleration over time and has units of m/s$^3$. A car, initially at rest, experiences a jerk of 5 m/s$^3$ for 10 seconds, and then a jerk of $-3$ m/s$^3$ for a time $t$. What is $t$ such that the car eventually returns to rest?
A: Jerk is the rate of change of the rate of change of velocity. We also know that acceleration is the rate of change of the rate of change of position:
 $$j = \frac{\Delta a}{\Delta t}, a = \frac{\Delta v}{\Delta t}, \quad a = \frac{\Delta v}{\Delta t}, v = \frac{\Delta x}{\Delta t}.$$
 These equations are exactly the same, with just the variable names changed! So, we can rewrite our position kinematics equations by making the substitution $j \leftrightarrow a, a \leftrightarrow v, v\leftrightarrow x$ (the deeper reason behind this working is calculus). So, we have $$\Delta v = a_0 \Delta t + \frac{1}{2} j \Delta t^2.$$ Thus, after the first segment, the car has velocity $\frac{5 \cdot 10^2}{2} = 250 m/s$ and acceleration $50 m/s^2$. Now we plug that into the equation we developed again,
 $$0 - 250 m/s = 50t - \frac{3}{2} t^2.$$
 Solving the quadratic, we get $t \approx 37.7 s.$

[1] Exercises
Q: A car is driven 225 km west and then 98 km southwest
($45^\circ$). What is the displacement of the car from the point
of origin (magnitude and direction)? Draw a diagram.
A: We can break it down into cartesian components, and we get
 $$\sqrt{(225 + 98/\sqrt 2)^2 + (98/\sqrt 2)^2} \approx 302 km.$$
 The direction is slightly south of west.

[2] Exercises
Q: The summit of a mountain, 2450 m above base camp, is measured on a map to be 4580 m horizontally from the camp in a direction $38.4^\circ$ west of north. What are the components of the displacement vector from camp to summit? What is its magnitude? Choose the x axis east, y axis north, and z axis up.
A: To find the components of the displacement vector, we calculate:
 \begin{align*}
 d_x &= -4580 \sin(38.4^\circ) \\
 d_y &= 4580 \cos(38.4^\circ) \\
 d_z &= 2450
 \end{align*}
 The magnitude of the displacement vector is:
 \[d = \sqrt{(-4580 \sin(38.4^\circ))^2 + (4580 \cos(38.4^\circ))^2 + (2450)^2} \approx 5194 m.\]

[3] Exercises
Q: A tiger leaps horizontally from a 7.5-m-high rock with a speed of 3.0 m/s. How far from the base of the rock will she land?
A: The time of flight is given by:
 \[t = \sqrt{\frac{2 \times 7.5}{9.8}}\]
 The distance from the base of the rock is:
 \[d = 3.0 \times \sqrt{\frac{2 \times 7.5}{9.8}} = 3.7 m\]

[4] Exercises

[19] Problems
Q: A cyclist intends to cycle up a 7.50° hill whose vertical
height is 125 m. The pedals turn in a circle of diameter
36.0 cm. Assuming the mass of bicycle plus person is
75.0 kg, (a) calculate how much work must be done against
gravity. (b) If each complete revolution of the pedals
moves the bike 5.10 m along its path, calculate the average
force that must be exerted on the pedals tangent to their
circular path. Neglect work done by friction and other
losses.
A: \begin{anum}
\item The work done against gravity while cycling up a hill is:
\[
W = mgh = 75.0 \text{kg} \cdot 9.81 \text{m/s}^2 \cdot 125 \text{m} = 91968.75 \text{J}.
\]
\item The average force exerted on the pedals, taking into account the actual travel along the slope and pedal mechanics:
\[
\text{Slope length} = \frac{125 \text{m}}{\sin(7.50^\circ)} \approx 957.63 \text{m}.
\]
Given each pedal revolution moves the bike 5.10 m,
\[
\text{Revolutions} = \frac{957.63 \text{m}}{5.10 \text{m}} \approx 187.77.
\]
\[
d = 3.1416 \cdot 0.36 \text{m} \cdot 187.77 = 213.53 \text{m}.
\]
\[
F = \frac{91968.75 \text{J}}{213.53 \text{m}} \approx 430.54 \text{N}.
\]
\end{anum}

[20] Challenge Problems
Q: A block of mass $m$ lies on the top of a fixed cylinder of radius $R$. It is given an infinitesimal kick to the right and begins sliding along the outside of the cylinder. At what angle $\theta$ from the top of the cylinder does the block lose contact?
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[width=4cm]{block_cylinder.png}
 \end{center}
A: Again, we use the idea that just before losing contact, the normal force is equal to $0$. So analyzing forces at the position $\theta$, with no normal force $$\frac{mv^2}{R} = mg \cos \theta.$$ Similarly, by conservation of energy, $$\frac 12 mv^2 = mg R - mg R \cos \theta.$$ Plugging in the first equation into the second, we get $$\frac 32 mg R \cos \theta = mgR \implies \cos \theta = \frac 23,$$ so we get $\boxed{\theta = \cos^{-1}(2/3)}.$

LESSON: ap-09
------------------------------------------------------------------------------

[1] Exercises
Q: What is the magnitude of the momentum of a 28 g sparrow flying with a speed of 8.4 m/s?
A: We have $p=mv=0.028\cdot 8.4=0.319 kg\cdot m/s$.

[2] Exercises
Q: A constant friction force of 25 N acts on a 65-kg skier for 15 s on level snow. What is the skier’s change in velocity?
A: The impulse provided is $I=F\Delta t=375 N\cdot s$ and this is equal to the change in momentum $\Delta p$. The change in velocity is then $\Delta v=\Delta p/m=5.77 m/s$.

[3] Exercises
Q: A 110-kg tackler moving at 2.5 m/s meets head-on (and holds on to) an 82-kg halfback moving at 5.0 m/s. What will be their mutual speed immediately after the collision?
{center}
 There is a quick solution by using conservation of momentum.
A: By conservation of momentum, the blocks must end up moving together at a speed $v = mv_0/(M + m)$. Their combined energy is therefore less, and the lost energy is equal to the work done by friction, $W_f = mg\mu L$. Hence,\begin{align*}
 mg\mu L&=\frac12mv_0^2-\frac12(M+m)\left(\frac{mv_0}{M+m}\right)^2\\
 mg\mu L&=\frac12mv_0^2-\frac12\frac{m^2}{M+m}v_0^2\\
 mg\mu L&=\frac12mv_0^2\left(1-\frac{m}{M+m}\right)\\
 L&=\frac{v_0^2}{2g\mu}\frac{M}{M+m}\\
 \Rightarrow v_0&=\boxed{\sqrt{2g\mu L\frac{M+m}{M}}}
 \end{align*}

LESSON: ap-10
