[1] Exercises
Q: A 2 kg mass moving at 5 m/s to the right and a 4 kg mass moving at 3 m/s to the left are left with no external forces on them. After a long time, what is the velocity of the center of mass of the system?
A: The velocity of the center of mass of the system is calculated using the formula:
 $$ v_{\text{CM}} = \frac{m_1 v_1 + m_2 v_2}{m_1 + m_2} = \frac{(2 \text{kg})(5 \text{m/s}) + (4 \text{kg})(-3 \text{m/s})}{2 \text{kg} + 4 \text{kg}}. $$
 This gives the velocity of the center of mass as
 $$ v_{\text{CM}} = \boxed{-\frac{1}{3} \text{m/s}}. $$

[2] Exercises
Q: A billiard ball weighing 0.55 kg is traveling at a speed of 2.2 m/s when it engages in a perfectly elastic collision with another billiard ball of the same mass, which is initially at rest. Determine the speeds of both billiard balls following the collision.
A: In a perfectly elastic collision, kinetic energy and momentum are conserved. Since the masses are equal and one ball starts at rest, the velocities are swapped, as discussed in lecturea and the notes above. Thus, the speeds of the billiard balls following the collision are
 $$ v_{1,\text{final}} = \boxed{0 \text{m/s}}, \quad v_{2,\text{final}} = \boxed{2.2 \text{m/s}}. $$

[3] Exercises
Q: A 0.5 kg soccer ball is kicked towards a goal and collides with a 0.3 kg stationary basketball. After the collision, the soccer ball moves at a speed of 2 m/s, while the basketball rolls away at 3 m/s. Calculate the coefficient of restitution for the collision between the two balls.
A: To determine the coefficient of restitution between the soccer ball and the basketball, we first calculate the initial velocity of the soccer ball using the conservation of momentum:
 $$m_{\text{soccer}} \cdot v_{\text{soccer, initial}} = m_{\text{soccer}} \cdot v_{\text{soccer, final}} + m_{\text{basket}} \cdot v_{\text{basket, final}},$$
 which gives \( v_{\text{soccer, initial}} = 3.8 \text{m/s} \). The coefficient of restitution is then calculated as:
 $$e = \frac{v_{\text{basket, final}} - v_{\text{soccer, final}}}{v_{\text{soccer, initial}} - v_{\text{basket, initial}}} = \boxed{0.263}.$$

[4] Exercises
Q: Two billiard balls of equal mass undergo a perfectly
elastic head-on collision. If one ball’s initial speed was
2.00 m/s, and the other’s was 3.60 m/s in the opposite
direction, what will be their speeds and directions after the collision?
A: In the case of the two billiard balls undergoing a perfectly elastic head-on collision, where both balls have equal mass, the velocities are simply exchanged due to the conservation of momentum and kinetic energy:
 $$v_{1, \text{final}} = -3.60 \text{m/s}, \quad v_{2, \text{final}} = 2.00 \text{m/s}.$$
 Thus, the first ball moves at \(-3.60 \text{m/s}\) and the second at \(2.00 \text{m/s}\) after the collision.

[5] Exercises
Q: A 1.5 kg bowling ball moving at 4 m/s comes into contact with a 0.5 kg stationary pin in a bowling alley. Following the impact, the bowling ball slows down to 3 m/s, whereas the pin shoots forward at 5 m/s. Determine the coefficient of restitution for this collision.
A: For the bowling ball and pin collision, using the coefficient of restitution equation:
 $$e = \frac{v_{\text{pin, final}} - v_{\text{bowling, final}}}{v_{\text{bowling, initial}} - v_{\text{pin, initial}}} = \boxed{0.5}.$$
 This coefficient of restitution indicates how much velocity is conserved in the collision.

[6] Exercises
Q: A radioactive nucleus at rest decays into a second
nucleus, an electron, and a neutrino. The electron and
neutrino are emitted at right angles and have momenta of
$9.6 \times 10^{-23}$ kgm/s and $6.2 \times 10^{-23}$ kgm/s respectively. Determine the magnitude and the direction of the momentum of the second (recoiling) nucleus.
A: In the decay problem, the momentum of the recoiling nucleus must conserve the total momentum vector sum of the electron and neutrino, which are at right angles:
 $$p_{\text{recoil}} = \sqrt{p_{\text{electron}}^2 + p_{\text{neutrino}}^2} = \boxed{1.14 \times 10^{-22} \text{kgm/s}},$$
 and the direction with respect to the electron's momentum is calculated as:
 $$\theta = \arctan\left(\frac{p_{\text{neutrino}}}{p_{\text{electron}}}\right) \approx \boxed{32.86^\circ}.$$

[7] Exercises
Q: A 0.6 kg tennis ball moving at 4 m/s collides elastically with a stationary 0.8 kg tennis ball. What are the velocities of the two tennis balls following the collision?
A: For an elastic collision, we use the conservation of momentum and kinetic energy. It is best to work in the center of mass frame, in which we have
$$v_{1,i} = \frac{16}{7}, v_{2,i} = -\frac{12}{7}.$$
Thus, they reversed, and adding back $v_{\text{cm}} = \frac{12}{7} m/s$, we get 
Solving these for the velocities of the tennis balls, we get:
\[
v_{1, \text{final}} = -\frac{4}{7} \text{m/s}, \quad v_{2, \text{final}} = \frac{24}{7} \text{m/s}.
\]

[8] Exercises
Q: A ball of mass m makes a head-on elastic collision with a second ball (at rest) and rebounds with a speed equal to 0.450 its original speed. What is the mass of the second ball?
A: We know from the problem statement that the rebound speed is 0.450 times the original speed. For an elastic collision where one ball is initially at rest, the final speeds are governed by the masses:
\[
v_{\text{final, 1}} = \frac{m_1 - m_2}{m_1 + m_2} v_{\text{initial, 1}}
\]
Setting this equal to \(-0.450 v_{\text{initial, 1}}\), we find that:
\[
m_2 = 2.64m_1
\]
So, the mass of the second ball is approximately \(2.64\) times the mass of the first.

[9] Exercises
Q: A 1.2 kg remote-controlled car traveling at 2 m/s on a smooth track hits a stationary 0.9 kg remote-controlled car in a perfectly elastic collision. Determine the speeds of both remote-controlled cars after the collision.
A: Applying conservation of momentum and kinetic energy, moving into the center of mass frame, we have
$$v_{1,i}' = \frac 67, v_{2,i}' = -\frac 87.$$
Thus, reversing their signs and adding on $v_{\text{cm}} = \frac 87$, we get
$$v_{1,f} = \frac 27, v_{2,f} = \frac {16}7.$$

[10] Problems
Q: A 0.280-kg croquet ball makes an elastic head-on
collision with a second ball initially at rest. The second
ball moves off with half the original speed of the first ball.
(a) What is the mass of the second ball? (b) What fraction
of the original kinetic energy ($\Delta KE/KE$) gets transferred
to the second ball?
A: \begin{anum}
\item Since the collision is elastic, we have that their relative speeds before and after the collisions are the same. Thus, the first ball's final speed must be $-v/2$, where $v$ is the initial velocity. Then, by conservation of momentum,
$$m_1v = -m_1v/2 + m_2v/2 \implies \frac 32 m_1 v = \frac 12 m_2 v.$$
Thus, $m_2$ is three times the mass of $m_1$, $0.840 kg$.
\item The final kinetic energy of the second ball is
$$KE_2 = \frac 12 (3m_1) (v/2)^2 = \frac 12 \frac 34 m_1 v^2,$$
so $\frac{\Delta KE}{KE} = \frac 34$.
\end{anum}

[11] Problems
Q: Billiard ball A of mass $m_A = 0.120$ kg moving with
speed $v_A = 2.80$ m/s strikes ball B, initially at rest, of
mass $m_B = 0.140$ kg. As a result of the collision, ball A
is deflected off at an angle of $30.0^\circ$ with a speed $v_A' = 2.10$ m/s. (a) Taking the x axis to be the original
direction of motion of ball A, write down the equations expressing the conservation of momentum for the components in the x and y directions separately. (b) Solve these equations for the speed, $v_B',$ and angle, $\theta_B'$, of ball B after
the collision. Do not assume the collision is elastic.
A: \begin{anum}
\item The equations expressing the conservation of momentum in the x and y directions separately are:
\begin{equation*}
m_A v_A = m_A v_{A}' \cos(30.0^\circ) + m_B v_{B}' \cos(\theta_B')
\end{equation*}
\begin{equation*}
0 = m_A v_{A}' \sin(30.0^\circ) + m_B v_{B}' \sin(\theta_B')
\end{equation*}
\item To solve these equations for the speed $v_B'$ and angle $\theta_B'$ of ball B after the collision, we can use the fact that the y-component of the momentum must be zero (since ball A was initially moving only in the x direction and there are no external forces in the y direction). This gives us two equations with two unknowns, which can be solved simultaneously to find $v_B'$ and $\theta_B'$.
\end{anum}

[12] Problems
Q: Two bumper cars in an amusement park ride collide elastically as one approaches the other directly from the rear. Car A has a mass of 435 kg and car B 495 kg,
owing to differences in passenger mass. If car A approaches
at 4.50 m/s and car B is moving at 3.70 m/s calculate
(a) their velocities after the collision, and (b) the change in momentum of each.
\begin{center}
 \includegraphics[width=5cm]{masdfasdfomentum.png}
\end{center}
A: \begin{anum}
\item The best way to calculate this is to move into the center of mass frame, where
 $$v_A' = 0.426, v_B' = -0.374,$$
 with $v_{\text{cm}} = 4.074 m/s$. So, reversing their signs and adding on the center of mass velocity,
 $$v_{A,f} = 3.65 m/s,v_{B,f} = 4.45 m/s.$$
 \item This is simply calculating the change in velocity of each and multiplying by the mass,
 $$\Delta p_A = m_A\Delta v_A = -370 kgm/s, \Delta p_B = m_B \Delta v_B = 370 kgm/s.$$
 Note that they cancel each other out, as desired.
\end{anum}

[13] Problems
Q: Two balls, of masses $m_A = m_B$ are suspended as shown below. The lighter ball is pulled away to a $66^\circ$ angle with the vertical and released. (a) What is the velocity of the lighter ball before impact? (b) What is the velocity of each ball after the elastic collision? (c) What
will be the maximum
height of each ball after
the elastic collision?
\begin{center}
 \includegraphics[width=5cm]{bruhhh.png}
\end{center}
A: \begin{anum}
\item Using conservation of energy, we have
 $$\frac 12 mv^2 = mg R (1 - \cos \theta) \implies v = \sqrt{2gR(1- \cos \theta)} = 2.02 m/s.$$
 \item Because they are equal mass, they will just exchange velocities, so $$v_{A,f} = 0, v_{B,f} = 2.02 m/s.$$
 \item By symmetry, the B ball will go up to $66^\circ$, which corresponds to $21 cm$ up.
\end{anum}

[14] Problems
Q: A novice pool player is faced with the corner pocket shot
shown below. Relative dimensions are also shown.
Should the player worry that this might be a “scratch shot,”
in which the cue ball will
also fall into a pocket?
Give details. Assume
equal-mass balls and an
elastic collision. Ignore spin. <em>Hint:</em> the challenge problem may be helpful for this problem.
\begin{center}
 \includegraphics[width=5cm]{sractch.png}
\end{center}
A: From the challenge problem, we know that when equal masses elastically collide they go off at 90 degree angles to one another. Drawing lines from the left pocket to the right pocket, we have that they are similar because the ratio of their legs are both $1:\sqrt{3}$. Thus, the two lines form a 90 degree angle with each other, meaning that the player <em>should</em> worry about a scratch shot.

[15] Challenge Problems
Q: A 0.5 kg billiard ball (A) moving at 2 m/s collides perfectly elastically with a stationary 0.5 kg billiard ball (B) and moves off at an angle $\theta$ above the horizontal while ball B moves off at $\phi $ below the horizontal. Prove that $\theta + \phi = 90^\circ$.
A: By conservation of momentum, the sum of the momentum vectors of the two balls must add to the original momentum vector of the first ball. However, since the masses of the balls are equal, this is equivalent to the final velocity vectors of the two adding to the original velocity vectors. This is shown graphically below.
\begin{center}
 \includegraphics[width=5cm]{velocity_addition.png}
\end{center}
 Furthermore, by conservation of energy, we have $v_1^2 + v_2^2 = v_i^2$, so by pythagorean's theorem, the two final velocity vectors must form right angles with each other, and we are done.

------------------------------------------------------------------------------
LESSON: ap-11
------------------------------------------------------------------------------

[1] Exercises
Q: If a particle undergoes SHM with amplitude 0.21 m, what is the total distance it travels in one period?
A: We swing back and forth for a distance $4\cdot 0.21=0.84 m$.

[2] Exercises
Q: An elastic cord is 61 cm long when a weight of 75 N hangs from it but is 85 cm long when a weight of 210 N hangs from it. What is the ``spring" constant k of this elastic cord?
A: Let the rest length be $l$. Then, by $F=kx$, we have $75=k(0.61-l)$ and $210 = k(0.85-l)$. Then, $k=(210-75)/(0.85-0.61)=562.5 N/m$.

[3] Exercises
Q: A fisherman’s scale stretches 3.6 cm when a 2.4-kg fish hangs from it. (a) What is the spring stiffness constant and (b) what will be the amplitude and frequency of oscillation if the fish is pulled down 2.1 cm more and released so that it oscillates up and down?
A: \begin{anum}
 \item We have $k=F/x=2.4\cdot 9.8/0.036=653 N/m$. 
 \item The new equilibrium position is that at which the fish is stretched. The amplitude of oscillation is then 2.1 cm, and the oscillation frequency is $\omega=\sqrt{k/m}=16.5 rad/s$.
 \end{anum}

[4] Exercises
Q: A 1.65-kg mass stretches a vertical spring 0.215 m. If the spring is stretched an additional 0.130 m and released, how long does it take to reach the (new) equilibrium position again?
A: The force exerted by the $1.65 \si{\kilo\gram}$ mass when the spring is stretched by $0.215 \si{\meter}$ is $F = mg = = 16.17 \si{\newton}$. So, $k \approx \frac{16.17 \si{\newton}}{0.215 \si{\meter}} \approx 75.14 \si{\newton\per\meter}$. The period of oscillation is $T = 2\pi \sqrt{\frac{m}{k}} = 2\pi \sqrt{\frac{1.65}{75.14}} \approx 0.931 \si{\second}$. It takes a quarter of the period to reach the new equilibrium position, so the time we're looking at is $t=T/4=0.233 s$.

[5] Exercises
Q: It takes a force of 91.0 N to compress the spring of a toy popgun 0.175 m to ``load" a 0.160-kg ball. With what speed will the ball leave the gun if fired horizontally?
A: The spring constant can be found via $k=F/x=91/0.175=520$. Then, we have the kinetic energy equal to the potential energy stored in the spring, so $$\frac 12 mv^2=\frac 12 kx^2\Rightarrow v=x\sqrt{\frac km}=0.175\sqrt{\frac{520}{0.16}}=9.97 m/s$$

[6] Exercises
Q: A mass resting on a horizontal, frictionless surface is attached to one end of a spring; the other end is fixed to a wall. It takes 3.6 J of work to compress the spring by 0.13 m. If the spring is compressed, and the mass is released from rest, it experiences a maximum acceleration of 12 m/s$^2$. Find the value of (a) the spring constant and (b) the mass.
A: \begin{anum} \item The work becomes the potential energy of the spring, so $$W=\frac 12 kx^2\Rightarrow k=\frac{2W}{x^2}=\frac{2\cdot 3.6}{0.13^2}=426 N/m$$
 \item The force exerted at maximum compression is then $F=kx$. This induces an acceleration $a$, so $ma=kx\Rightarrow m=kx/a=426\cdot 0.13/12=4.6 kg.$ \end{anum}

[7] Exercises
Q: A pendulum has a period of 1.85 s on Earth. What is its period on Mars, where the acceleration of gravity is about 0.37 that on Earth?
A: On the Earth, the pendulum has period $T=2\pi \sqrt{l/g_E}$ where $g_E$ is the gravity on Earth. Then, on Mars it will have a period $$T'=2\pi\sqrt{\frac{l}{0.37 g_E}}=\frac{T}{\sqrt{0.37}}=\frac{1.85}{\sqrt{0.37}}=3.04 \unit s$$

[8] Exercises
Q: How long must a simple pendulum be if it is to make exactly one swing per second? (That is, one complete oscillation takes exactly 2.0 s.)
A: We have $$T=2\pi\sqrt{\frac{L}{g}}\Rightarrow L=\frac{T^2}{4\pi^2}g=\frac{2^2}{4\pi^2}\cdot9.8=1 m$$ since the period should be double the time of one swing.

[9] Exercises
Q: What is the period of a simple pendulum 47 cm long (a) on the Earth, and (b) when it is in a freely falling elevator?
A: \begin{anum}
 \item We have the period as $$T=2\pi\sqrt{\frac{0.47}{9.8}}=1.38 s$$
 \item In a freely falling elevator, the pendulum becomes weightless (think about the tension in the string if the bob is free falling -- it has to be 0!). Thus effectively $g=0$, so the period is infinite (there are no oscillations at all).
 \end{anum}

[10] Exercises
Q: Your grandfather clock’s pendulum has a length of 0.9930 m. If the clock runs slow and loses 21 s per day, how should you adjust the length of the pendulum?
A: We cannot simply plug in $g$, etc to our formula because rounding errors will be larger than the actual answer. Instead, note that $f\propto 1/\sqrt{L}$, which means $L\propto 1/f^2$. In a day, there are 86400 seconds, but we know the clock does only 86379 swings. Thus the ratio of frequencies is $f_0/f=86400/86379$ (where $f_0$ is the correct frequency). But notice that we have the ideal length of the pendulum to be $L_0$, where $$L_0=L\frac{f^2}{f_0^2}=0.9930\cdot\frac{86379^2}{86400^2}=0.9925$$Thus we shorten the length by $0.5$ mm.

[11] Problems
Q: A mass $m$ at the end of a spring oscillates with a frequency of 0.83 Hz. When an additional 780-g mass is added to $m$, the frequency is 0.60 Hz. What is the value of $m$?
A: Using the formula, we have:
 \[ f_1 = \frac{1}{2\pi} \sqrt{\frac{k}{m}} \qquad f_2 = \frac{1}{2\pi} \sqrt{\frac{k}{m + 0.780}} \]

 Dividing the two equations gives:
 \[ \frac{f_1}{f_2} = \sqrt{\frac{m + 0.780}{m}} \]
 So $$m=\frac{0.78}{\frac{f_1^2}{f_2^2}-1}=\frac{0.78}{\frac{0.83^2}{0.60^2}-1}=0.853 kg$$

[12] Problems
Q: If one oscillation has 3.0 times the energy of a second one of equal frequency and mass, what is the ratio of their amplitudes?
A: Since the energy is $\frac 12 kA^2$, if one oscillation has 3.0 times the energy, its amplitude is $\sqrt{3}$ times as great.

[13] Problems
Q: A balsa wood block of mass 52 g floats on a lake, bobbing up and down at a frequency of 3.0 Hz. (a) What is the value of the effective spring constant of the water? (b) A partially filled water bottle of mass 0.28 kg and almost the same size and shape of the balsa block is tossed into the water. At what frequency would you expect the bottle to bob up and down? Assume SHM.
A: \begin{anum}
 \item The bobbing of the balsa wood block can be treated as SHM with an effective spring constant $k$ representing the restoring force provided by the buoyancy force of the water. The frequency of the motion is given as $f = 3.0$ Hz. We have $f = \frac{1}{2\pi}\sqrt{\frac{k}{m}}$, where $m$ is the mass of the block, we can solve for $k$. Rearranging the formula gives:
 \[ k = \left(2\pi f\right)^2 \times m \]
 Plugging in the values $f = 3.0$ Hz and $m = 52 \text{g} = 0.052 \text{kg}$, we find:
 \[ k = (2\pi \times 3.0)^2 \times 0.052 \approx 18.48 N/m. \]
 \item When the water bottle is tossed into the water, it displaces a similar volume of water, so the effective $k$ remains approximately the same. The frequency then is 
 \[
 f = \frac{1}{2\pi} \sqrt{\frac{k}{m}} = \frac{1}{2\pi} \sqrt{\frac{18.48}{0.28}} \approx 1.29 \text{Hz}.
 \]
 \end{anum}

[14] Problems
Q: At what displacement of a SHO is the energy half kinetic and half potential?
A: Half of the total energy is in potential form $\frac 12 kx^2$ and half is in kinetic form. Since the total energy is $\frac 12 kA^2$, we have $\frac12 kx^2 = \frac12 \cdot \frac12 kA^2$, giving $x=\frac{A}{\sqrt 2}$.

[15] Problems
Q: A block of mass $m$ is supported by two identical parallel vertical springs, each with spring stiffness constant $k$. What will be the frequency of vertical oscillation?
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[scale=0.2]{kay}
 \end{center}
A: Each spring provides a restoring force, so the total force is $F=-2kx$. Hence for small displacements the effective spring constant is $2k$, and we have $$\omega = \sqrt{\frac{2k}{m}}.$$

[16] Problems
Q: A simple pendulum oscillates with an amplitude of 10.0$^\circ$. What fraction of the time does it spend between $5.0^\circ$ and $-5.0^\circ$? Assume SHM.
A: In SHM, the fraction of time spent within half the amplitude (i.e.\ between $A/2$ and $-A/2$) is $1/3$. A quick way to see this is to note that $|x| \le A/2$ corresponds to $|\cos(\theta)| \le 1/2$, which occupies $2\pi/3$ of the full $2\pi$ cycle.

[17] Problems
Q: A clock pendulum oscillates at a frequency of 2.5 Hz. At $t = 0$, it is released from rest starting at an angle of 12$^\circ$ to the vertical. Ignoring friction, what will be the position (angle in radians) of the pendulum at (a) $t = 0.25$ s, (b) $t = 1.60$ s, and (c) $t = 500$ s?
A: First, convert the amplitude to radians: $12^\circ = 12\pi/180 \approx 0.21 \text{rad}$. Since it is released from rest at maximum displacement, we can write 
 \[
 \theta(t) = 0.21 \cos\bigl(2\pi(2.5) t \bigr).
 \]
 (a) For $t = 0.25 \text{s}$, $\omega t = 2\pi(2.5)\times 0.25 = 2\pi \times 0.625 = 1.25\pi$. Then
 \[
 \cos(1.25\pi) = \cos\left(\frac{5\pi}{4}\right) = -\frac{\sqrt{2}}{2} \approx -0.707,
 \]
 so $\theta(0.25) \approx 0.21 \times (-0.707) = -0.148 \text{rad}.$

 (b) For $t = 1.60 \text{s}$, $\omega t = 2\pi(2.5)\times 1.6 = 2\pi \times 4 = 8\pi$, so
 \[
 \cos(8\pi) = 1,\quad \theta(1.60) = 0.21 \text{rad}.
 \]

 (c) For $t = 500 \text{s}$, $\omega t = 2\pi(2.5)\times 500 = 2500\pi$. Since $2500\pi$ is an even multiple of $\pi$, $\cos(2500\pi) = 1$. Hence
 \[
 \theta(500) = 0.21 \text{rad}.
 \]

[18] Challenge Problems — $F=ma$
Q: A pendulum of length $L$ oscillates inside a box. A person picks up the box and gently shakes it horizontally with frequency $\omega$ and a fixed amplitude for a fixed time. The final amplitude can be maximized if $\omega$ satisfies \begin{enumerate}[label = (\Alph*)]
\item $\omega=\sqrt{g/L}$
\item $\omega=2\sqrt{g/L}$
\item $\omega=(1/2)\sqrt{g/L}$
\item There will be no effect on the amplitude for any value of $\omega$.
\item None of the above.
\end{enumerate}
A: We want to shake the box at the natural (resonant) frequency of the pendulum, which is $\sqrt{\tfrac{g}{L}}$. Thus the answer is (A).

[1] Exercises
Q: Express the following angles in radians: (a) $45.0^\circ$, (b) $60.0^\circ$, (c) $90.0^\circ$, (d) $360.0^\circ$, and (e) $445^\circ$. Give as fractions of $\pi$.
A: \begin{anum}
\item For $45.0^\circ$, the angle in radians is $\frac{\pi}{4} \approx 0.7854$.
\item For $60.0^\circ$, the angle in radians is $\frac{\pi}{3} \approx 1.0472$.
\item For $90.0^\circ$, the angle in radians is $\frac{\pi}{2} \approx 1.5708$.
\item For $360.0^\circ$, the angle in radians is $2\pi \approx 6.2832$.
\item For $445^\circ$, the angle in radians is $\frac{445}{180} \pi \approx 7.7667$.
\end{anum}

[2] Exercises
Q: The Sun subtends an angle of about $0.5^\circ$ to us on Earth, 150 million km away. Estimate the radius of the Sun.
A: Using the small angle approximation, $\theta \approx \frac{r}{d}$, where $r$ is the radius of the Sun and $d = 150 \times 10^6$ km is the distance from Earth to the Sun. The angle $\theta = 0.5^\circ$ in radians is $\frac{\pi}{360}$. Therefore, the radius of the Sun is calculated as follows:
 \[
 r = d \cdot \theta = 150 \times 10^6 \text{ km} \cdot \frac{\pi}{360} \approx 1,309,000 \text{ km}.
 \]

[3] Exercises
Q: The blades in a blender rotate at a rate of 6500 rpm.
When the motor is turned off during operation, the blades
slow to rest in 4.0 s. What is the angular acceleration as the
blades slow down?
A: The initial angular velocity of the blades is
 \[
 \omega_{\text{initial}} = 6500 \text{rpm} \times \frac{2\pi \text{rad}}{60 \text{s}} \approx 680.68 \text{rad/s}.
 \]
 The blades slow to rest in $4.0$ seconds, so the final angular velocity, $\omega_{\text{final}}$, is $0 \text{rad/s}$. The angular acceleration, $\alpha$, is calculated as:
 \[
 \alpha = \frac{\omega_{\text{final}} - \omega_{\text{initial}}}{\Delta t} = \frac{0 - 680.68 \text{rad/s}}{4.0 \text{s}} \approx -170.17 \text{rad/s}^2.
 \]

[4] Exercises
Q: A wheel starts from rest and has a constant angular acceleration of 4 radians per second squared. After 5 seconds, calculate the angular velocity of the wheel. Also, determine the total angle in radians through which the wheel has turned during this time.
A: \begin{anum}
\item The angular velocity of the wheel after 5 seconds is calculated using the angular acceleration $\alpha = 4 \text{rad/s}^2$:
 \[
 \omega = \alpha \cdot t = 4 \text{rad/s}^2 \cdot 5 \text{s} = 20 \text{rad/s}.
 \]
 \item The total angle $\theta$ in radians through which the wheel has turned is given by the formula for the angle turned under constant angular acceleration:
 \[
 \theta = \frac{1}{2} \alpha t^2 = \frac{1}{2} \cdot 4 \text{rad/s}^2 \cdot (5 \text{s})^2 = 50 \text{rad}.
 \]
\end{anum}

[5] Exercises
Q: A spinning disk has an initial angular velocity of 10 radians per second and a constant angular deceleration of 2 radians per second squared. Calculate the time it will take for the disk to come to a complete stop. If the disk has a radius of 0.5 meters, find the linear distance traveled by a point on the rim of the disk until it stops.
A: The time for the disk to stop can be found using the equation:
\[
\omega = \omega_0 + \alpha t,
\]
where \(\omega_0 = 10 \text{rad/s}\) and \(\alpha = -2 \text{rad/s}^2\). Setting \(\omega = 0\),
\[
t = \frac{-\omega_0}{\alpha} = 5 \text{s}.
\]
The angular displacement \(\theta\) by the time it stops is given by:
\[
\theta = \omega_0 t + \frac{1}{2} \alpha t^2 = 25 \text{rad}.
\]
The linear distance \(s\) traveled by a point on the rim is \(\theta r\),
\[
s = \theta r = 12.5 \text{m}.
\]
Thus, the solutions are:
\[
t = \boxed{5 \text{s}}, \quad s = \boxed{12.5 \text{m}}.
\]

[6] Exercises
Q: A ferris wheel with a radius of 10 meters rotates at a constant speed making one complete revolution every 2 minutes. Calculate the angular velocity of the ferris wheel in radians per second. Then, determine the linear velocity of a passenger at the edge of the ferris wheel.
A: The angular velocity \(\omega\) of the Ferris wheel is calculated by:
\[
\omega = \frac{2\pi}{T},
\]
where \(T = 120 \text{s}\). Thus,
\[
\omega = 0.0524 \text{rad/s}.
\]
The linear velocity \(v\) of a passenger at the edge is:
\[
v = \omega r = 0.524 \text{m/s}.
\]
Therefore, the solutions are:
\[
\omega = \boxed{0.0524 \text{rad/s}}, \quad v = \boxed{0.524 \text{m/s}}.
\]

[7] Exercises
Q: A hoop is rolling without slipping on a horizontal surface and has an initial speed of 1 meter per second. If it has a constant angular acceleration of 0.5 radians per second squared, calculate its angular velocity after 4 seconds. Assume the radius of the hoop is 0.3 meters, and determine the linear velocity of the hoop at this time.
A: The angular velocity \(\omega\) after \(t = 4 \text{s}\) is:
\[
\omega = \alpha t = 2 \text{rad/s}.
\]
The linear velocity \(v\) of the hoop is:
\[
v = r\omega = 0.6 \text{m/s}.
\]
Therefore, the solutions are:
\[
\omega = \boxed{2 \text{rad/s}}, \quad v = \boxed{0.6 \text{m/s}}.
\]

[8] Exercises
Q: What is the linear speed, due to the Earth’s rotation,
of a point (a) on the equator, (b) on the Arctic Circle
(latitude $66.5^\circ$ N), and (c) at a latitude of $42.0^\circ$ N?
A: \begin{anum}
\item The linear speed \(v\) at the equator is \(463.31 \text{m/s}\).
\item At the Arctic Circle, the speed is \(184.75 \text{m/s}\).
\item At a latitude of \(42.0^\circ\) N, the speed is \(344.31 \text{m/s}\).
\end{anum}

[9] Exercises
Q: A rotating merry-go-round makes one complete revolution in 4.0 s. (a) What is the linear speed of a child seated 1.2 m from the center? (b) What is her acceleration (give components)? Don't think too hard about part b.
A: \begin{anum}
\item The linear speed of the child is \(1.885 \text{m/s}\).
\item The centripetal acceleration is \(2.961 \text{m/s}^2\).
\end{anum}

[10] Exercises
Q: A turntable of radius $R_1$ is turned by a circular rubber
roller of radius $R_2$ in contact with it at their outer edges.
What is the ratio of their angular velocities, $\omega_1 / \omega_2$?
A: The ratio of the angular velocities \(\omega_1 / \omega_2\) when the turntable and rubber roller are in contact at their edges is:
\[
\frac{\omega_1}{\omega_2} = \frac{R_2}{R_1}.
\]
This relationship arises because the linear velocities at the contact point must be equal, leading to:
\[
R_1 \omega_1 = R_2 \omega_2.
\]
Therefore, the solution is:
\[
\frac{\omega_1}{\omega_2} = \boxed{\frac{R_2}{R_1}}.
\]

[11] Problems
Q: A car is traveling around a circular track with a constant speed of 20 meters per second. The radius of the track is 50 meters. (a) Calculate the angular velocity of the car in radians per second. (b) If the car continues at this speed, how long will it take to complete one full lap around the track?
A: \begin{anum}
\item The angular velocity $\omega$ of the car can be calculated using the formula $\omega = \frac{v}{r}$, where $v$ is the speed of the car and $r$ is the radius of the circular track. Substituting the given values, $\omega = \frac{20,\text{m/s}}{50,\text{m}} = 0.4 \text{rad/s}$. Thus, the angular velocity of the car is \boxed{0.4 \text{rad/s}}.

\item To find the time it takes to complete one full lap around the track, we use the circumference of the circle, which is given by $2\pi r$. The time to complete one lap, $t$, is then calculated as $t = \frac{\text{circumference}}{v}$ where $v$ is the speed of the car. Substituting the values, $t = \frac{2\pi \times 50,\text{m}}{20,\text{m/s}} \approx 15.71,\text{s}$. Hence, it takes approximately \boxed{15.71,\text{s}} to complete one lap.
\end{anum}

[12] Problems
Q: A large spool of rope rolls on the ground with the end of
the rope lying on the top edge of the spool. A person grabs
the end of the rope and walks a distance $\ell$, holding onto it, as shown below. The spool rolls behind the person
without slipping. What length of rope unwinds
from the spool? How far
does the spool’s center of
mass move?
\begin{center}
 \includegraphics[width=5cm]{spoollll.png}
\end{center}
A: Suppose the person walks with velocity $v$. Then, the string has linear speed $v$, which from rolling without slipping is equal to
 $$v = 2\omega R.$$
 So, if this motion happens over time $t$, the amount that unwinds is $\omega R t$, which is related to $\ell$ by
 $$vt = 2\omega Rt \implies \ell = 2 x \implies x = \boxed{\frac \ell 2}.$$
 The amount that the COM is also given by $\omega R t$, so the distance of the COM is $\boxed{\ell / 2}$ as well.

[13] Problems — HRK
Q: Wheel $A$ of radius $r_A = 10.0 cm$ is coupled by a belt $B$ to wheel $C$ of radius $r_C = 25.0 cm$ as shown. Wheel $A$ increases its angular speed from rest at a uniform rate of $1.60 rad/s^2$. Determine the time for wheel $C$ to reach a rotational speed of $100 rev/min$, assuming the belt does not slip.
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[width=6cm]{hrk_belt.png}
 \end{center}
A: Given that wheel \( A \) has an angular acceleration \( \alpha_A = 1.60 \text{rad/s}^2 \) and wheel \( C \) has to reach an angular speed of \( 100 \text{rev/min} \), we first convert the angular speed to radians per second using the conversion \( 1 \text{rev} = 2\pi \text{rad} \) and \( 1 \text{min} = 60 \text{s} \), resulting in an angular speed for wheel \( C \) of \( \omega_{C_{\text{final}}} = 100 \times \frac{2\pi}{60} \text{rad/s} \).

Since the belt does not slip, the linear speed at the edge of both wheels must be the same, so \( v_A = r_A\omega_A \) and \( v_C = r_C\omega_C \), where \( \omega_A \) and \( \omega_C \) are the angular velocities of wheels \( A \) and \( C \), respectively. Consequently, \( \omega_A r_A = \omega_C r_C \).

Given that \( \omega_A = \alpha_A t \) because wheel \( A \) starts from rest, we can substitute \( \omega_A \) in the previous equation and get \( \alpha_A t r_A = \omega_C r_C \). We can solve for time \( t \) when wheel \( C \) reaches its final angular velocity \( \omega_{C_{\text{final}}} \):

\[
t = \frac{\omega_{C_{\text{final}}} r_C}{\alpha_A r_A}.
\]

Substituting the values, we get \( t = \frac{10.47 \text{rad/s} \times 0.25 \text{m}}{1.60 \text{rad/s}^2 \times 0.10 \text{m}} \approx 16.36 \text{s} \).

Thus, the time for wheel \( C \) to reach a rotational speed of \( 100 \text{rev/min} \) is \boxed{16.36 \text{s}}.

[14] Problems
Q: A cyclist accelerates from rest at a rate of $1.00 m/s^2.$ How
fast will a point at the top of the rim of the tire (diameter of 68.0 cm) be moving after 2.25 s? [Hint: At
any moment, the lowest point on the tire is in contact with
the ground and is at rest—see below]
\begin{center}
 \includegraphics[width=5cm]{whell_bicycle.png}
\end{center}
A: We have from rolling without slipping that the top of the wheel is always moving at twice the speed of the center of the tire. Thus, after 2.25 s, we have
 $$v_{\text{top}} = 2 \cdot 1.00 \cdot 2.25 = \boxed{4.50 m/s}.$$

[15] Problems
Q: Let us treat a helicopter rotor blade as a long thin
rod, as shown below. If each of the three rotor
helicopter blades is 3.75 m long and has a mass of 135 kg,
calculate the moment of inertia of the three rotor blades
about the axis of rotation.
\begin{center}
 \includegraphics[width=5cm]{rotorr.png}
\end{center}
A: We simply add up the moment of inertias of each of the rods,
 $$I_{\text{tot}} = 3I = 3\cdot \frac 13 m \ell^2 = m \ell^2.$$
 Plugging in the numbers, $I = (135 kg)(3.75 m)^2 = \boxed{1900 kg m^2}.$

[16] Problems — $F=ma$
Q: A system of cylinders and plates is set up as shown. The cylinders all have radius $r$, and roll without slipping to the right with angular velocity $\omega$. What is the speed of the top plate?
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[width=5cm]{fma_stack_roll.png}
 \end{center}
A: As with the earlier problems, because the top of the wheels move at $v_{\text{cm}} + \omega r = 2\omega r$ when rolling without slipping, this effect gets multiplied across all the stacks (simply take the frame of each of the plates as you compute up the stack), thus the speed of the top plate is $3 \times 2\omega r = \boxed{6\omega r}.$

[17] Challenge Problems
Q: If you roll a quarter around another quarter, how many times does the quarter spin by the time it gets back to its original position? This is the subject of a "viral" video from one of those <a href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=kN3AOMrnEUs" target="_blank" rel="noopener">clickbait youtubers</a>.
A: Suppose you roll one of the quarters an angle $\theta$ around the stationary quarter. Then, by rolling without slipping the quarter that you're moving must rotate $\theta$ relative to the "surface" that it is moving on. But since the normal to the surface rotates an angle $\theta$ in this movement, the actual angle w.r.t. to the vertical that the quarter you are moving actually turns through is $2\theta$. Thus, the quarter spins <strong>twice</strong> by the time it gets back to its original position!

[1] Exercises
Q: Calculate the net torque about the axle of the wheel shown in the figure. Assume that a friction torque of 0.60 $ m\cdot N$ opposes the motion.
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[scale=0.2]{bowl}
 \end{center}
A: All the forces are fired perpendicular to their respective radii. Let us define the clockwise direction as positive. Then, $$18\cdot 0.24+35\cdot 0.12-28\cdot 0.24=18 m\cdot N$$So indeed the wheel will try to spin in the clockwise direction. Subtracting the friction, we have the net torque equal to $18-0.6=17.4 m\cdot N.$

[2] Exercises
Q: A person exerts a horizontal force of 42 N on the end of a door 96 cm wide. What is the magnitude of the torque if the force is exerted (a) perpendicular to the door and (b) at a 60.0$^\circ$ angle to the face of the door?
A: \begin{anum}
 \item Here the torque is $42\cdot 0.96=40.32 m\cdot N$.
 \item Here the torque is $42\cdot 0.96\cdot \sin(60)=34.92 m\cdot N.$
 \end{anum}

[3] Exercises
Q: Determine the net torque on the 2.0-m-long uniform beam shown in the figure. All forces are shown. Calculate about (a) point $C$, the center of mass, and (b) point $P$ at one end.
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[scale=0.2]{board}
 \end{center}
A: \begin{anum}
 \item About the point $C$ we can neglect the middle force, since it passes through $C$. Setting the clockwise direction as positive, we have $$56\cdot 1\cdot \sin(32)-52\cdot 1\cdot \sin(58)=-14.42 m\cdot N$$
 \item About the point $P$, we neglect the bottom force. Again taking clockwise as positive, the torque here is $$56\cdot 2\cdot \sin(32)-65\cdot 1\cdot \sin(45)=-13.39 m\cdot N$$
 \end{anum}

[4] Exercises
Q: A merry-go-round accelerates from rest to 0.68 rad/s in 34 s. Assuming the merry-go-round is a uniform disk of radius 7.0 m and mass 31,000 kg, calculate the net torque required to accelerate it.
A: First, the moment of inertia is $I=(1/2)mR^2=(1/2)\cdot 31000\cdot 7^2=759500 kg \cdot m^2$. The acceleration of the merry-go-round is $\alpha = \omega/t=0.68/34=0.02 rad/s^2$. Then, the torque is $\tau=I\alpha=15190 m\cdot N.$

[5] Exercises
Q: A grinding wheel is a uniform cylinder with a radius of 8.50 cm and a mass of 0.380 kg. Calculate (a) its moment of inertia about its center, and (b) the applied torque needed to accelerate it from rest to 1750 rpm in 5.00 s. Take into account a frictional torque that has been measured to slow down the wheel from 1500 rpm to rest in 55.0 s.
A: \begin{anum}
 \item The moment of inertia of the grinding wheel about its center can be calculated using the formula for the moment of inertia of a cylinder about its central axis: $I = \frac{1}{2} m r^2$. Substituting the given values, we have $I = \frac{1}{2} \times 0.380 \times (0.085)^2 = 1.15 \times 10^{-3} \text{kg} \cdot \text{m}^2$.
 \item To find the applied torque needed to accelerate the wheel, we first convert the final angular velocity to radians per second: $\omega_{\text{final}} = \frac{1750 \times 2\pi}{60} = 183.33 \text{rad/s}$. The angular acceleration $\alpha$ can be found using $\alpha = \frac{\Delta \omega}{\Delta t}$. So, $\alpha = \frac{183.33}{5} = 36.67 \text{rad/s}^2$. The torque can then be calculated using $\tau = I \alpha$. Substituting the values, $\tau = 1.15 \times 10^{-3} \times 36.67 = 4.22 \times 10^{-2} \text{N} \cdot \text{m}$.
\end{anum}

[6] Exercises
Q: A softball player swings a bat, accelerating it from rest to 2.6 rev/s in a time of 0.20 s. Approximate the bat as a 0.90-kg uniform rod of length 0.95 m, and compute the torque the player applies to one end of it.
A: To calculate the torque applied to one end of the bat, we first need to find the moment of inertia of the bat about its end. For a rod rotating about one end, $I = \frac{1}{3} m L^2$. Substituting the given values, we have $I = \frac{1}{3} \times 0.90 \times (0.95)^2 = 0.807 \text{kg} \cdot \text{m}^2$. Now, we can find the angular acceleration using $\alpha = \frac{\Delta \omega}{\Delta t}$, where $\Delta \omega = \frac{2.6 \times 2\pi}{60} = 0.273 \pi \text{rad/s}$ (since $1 \text{rev/s} = 2\pi \text{rad/s}$). So, $\alpha = \frac{0.273 \pi}{0.20} = 1.36 \pi \text{rad/s}^2$. The torque applied can then be calculated using $\tau = I \alpha$, giving $\tau = 0.807 \times 1.36 \pi = 10.78 \text{N} \cdot \text{m}$.

[7] Exercises
Q: A dad pushes tangentially on a small hand-driven merry-go-round and is able to accelerate it from rest to a frequency of 15 rpm in 10.0 s. Assume the merry-go-round is a uniform disk of radius 2.5 m and has a mass of 560 kg, and two children (each with a mass of 25 kg) sit opposite each other on the edge. Calculate the torque required to produce the acceleration, neglecting frictional torque. What force is required at the edge?
A: \begin{anum}
 \item First, let's find the moment of inertia of the merry-go-round. For a uniform disk rotating about its central axis, $I = \frac{1}{2} m R^2$. Substituting the given values, we have $I = \frac{1}{2} \times 560 \times (2.5)^2 = 1750 \text{kg} \cdot \text{m}^2$. Now, we can find the net torque required to produce the acceleration using $\tau = I \alpha$, where $\alpha$ is the angular acceleration. From the given frequency and time, we can calculate the final angular velocity: $\omega_{\text{final}} = 15 \times \frac{2\pi}{60} = \frac{\pi}{2} \text{rad/s}$. So, $\alpha = \frac{\omega_{\text{final}}}{t} = \frac{\pi/2}{10} = \frac{\pi}{20} \text{rad/s}^2$. Substituting into the torque formula, $\tau = 1750 \times \frac{\pi}{20} = 275 \text{N} \cdot \text{m}$.
 \item To find the force required at the edge, we can use the formula $\tau = Fr$, where $F$ is the force applied at the edge and $r$ is the radius. Rearranging for $F$, we get $F = \frac{\tau}{r}$. Substituting the calculated torque and radius, we have $F = \frac{175}{2} \pi \times \frac{1}{2.5} = 55 \text{N}$.
\end{anum}

[8] Exercises
Q: To get a flat, uniform cylindrical satellite spinning at the correct rate, engineers fire four tangential rockets as shown in the figure. Suppose that the satellite has a mass of 3600 kg and a radius of 4.0 m, and that the rockets each add a mass of 250 kg. What is the steady force required of each rocket if the satellite is to reach 32 rpm in 5.0 min, starting from rest?
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[scale=0.2]{rocket}
 \end{center}
A: First, let's find the moment of inertia of the satellite. Since it's a cylinder, $I = \frac{1}{2} m R^2$. The mass of the satellite is given as 3600 kg and its radius is 4.0 m, so $I = \frac{1}{2} \times 3600 \times (4.0)^2 = 28800 \text{kg} \cdot \text{m}^2$. Now, we can find the angular acceleration using the final angular velocity and time: $\omega_{\text{final}} = 32 \times \frac{2\pi}{60} = \frac{16}{15} \pi \text{rad/s}$. So, $\alpha = \frac{\omega_{\text{final}}}{t} = \frac{16}{15\cdot 300} \pi = \frac{16\pi}{4500} \text{rad/s}^2$. The total moment of inertia considering the additional mass added by the rockets is $I_{\text{total}} = I_{\text{satellite}} + 4 \times m_{\text{rocket}} \times R^2$, where $m_{\text{rocket}}$ is the mass of each rocket. Substituting the given values, we have $I_{\text{total}} = 28800 + 4 \times 250 \times (4.0)^2 = 28800 + 16000 = 44800 \text{kg} \cdot \text{m}^2$. Now, we can find the torque using $\tau = I_{\text{total}} \alpha$, giving $\tau = 44800 \times \frac{16\pi}{4500} = \frac{28672}{225} \pi \text{N} \cdot \text{m}$. Finally, the force required from each rocket is $F = \frac{\tau}{R} = \frac{\frac{28672}{225} \pi}{4.0} = 100 \text{N}$.

[9] Exercises
Q: A centrifuge rotor has a moment of inertia of $3.25\times 10^{-2} kg\cdot m^2$. How much energy is required to bring it from rest to 8750 rpm.
A: To calculate the energy required to bring the centrifuge rotor to 8750 rpm, we first need to convert the final angular velocity to radians per second: $\omega_{\text{final}} = 8750 \times \frac{2\pi}{60} = \frac{875}{6} \pi \text{rad/s}$. Now, we can use the formula for rotational kinetic energy: $K = \frac{1}{2} I \omega^2$. Substituting the given values, we have $K = \frac{1}{2} \times 3.25 \times 10^{-2} \times \left(\frac{875}{6} \pi \right)^2 = 1866 \text{J}$.

[10] Problems
Q: A solid, uniformly cylindrical pulley of mass $M$ and radius $R$ has two masses $m_1$ and $m_2$ passed over it, with $m_2>m_1$. Find the acceleration of the masses, for (a) $M=0$ and (b) $M\neq 0$. (Hint: for this part the tension on either side of the string must be different.)
A: \begin{anum}
 \item When $M=0$, the pulley is effectively a frictionless massless pulley. The tension in the string is the same on both sides. Using Newton's second law, the acceleration of both masses is $a = \frac{m_2 - m_1}{m_1 + m_2} g$.
 \item When $M \neq 0$, the tension on either side of the pulley must be different. Let the tension from the side with $m_2$ be $T_2$ and the tension from the side with $m_1$ be $T_1$. Then, we have $m_2g-T_2=m_2a$ and $T_1-m_1g=m_1a$. Also, by torque on the pulley, $(T_2-T_1)R=(1/2)MR^2\alpha=(1/2)MRa$. Solving this system of equations yields $$a=\frac{m_2g-m_1g}{M/2+m_2+m_1}$$
\end{anum}

[11] Problems
Q: A hammer thrower accelerates the hammer (mass = 7.30 kg) from rest within four full turns (revolutions) and releases it at a speed of 26.5 m!s. Assuming a uniform rate of increase in angular velocity and a horizontal circular path of radius 1.20 m, calculate (a) the angular acceleration, (b) the (linear) tangential acceleration, (c) the centripetal acceleration just before release, (d) the net force being exerted on the hammer by the athlete just before release, and (e) the angle of this force with respect to the radius of the circular motion. Ignore gravity.
A: \begin{anum}
 \item The angle $\theta$ passed through is $8\pi$. When released, the angular velocity is $\omega=v/r=26.5/1.2=22.08 rad/s$. Then, since $2\alpha\theta=\omega^2$, we have $\alpha=\omega^2/2\theta=22.08^2/(2\cdot 8\pi)=9.7 rad/s^2$. 
 \item The tangential acceleration is just $a_t=R\alpha=1.2\cdot 9.7=11.64 m/s^2.$
 \item The centripetal acceleration is $a_r=\omega^2 R=22.08^2\cdot 1.2=585 m/s^2$. 
 \item By adding the vectors of acceleration by the pythagorean theorem, net force exerted on the hammer is $F=m\sqrt{a_t^2+a_r^2}=7.3\sqrt{11.64^2+585^2}=4271 N$.
 \item If the angle of the force is $\theta$, then $\tan\theta=a_t/a_r$, so $\theta=\tan^{-1}(a_t/a_r)=\tan^{-1}(11.64/585)=1.14^\circ$.
\end{anum}

[12] Problems
Q: A merry-go-round has a mass of 1440 kg and a radius of 7.50 m. How much net work is required to accelerate it from rest to a rotation rate of 1.00 revolution per 7.00 s? Assume it is a solid cylinder.
A: The final angular velocity can be calculated using the formula $\omega = \frac{2\pi}{T}$, where $T$ is the period. So, $\omega = 0.90 \text{rad/s}$. The work done is given by the final kinetic energy, $W = \frac{1}{2} I \omega^2$, where $I$ is the moment of inertia of the merry-go-round. For a solid cylinder, $I = \frac{1}{2} M R^2$. Substituting the values, $W=\frac 12(\frac 12 MR^2)\omega^2=\frac 12(\frac 12 1440\cdot 7.5^2)0.90^2=16400 J$.

[13] Problems
Q: A ball of radius $r$ rolls on the inside of a track of radius $R$. If the ball starts from rest at the vertical edge of the track, what will be its speed when it reaches the lowest point of the track, rolling without slipping?
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[scale=0.3]{ball}
 \end{center}
A: We assume the ball falls a height $R$, so its potential energy is $mgR$. If the velocity is $v$ at the bottom, then the translational kinetic energy is $\frac 12 mv^2$ and the rotational kinetic energy is $\frac 12 I\omega^2=\frac 12\frac 25mR^2\omega^2=\frac 15mv^2$. Then by conservation of energy, $$\frac{7}{10}mv^2=mgR\Rightarrow v=\sqrt{\frac{10gR}{7}}$$

[14] Problems
Q: A ladder leans against a wall at an angle $\theta$ to the floor. 
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[scale=1]{bleh}
 \end{center}
 There is no friction between the wall and the ladder, but there is friction $\mu$ between the ladder and the floor. The ladder has length $L$ and mass $m$. The ladder is in static equilibrium.
 \begin{anum}
 \item Using torque balance, find the normal force between the ladder and wall.
 \item Find the minimum $\mu$ needed for static equilibrium.
 \end{anum}
A: \begin{anum}
 \item Take the contact point to the ground as our pivot. Then the only forces are the normal force, pointing to the right and the gravitational force, pointing downward from the center of mass. These torques must balance, so $$NL\sin\theta = mg\frac L2\cos\theta\Rightarrow N=\frac{mg}{2\tan\theta}$$
 \item By vertical force balance, the normal force from the ground $N_g$ must balance the force of gravity, so $N_g=mg$. The frictional force must then balance the horizontal normal force from the wall, so $N_g\mu = N$. Thus we solve to get $$\mu = \frac{N}{N_g}=\frac{1}{2\tan\theta}$$
 \end{anum}

[15] Problems
Q: A traffic light hangs from a pole as shown in the figure. The uniform aluminum pole $AB$ is 7.20 m long and has a mass of 12.0 kg. The mass of the traffic light is 21.5 kg. Determine (a) the tension in the horizontal massless cable $CD$, and (b) the vertical and horizontal components of the force exerted by the pivot $A$ on the aluminum pole.
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[scale=0.2]{traffic}
 \end{center}
A: \begin{anum}
 \item We take the point $A$ as our pivot. Notice that the tension from the string exerts a torque $Tr_\perp$, and from the diagram $r_\perp= 3.8 m$. The other torques on the pole and light are found via $$12\cdot 9.8\frac{7.2}{2}\cos(37)+21.5\cdot 9.8\cdot 7.2\cos(37)=1550 mN$$and they try to counteract the torque from the string, so $$T=\frac{1550}{3.8}=408 \unit N$$
 \item By force balance, the vertical force must balance the gravitational weight of the light and pole, which is $$F_y=12\cdot 9.8+21.5\cdot 9.8=328 \unit N$$The horizontal force balances the tension from the string, so $$F_x=408 \unit N$$
 \end{anum}

[16] Challenge Problems
Q: A 1.80-m-long pole is balanced vertically with its tip on the ground. It starts to fall and its lower end does not slip. What will be the speed of the upper end of the pole just before it hits the ground? [Hint: Use conservation of energy.]
A: Notice that since there is no external force in the horizontal direction, the center of mass velocity of the pole is always vertical. When the upper end is about to hit the ground, the pole is essentially pivoted at its other end on the ground, and rotating about that point so that the center of mass is essentially moving vertically downward. In this case, the inertia of the pole is $\frac 13 mL^2$. By conservation of energy the angular velocity at this moment is $$\frac{mgL}{2}=\frac 13 mL^2\omega^2\Rightarrow \omega=\sqrt{\frac{3g}{2L}}$$ Then the velocity of the other end is just $$v=\omega L=\sqrt{\frac{3gL}{2}}=\sqrt{\frac{3\cdot 9.8\cdot 1.8}{2}}=5.14 m/s$$

[1] Exercises
Q: The Leaning Tower of Pisa is 55 m tall and about 7.7 m radius. The top is 4.5 m off-center. How much farther can it lean before the tower has to fall?
A: Once the center of mass tips past the bottom base of the tower, it will tip. If the center of mass is past 7.7 m off center, then the top will be two times that; 15.4 m off center. Thus the tower can lean 11.1 m extra at the top before it tips.

[2] Exercises
Q: A uniform steel beam has a mass of 940 kg. On it is resting half of an identical beam. What is the vertical support force at each end?
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[scale=0.2]{beams}
 \end{center}
A: For the force on the left, we can do torque balance about the right beam. Here we have $$F_L\ell =Mg\frac{\ell}{2}+\frac 12 Mg\frac{3\ell}{4}\Rightarrow F_L=\frac 78 Mg$$For the force on the right, we can do torque balance about the left beam. Here we have $$F_R\ell =Mg\frac{\ell}{2}+\frac 12 Mg\frac{\ell}{4}\Rightarrow F_L=\frac 58 Mg$$

[3] Exercises
Q: Calculate $F_A$ and $F_B$ for the beam shown. The downward forces represent the weights of machinery on the beam. Assume the beam is uniform and has a mass of 280 kg.
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[scale=0.25]{beams!}
 \end{center}
A: For the force on the left, we can do torque balance about the right beam. Here we have $$F_A\cdot 10=4300\cdot 8+3100\cdot 4+2200\cdot 1+280\cdot 9.8\cdot 5\Rightarrow F_L=6272 \unit N$$
 For the force on the right, we can do torque balance about the left beam. Here we have $$F_B\cdot 10=4300\cdot 2+3100\cdot 6+2200\cdot 9+280\cdot 9.8\cdot 5\Rightarrow F_R=6072 \unit N$$

[4] Exercises
Q: A person stands, hands at his side, on a platform that is rotating at a rate of 0.90 rev/s. If he raises his arms to a horizontal position, the speed of rotation decreases to 0.60 rev/s. (a) Why? (b) By what factor has his moment of inertia changed?
A: \begin{anum}
 \item Angular momentum is conserved, but his moment of inertia increases, so his angular velocity decreases. 
 \item The factor of change is $0.9/0.6=1.5$ times greater.
 \end{anum}

[5] Exercises
Q: (a) What is the angular momentum of a figure skater spinning at 3.0 rev/s with arms in close to her body, assuming her to be a uniform cylinder with a height of 1.5 m, a radius of 15 cm, and a mass of 48 kg? (b) How much torque is required to slow her to a stop in 4.0 s, assuming she does not move her arms?
A: \begin{anum}
 \item Note that the angular velocity, in radians per second, is $3\cdot 2\pi$. We have $$L=I\omega=\frac 12 MR^2\omega=\frac 12 \cdot 48\cdot 0.15^2\cdot 3\cdot 2\pi =10.18 kg \cdot m^2/s$$
 \item Using $\tau = \Delta L/\Delta t$, we have $\tau=10.18/4=2.55 N\cdot m$.
 \end{anum}

[6] Exercises
Q: A potter’s wheel is rotating around a vertical axis through its center at a frequency of 1.5 rev/s. The wheel can be considered a uniform disk of mass 5.0 kg and diameter 0.40 m. The potter then throws a 2.6-kg chunk of clay, approximately shaped as a flat disk of radius 7.0 cm, onto the center of the rotating wheel. What is the frequency of the wheel after the clay sticks to it? Ignore friction.
A: Let the initial and final frequencies be $f$ and $f'$. The angular momentum is conserved, so we have $$\frac 12 MR^2f=\left(\frac 12 MR^2+\frac12 mr^2\right)f'$$ so rearranging yields $$f'=\frac{MR^2}{MR^2+mr^2}f=\frac{5\cdot 0.2^2}{5\cdot 0.2^2+2.6\cdot 0.07^2}1.5=1.41 rev/s$$

[7] Problems
Q: What is the mass of the diver in the figure if she exerts a torque of 1800 mN on the board, relative to the left (A) support post?
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[scale=0.25]{DIVE}
 \end{center}
A: We have $mg\cdot 4=1800$ so $m=1800/(4\cdot 9.8)=46 \unit {kg}$.

[8] Problems
Q: Calculate the forces $F_A$ and $F_B$ that the supports exert on the diving board of the previous problem when a 52-kg person stands at its tip. (a) Ignore the weight of the board. (b) Now take into account the board’s mass of 28 kg. Assume the board is uniform.
A: \begin{anum}
 \item To find $F_A$ we balance torques about the $B$ beam. Here we have $$46\cdot 9.8\cdot 3=F_A\cdot 1\Rightarrow F_A=1352 \unit N$$ and this force pulls downward. To find $F_B$ we balance torques about the $A$ beam so $$46\cdot 9.8\cdot 4=F_B\cdot 1\Rightarrow F_B=1803 \unit N$$ and this force pushes upward.
 \item To find $F_A$ we balance torques about the $B$ beam. Here we have $$46\cdot 9.8\cdot 3+28\cdot 9.8\cdot 1=F_A\cdot 1\Rightarrow F_A=1627 \unit N$$ and this force pulls downward. To find $F_B$ we balance torques about the $A$ beam so $$46\cdot 9.8\cdot 4+28\cdot 9.8\cdot 2=F_B\cdot 1\Rightarrow F_B=2352 \unit N$$ and this force pushes upward.
 \end{anum}

[9] Problems
Q: What is the angular momentum of a 0.270-kg ball revolving on the end of a thin string in a circle of radius 1.35 m at an angular speed of 10.4 rad/s?
A: We have $$L=I\omega=\frac 25MR^2\omega=\frac 25\cdot 0.27\cdot 1.35^2\cdot 10.4^2=21.29 kg\cdot m^2/s$$

Q: Find the tension in the two wires supporting the traffic light shown.
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[scale=0.25]{trafec}
 \end{center}
A: Let the tension in the left wire be $T_1$ and in the right $T_2$. By force balance in the $x$ and $y$ directions, $$T_1\sin(53)+T_2\sin(37)=33\cdot 9.8\qquad T_1\cos(53)=T_2\cos(37)$$and solving the system we get $$T_1=258 \unit N\qquad T_2=195 \unit N$$

[11] Problems
Q: Two wires run from the top of a pole 2.6 m tall that supports a volleyball net. The two wires are anchored to the ground 2.0 m apart, and each is 2.0 m from the pole. The tension in each wire is 115 N. What is the tension in the net, assumed horizontal and attached at the top of the pole?
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[scale=0.25]{voleibol}
 \end{center}
A: We only need to find the horizontal tension exerted by the ropes, because that is the component balanced out by the net (the pole will balance out the vertical forces). By some similar triangles and trig, the force is just $$T=2\cdot 115\cdot \frac{2}{\sqrt{2^2+2.6^2}}\cdot \cos(30)=121.4 \unit N$$

[12] Problems
Q: A uniform disk turns at 3.3 rev/s around a frictionless central axis. A nonrotating rod, of the same mass as the disk and length equal to the disk’s diameter, is dropped onto the freely spinning disk. They then turn together around the axis with their centers superposed. What is the angular frequency in rev/s of the combination?
\begin{center}
 \includegraphics[scale=0.25]{wrink}
 \end{center}
A: Let the masses of the objects be $M$. By conservation of angular momentum, $$\frac 12 MR^2f=\left(\frac 12 MR^2+\frac 1{12}M(2R)^2\right)f'\Rightarrow f'=\frac{1/2}{1/2+1/3}f=1.98 rev/s$$

[13] Problems
Q: An asteroid of mass $1.0 \times 10^5$ kg, traveling at a speed of 35 km/s relative to the Earth, hits the Earth at the equator tangentially, in the direction of Earth’s rotation, and is embedded there. Use angular momentum to estimate the percent change in the angular speed of the Earth as a result of the collision. (You can search up the radius and mass of the Earth and assume it is a uniform sphere.)
A: A day is 24 hours, which is 86400 seconds. The frequency of revolution is then $f=1/86400=1.16\times 10^{-5} rev/s$, and the angular velocity is $\omega_0 = 2\pi f=7.29\times 10^{-5} rad/s$. The moment of inertia of the Earth, using $M=5.97\times 10^{26} kg$ and $R=6.37\times 10^{6} m$, is then $I_E=(2/5)MR^2=(2/5)\cdot 5.97\times 10^{26}\cdot (6.37\times 10^{6})^2=9.69\times 10^{39} kg\cdot m^2.$ The initial angular momentum of the asteroid is $mvR=1.0\times 10^5\cdot 35000\cdot 6.37\times 10^{6}=2.23\times 10^{16} kg \cdot m^2/s$. The difference in angular velocity this additional momentum imparts on the Earth (we can neglect the inertia of the embedded asteroid because it is so small) is $L/I_E=2.23\times 10^{16}/9.69\times 10^{39}=2.30\times 10^{-24} rad/s$ which is a fraction $2.30\times 10^{-24}/7.29\times 10^{-5}=3.16\times 10^{-20}$ of the original angular velocity. This is such a small amount!

[14] Challenge Problems
Q: Four bricks are to be stacked at the edge of a table, each brick overhanging the one below it, so that the top brick extends as far as possible beyond the edge of the table. (a) To achieve this, show that successive bricks must extend no more than (starting at the top) 1/2, 1/4, 1/6, and 1/8 of their length beyond the one below. (b) Is the top brick completely beyond the base? (c) Determine a general formula for the maximum total distance spanned by $n$ bricks if they are to remain stable. (Hint: build the stack from the top, and find the maximum distance to the left to which you can place each subsequent block.) (d) A builder wants to construct a corbeled arch based on the principle of stability discussed in (a) and (c) above. What minimum number of bricks, each 0.30 m long and uniform, is needed if the arch is to span 1.0 m?
\begin{center}
 \includegraphics[scale=0.2]{arch}
\end{center}
A: \begin{anum}
 \item At the top, notice that we can place the top block such that its CM does not extend beyond the ledge of the second block, which is 1/2 the length. For the the third block, note that the new CM of the first and second blocks cannot extend beyond the edge of the third block. The CM is indeed now a distance 1/4 away. The same logic can be applied to the other blocks. 
 \item Note that the right end of the top block is a distance $1/2+1/4+1/6+1/8=1.04$ of a length beyond the edge of the table, so yes it does extend beyond the base of the table.
 \item Let each block have mass $M$ and length $L$. Notice that if there are $n-1$ existing blocks, I can place another block below then so long as the COM of these blocks is covered by the ``ledge" of the next block. Thus, I can displace the next block at a maximal distance of $$\Delta L=\frac{L}{2}\frac{M}{M(n-1)+M}=\frac{L}{2}\frac{1}{n}$$ further out from the rest of the stack. Adding these lengths up, we effectively have the distance $$d=\frac{L}{2}\sum_{i=1}^{n}\frac{1}{i}$$
 \item As in the figure we want the second block to extend a distance 0.5 m, so the entire stack extends a distance $d=0.65$ m. Testing some values of $n$ for our stack with $L=0.3$, we need $n$ at least 43. 
 \end{anum}

LESSON: ap-15

[1] Problems
Q: Imagine you're drinking water out of a cup through a straw. What is the maximum theoretical length of the straw that allows you to suck the water up, even if you had a vacuum pump?
A: The largest possible pressure differential you can make is $P_0$, so this equals $\rho_wgh$. Thus $h=P_0/\rho_wg=\boxed{10.3 m}$.

[2] Problems
Q: A cup is filled on one half with a fluid of density $\rho_1$, and on the other half with a fluid of density $\rho_2>\rho_1$ as shown. Is the fluid stable in this position, or will it move? If it moves, how should it move? Assume the fluids do not mix.
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[scale=0.17]{cup}
 \end{center}
A: The fluid is <span style="border:1px solid #555;padding:.1em .4em;font-weight:600;">not stable</span>, because the greater density on the right means a greater pressure existing on the right, leading fluid parcels along the interface to move toward the left. After a long time, all the heavier fluid will end up on the bottom as to minimize the gravitational potential energy on the system.

[3] Problems
Q: Imagine enclosing the surface $S$ in Theorem (another problem) with a portion of area $A$ along the bottom to make a closed shell. Now, fill the shell with a fluid with pressure $P$. By using Newton's laws in the $z$ direction, explain why the total force in the $z$ direction on the surface $S$ has magnitude $PA$.
A: By Newton's third law, the total force exerted by the gas on the shell must be 0, so the shell cannot move. We can easily see that the force the gas exerts on the bottom of area $A$ is $PA.$ As a result, for the total force to be 0 on the entire shell, the force the gas exerts on the surface $S$ must also be $\boxed{PA}$.

[4] Problems
Q: A meniscus, which results from surface tension, is when water rises slightly at the contact point to a container as shown along a curved surface. If the atmospheric pressure is $P_0$, what is the pressure in the water at points $A$, $B$, and $C$? Hint: At an interface between two mediums, the pressure is only the same on both sides of the interface if the interface has no curvature. 
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[scale=0.15]{men}
 \end{center}
A: Using the hint, we know that since $B$ is on the interface of the water and air on a flat surface, the pressure is $\boxed{P_B=P_0}$ there. The pressures at $A$ and $C$ follow easily with $\boxed{P_A=P_0-\rho gh}$ and $\boxed{P_C=P_0+\rho gH}$.

[5] Problems
Q: As an extension of the previous problem, let a sphere of water have radius $r$ and surface tension $\gamma$. The force of surface tension is found as $\gamma \ell$, where $\ell$ is the length of the interface between two mediums. Find the pressure in the sphere as a function of $r$, $\gamma$, and $P_0$. Hint: Split the sphere in half and use force balance.
A: If we split the sphere in half, we consider the forces along the symmetry axis of the sphere. The net force due to pressure on the sphere is due to that acting on its hemispherical surface. There are two components to this -- the outward pressure from the water and the inward pressure from the air. Using Theorem (another problem), the net force is simply the pressure differential times the area of the projection of the hemisphere onto a flat surface, which is just a circle of radius $r$. Hence, the net force due to pressure is $(P-P_0)\pi r^2$. This net force is balanced by the force of surface tension, which acts along the circumference of the circular base of the sphere: $2\pi r\gamma$. Equating these two forces, we get $$(P-P_0)\pi r^2=2\pi r\gamma\Rightarrow P=\boxed{P_0+\frac{2\gamma}{r}}$$

[6] Problems
Q: An aluminum ball of density $\rho$ and volume $V$ sinks in water of density $\rho_w$. \begin{anum}
 \item If the aluminum ball falls a distance $h$, what is the change in gravitational potential energy of the system?
 \item Imagine you don't know anything about buoyancy. Using part (a), find the net force acting on the aluminum ball as it sinks. 
 \item Use your result from (b) to derive the buoyancy force $F_B$ on the aluminum ball. 
 \end{anum}
A: \begin{anum}
 \item As the ball moves down, it moves into the location of a sphere of water at its final position. The sphere of water then moves up to fill in the ball's initial position. The movement of the ball downward results in a decrease $-\rho Vgh$ of potential energy, and the movement of the water upward results in an increase $\rho_w Vgh$ of potential energy. The net change in potential energy is $$\Delta U=\boxed{\rho_wVgh-\rho Vgh}$$
 \item The work done by the net force on the ball when it sinks a distance $h$ is equal to $Fh$, which equals the negative of the change in potential energy. Setting $-Fh=\Delta U$ yields $$F=\boxed{\rho Vg-\rho_wVg}$$
 \item The gravitational force is $\rho Vg$, so the buoyancy force is the other component of the force equation, $F_B=\boxed{\rho_wVg}$.
 \end{anum}

[7] Problems
Q: Water with pressure $P_1$ is flowing through a pipe of cross-sectional area $A_1$ at a speed $v_1$ before the pipe narrows into a cross-sectional area $A_2$ without loss of energy. Find the new pressure of the water $P_2$.
A: By Bernoulli's law, $$P_1+\frac12\rho v_1^2=P_2+\frac12\rho v_2^2$$By continuity, $v_2=(A_1/A_2)v_1$. Combining both equations, we get $$P_2=\boxed{P_1+\frac12\rho v_1^2\left(1-\frac{A_1^2}{A_2^2}\right)}$$

[8] Problems
Q: A hose with gauge pressure $P$, cross sectional area $A$, and water velocity $v$ is fed into a sprinkler that narrows the stream into a cross-sectional area $S$ as it spits out the water into the air. \begin{anum}\item Find the change in the kinetic energy per unit volume of the water between when it is in the hose and when it is just released into the air by the sprinkler.
 \item Find the work done by external forces per unit volume of the water between the same two points discussed in part (a).
 \item Find the energy lost to frictional forces per unit volume of water. 
 \item What must $P$ be so that the energy lost in part (c) equals 0? You'll see that this matches Bernoulli's law.
 \end{anum}
A: \begin{anum}
 \item By mass continuity, the speed $u$ of the water when it exits the sprinkler is $u=(A/S)v$. Per unit volume, the change in energy is thus $$\Delta K=\frac12\rho u^2-\frac12\rho v^2=\boxed{\frac12\rho v^2\left(\frac{A^2}{S^2}-1\right)}$$
 \item The work comes from the gauge pressure $P$. The force produced by any pressure is $PA$, so the work produced by the pressure is $W=PA\Delta x=P\Delta V$. Thus, per unit volume the work done is simply $$W=\boxed{P}$$
 \item The work from part (b) contributes to both energy loss and the increase in kinetic energy of the fluid, so $$E_{\text{lost}}=W-\Delta K=\boxed{P-\frac12\rho v^2\left(\frac{A^2}{S^2}-1\right)}$$
 \item For teh energy lost to equal 0, we require $$P=\boxed{\frac12\rho v^2\left(\frac{A^2}{S^2}-1\right)}$$which upon some rearranging matches the form of Bernoulli's law: $$P+\frac12 \rho v^2=\frac12\rho u^2$$
\end{anum}

[9] Problems
Q: A classic paradox in fluid statics is descrbibed as follows. I fill three cups with the same base area $A$ with the same volume $V$ of water. The water has density $\rho$. The walls of the three cups are slanted at different angles, however, so that the water levels in each cup end up being different.
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[scale=0.2]{wearofi}
 \end{center}
 The three cups are weighed on scales. Alice says they should have the same weight, since they contain the same amount of water. However, Bob argues that since the heights of the water levels in each cup are different, their pressures at the base are different. This should result in a larger force on the bottom of the middle cup than the one on the right, leading to different scale readings. Who is right, and what is wrong with the other person's argument?
A: <span style="border:1px solid #555;padding:.1em .4em;font-weight:600;">Alice is correct</span>. The issue with Bob's logic is that he forgot the force that the water exerts on the walls of the cup. If the walls are not slanted, the force exerted on the walls is perfectly horizontal. If the walls are slanted inward like in the middle cup, the water exerts an upward force on the cup that perfectly balances out the increased downward force on the base of the cup. If the walls are slanted outward like in the right cup, the water exerts an additional downward force along with the force on the base. As a result, the total force exerted on the scale by the cup is the same in all cases.

[10] Problems
Q: A cup of height $H$ is filled to the brim with water. A hole is punctured at some height $h$ above the ground and water begins leaking out horizontally.
 \begin{anum}
 \item Find the speed $v$ of the water as it begins leaking out of the water.
 \item (Challenge) At what height should $h$ be so that the water reaches the maximum distance from the base of the cup? What is this maximum distance?
 \end{anum}
A: \begin{anum}
 \item We apply Bernoulli's law to the surface of the water in the cup and at the hole. The water at both places is exposed to the atmosphere so they both have pressure $P_a$. In addition, the hole is small so the velocity of the water at the surface of the cup is very small compared to the velocity of water coming out of the hole (this follows from mass continuity). Therefore Bernoulli's law states that $$\rho g(H-h)=\frac 12\rho v^2\Longrightarrow \boxed{v = \sqrt{2g(H-h)}}$$
 \item We can use basic kinematics to find the distance the water travels. Note that the water has no vertical velocity initially. Therefore in the vertical direction we have $h = \frac 12 gt^2\Longrightarrow t = \sqrt{\frac{2h}{g}}$. The distance is simply $$d=vt =2\sqrt{h(H-h)}$$We want to find the $h$ such that $\sqrt{h(H-h)}$ is maximal, or equivalently where $h(H-h)$ is maximal. We can easily plot this and see that it is a parabola with roots $h=0,H$. The maximal point is exactly in between the two roots, which means the optimal $h$ is $\boxed{h_{\text{max}}=\frac{H}{2}}$. Plugging this in, the maximal distance is then $$d_{\text{max}}=2\sqrt{h_{\text{max}}(H-h_{\text{max}})}=\boxed{H}$$
 \end{anum}

[11] Problems — Challenge
Q: A car is turning left along a circular track of radius $r$ at a constant speed $v$. A cylindrical beaker is placed vertically inside the car. The beaker has a small hole on its right side. If the water's highest point in the beaker is a height $h$ above the hole, at what instantaneous speed does the water escape the hole, from a passenger's perspective?
A: As the car accelerates, we consider it as an accelerated frame of reference, where gravity now gains a component $a=v^2/r$ to the right as shown: \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[scale=0.2]{water}
 \end{center}
 Now, $g'=\sqrt{g^2+a^2}$ by the Pythagorean theorem. Everything we know about fluids still holds in this new frame with the adjusted $g'$, so we simply need to find the new height $h'$ of the opening under the water surface. By similar triangles, it holds that $h'=(g/g')h$. Then, the speed of the water coming out is just $$u=\sqrt{2g'h'}=\boxed{\sqrt{2gh}}$$

[12] Problems
Q: A boat of mass $M$, rim height $H$, base area $A$, and negligible volume is placed in a lake with water of density $\rho$ as shown. 
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[scale=0.15]{boat}
 \end{center}
 A hole is poked in the bottom of the boat with cross sectional area $S$ and water starts flowing in. Assume the boat sinks very slowly.
 \begin{anum}
 \item Find the initial speed $v_0$ of the water flowing into the boat.
 \item What volume of water is in the boat when it is about to capsize?
 \item Find the amount of time it takes the boat to sink.
 \end{anum}
A: \begin{anum}
 \item By force balance the depth of the boat's base $h$ underwater must displace a mass of water equal to $M$, so we have $M=\rho Ah$. By Bernoulli's law, $$v_0=\sqrt{2gh}=\boxed{\sqrt{\frac{2gM}{\rho A}}}$$
 \item The weight of water displaced by the ``air" portion of the boat must always stay the same to balance the force of the boat (note that the water in the boat feels no effective force, since its weight is equal to the water it displaces), so when the boat is about to capsize it's filled with a height $H-h$ of water. Thus, $$V=A(H-h)=\boxed{AH-\frac M\rho}.$$
 \item Notice that the difference in pressure between just outside the hole and just inside will always be $\rho g(H-h)$, so by Bernoulli's law the velocity of water flow into the boat is always $v=\sqrt{2g(H-h)}$. The rate of volume increase of water in the boat is thus $vS$, so using the result from (b) the time it takes for the boat to sink is $$t=\frac{V}{vS}=\frac{A(H-h)}{S\sqrt{2g(H-h)}}=\boxed{\frac{A}{S}\sqrt{\frac{H-M/A\rho}{2g}}}$$
 \end{anum}
