[1] Concept Checks
Q: Figure out the units of the gravitational constant $G$, knowing that the force of gravity between two objects with masses $m_1$ and $m_2$ separated by a distance $r$ is given by $F = \frac{Gm_1m_2}{r^2}$.
A: After rearranging the equation to $G = \frac{Fr^2}{m_1m_2}$, all there's left to do is plugging in the units of the individual quantities. This should give you $[G] \sim kg^{-1m^3s^{-2}}$

[2] Concept Checks
Q: The range of a projectile (which we'll cover in two weeks time) is proportional to $\sin (k\theta)$, where $\theta$ is the launch angle and $k$ is some numerical constant. Determine $k$ using limiting cases and intuition.
A: We know that at $\theta = \pi/2$ we should get $0$. Thus, we want $k$ to be even. Furthermore, if $k > 2$, then it would cycle to negatives by the time it gets to $\pi/2$, which is unphysical, so $\boxed{k = 2}$.

[3] Concept Checks
Q: Let's say you measure the length of a string using a ruler, and your result is $23.3 \pm 0.1$ cm. According to Eq. (another problem), will your uncertainty decrease by $\sqrt {10}$ if you measure the string 10 times? If not, can you think of an experiment where the uncertainty does decrease by $\sqrt {10}$ with 10 trials?
A: No, it will not decrease. This is the idea of <strong>systematic</strong> vs. <strong>statistical</strong> error. Their names are self-descriptive. Statistical goes down over time (law of large numbers), whereas systematic can be things like calibration error in equipments, and these do not decrease over trials.

[4] Concept Checks
Q: Some food for thought.
 \begin{anum}
 \item Write down the error of the product of $xy$ in terms of $\delta x, x, \delta y, y$.
 \item Now, setting $x = y$, what is the error in $x^2$. Does it match our formula from class: $\delta (x^n) = |n| x^{n-1} \delta x$? If not, what's wrong?
 \end{anum}
A: \begin{anum}
 \item From our error analysis rules, $$\frac{\delta (xy)}{xy} = \sqrt{\left(\frac{\delta x}{x}\right)^2 + \left(\frac{\delta y}{y}\right)^2}.$$
 \item Plugging in $x=y$, we get $\delta (x^2) = x^2 \frac{\sqrt 2 \delta x}{x} = \sqrt 2 x \delta x.$ This does not match! The issue is in independent variables.
 <div style="background:#fff8e1;border-left:4px solid #e0a800;padding:.5em 1em;margin:.5em 0;"><strong>Independence:</strong> Take care to check whether or not your variables are independent when carrying out error analysis!
 
 In part (a), we treat $x$ and $y$ as independent. However, with something like $x^2$, $x$ and $x$ are obviously not independent! The discrepancy intuitively makes sense because if $x$ is shifted positive, then $x$ is also shifted positive, increasing the error of $x^2$, but when they are independent, one could be shifted positive while the other is shifted negative, resulting in a smaller error.</div>
 \end{anum}

[5] Concept Checks — Morin
Q: Two massless strings of equal length support a mass $m$ from a ceiling, making a angle of $2\theta$ between them. Which of the following quantities is the tension (that is, force) $T$ in each string?
\begin{enumerate}[label = (\Alph*)]
 \item $\frac{mg}{2}$
 \item $\frac{mg\sin\theta}{2}$
 \item $\frac{mg\cos \theta}{2}$
 \item $\frac{mg}{2\sin\theta}$
 \item $\frac{mg}{2\cos \theta}$
\end{enumerate}
A: We can look at $\theta = 0$. In that case the two strings should be directly holding up the mass, so the tension should be $mg/2$. That eliminates choices B and D. Now as $\theta \rightarrow \pi / 2$, we see that choice C goes to 0 which doesn't make sense and choices A remains constant which also doesn't make sense. So, it is <strong>E</strong>.

[6] Exercises — $F=ma$
Q: Assume that the drag force for a fish in water depends only on the typical length scale of the fish $R$, its velocity $v$, and the density of water $\rho$. A pufferfish is about 10 cm in length and swims at about 5 m/s. How fast does a clown fish, about 1 cm in length, need to swim such that it experiences the same drag force as the pufferfish?
\begin{enumerate}[label=(\Alph*)]
 \item 5 m/s
 \item 16 m/s
 \item 50 m/s
 \item 500 m/s
 \item 2500 m/s
\end{enumerate}
A: First, let's write out the relevant units.
$$\quad [R] \sim m, \quad [v] \sim m/s, \quad [\rho] \sim kg/m^3, \quad{F} \sim kgm/s^2$$
where $F$ is the drag force. 

We can combine $R$, $v$, and $\rho$ into an expression $R^2v^2\rho$ that will have the same units as $F$. Hence, we can say $$F \sim R^2v^2\rho.$$

So, to match the force experienced by a puffer fish of length $10$ cm and velocity $5$ m/s, a clown fish of length $1$ cm must swim at a velocity of $50$ m/s, or answer choice <strong>C</strong>.

[7] Exercises
Q: Kepler's Third Law says that the period of a planet's orbit $T$ depends on the gravitational constant $G$, the mass of the star $M$, and the length of the semimajor axis $a$ of the orbit. Derive Kepler's Third Law using dimensional analysis, up to a numerical factor.
A: First, we identify the relevant quantities to find period $T$. We have the semi-major axis $a$, the mass of the sun $M$, the gravitational constant $G$, and possibly the mass of the orbiting object $m$. Let's first write out the units of each of these:
 $$\quad [T] \sim s, \quad [a] \sim m, \quad [G] \sim m^3/s^2kg, \quad [M,m] \sim kg.$$

 From here, it is easy to see that we combine these variables into a dimensionless quantity 
 $$ \cfrac{GMT^2}{a^3} \propto 1 \implies T^2 \propto a^3.$$

 Note that we didn't end up using the mass of the orbiting object. To be clear, we don't know whether we should have used $M$ or $m$ when creating our dimensionless quantity. However, in the end it doesn't matter when proving $T^2 \propto a^3$. As a challenge, see if you can derive the whole relationship!

[8] Exercises
Q: Using your result for Kepler's Third Law, find the relative error $\delta a/a$ in your measurement of a comet's semimajor axis $a$ given that you measure its orbital period to be $300 \pm 1$ day, and that its star's mass is $(3.3\pm 0.1)\times 10^{30}$ kg.
A: Using our rule, $$\frac{\delta a}{a} = \sqrt{\left(\frac{\delta(M^{1/3})}{M^{1/3}}\right)^2 + \left(\frac{\delta(T^{2/3})}{T^{2/3}}\right)^2}.$$
 Applying the power rule as well, $$\frac{\delta a}{a} = \sqrt{\left(\frac{1}{3}\frac{\delta M}{M}\right)^2 + \left(\frac{2}{3}\frac{\delta T}{T}\right)^2}.$$ Simply plugging in the numbers, we get $\boxed{\delta a / a = 1.03 \%}$.

[9] Exercises — $F=ma$
Q: A rod moves freely between the horizontal floor and the slanted wall. When the end in contact with the floor is moving at $v$, what is the speed of the end in contact with the wall?
\begin{center}
 \includegraphics[width = 0.4 \linewidth]{17_16.PNG}
\end{center}
(A) $v\dfrac{\sin \theta}{\cos(\alpha - \theta)}$ \quad (B) $v\dfrac{\sin (\alpha - \theta)}{\cos(\alpha + \theta)}$ \quad (C) $v\dfrac{\cos (\alpha - \theta)}{\sin(\alpha + \theta)}$ \quad (D) $v\dfrac{\cos \theta}{\cos (\alpha - \theta)}$ \quad (E) $v\dfrac{\sin \theta}{\cos(\alpha + \theta)}$
A: If we set $\alpha = 2\theta$, by symmetry (an isosceles triangle is created), it should be equal to $v$. This only leaves <strong>D</strong>.

[10] Exercises
Q: When you look at the quantum mechanics of an atom, there are a couple of physical constants that are relevant.
 \begin{itemize}
 \item $\hbar$, planck's reduced constant,
 \item $e$, the fundamental charge,
 \item $k$, Coulomb's constant,
 \item and $c$, the speed of light.
 \end{itemize}
 \begin{anum}
 \item Come up with a combination of these four constants that is dimensionless. Your expression, or some power of it <span class="footnote">[Because, of course, if you found a quantity $\alpha$ that's dimensionless, $\alpha^x$ is also dimensionless, so you might not have found the fine structure constant.]</span>, is called the <em>fine structure constant</em>. <em>Hint:</em> as is a theme throughout this problem set, using physical intuition about real physics laws should help simplify your work.
 \item Let $N$ be the maximum achievable atomic number, limited by relativity. Estimate it using your work in the previous part.
 \end{anum}
A: \begin{anum}
 \item As before, the first step is always to write out the units of the constants. Probably the most obscure constant here is $\hbar$, which you will encounter later on in your studies of quantum physics. For now, we will simply state the units.
 $$\quad [\hbar] \sim kgm^2/s, \quad [e] \sim e, \quad [k] \sim kgm^3/s^2e^2, \quad [c] \sim m/s.$$
 Note we use $e$ to represent a unit of charge and also the fundamental charge. These can be combined into a dimensionless quantity $\frac{ke^2}{\hbar c}$. The simplest way to get to this is that we know that $$U = \frac{ke^2}{r}, E = \frac{hc}{\lambda},$$
 and so we see that $\frac{ke^2}{hc}\frac{\lambda}{r}$ would be a dimensionless quantity. Since $\lambda / r$ is dimensionless, we can simply take the left fraction, giving $\alpha = \tfrac{ke^2}{\hbar c}$.
 
 You may search up the numerical values of these constants online. Plugging the values into our dimensionless expressions, we get a value of roughly $\boxed{0.0073 \approx 1/137}$. That's the fine structure constant for you!
 \item Any power of $\alpha \approx 1/137$ is going to be dimensionless. We know that $N$ must be dimensionless and we also expect it to be $\sim 10^2$ from basic chemistry. Thus, we can guess $1/\alpha \approx \boxed{137}$.
 \end{anum}

[11] Exercises — Morin
Q: Consider the line described by $ax + by + c = 0$. Which of the following expressions is the distance from this line to the point $(x_0, y_0)$?
 \begin{enumerate}[label=(\Alph*)]
 \item $\frac{bx_0+ay_0+c}{\sqrt{a^2+b^2}}$
 \item $\frac{ax_0+by_0+c}{\sqrt{a^2+b^2}}$
 \item $\frac{ax_0+by_0}{\sqrt{a^2+b^2}}$
 \item $\frac{ax_0+by_0+c}{\sqrt{a^2+b^2+c^2}}$
\end{enumerate}
A: First, we know that $c$ must be in the expression as that changes the line. This eliminates (C). Now, set $a = 0$. Then, the closest distance is $y_0 +c/b$ as it's a straight line. This only leaves <strong>B</strong>.

[12] Exercises — $F=ma$
Q: Alice and Bob are working on a lab report. Alice measures the period of a pendulum to be $1.013 \pm 0.008 \text{s}$, while Bob independently measures the period to be $0.997 \pm 0.016 \text{s}$. Alice and Bob can combine their measurements in several ways.
\begin{enumerate}
 \item Keep Alice's result and ignore Bob's
 \item Average Alice's and Bob's results
 \item Perform a weighted average of Alice's and Bob's results, with Alice's result weighted 4 times more than Bob's

\end{enumerate}
How are the uncertainties of these results related?

\begin{enumerate}[label=(\Alph*)]
 \item Method 1 has the lowest uncertainty, and method 2 has the highest
 \item Method 3 has the lowest uncertainty, and method 2 has the highest
 \item Method 2 has the lowest uncertainty, and method 1 has the highest
 \item Method 3 has the lowest uncertainty, and method 1 has the highest
 \item Method 1 has the lowest uncertainty, and method 3 has the highest. 
\end{enumerate}
A: Let's first list out what each of the options want for our result $R$. Let $A$ be Alice's result and $B$ be Bob's result. 
\begin{enumerate}
\item $R$ = $A$
\item $R$ = $\frac{A+B}{2}$
\item $R$ = $\frac{4A + B}{5}$
\end{enumerate}

Note that, if some result $x$ has absolute uncertainty $\delta x$, then the result $cx$ for some constant $c$ will have absolute uncertainty $c\delta x$. You may use rule 3 from the lecture to prove this. Using this insight, along with the addition rule (rule 1), we can solve for the absolute uncertainties in each of these cases. 
\begin{enumerate}
\item $\delta R$ = $\delta A$
\item $\delta R$ = $\frac{\sqrt{\delta A^2 + \delta B^2}}{2} = \frac{\sqrt{\delta A^2 + \delta (2A)^2}}{2} = \frac{\sqrt{5}}{2}\delta A$
\item $R$ = $\frac{sqrt{\delta(4A)^2 + \delta B^2}}{5} = \frac{\sqrt{16\delta A^2 + 4\delta A^2}}{5} = \frac{2}{\sqrt{5}}\delta A$


\end{enumerate}
From here, we see that method 3 gives the lowest uncertainty, and method 2 gives the highest uncertainty. The reason why method 3 gives lower uncertainty compared to method 1 despite including Bob's higher uncertainty is because more measurements will always help reduce uncertainty; higher uncertainty measurements simply have to be taken with less importance (as we see here, Bob's measurement is 1/4 as important as Alice's when calculating the final result).

[13] Exercises — $F=ma$
Q: A mass is attached to one end of a rigid rod, while the other end of the rod is attached to a fixed horizontal axle. Initially the mass hangs at the end of the rod and the rod is vertical. The mass is given an initial kinetic energy $K$. If $K$ is very small, the mass behaves like a pendulum, performing small-angle oscillations with period $T_0$. As $K$ is increased, the period of the motion for the mass
\begin{enumerate}[label=(\Alph*)]
 \item remains the same.
 \item increases, approaching a finite constant.
 \item decreases, approaching a finite non-zero constant.
 \item decreases, approaching zero.
 \item initially increases, then decreases.
\end{enumerate}
A: Recall that a pendulum makes the assumption that acceleration in the direction of motion is proportional to $\sin{\theta} \sim \theta$ at small angles. Specifically, the acceleration experienced by the bob of the pendulum equals $g\sin\theta$, but we approximate this to just be $g\theta$ to derive the formula for an ideal pendulum $T = 2\pi\sqrt{l/g}$. At larger angles however, $\theta$ outgrows $\sin{\theta}$, meaning $g\theta$ is over estimating the acceleration and our ideal formula is predicting the pendulum to be swinging faster than it actually is. Really, as amplitude increases, the period of a pendulum will actually increase. <br>
 Now, what if we keep on increasing the initial kinetic energy $K$? We figured out that as amplitude increases, oscillations will take longer. But, if $K$ goes to infinity, the pendulum will start to go in circles! And as $K$ gets bigger, the pendulum will go around in circles even faster. So, its period will decrease. Hence, the correct answer is <strong>E</strong>.

[14] Challenge Problems
Q: You're at the IPhO. You've just taken $n$ measurements with value $x_i$ and uncertainty $\delta x_i$. What's the best weighted average you can take here to minimize your uncertainty?
A: If we weight each with $\alpha_i$, we want to minimize $$\sum_i (\alpha_i \delta x_i)^2.$$
 You can minimize this either through <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lagrange_multiplier" target="_blank" rel="noopener">Lagrange multipliers</a>, or through math olympiad inequalities, and in any case, you will find $$\alpha_i \propto 1/\delta x_i^2$$ as the optimal weighting to reduce the quadrature error.

[15] Challenge Problems
Q: You have $N$ weights and you want to find their average weight. Your scale reads with an uncertainty of $\delta w$. What's the minimum error you can get in the average weight $\overline w$? <em>Hint:</em> the first procedure you think of to measure their average weight is probably not the most optimal.
A: It's a bit hard to think of, but the most optimal way to measure this average is to measure all $N$ weights at once because the scale reads with a fixed uncertainty. Thus, when dividing by $N$ for the average, the error goes dwon by $1/N$, which is the best possible here (better than $1/\sqrt N$ for typical average).

[16] Challenge Problems — $F=ma$
Q: A water hose is at ground level against the base of a large wall. By aiming the hose at some angle, and squirting water at a speed $v$, one wets a region on the wall, as shown below. If the speed of the water is doubled, what is the new region that can be wetted? Ignore the effect of water splashing beyond the point of contact. In the answer choices, the dotted line marks the initial wetted region.
\begin{center}
 \includegraphics[width=\linewidth]{19b_13.PNG}
\end{center}
A: Note how little information the problem gives us! The only pertinent variables are $v$, $\theta$, and $g$. The only way to combine these three variables to get units of distance is $\frac{v^2}{g}$. Therefore, when we double the velocity, both the vertical and horizontal distances must be proportional nothing other than $v^2$. Hence, answer choice D is the correct answer.

[17] Challenge Problems — IPhO, but don't be scared!
Q: Stefan Boltzmann's law states that the power same dimensions as force times velocity) per area radiated by a blackbody with temperature $T$ is given by $P=\sigma T^4$. It turns out that $\sigma$ can be written in terms of other fundamental constants and a dimensionless prefactor. Your answer should be a combination of some or all of the following: $\hbar,c,G,k_B$. <em>Hint:</em> This is physics! Use some physical intuition to simplify your work at the start.
A: First, there is one uneccessary constant in the mix, $G$! Stars are held together by gravity, for sure, but the process of radiation has nothing to do with gravity (a plain old hot rock on the ground radiates heat as well). Thus, we only have to work with $\hbar, c, k_B$. We know that $k_BT$ gives units of energy, so $$[k_B^4 T^4 ] \sim J^4.$$ We also know that $[\hbar c ] \sim J m$, so, $$\left [ \frac{k_B^4 T^4}{(\hbar c)^3}\right ] \sim \frac{J}{m^3}.$$ We want $J/m^2s$, so we just we just multiply by a $c$, and we get $$\left[\frac{k_B^4 T^4}{\hbar^3 c^2}\right] \sim \frac{J}{m^2 s},$$ so we can say $\boxed{\sigma \sim \frac{k_B^4}{\hbar^3c^2}}.$

LESSON: fma-02
------------------------------------------------------------------------------

[1] Concept Checks
Q: A car traveling at 5 m/s begins to slow with a deceleration of 2 $ m/s^2$. How long are the skid marks made by the car?
A: Applying $v_f^2 = 0 = v_i^2 - 2ax$, we get $x = \frac{v^2}{2a} = \boxed{6.25 m}$.

[2] Concept Checks
Q: You throw a ball up at 5 m/s. How long does it take for it to come back down?
A: By symmetry it should be moving downward at 5 m/s when it gets all the way back down, so applying the definition of acceleration, we get $\Delta t = \frac{10 m/s}{9.8 m/s^2} = \boxed{1.0 s}$.

[3] Concept Checks
Q: How far up does the ball in the previous problem travel? You could use either a kinematic equation or an average velocity idea.
A: We can use an average velocity idea, it goes for $\Delta t / 2$ at an average upward velocity of $2.5 m/s$, or we can use the same equation from problem 2, which gives us $\Delta y = \frac{v^2}{2g} = \boxed{1.3 m}$.

[4] Concept Checks
Q: A car originally moving at $7 m/s$ begins accelerating at $3 m/s^2$. How far does it travel in $5 s$?
A: This is just plugging into $\Delta x = v_0 \Delta t + \frac 12 a \Delta t^2 = \boxed{72.5 \unit m}$.

[5] Concept Checks
Q: If drops of water are released from rest from a dispenser at equal time intervals $\tau$, will they collide with the floor at equal time intervals? What if the dispenser was moving upward at a constant speed? What about downward?
A: <span style="border:1px solid #555;padding:.1em .4em;font-weight:600;">Yes, they will collide at equal time intervals in all cases.</span> While the distance between the drops grows as they are dropped, the rate at which they impact the floor will always be the same (imagine the drops that hit the floor are ``recycled" back into the dispenser). The only difference between the cases is that the time intervals are larger if the dispenser was moving upward, and smaller if the dispenser was moving downward. Another way to think about this is that if the rate of collision increased or decreased, we would eventually reach a point where we have either infinite or 0 collisions per second, which is unreasonable.

[6] Concept Checks
Q: In the following $v$ vs $t$ graph, mark the points of maximal a) displacement, b) velocity, c) acceleration, and d) jerk. 
\begin{center}
\includegraphics[width=7cm]{samplegraph1.png}
\end{center}
A: Since we are looking at a $v$ vs $t$ graph, the displacement is the area under the curve, so it's maximal at the end of the motion. The maximal velocity is clearly the maximal point on the graph. Since acceleration is the slope, the maximal acceleration is found when the slope is maximal. Jerk is slightly more tricky, since it's the change in acceleration over time. Thus, we're looking for the point where <em>slope changes the fastest</em>. We can see that the slope instantaneously changes where the graph is sharp, which indicates a very high jerk. Thus, all the points are plotted as shown.

[7] Concept Checks
Q: A car and a truck are driving towards each other on a road, both at 40 m/s. The car has a maximal deceleration of 5 m/s$^2$ and the truck has a maximal deceleration of 3 m/s$^2$. The car and the truck notice each other at the same time and start braking. What is the minimal distance $d$ between them at this moment to avoid a collision?
A: We will use the kinematic formula $v^2=2ad$. The car will stop in a distance $d_1=v^2/2a=40^2/2\cdot5=160$ m, and likewise the truck stops in a distance $d_2=267$ m. The minimal distance is thus $d=d_1+d_2=\boxed{427 m}$.

[8] Exercises
Q: A car (again) passes policeman $A$ whose speedometer reads $4 m/s$ and then passes policeman $B$ whose speedometer reads $8 m/s$. If policemen $A$ and $B$ are stationed $20 m$ apart, how much time passed between the car passing policeman $A$ and the car passing policeman $B$?
A: We have that the average speed is $\frac{8+4}{2} = 6 m/s$, so the time in between is $\Delta t = \Delta x / v = \boxed{3.3 s}$.

[9] Exercises — HRK
Q: A stone is thrown vertically upward. On its way up it passes point $A$ with speed $v$, and point $B$, $3.00 m$ higher than $A$, with speed $v/2$. Calculate (a) the speed $v$ and (b) the maximum height reached by the stone above point $B$.
A: ~
\begin{anum}
 \item We use $(v/2)^2 = v^2 - 2g\Delta x$ again, which gives us $\frac{3}{4} v^2 = 2g\Delta x \implies v = \sqrt{\frac{8}{3} g \Delta x} = \boxed{8.9 m/s}$.
 \item This is equivalent to problem 2, now that we know $v$. We have $h = \frac{(v/2)^2}{2g} = \frac{\Delta x}{3} = \boxed{1.00 m}$.
\end{anum}

[10] Exercises
Q: Jerk is defined as change in acceleration over time and has dimensions of $[L]/[T]^3$. A car, initially at rest, experiences a jerk of 5 m/s$^3$ for 10 seconds, and then a jerk of $-3$ m/s$^3$ for a time $t$. What is $t$ such that the car eventually returns to rest?
A: We haven't dealt with Jerk yet. If you're famililar with calculus, it should be relatively straightforward --- simply integrating. However, if you're not, here's a way to reason through it. Jerk is the rate of change of the rate of change of velocity. We also know that acceleration is the rate of change of the rate of change of position:
 $$j = \frac{\Delta a}{\Delta t}, a = \frac{\Delta v}{\Delta t}, \quad a = \frac{\Delta v}{\Delta t}, v = \frac{\Delta x}{\Delta t}.$$
 These equations are exactly the same, with just the variable names changed! So, we can rewrite our position kinematics equations by making the substitution $j \leftrightarrow a, a \leftrightarrow v, v\leftrightarrow x$ (the deeper reason behind this working is calculus). So, we have $$\Delta v = a_0 \Delta t + \frac{1}{2} j \Delta t^2.$$ Thus, after the first segment, the car has velocity $\frac{5 \cdot 10^2}{2} = 250 m/s$ and acceleration $50 m/s^2$. Now we plug that into the equation we developed again,
 $$0 - 250 m/s = 50t - \frac{3}{2} t^2.$$
 Solving the quadratic, we get $t \approx \boxed{37.749 s}.$

[11] Exercises — $F=ma$
Q: A ball is bouncing vertically between a floor and ceiling, which are both horizontal and separated by 4 m. All collisions are perfectly elastic, and when the ball hits the floor, it has a speed of 12 m/s. How long does a complete up-down cycle take?
A: For convenience we'll use $g= 10$ for this problem. We can write $4 = v_0 t - \frac 12 gt^2 = 12 t - 5 t^2$, so solving the quadratic we get $t = \frac 25, 2$. The second, larger time would be for if there was no ceiling and it went all the way up and back down. So, it takes $0.4 \unit s$ to get from bottom to top. This means the complete cycle, by symmetry takes double that, so $\boxed{0.8 \unit s}$.

[12] Exercises — Morin
Q: A ball is dropped, and then another ball is dropped from the same spot one second later. As time goes on while the balls are falling, the distance between them (ignoring air resistance, as usual):
 \begin{enumerate}[label=(\Alph*)]
 \item decreases
 \item remains the same
 \item increases and approaches a limiting value
 \item increases steadily
 \end{enumerate}
A: The answer is <strong>(D)</strong>. At the moment the second ball is released, the first ball effectively has some initial velocity $v_0$ downward while the second ball has $0$ initial velocity. So, $y_1(t) = y_0 + v_0t + \frac{1}{2}gt^2$ while the second ball has $y_2(t) = \frac 12 gt^2$, so $y_1-y_2 = y_0 + v_0t$ which steadily increases over time.

[13] Exercises — $F=ma$
Q: The ball starts at point $A$, turns around at point $B$, and returns to point $A$. Which of the following
shows the <em>speed</em> of the ball as a function of time?
\begin{center}
\includegraphics[width=14cm]{2021_fma_1_choices.png}
\end{center}
A: Notice that this says speed, so it should be positive at all times. That only leaves choices (B) and (C). We haven't learned about inclined planes yet <span class="footnote">[my apologies.]</span> but they have constant acceleration, which means that the $v$ vs. $t$ graph should be a straight line. So, we get <strong>(C)</strong>.

[14] Exercises — $F=ma$
Q: Which of the following shows the <em>horizontal velocity</em> of the ball as a function of time?
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[width=14cm]{2021_fma_2_choices.png}
 \end{center}
A: This time since it is velocity it should change sign. Furthermore, because the transition from straight to slope is smooth and we're looking at only the <em>horizontal</em> velocity, it should dip. This is because the overal velocity should remain the same, but it tilts at an angle, making the horizontal component smaller. This only leaves choice <strong>(E)</strong>.

[15] Challenge Problems — $F=ma$
Q: The <strong>velocity versus position</strong> plot of a particle is shown below. Which of the following choices is the correct <strong>acceleration vs. position</strong> plot of the particle?
\begin{center}
 \includegraphics[scale = 0.2]{sws}
 \includegraphics[width = \linewidth]{aws}
\end{center}
A: Suppose the particle starts at $x = 0$. Then its velocity is zero, meaning it remains at 0, and its acceleration is zero. So the correct acceleration plot must start at $(0,0)$. It remains to determine whether the plot of acceleration vs time curves upward or is a straight line. \\
 Suppose it is a straight line, as shown in choice (C). Then the average force on the particle traveling from some very small value to some $x$ is proportional to $x$. The energy of the particle, which is the average force multiplied by $x$, is then proportional to $x^2$. Because the energy is the square of the velocity, we have $v^2 \propto x^2$, or simply $v \propto x$, as desired, so choice <strong>(C)</strong> is the one consistent with the shown $v$ vs $x$ plot. \\
 For a calculus-based solution, the velocity is given by $v = kx$ for some $k$. Differentiating both sides with respect to time, we find $a = kv = k^2x$, so again we find that the acceleration is a straight line starting at zero and sloping upward.

[16] Challenge Problems — Kamal
Q: Two balls are thrown off a building in opposite horizontal directions with velocities $v_1$ and $v_2$. Find the time $t$ where the balls' velocities are perpendicular to each other and their separation $d$ at that time.
A: The horizontal speeds of these balls are constant. $v_y$ of both balls is the same at every moment, $v_y=gt$. Using some trig and visualization, we see that for their velocities to be perpendicular, the time t must satisfy $\frac{v_{2y}}{v_2}=\frac{v_1}{v_{1y}}$. $\frac{gt}{v_2}=\frac{v_1}{gt}$. Solving for t, we get
$$t=\boxed{\frac{\sqrt{v_1v_2}}{g}}.$$
Their separation d is simple to find, since we know the time and their horizontal velocities are constant.
$$d=(v_1+v_2)t=\boxed{\frac{\sqrt{v_1v_2}(v_1+v_2)}{g}}.$$

[17] Challenge Problems — $F=ma$
Q: A train starts from city $A$ and stops in city $B$. The distance between the cities is $s$. The train's maximal acceleration is $a_1$ and its maximum deceleration is $a_2$ (in absolute value). What is the shortest time in which the train can travel between $A$ and $B$?
 \begin{enumerate}[label=(\Alph*)]
 \item $2\sqrt{\frac{s}{a_1+a_2}}$
 \item $2\sqrt{\frac{s}{\sqrt{a_1a_2}}}$
 \item $\sqrt{2\frac{s(a_1+a_2)}{a_1a_2}}$
 \item $\sqrt{\frac{2sa_2}{a_1(a_1+a_2)}}$
 \item $\sqrt{\frac{s\sqrt{a_1a_2}}{(a_1+a_2)^2}}$
 \end{enumerate}
A: As discussed in the similar lecture problem, we accelerate initially at max and decelerate maximally, meaning $a_1 t_1 = v_{\text{max}} = a_2 t_2$, so $t_1:t_2 = a_2:a_1$,=. Thus, if the total travel time is $t$, then using ratios, it accelerates for $\frac{a_2}{a_1+a_2}t$ and decelerates for $\frac{a_1}{a_1+a_2}t$. And each segment covers a distance of $\frac 12 a_i t_i^2$, so
 $$\frac 12 a_1 \left(\frac{a_2}{a_1+a_2}t\right)^2 + \frac 12 a_2 \left(\frac{a_1}{a_1+a_2}t\right)^2 = \frac 12 \frac{a_1a_2}{a_1+a_2}t^2 = s.$$
 So, solving for $t$, we get $t = \sqrt{2 \frac{s(a_1+a_2)}{a_1a_2}}$, which corresponds to answer <strong>(C)</strong>. If you plug in the numbers for the lecture problem you should get the same answer!

[18] Challenge Problems
Q: The manufacturer of a clock screwed up real bad and placed the minute hand on the edge of the clock face. The minute hand pivots around its hinge at an angular velocity $\omega$. The radius of the clock face is $r$. Find the speed of the intersection point (red) between the rim of the clock face and the minute hand during the motion as a function of $\theta$, the angle between the minute hand and the vertical.
\begin{center}
 \includegraphics[width = 0.25\linewidth]{clock2}
\end{center}
A: \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[width = 0.25\linewidth]{clocksol}
\end{center}
Note that the velocity $v$ of the intersection point must be tangent to the circle. This velocity will have a component parallel to the rod and perpendicular to the rod as it moves up the rod and with the rod rotationally. The component of the velocity $u$ perpendicular to the minute hand is equal to $u=\omega l=2\omega r\cos\theta.$ By angle chasing, we find that the angle between $u$ and $v$ is also $\theta$. By the right triangle, they must be related by $u=v\cos\theta$, so $v=\boxed{2\omega r}$. Perhaps surprisingly, it's independent of the angle!

Alternatively, note that if you connect a radius from the center of the circle to the intersection point, then we can create an inscribed angle. Consider the intersection point at two times $t_1$ and $t_2$ where the rod at the two time points is separated by an angle $\theta$. Then, by the inscribed angle theorem, the two radii are separated by an angle $2\theta$, and since the intersection point moves along the circle, it means it has angular velocity $2\omega$, and so we immediately get $2\omega r$. This explains the independence of angle!

[19] Challenge Problems — $F=ma$
Q: You have 5 different strings with weights tied at various points, all hanging from the ceiling, and reaching down to the floor. The string is released at the top, allowing the weights to fall. Which one will create a regular, uniform beating sound as the weights hit the floor?
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[width=12cm]{2011_fma.png}
 \end{center}
A: Let's say that we want to create an interval $\Delta t$ between the sounds. Then, the $k+1$st mass should land at time $k\Delta t$. And since it starts from rest, it means the $k+1$st covers a distance of $\frac 12 g (k\Delta t)^2 \propto k^2$. So, the different masses' distances from the bottom should increase quadratically. This is the case for <strong>(D)</strong>.

[20] Challenge Problems
Q: Evan's boat can move at a speed $u$ in still water. However, today he is in a fast river that flows at a speed $v = 2u$. He starts off a perpendicular distance $r$ from a pier as shown (see figure below). There is a way Evan can power his boat such that he ends up the least distance downstream when he reaches the riverbank. Find this least distance $d$.
\begin{center}
 \includegraphics[width = 0.4\linewidth]{boat}
\end{center}
A: His net velocity is the sum of the velocities $v$ and $u$, the latter of which Evan can determine the direction. By velocity addition, this looks like this:
\begin{center}
 \includegraphics[width = 0.3\linewidth]{vu}
\end{center}
Since we can determine the direction of $u$, the resultant vector $v+u$ can end at any point along the given circle. To minimize the distance we move downstream, we would like $v+u$ to be tangent to the circle like so:
\begin{center}
 \includegraphics[width = 0.3\linewidth]{vu2}
\end{center}
And since $v = 2u$, our optimal heading is at an angle of $30^\circ$ to the horizontal. This means the minimum distance we can end up downstream is $$d=\boxed{\sqrt3 r}.$$

[21] Challenge Problems
Q: Suppose Evan isn't as bright as he was in Problem Problem 20 and decides to align his rudder directly toward the pier at all times. Assume here that $v=u$. How far does Evan end up downstream when he reaches the riverbank? Also, which of the following graphs is Evan's correct trajectory? (Challenge problem, optional)

\begin{center}
\includegraphics[width=0.8\linewidth]{boat_choices.png}
\end{center}
A: The key insight into this problem is that Evan is heading at the same velocity toward the pier and to the right at all times. Observe an arbitrary position here. 
\begin{center}
\includegraphics[width = 0.3\linewidth]{boat4} 
\end{center}The two components of velocity are indicated: one caused by the river and one caused by Evan's boat. We can see that the rate at which Evan moves toward the pier is identical to the rate he moves to the right; specifically, $u(1+\cos\theta)$, where $\theta$ is the angle between the vectors. Hence, (and here's the important bit), <strong>given an arbitrary vertical line placed a distance $r$ downstream, Evan will remain equidistant from that vertical line and the pier at all times, because his component of velocity in those directions is equal at all times.</strong> Sound familiar? This is the definition of a parabola, where the pier is the focus and the line is the directrix. By this parabola, the final distance downstream is $d=r/2$ and the correct multiple choice option is <strong>(E)</strong>.

[22] Challenge Problems — Kalda
Q: A car accelerates on a slippery ground
so that the wheels are always at the limit of slipping (e.g. via using
an electronic traction control). Such an acceleration would result
in the velocity vs time graph as given in the figure.
\begin{center}
\includegraphics[width=0.8\linewidth]{kaldacar.png}
\end{center}
Because of a manual gear change, there is time period of
$\tau_1 = 0.5$ s during which there is no driving force (so that the
car decelerates due to air friction). Except for that period, the
acceleration follows the law given by the graph. As a result, the
terminal velocity $v_t = 40$ m/s is reached $\tau_2 = 1.0$ s later than it
would have been reached, if there were no delay caused by the gear
change. Upon reaching the terminal velocity, the car continues
moving at constant speed. In your calculations, you can assume
that the air friction was constant during the gear change period.
\begin{anum}
\item At which speed the gear was changed? 
\
\item How many meters shorter distance will be covered during the first 100 seconds, as compared to ideal acceleration (i.e.
without the delay due to the gear change)?
\end{anum}
A: \begin{anum}
 \item When the driving force stops, the acceleration is reduced by $F_f /m = \mu g$, i.e. from the slope at the current point of the graph we need to subtract the slope of it at the origin. A close-up sketch of the graph around the period of gear change is given in the figure below. After the gear change, the new graph follows the ideal graph, but is shifted rightwards by $\tau_2$, this shift is marked also in Fig. Since $2\tau_1 = \tau_2$, the ascending and descending slopes in that close-up sketch must be of equal steepness, i.e. $a = \mu g/2$. So, the gear change takes place at that speed, when the acceleration is twice smaller than at zero-speed. From the graph we can find that $v_0 \approx \boxed{25 m/s}$.
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[scale=0.3]{kaldasol}
 \end{center}
 \item The distance difference is the area $S$ between the actual $v(t)$ graph and the ideal one. These graphs coincide for $v < v_0$ and upon achieving the value $v = v_t$. So, the area $S$ is enclosed into the range $v_t > v > v_0$, where the actual graph is, in fact, just the ideal graph, but shifted rightwards by $\tau_2$. This area has a shape of a narrow curved stripe, the horizontal width of which is at every value of $v$ equal to $\tau_2$. One can divide this stripe into tiny horizontal layers of height $\delta$ and width $\tau_2$. If we sum up the surface areas of these layers, we can bring $\tau_2$ before the braces; then, the sum of the layer widths goes into the braces and yields $v_t - v_0$. So, the area $S = \tau_2(v_t-v_0) \approx \boxed{15 m}$.
 \end{anum}

[23] Challenge Problems — $F=ma$
Q: A stream of sand is dropped out of a helicopter initially moving at a constant speed $v$ to the right. The helicopter suddenly turns and begins moving a constant speed $v$ to the left. Neglecting air resistance on the sand, what is the shape of the stream of sand, as viewed from the ground? The black dot represents the helicopter.
 \begin{center}
\includegraphics[width=0.95\linewidth]{18a_171.png}
\end{center}
A: Consider all the particles of sand that were dropped when the helicopter was moving forward. They all have horizontal velocity component $v$ at all times, and so they must have the same $x$ component. Similarly, all the particles that were dropped while moving backward have horizontal velocity component $-v$, and so they should also have the same $y$ component, but also attached to the helicopter as the helicopter is moving backward at $v$ too. Thus, it should be two vertical lines, choice <strong>(D)</strong>.

------------------------------------------------------------------------------
LESSON: fma-03
------------------------------------------------------------------------------

[1] Projectile motion
Q: A ball is thrown into the air. 
 \begin{anum}
 \item Neglecting air resistance, what speed must it be thrown at to reach space? (100 km above the surface of the Earth)
 \item Taking air resistance into account, does it take longer for the ball to rise up to its highest point or for the ball to fall back down from its highest point?
 \end{anum}
A: \begin{anum}
 \item We simply use $v^2=2gh$, so $v=\sqrt{2gh}=\boxed{1400 m/s}.$
 \item Notice the ball is traveling the same distance in both cases, so what matters is the acceleration of the ball while it's moving. If the acceleration is larger, then the time it takes to travel the distance is less. Since air resistance and gravity both pull down on the object while it's traveling up, but oppose each other while it's traveling down, the acceleration is larger for the former case. Thus, it takes <span style="border:1px solid #555;padding:.1em .4em;font-weight:600;">longer for the ball to fall back down</span>.
 \end{anum}

[2] Projectile motion
Q: At what angle should a ball be thrown on a horizontal surface so that it has:
 \begin{anum}
 \item The longest air time?
 \item The maximal height?
 \item The furthest horizontal distance?
 \end{anum}
A: \begin{anum}
\item We want to dedicate all our speed to the vertical direction, so $\theta=\boxed{90^\circ}$. 
\item By the same logic as part (a), $\theta=\boxed{90^\circ}$.
 \item As derived in lecture, the distance a ball travels when thrown on a horizontal surface is $d=\frac{v^2\sin2\theta}{g}$. The $\sin$ function is maximized when the argument $2\theta=90^\circ$, so $\theta=\boxed{45^\circ}.$
 \end{anum}

[3] Projectile motion
Q: A ball is thrown up in the air at a 60$^\circ$ angle at a speed $10$ m/s. Find:
 \begin{anum}
 \item The maximal height $h$ of the ball.
 \item The total distance traveled $d$ of the ball.
 \item The time in the air $t$ of the ball.
 \end{anum}
A: \begin{anum}
 \item Starting with the vertical direction of motion, we can see that $v_y=v\sin\theta=8.66 m/s.$ The maximal height can then be found from $v_y^2=2gh\Rightarrow h=v_y^2/2g=\boxed{3.82 m}.$
 \item Looking at the vertical direction, we can find the amount of time the ball is in the air by $t=\Delta v/g$. It moves up originally at $+v_y$, and ends up moving down at $-v_y$, so $t=2v_y/g=1.77 s.$ Then, the total horizontal distance is $d=v_xt=vt\cos\theta=\boxed{8.85 m}.$
 \item We already found the time in the air: $t=\boxed{1.77 s}.$
 \end{anum}

[4] Projectile motion
Q: Solve the general case of a ball thrown off a cliff. The cliff has height $h$, the ball is thrown at a speed $v$, and at an angle $\theta$ above the horizontal. Find the total horizontal distance $d$ the ball travels.
A: Analyzing the $y$ direction first, we have an initial speed upward $v_y=v\sin\theta$, gravity pulls down at $-g$, and our final height is $-h$ relative to our starting point (we have taken the upward direction to be positive). Then, we have $$-h=v_yt-\frac12 gt^2\Rightarrow \frac12gt^2-v_yt-h=0$$Since we have a quadratic and we want to find $t$, we will use the quadratic formula to find $$t=\frac{v_y\pm \sqrt{v_y^2+2gh}}{g}$$But since $t$ has to be positive and $\sqrt{v_y^2+2gh}>v_y$, we need to take the solution $$t=\frac{v_y+ \sqrt{v_y^2+2gh}}{g}.$$Then, we can find the distance $d$ simply by considering the motion in the $x$ direction, where $d=v_xt=v\cos\theta t$. Combining all our info, $$d=v\cos\theta\left(\frac{v_y+ \sqrt{v_y^2+2gh}}{g}\right)=\boxed{v\cos\theta\left(\frac{v\sin\theta+ \sqrt{v^2\sin^2\theta+2gh}}{g}\right)}$$

[5] Projectile motion
Q: Suppose you stand at the bottom of a cliff of height $h$ and a distance $d$ from its base. At what speed $v$ and angle $\theta$ should you throw the ball so that it hits the top of the cliff moving perfectly horizontally?
A: The trajectory of the ball going up is the same as if the ball had been thrown horizontally from the cliff and landing precisely at the distance $d$ from the bottom (imagine time moving in reverse, or if the ball bounces off a perfectly elastic wall just as it reaches the top of the cliff). Then, we can find the speed at the top of the cliff (which is in the $x$ direction), and combining that with information about the $y$ directional speed at the bottom, we can find $v=\sqrt{v_x^2+v_y^2}$ and $\theta=\tan^{-1}(v_y/v_x).$ If we threw the ball at $v_x$ horizontally off the cliff, it takes a time given by $\frac12 gt^2=h \Rightarrow t=\sqrt{\frac{2h}{g}}$ mandated by the motion in the $y$ direction. Then, we have $d=v_xt=v_x\sqrt{\frac{2h}{g}},$ which we can solve for $v_x$ by $v_x=d\sqrt{\frac{g}{2h}}$. Notice also we can find the speed $v_y$ at the bottom of the cliff by $v_y^2=2gh\Rightarrow v_y=\sqrt{2gh}.$ With this info, we can find $v$ and $\theta$ via \begin{gather*}
 v=\sqrt{v_x^2+v_y^2}=\sqrt{\frac{gd^2}{2h}+2gh}\\
 \theta=\tan^{-1}\frac{v_y}{v_x}=\boxed{\tan^{-1}\frac{2h}{d}}
 \end{gather*}

[6] Projectile motion
Q: A ball of radius $R$ is sliding very quickly across a smooth surface at speed $v\gg \sqrt{gR}$ when it bounces elastically off a small rectangular ledge of height $h\ll R$. What is the maximal height $H$ to which the ball rises?
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[scale=0.15]{ball.png}
 \end{center}
A: By a little bit of geometry, we can see that the radial impact force on the ball is directed at some angle $\theta$ as shown, to the vertical:
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[width=0.6\linewidth]{ballsol}
 \end{center}If that angle is $\theta$, then we can see that the situation is equivalent to the ball bouncing off an inclined plane at angle $\theta$, since the impact force in that case is also directed at an angle $\theta$ to the vertical. As a result of such a collision, the ball would move upward at an angle of $2\theta$ to the horizon. Thus, we just need to find the angle $\theta$, which would allow us to find the vertical speed $v_y$ and subsequently the height $H$. By a little bit of geometry, we have the following diagram:
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[width=0.3\linewidth]{ballsol2}
 \end{center}By the Pythagorean theorem, the missing side of the right triangle has length $\sqrt{R^2-(R-h)^2}=\sqrt{2Rh-h^2}=\sqrt{2Rh},$ where the last approximation was because we know that $h$ is very small compared to $R$. Then, the angle is simply found by $\sin\theta=\sqrt{2Rh}/R=\sqrt{2h/R}$. Again, since $h\ll R$, we can use the small angle approximation that $\sin\theta=\theta$, so $\theta=\sqrt{2h/R}.$ From earlier, this means the ball equivalently shoots upward at an angle $2\theta=2\sqrt{2h/R}$ to the horizontal at speed $v$, so the initial vertical speed is $v_y=v\sin(2\theta)=v\cdot 2\theta=2v\sqrt{2h/R}.$ Finally, we use the kinematic equation $v_y^2=2gH$ to find $$H=\frac{v_y^2}{2g}=\boxed{\frac{4v^2h}{gR}}.$$

[7] Projectile motion
Q: You and your friend stand on opposite sides of a wall, each at a distance $d$ from the base of the wall. The wall has height $h$. What is the minimal speed you will need to throw the ball at in order for your friend to catch it on the other side?
A: Note the parabola of the trajectory must be symmetric about the wall in order for both of you to catch it, so the optimal strategy is such that the ball barely grazes over the wall while it moves horizontally above the wall. Thus, notice that the problem is essentially equivalent to problem 6. Thus, our answer is just $$v=\boxed{\sqrt{\frac{gd^2}{2h}+2gh}}$$

[8] Projectile motion — USAPhO
Q: A ball bounces perfectly elastically between points on two inclined planes at angle $\theta$ and a distance $D$ apart. The angle between the trajectories at the impact points is $\phi\neq 0$, and the speed at the impact points is $v$. Find $\phi$ in terms of the other variables.
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[scale=0.3]{usapho.png}
 \end{center}
A: Notice that for two trajectories to travel the same distance, they must have the same $\sin2\alpha$ factor in the distance formula we have derived again and again: $$D=\frac{v^2\sin2\alpha}{g}$$Notice that the average of these two angles must be $45$ degrees for this to happen, so $\theta=45^\circ.$ Then, $$D=\frac{v^2\sin(2(45\pm \phi/2))}{g}=\frac{v^2\sin(90\pm\phi)}{g}$$ Since the range of the sin function is restricted to $[-90^\circ,90^\circ],$ we choose the value of $\phi$ that lets the argument in sin be within this range:\begin{align*}
 D&=\frac{v^2\sin(90-\phi)}{g}\\
 \sin^{-1}\left(\frac{gD}{v^2}\right)&=90-\phi\\
 90-\sin^{-1}\left(\frac{gD}{v^2}\right)&=\phi\\
 \boxed{\cos^{-1}\left(\frac{gD}{v^2}\right)}&=\phi
 \end{align*}

[9] Olympiad kinematics — Kalda
Q: Two planes fly at the same height with speeds $v_1 = 800 km/h$ and $v_2 = 600 km/h$, respectively. The planes approach each other; at a certain moment of time, the plane trajectories are perpendicular to each other and both planes are at the distance $a = 20 km$ from the intersection points of their trajectories. Find the minimal distance between the planes during
their flight, assuming their velocities will remain constant.
\begin{center}
 \includegraphics[width=0.3\linewidth]{planeee.png}
\end{center}
A: Moving into the frame of one plane will help solving the problem. In the frame of the red plane, the blue plane picks up an extra velocity $v_y=-v_2=-600 km/h$ downward, while the red plane remains at rest. In this frame, the blue plane will travel in a straight line as shown:
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[width=0.4\linewidth]{planesol}
 \end{center}
 By the similar triangle formed, we have $$\frac{d}{5}=\frac{800}{1000}\Rightarrow \boxed{d=4 km}.$$

[10] Olympiad kinematics — Morin
Q: For a given initial speed $v_0$, what inclination angle should a ball be thrown so that it travels the maximum distance along a slope upward at an angle $\beta$ (or sloped downward, if $\beta$ is negative)?
A: The ball is thrown at an angle $\theta$ from the horizontal (so $\theta - \beta$ from the incline). The incline is sloped at $\beta$, so the equation for the incline is $y=\tan\beta x$.

The path of the ball given in terms of $t$ is $x=(v\cos\theta)t$ and $y=(v\sin \theta)t - \frac{1}{2}gt^2$. We solve for $t$ that satisfies $y=\tan\beta x$, where the trajectory of the ball intersects the incline. We find that (in addition to $t=0$),
$$t=\frac{2v}{g}(\sin\theta - \tan\beta\cos\theta).$$
Plugging into the equation for $x$, we get
$$x=\frac{2v^2}{g}(\sin\theta\cos\theta - \tan\beta\cos^2\theta).$$
Now, we maximize the value for $x$ by finding $\theta$ where $dx/d\theta = 0$. Some math and trig subs later, we find that
$$\theta=\boxed{\frac{1}{2}\left(\beta + \frac{\pi}{2}\right)}.$$
This is where $\theta$ bisects the angle between the ground and the direction normal to the incline.

[11] Olympiad kinematics
Q: You stand in a valley, looking up at a cliff that rises a height $h$ above you. You stand a distance $d$ from the base of the cliff. What is the minimal speed you need to throw the ball at in order to hit the top of the cliff? (Hint: use the answer to the previous problem)
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[scale=0.15]{cliff}
 \end{center}
A: Imagine drawing a line from your starting point to the ridge of the cliff so that we form an inclined plane of angle $\beta=\tan^{-1}(H/d)$. Then, this problem is equivalent to the previous problem. Assuming we throw the ball upward at the optimal angle $\theta$, we can find the smallest speed necessary. Plugging in $\theta$ into the expression for $x$ (now $d$) and using double angle identities:\begin{align*}
 d&=\frac{2v^2}{g}\left(\frac{\sin(2\theta)}{2}-\tan\beta\frac{\cos2\theta+1}{2}\right)\\
 &=\frac{v^2}{g}(\sin(\beta+\frac{\pi}{2})-\tan\beta(\cos(\beta+\frac{\pi}{2})+1))\\
 &=\frac{v^2}{g}(\cos\beta+\tan\beta\sin\beta-\tan\beta)\\
 &=\frac{v^2}{g}\frac{1-\sin\beta}{\cos\beta}
 \end{align*}and using $\beta=\tan^{-1}(H/d),$ and setting $\sqrt{H^2+d^2}=L$ we can find $v$ in terms of the other lengths: \begin{align*}
 v&=\sqrt{\frac{gd\cos\beta}{1-\sin\beta}}\\
 &=\sqrt{\frac{gd(d/L)}{1-(H/L)}}\\
 &=\sqrt{\frac{gd^2}{L-H}}\\
 &=\sqrt{\frac{gd^2}{\sqrt{H^2+d^2}-H}}\\
 &=\boxed{\sqrt{g(\sqrt{H^2+d^2}+H)}}
 \end{align*}

[12] Olympiad kinematics — $F=ma$
Q: This problem might look a bit familiar! This time use real kinematic techniques to derive the answer you got before. A rod moves freely between the horizontal floor and the slanted wall. When the end in contact with the floor is moving at $v$, what is the speed of the end in contact with the wall?
\begin{center}
 \includegraphics[width = 0.4 \linewidth]{17_16.PNG}
\end{center}
A: When the end in contact with the floor moves at $v$, we can find the projection of this velocity in the direction of the rod as $v\cos\theta$. This must be the projection of the speed of the other end in the direction of the rod. If the speed of the other end is $u$, then the projection (by angle chasing) is $u\cos(180-\theta-(180-\alpha))=u\cos(\alpha-\theta).$ Equating these, we solve for $u$ as $$u=\boxed{v\frac{\cos\theta}{\cos(\alpha-\theta)}}$$

[13] Olympiad kinematics — $F=ma$
Q: In the frame of the box, which of the following is a possible path followed by the particle?
\begin{center}
 \includegraphics[scale=.65]{pbox-1.PNG}
\end{center}
A: Moving into the frame of the box, every object inside it obtains an extra component of acceleration to the left, which effectively means that ``gravity" in this frame is tilted to the left. Thus, the path should still be a parabola, but tilted to the right to account for the rotated direction of gravity. Therefore, the answer is <strong>(C)</strong>.

[14] Olympiad kinematics — $F=ma$
Q: If the magnitude of the acceleration of the box is chosen correctly, the launched particle will follow
a path that returns to the point that it was launched. In the frame of the box, which path is
followed by the particle?
\begin{center}
 \includegraphics[scale=.65]{pbox-2.PNG}
\end{center}
A: We need the trajectory to be a parabola, so <strong>(A)</strong> is the only possible solution (this one is like throwing the ball ``straight up" against the tilted direction of gravity.

[15] Olympiad kinematics
Q: A closet door is modeled as two identical planks connected by a hinge. One end of the door is fixed while the other slides along a set of rails at a constant speed $v$. At the given moment, the (obtuse) angle at the hinge is $\theta$. Find the speed $u$ of the hinge.
\begin{center}
 \includegraphics[width = 0.5\linewidth]{aoa}
\end{center}
A: Constraints on the left plank demand that the hinge must move at a velocity perpendicular to the plank; the plank cannot stretch nor compress. 
\begin{center}
 \includegraphics[width = 0.8\linewidth]{closetsol.png}
\end{center}
We have the angles in the figure shown. The other plank cannot stretch nor compress either, so the parallel component of velocity of its ends must be equal:
$$u\cos(\theta-90) = v \cos(90-\theta/2)$$
We can do some trig cleanup:
\begin{align*}
 u&=\frac{v\sin\theta/2}{\sin\theta}\\
 &=\boxed{\frac{v}{2\cos\theta/2}}
\end{align*}

[16] Olympiad kinematics
Q: A ball starts at the bottom of a very narrow but very deep well. The well has a width $w$, and a depth $h\gg w$. It is initially thrown at an angle of 30$^\circ$ to the horizontal with speed $v$. What must $v$ be so that the ball escapes the well? All collisions with the walls of the well are elastic.
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[scale=0.1]{well}
 \end{center}
A: In lecture, we showed that the trajectory of the ball is just a repeated reflection of the trajectory it takes if the well was not there. Thus, since we want to find the case where the ball is able to rise up to the top of the well, the problem simply reduces to finding the minimal speed necessary for the ball to rise to a height $h$. Our vertical velocity is $v_y=v\sin30=0.5v,$ and using $v_y^2=2gh$ we get $v=\boxed{2\sqrt{2gh}}.$

[17] Challenge Problems
Q: Four snails sit at the corners of a square $ABCD$ of length $a$. Each snail starts moving toward the one adjacent to it in the clockwise direction at a speed $v$ (i.e. snail $A$ moves toward snail $B$, snail $B$ moves toward snail $C$, etc). Find the time it takes for the snails to meet. Generalize your answer to $N$ snails in a regular $N$-gon of side length $a$.
A: Note that the rate at which the distance between the snails decreases is equal to $v$ -- one snail is moving toward the other at speed $v$, while the other is moving perpendicularly to the side in question, thus contributing nothing to the decrease in side length. Therefore, our answer is simply $$t=\boxed{a/v}$$
 For a more general case, let the internal angle of the polygon be $\theta$. Then, while one snail moves toward another, the component of the other snail's motion in the direction of the side between them is equal to $v\cos\theta$. We can see this in the diagram shown:
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[scale=0.2]{snailsol}
 \end{center}Then, the rate at which the length of the side decreases is $v+v\cos\theta$ (note that $\cos\theta$ is negative here). We can find $\theta$ from the standard formula that the total measure of all the angles in an $N$-gon is $180(N-2),$ so the angle $\theta=180(N-2)/N$. Therefore, the time it takes for the snails to meet is \begin{align*}
 t=\frac{a}{v(1+\cos\theta)}=\boxed{\frac{a}{v\left(1+\cos\left(\frac{180(N-2)}{N}\right)\right)}}
 \end{align*}

[18] Challenge Problems
Q: In this problem, we derive the envelope parabola. (Calculus required)
 \begin{anum}
 \item To start, write down the function $y(x)$ for a projectile launched at velocity $v_0$ at an angle $\theta$ to the horizontal from $(0,0)$.
 \item Now, for a given fixed $x$, maximize $y$ with varying $\theta$. That is, take the derivative with respect to $\theta$ to find the $\theta$ that maximizes in terms of the other variables and plug it back into $y(x)$. You should get a parabola as a function of $x$. This may be a bit messy, but it's helpful to do such derivations at least once in a while --- it's a useful skill, especially on the USAPhO and beyond.
 \end{anum}
 So the set of all possible parabolic trajectories launched from a single point form a parabola themselves! This trick can be used to solve a lot of olympiad problems -- see Kalda for more, or IPhO 2012!
A: \begin{anum}
 \item Looking at the vertical direction, we can write $y$ as a function of $t$ using the standard kinematic equation. The initial speed in the $y$ direction is $v_0\sin\theta$, so $$y(t)=(v_0\sin\theta)t-\frac12gt^2$$Note we can also write $x(t)$ as $x(t)=(v_0\cos\theta)t$. Solving for $t$ in terms of $x$ and plugging it in for $y(t)$, we can find $y(x)$:$$y(x)=(v_0\sin\theta)\frac{x}{v_0\cos\theta}-\frac12g
 \left(\frac{x}{v_0\cos\theta}\right)^2=\boxed{x\tan\theta-\frac{gx^2}{2v_0^2}\sec^2\theta}$$
 \item Taking the derivative with respect to $\theta$ and setting it to 0, \begin{align*}
 0&=x\sec^2\theta-\frac{gx^2}{2v_0^2}(2\sec\theta)(\sec\theta\tan\theta)\\
 0&=x-\frac{gx^2}{v_0^2}\tan\theta\\
 \tan\theta&=\frac{v_0^2}{gx}
 \end{align*}
 We can plug this back into $y(x)$, by using the trigonometric formula $\tan^2\theta+1=\sec^2\theta$. \begin{align*}
 y(x)&=x\cdot\frac{v_0^2}{gx}-\frac{gx^2}{2v_0^2}\left(1+\frac{v_0^4}{g^2x^2}\right)\\
 &=\frac{v_0^2}{g}-\frac{gx^2}{2v_0^2}-\frac{v_0^2}{2g}\\
 &=\boxed{\frac{v_0^2}{2g}-\frac{gx^2}{2v_0^2}}
 \end{align*}
 A sidenote on why we did this: when finding $y(x)$, we found the trajectory of $y$ given some firing angle. By fixing $x$ and maximizing $y$ with respect to $\theta$, we find the optimal firing angle such that we can hit the greatest $y$ for that value of $x$. By plugging this $\theta$ back into our equation, we essentially find the greatest possible height for each value $x$, which creates an envelope parabola containing all the possible trajectories. 
 \end{anum}

[19] Challenge Problems
Q: An elastic and frictionless ball is thrown up a ramp angled at $\theta$ to the horizon. If the ball bounces $n$ times before returning to the start point, what angle was the ball thrown at relative to the ramp?
A: Let's tilt axes. In the direction normal to the plane, the component of gravity is $g_y=g\cos\theta$ and in the direction parallel, $g_x=g\sin\theta.$ Let the angle of throw relative to the plane be $\alpha$, such that the angle to the horizontal is $\beta = \alpha + \theta.$ Let the time $t$ equal the time between bounces. Then, in the normal direction, we must have $$t=\frac{2v\sin\alpha}{g\cos\theta},$$ and in the parallel direction, $$nt=\frac{2v\cos\alpha}{g\sin\theta}.$$ Dividing through and rearranging, \begin{align*}
 \frac{1}{n}&=\frac{\tan\alpha}{\tan\theta}\\
 \alpha &= \tan^{-1}\left(\frac{\tan\theta}{n}\right)
\end{align*}so we have $$\beta = \alpha + \theta=\boxed{\tan^{-1}\left(\frac{\tan\theta}{n}\right)+\theta}.$$

[20] Challenge Problems — NBPhO 2020
Q: The interior of a modern museum is a perfect, right cone with half apex angle $60^\circ$ (i.e. the walls are slanted by $60^\circ$ with respect to the vertical). Launched from the center of the cone's base, the minimal speed needed for a projectile to reach the apex (i.e. the highest point of the walls is $v$). What is the minimal speed required to reach the wall of the cone?
A: If the distance from the base to the apex is $H$, then
from the kinematic formula, $2gH = v^2.$
Let the shortest distance from the base to the wall be $h$ and the sought minimal speed $v_1$. From geometry, $h = H \sin \theta$, where $\theta=60^\circ.$ Letʼs consider a new system of coordinates where the two axes $x$ and $y$ are parallel and perpendicular to the wall, respectively. Gravitational acceleration thus has components $g_x = g\cos\theta$ and
$g_y = g\sin\theta.$ It is clear that the motion along
the $x$ axis doesnʼt affect whether the projectile hits the wall, because the motions in the two axes are independent. One thus has to set the component of $v_1$ in the $x$ direction to equal 0; the original speed is entirely in the $y$ direction. Then, the problem reduces to hitting a projectile into a conventional ceiling of height $h$ in effective gravity $g\sin\theta$. Thus, from energy conservation, $$gh\sin\theta = gH \sin^2\theta =\frac{v_1^2}{2}$$ and so $$v_1=v\sin\theta = \boxed{\frac{\sqrt 3}{2} v}.$$

[21] Challenge Problems
Q: Two identical vertical hoops positioned on the floor are connected by a small ring as shown. The ring is pulled down at a speed $v$. Find the speed $u$ of the hoops in terms of $v$ and $\theta$. 
\begin{center}
 \includegraphics[width = 0.5\linewidth]{rings}
\end{center}
A: Let's look at the hoop on the left. In the frame of the hoop, the ring gains an additional velocity $u$ to the right while the hoop is stationary. Here, the ring must move along the rim of the hoop, i.e. its velocity must be tangent to the hoop. Thus we can form a right triangle:
\begin{center}
 \includegraphics[width = 0.3\linewidth]{ringsol}
\end{center}
So, $$u=\boxed{v\tan\theta}.$$

[22] Challenge Problems — Kalda
Q: A hinged structure consists of two links of length
$2l$. One of its ends is attached to a wall, the other is moving
at distance $3l$ from the wall at constant vertical velocity $v_0$. Find the acceleration of the hinge connecting the links when \begin{anum}
 \item the link closer to the wall is horizontal.
 \item the velocity of the connection point is zero.
\end{anum} 
\begin{center}
 \includegraphics[scale=0.2]{kald1}
\end{center}
A: (Diagram from PhODS)
\begin{center}
 \includegraphics[scale=0.2]{kaldasol}
\end{center}
 \begin{anum}
 \item First, note that because the projections of velocity along the direction of the rod must be equal on both ends, the hinge must move vertically upward at $v$ (notice how this also follows the fact that the left rod can only rotate, and thus the velocity must be perpendicular to its end). Using the definition of centripetal acceleration about the left rod, the horizontal component of the acceleration is $v_0^2/2l$. Then, since the velocity of the joint and the end are equal, there can be no centripetal acceleration about the right rod, so the acceleration component of the hinge parallel to the right rod must be 0, and the actual acceleration must be perpendicular to the right rod. Then, the projection of this acceleration in the direction of the left rod is $a\cos30^\circ$, and must equal the acceleration we found earlier: $$\frac{v_0^2}{2l}=a\cos30^\circ\Rightarrow a=\boxed{\frac{v_0^2}{\sqrt 3l}}$$
 \item If we take the frame moving upward at speed $v$, observe that the hinge and the right end of the rod are stationary but the left end of the left rod moves down at speed $v$. This is equivalent to saying that we're moving the left rod instead, and the right rod is anchored to the wall. Thus, we have the exact same set up as before, but at the later time that we desire (the hinge is at rest in this frame). Shifting into a constant velocity frame does not affect accelerations, so the acceleration is the same here: $$a=\boxed{\frac{v_0^2}{\sqrt 3l}}$$
 \end{anum}

LESSON: fma-04
