[1] Concept checks
Q: A massless airplane with 5 massless engines (that all provide the same thrust) is filled with 5 elephants. Unfortunately, the plane is too heavy to lift off. Engineers propose either adding another engine or removing an elephant to solve the issue. Which choice should they make to maximize their chances of a successful liftoff?
A: We need to achieve an adequate thrust-to-weight ratio to have liftoff: $T/W$. Let the original ratio be $r$. If we add on engine, our ratio becomes $(6/5)r=1.2r$, and if we remove an elephant, our ratio becomes $(5/4)r=1.25r$. Thus, we should <span style="border:1px solid #555;padding:.1em .4em;font-weight:600;">remove an elephant</span> to maximize the chance of liftoff.

[2] Concept checks — $F=ma$
Q: Two masses are attached with pulleys by a massless rope on an inclined plane as shown. All surfaces are frictionless. If the masses are released from rest, then the inclined plane
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[width=0.3\textwidth]{fma2017}
 \end{center}
 \begin{enumerate}[label = (\Alph*)]
\item accelerates to the left if $m_1 < m_2$
\item accelerates to the right if $m_1 < m_2$
\item accelerates to the left regardless of the masses
\item accelerates to the right regardless of the masses
\item does not move
\end{enumerate}
A: If we look at the system as a whole, there is no external force that pushes the system horizontally, so <strong>(E)</strong> the inclined plane will not move.

[3] Concept checks
Q: A block of mass $m$ is placed on top of a plank of mass $M$, which in turn lies on a frictionless surface. A vertical force $F > (M +m)g$ is applied to the plank via a light string tied around it such that the plank begins to rise, carrying the block with it. What is the magnitude of the normal force between the plank and the block, in terms of $F$?
A: If we look at the system as a whole, the net force equation is $F-(m+M)g=(m+M)a$, so $a=F/(m+M)-g$. If we look at the block only, then the net force equation on it is $N-mg=ma$. Since the acceleration of the box must equal the acceleration of the system, we have $$N=ma+mg=\boxed{F\frac{m}{m+M}}$$

[4] Concept checks
Q: \begin{anum}
 \item A circular rubber band is placed around a smooth cylinder such that it has tension $T$. What's the normal force between a small segment of this rubber band (let's say it has an arc length of $\theta$, where $\theta$ is very small) and the cylinder?
 \item The speed of transverse waves in a string is determined by $v_T=\sqrt{T/\mu}$, where $T$ is the tension in the string and $\mu$ is the mass per unit length of the string. A circular ring of string of radius $R$ is spinning around its axis at an angular speed $\omega$ (such that each point on the ring is moving at a speed $v=\omega R$). Find the speed of waves in this string. 
\end{anum}
A: \begin{anum}
 \item If we analyze the small piece of string, notice that by force balance, we have two components of tension pulling it at each end at an angle $\theta/2$ to the tangent, resulting in an inward force of $$2T\sin(\theta/2)\approx T\theta.$$ The normal force must balance this inward force on the string, so $$N=\boxed{T\theta}$$
 \item The inward tension (equal to what was found in part a) must supply the necessary centripetal acceleration, which is $a=v^2/R=\omega^2R.$ The small piece of string has a mass $m=\mu R\theta$, so we have \begin{align*}
 T\theta=ma=m\omega^2R=\mu \theta\omega^2R^2\Rightarrow T=\mu\omega^2R^2
 \end{align*}and so $$v_T=\sqrt{\frac{T}{\mu}}=\sqrt{\omega^2R^2}=\boxed{\omega R}$$
 \end{anum}

[5] Concept checks
Q: Two masses with $m_1 > m_2$ along with a mystery mass $M$ are suspended from a pulley contraption as shown. The pulleys are massless. If the system is released from rest and the mystery mass stays still, what is its mass $M$?
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[width=0.3\textwidth]{pulley3}
 \end{center}
A: The smaller pulley must have zero net force acting on it. Since the mystery mass stays still, the small pulley stays still -- thus, we can assume that it is fixed in place. Now, the tension in the rope connecting $m_1$ and $m_2$ can be found by the standard Atwood's derivation. The acceleration of $m_1$ is $g(m_1-m_2)/(m_1+m_2),$ which implies that the tension in the string is $$T=m_1g-m_1a_1=\frac{2m_1m_2}{m_1+m_2}g$$The tension in the other string must then be $2T$ for the net force on the small pulley to be 0, so the large mass has a weight $Mg=2T$. Finally, \begin{align*}
 M=\frac{2T}{g}=\boxed{\frac{4m_1m_2}{m_1+m_2}}
 \end{align*}

[6] Concept checks
Q: A box of mass $m$ is kept still on a wall by a force $F$ directed at an angle $\theta$ to the vertical. The coefficient of friction between the wall and the box is $\mu$. What is the minimum magnitude of $F$? At what angle $\theta$ will it be impossible to keep the box still no matter how large $F$ is?
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[width=0.2\textwidth]{blockwall}
 \end{center}
A: Balancing forces in the $x$ direction, we have $N = F \sin \theta$. In the $y$ direction,
we have $F\cos\theta + f = mg$ (friction points upward). At minimum $F$, $f$ is at its maximum, $f = N\mu$. Hence we have
\begin{align*}
 F \cos \theta + N\mu &= mg\\
F \cos \theta + F\mu \sin \theta &= mg\\
F &=\boxed{\frac{mg}{\cos \theta + \mu \sin \theta}}
\end{align*}
At a certain angle (obviously with $\theta > 90^\circ$), it will be impossible to keep the box up; this
is because the (very large) force of friction cannot overcome the (also very large) component
of $F$ pushing the box downward. For these very large forces, we can effectively neglect the
weight of the box. At the critical angle, the frictional force $F \mu \sin \theta$ is just enough to balance
the vertical component of $F$, which is $F \cos \theta$ (this will be negative for $\theta> 90^\circ$). Thus we have \begin{align*}
F \mu \sin \theta + F \cos \theta &= 0\\
\tan \theta &=-\frac 1\mu\\
\theta&=\boxed{\tan^{-1}\left(-\frac 1\mu\right)}
\end{align*}
As expected, this would yield $90^\circ<\theta< 180^\circ$ since $\mu > 0$.

[7] Concept checks
Q: A person of mass $M$ is initially standing next to a box of mass $m$ as shown. The coefficient of friction between the person and the ground is $\mu\ll 1$, while the box is on rollers. The person pushes the box to make it move to the right, but the person is not allowed to move from where they are standing. If the person’s arm length is $L$, what is the maximum possible final speed of the box?
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[width=0.2\textwidth]{blockperson}
 \end{center}
A: As the person pushes the box with a force $F$, an equal and opposite force pushes them to the left. However, they are not allowed to move, so friction $f$ must counteract $F$. To give the box the maximal speed, the person must push with a maximal force $F$. $F$ cannot exceed the maximum static friction force $Mg\mu$, since otherwise the person will start slipping. Thus, we maximize $F$ by setting it equal to $Mg\mu$. The corresponding acceleration for the box is then
\begin{align*}
a =\frac Fm=\frac{Mg\mu}{m}.
\end{align*}
The box is accelerated until it leaves contact with the person’s hands, so it accelerates for a
distance $L$. By the kinematic equation $v^2 = 2ad,$ the maximal final velocity is$$
v =\sqrt{2aL}=\boxed{\sqrt{\frac{2Mg\mu L}{m}}}.
$$

[8] Concept checks
Q: When a block is pushed directly up a rough inclined plane at an initial speed $v$, it travels a distance $l_1$ before coming to rest. If it were pushed down the plane at that speed, it travels a distance $l_2$ before it comes to rest. If the block is pushed sideways along the incline such that it moves perfectly horizontally, what distance $l$ will it travel before coming to rest?
A: Let the frictional component of acceleration on the block be $a_f$ (which is $g\mu\cos\theta$), and the gravitational component to be $a_g$ (or $g\sin\theta$). If pushed upward, the block experiences an acceleration $a_g+a_f$, and if pushed down experiences an acceleration $a_g-a_f$. If the block is pushed horizontally, it experiences only $a_f$. Then, by kinematics we have \begin{gather*}
 v^2=2l_1(a_g+a_f)=2l_2(a_g-a_f)=2la_f
 \end{gather*}Solving this system of equations, we get $$l=\boxed{\frac{2l_1l_2}{l_1+l_2}}$$

[9] Concept checks
Q: A chain is passed over a frictionless log of radius $r$ and starts slipping off. Find the minimal speed of the chain $v$ such that it loses contact with the log.
A: This question isn't well posed -- sorry! We may include a variant on a future homework assignment.

[10] Exercises — Morin
Q: Consider the Atwood’s machine in the figure below. It consists of three pulleys, a short piece of string connecting one mass to the bottom pulley, and a continuous long piece of string that wraps twice around the bottom side of the bottom pulley, and once around the top side of the top two pulleys. The two masses are $m$ and $2m$. Assume that the parts of the string connecting the pulleys are essentially vertical. Find the accelerations of the masses.
\begin{center}
 \includegraphics[scale=0.18]{morin2}
 \end{center}
A: Let the tension in the string be $T$. Then, setting positive acceleration as downward, we have \begin{gather*}
 ma_1=mg-T\\
 2ma_2=2mg-4T
 \end{gather*}The length of the string is constant, so if the position of $m$ is $x_1$ and the position of $2m$ is $x_2$, then $x_1+4x_2=\text{const}$, leading to $$a_1+4a_2=0$$Solving the system of three equations, we get \begin{gather*}
 \boxed{a_1=\frac{4g}{9}}\\
 \boxed{a_2=-\frac{g}{9}}
 \end{gather*}

[11] Exercises — Morin
Q: Find the accelerations of the masses in the following system of pulleys. 
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[scale=0.18]{morin1}
 \end{center}
A: Let the large pulley's acceleration be denoted $a_1$ and the smaller pulley $a_2$. Notice there is one main string that wraps around both masses. If the tension in this string is $T$, the force equations on the pulleys are \begin{gather*}
 ma_1=mg-2T\\
 ma_2=mg-T
 \end{gather*}The length of the string is equal to $2x_1+x_2$, so $$2a_1+a_2=0$$Solving the system yields \begin{gather*}
 \boxed{a_1=-\frac g5}\\
 \boxed{a_2=\frac{2g}{5}}
 \end{gather*}

[12] Exercises
Q: A V-shaped trough is inclined at an angle $\alpha$ to the horizontal, with the sides of the trough inclined at an angle $\theta$. A cube slides down the trough as shown. Find the acceleration of the cube, given that the coefficient of friction is $\mu$.
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[scale=0.2]{trough.png}
 \end{center}
A: Let us first rotate the axes so the trough is flat. Then, we have components of gravity $mg \cos \alpha$ pointing downward and $mg \sin \alpha$ pointing along the trough. Thus, in the ``side view," we have the following diagram:
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[scale=0.2]{sol3}
 \end{center}
 The box cannot sink into the trough, so the vertical forces in this view must balance:
 \begin{align*}2N \cos \theta = mg \cos \alpha \Rightarrow N =\frac{mg \cos \alpha}{2 \cos \theta}
.\end{align*}
The frictional force pointing opposite the direction of motion is thus
$$f = 2N\mu =\frac{mg\mu \cos \alpha}{\cos \theta}$$
since it acts at both contact points with the trough. Finally, the net force $F = ma$ pointing
along the trough is $mg \sin \alpha - f$, so we have
\begin{align*}
ma &= mg \sin \alpha -\frac{mg\mu \cos \alpha}{\cos \theta}\\
\Rightarrow a &= \boxed{g\left(\sin \alpha-\mu\frac{\cos \alpha}{\cos \theta}\right)}
\end{align*}

[13] Exercises
Q: Examine the classic box-wedge system where the box has mass $m$ and the wedge has mass $M$ and angle $\theta$. Find the acceleration of the box in this case.
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[scale=0.15]{wedgebox.png}
 \end{center}
A: Intuitively the wedge should move to the left. Let's say its acceleration to the left is $a$. Then, in the rest frame of the wedge, the wedge is fixed while the block gains a component of gravitational force $ma$ to the right. Then, the total force acting on the box pointing down the plane is $mg\sin\theta+ma\cos\theta$, leading to an acceleration of the box $A'$ (in the wedge frame) equal to \begin{align*}
 A'=g\sin\theta-a\cos\theta
 \end{align*}This acceleration vector points parallel to the plane. Now, moving back into the lab frame we must add the $a$ vector of our frame to $A'$ to find $A$, the true acceleration of the box. In other words, $\vec A=\vec{a}+\vec{A}'$. Adding the vectors by first decomposing $A'$ into $x$ and $y$ components and using the Pythagorean thoerem, we get $$A=\sin\theta\sqrt{g^2+a^2}$$Now, we need to find $a$. Notice that the normal force from the box is the only supplier of the acceleration, and its component in the $x$ direction is $N\sin\theta$. Thus, $Ma=N\sin\theta$. Taking a look at the box again in the accelerating frame, the constraint along the surface of the plane is $N=mg\cos\theta-ma\sin\theta$. Solving for $a$ with these two equations yields $$a=\frac{mg\sin\theta\cos\theta}{M+m\sin^2\theta}$$Finally, plugging into $A$ yields $$A=\boxed{\frac{g\sin\theta}{M+m\sin^2\theta}\sqrt{M^2+m^2\sin^2\theta+2Mm\sin^2\theta}}$$

[14] Exercises
Q: A box of mass $m$ is placed on top of two inclined planes, both of angle $\theta$ and mass $M$. 
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[scale=0.15]{box.png}
 \end{center}

 \begin{anum}
 \item Neglecting friction everywhere, find the acceleration of the mass $m$.
 \item If there is friction $\mu$ between only the box and planes, find the acceleration of the mass $m$.
 \item Assume there is no friction between the box and the planes, but there exists friction $\mu$ between the planes and the ground. In this case, find the acceleration of the mass $m$. 
 \end{anum}
A: \begin{anum}
 \item Drawing in forces of interaction,
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[scale=0.2]{wedge}
 \end{center}
 Let the box have acceleration $a_1$ and the wedges $a_2$. Then, balancing forces on the box and one of the wedges, we yield two equations:\begin{gather*}
 ma_1=mg-2N\cos\theta\\
 Ma_2=N\sin\theta
 \end{gather*}From analyzing the interaction between the box and the wedges, if the box moves down by a small distance $y$, the wedges must move outward by a distance $x=y\cot\theta.$ Thus, $$a_2=a_1\cot\theta.$$Solving the system of equations yields $$a_1=\boxed{\frac{m\sin^2\theta g}{m\sin^2\theta+2M\cos^2\theta}}$$
 \item Drawing in the forces on the box, \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[scale=0.15]{wedgesol3.png}
 \end{center}
 The introduction of friction $f=N\mu$ changes our force equations. Noting that the force vectors are reversed for the wedges by Newton's 3rd law, \begin{gather*}
 ma_1=mg-2N\cos\theta-2N\mu\sin\theta\\
 Ma_2=N\sin\theta-N\mu\cos\theta
 \end{gather*}The equation of constraint is the same, so solving the system yields $$a_1=\boxed{\frac{mg(\sin^2\theta-\mu\sin\theta\cos\theta)}{m\sin^2\theta+2M\cos^2\theta+(2M-m)\mu\sin\theta\cos\theta}}$$
 \item Drawing in the forces, we now have two types of normal force we must consider.
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[scale=0.15]{bal332}
 \end{center}
 The force equations are now \begin{gather*}
 ma_1=mg-2N_1\cos\theta\\
 Ma_2=N_1\sin\theta-N_2\mu
 \end{gather*}Balancing forces in the vertical direction, $$N_1\cos\theta+Mg=N_2$$ The constraint is the same, so solving the system of 4 equations yields $$a=\boxed{\frac{mg-\frac{2Mg\mu\cos\theta}{\sin\theta-\mu\cos\theta}}{m+\frac{2M\cot\theta}{\sin\theta-\mu\cos\theta}}}$$
 \end{anum}

[15] Exercises
Q: A bob of mass $m$ (in gray) is placed on an inclined plane of mass $M$ and angle $\theta$. The bob is passed through a pulley and attached to the wall via a string. Find the accelerations of the bob and the plane. 
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[scale=0.15]{ballincline.png}
 \end{center}
A: The wedge is going to accelerate to the left, so let's denote its acceleration as $a$. Let the tension in the string be $T$, and the normal force between the wedge and the ball be $N$. Then, $$Ma=T(1-\cos\theta)+N\sin\theta$$In the frame of the wedge, the ball obtains an extra component of weight $ma$ to the right. Applying force balance parallel and perpendicular to the plane (with the acceleration of the ball being $A$), \begin{gather*}
 mA=mg\sin\theta+ma\cos\theta-T\\
 N=mg\cos\theta-ma\sin\theta
 \end{gather*}Our final equation can be found via the constraint on the length of the string. If the plane moves a distance $x$ toward the wall, this frees up a length $x$ of string between the pulley and the ball. Relating this to our accelerations, we have $a=A$. Solving, we have $$a=A=\boxed{\frac{mg\sin\theta}{M+2m(1-\cos\theta)}}$$where of course the accelerations have different directions, even though they have the same magnitude.

[16] Challenge Problems — PPP
Q: A long, heavy, flexible rope with mass $\rho$ per unit length is stretched by a constant force $F$. A sudden movement causes a circular loop to form at one end of the rope. The loop then runs (rolls) along the rope with speed $c$ as shown in the figure. What is the speed $c$? (Hint: try a change of reference frame)
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[scale=0.18]{loop}
 \end{center}
A: Following the hint, let's consider a reference frame that moves at a constant velocity to the right at $c$. In this frame, the shape of the circular loop is fixed while the entire string runs backward along the loop at speed $c$. Let the loop have radius $R$. Analyzing forces on a small piece of string with angular width $\theta$, (in the same way as solution 6b), $$F\theta=m\frac{c^2}{R}=\rho \theta c^2\Rightarrow c=\boxed{\sqrt{\frac{F}{\rho}}}$$

[17] Challenge Problems
Q: A cylinder of mass $m$ and radius $r$ initially rests on two supports of the same height, initially placed next to each other. One support is stationary, while the other slides from under the cylinder at a constant velocity $v$. Determine the normal force $N$ exerted by the stationary support on the cylinder when the points of contact between the cylinder and the supports are a distance $r\sqrt2$ apart. <em>Note: The original problem statement supplied a hint that was incorrect and overparameterized the system.</em>
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[scale=0.15]{ballsupport}
 \end{center}
A: Notice that as the support moves, the axis of the cylinder moves along a circle of radius $r$ about the point of contact $A$ with the stationary support. Thus, at the given moment the velocity $u$ of the axis moves perpendicularly to the radius drawn to $A$. Since the axis moves in a circle, we have centripetal acceleration in the direction along the radius:
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[scale=0.2]{ballreal}
 \end{center}
 Notice the normal force from the other support is perpendicular to the other forces, so it contributes no net force. Then, balancing forces yields $$m\frac{u^2}{r}=\frac{mg}{\sqrt2}-N$$Since the axis of the cylinder is always halfway between the two supports, it should move at a horizontal velocity $v/2$. This means that $u/\sqrt2=v/2$, so $u=v/\sqrt2$. Combining these equations, $$N=\frac{mg}{\sqrt2}-m\frac{u^2}{r}=\boxed{\frac{mg}{\sqrt2}-m\frac{v^2}{2r}}$$

[18] Challenge Problems — PPP
Q: Find the location of the center of mass (the point at which a centripetal or gravitational force can be assumed to act on) of a uniform semicircular arc of radius $R$.
\begin{center}
 \includegraphics[width=0.2\textwidth]{arc}
 \end{center}
A: Imagine appending another semicircular arc to make this arc a complete circle. If this arc were to spin at an angular velocity $\omega$, then we know from solution 6b that the tension in the arc is $T=\mu\omega^2R^2,$ where $\mu$ is the mass per unit length. Then, analyzing forces on solely the semicircle: \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[scale=0.15]{arc2}
 \end{center}If the distance from the center of mass to the center of the circle is $d$, we can see that $$2T=m\omega^2d=\mu\pi R\omega^2d\Rightarrow d=\boxed{\frac{2}{\pi}R}$$

[19] Challenge Problems
Q: Two balls are placed on a weightless support formed by two smooth inclined planes that both form an angle $\theta$ with the horizontal. The support can slide without friction along a horizontal plane. The upper ball of mass $m_1$ is released. Determine the condition under which the lower ball of mass $m_2$ starts moving up the support at this moment.
\begin{center}
 \includegraphics[width=0.25\textwidth]{climb}
\end{center}
A: Since the support is weightless, the net force on it must be 0. This means the mass $m_2$ must rise up the left inclined plane, as to keep the net horizontal force on the support (generated by the normal force between the support and the masses) equal to 0. However, the support may in fact accelerate (the $F=ma$ equation is still valid if $0=0a$ but $a\neq 0$). Let's assume the support accelerates to the right at $a$. Then, shifting into the rest frame of the support, each mass gains a weight $ma$ towards the left. \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[scale=0.18]{support}
 \end{center}Notice the normal forces $N$ have to be equal in magnitude to maintain 0 net force on the support. Balancing forces on the balls perpendicular to each plane, \begin{gather*}
 N=m_2g\cos\theta+m_2a\sin\theta\\
 N=m_1g\cos\theta-m_1a\sin\theta
 \end{gather*}The condition that $m_2$ starts rolling upward is that $m_2a\cos\theta > m_2g\sin\theta$. Notice we can solve for $a$ using the two force equations from earlier, where $a=(m_1-m_2)g\cot\theta/(m_1+m_2).$ Then, the condition we need can be found as $$\frac{m_1-m_2}{m_1+m_2}>\tan^2\theta$$Or alternatively, using trig identities, $$\boxed{m_2<m_1\cos2\theta}$$

[20] Challenge Problems — PPP
Q: There is a parabolic-shaped bridge across a river of width $d=100$ m. The highest point of the bridge is $h=5$ m above the level of the banks. A car of mass $m=1000$ kg is crossing the bridge at a constant speed of $v=20$ m/s.
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[width=0.4\textwidth]{car}
 \end{center}
 Find the normal force between the bridge and the car when the car is halfway across the bridge.
A: It would be easy to find the normal force if the car were on a circular track, but it's not. However, we are able to find the radius of curvature of the track at the top. Notice that the bridge is in the shape of a parabola, which is the shape of a projectile in gravity. If a projectile were to follow the exact trajectory of the bridge, then we can relate its velocity at the top $u$ to its acceleration $a_\perp$ to the velocity to find the radius of curvature: $$R=\frac{u^2}{a_\perp}$$Notice that the velocity at the top is purely the $x$-direction velocity, so we can relate it to $d$ by $d=ut$, where $t$ is the time in the air. We can also find the time by using the height $h$. We have that $(1/2)g(t/2)^2=h \Rightarrow t=2\sqrt{2h/g}.$ Thus, $v=d/t=(d/2)\sqrt{g/2h}.$ Notice that $a_\perp$ is just $g$. Thus, $$R=\frac{u^2}{a_\perp}=\frac{d^2}{8h}$$ Now, we can solve the problem as we usually would with a particle on a curved track. The net force equation says that $$mg-N=m\frac{v^2}{R}\Rightarrow N=m\left(g-\frac{8hv^2}{d^2}\right)=\boxed{8200 N}$$

[21] Challenge Problems
Q: A person hoists one of two loads of equal mass at a constant velocity $v$. Initially, the upper pulley is prevented from rotating by some force. At the moment when the two loads are at the same height $h$, the upper pulley is released. What load hits the floor first, and at what time $t$ does this happen? Assume that the person continues to pull the rope at the same constant velocity $v$. The pulleys and ropes are massless. 
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[width=0.22\textwidth]{person}
\end{center}
A: The condition of the person pulling on the rope allows the rope to be in tension the entire time. Otherwise, the system is equivalent to having the free end of the rope fixed. Using this, let the tension in the lower string be $T$. Then, the force equations are \begin{gather*}ma_1=mg-T\\
 ma_2=mg-2T\end{gather*}
 And the equation of constraint is $a_1=-a_2$. Solving the system, $a_1=2g/5$, which means the left block accelerates downward and hits the ground first. Since the block initially moves up at speed $v$, we can use the kinematic equation $$h=-vt+\frac12\frac{2g}{5}t^2$$which has the solution $$t=\boxed{\frac{5v}{2g}+\sqrt{\left(\frac{5v}{2g}\right)^2+\frac{5h}{g}}}$$

------------------------------------------------------------------------------
LESSON: fma-06
------------------------------------------------------------------------------

[1] Concept Checks
Q: You swing a mass $m$ in a circle of radius $r$ such that it moves at constant velocity $v$. How much work do you do over time $\Delta t$?
A: Because the force is perpendicular to the velocity, the work done is 0.

[2] Concept Checks
Q: Suppose the force of air friction of a plane is proportional to $v^2,$ the square of the speed of the plane. If the power output of a plane is doubled, what factor is the maximum speed of the plane multiplied by?
A: Because $P = Fv$, we have $P \propto v^3$, so $\boxed 2^{1/3}$.

[3] Concept Checks
Q: At height $h_1$ from the ground, a basketball of mass $m$ is falling with velocity $v_1$, and at a later time, the basketball is falling with velocity $v_2$ at height $h_2$ from the ground. Find the change in kinetic energy of the basketball in two ways:
 \begin{anum}
 \item Explicitly calculate the kinetic energy at each time-point.
 \item Use conservation of energy. Do your results agree?
 \end{anum}
A: \begin{anum}
 \item It's $\frac 12 mv_1^2$ and $\frac 12 mv_2^2$, so $\frac{1}{2} m (v_2^2 - v_1^2)$.
 \item By conservation of energy, $\Delta K = - \Delta U = mg(h_1 - h_2)$. This does agree, we can use our kinematics equation to write $v_2^2 = v_1^2 + 2g(h_1 - h_2)$, which if we substitute gives us $\Delta K = m\cdot \frac 12 \left(v_2^2 - v_1^2\right)$, which agrees.
 \end{anum}

[4] Concept Checks
Q: Alice and Bob play tug of war, with a heavy mass (such that it doesn't lift off the ground) in between their two ropes as shown below. Alice pulls with force $F$ perfectly horizontally, while Bob, pulls at an angle $\theta$ above the horizontal. Eventually, the block moves horizontally at constant velocity $v$. 
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[width=8cm]{tug_of_war.png}
 \end{center}
 \begin{anum}
 \item What is Alice's rate of work (power)? What about Bob's?
 \item How does Bob's power vary with $\theta$? Can you explain this result simply?
 \end{anum}
A: \begin{anum}
 \item From the definition of power, Alice's power is $P_A = -Fv$. For Bob, we have $P_B = \mathbf F \cdot \mathbf v = vF_x$. and by balancing forces on the mass, $F_x = F$, so $P_B = Fv$.
 \item It does not vary with $\theta$. We can explain this by conservation of energy. The block has the same velocity, so the net work done on it must be 0, and so $P_A + P_B = 0$, so $P_B = -P_A = -(-Fv)$.
 \end{anum}

[5] Concept Checks — $F=ma$
Q: \begin{anum}
A car is driving against the wind at a constant speed $v_0$ relative to the ground. The wind direction is always opposite to the car’s velocity, but its speed fluctuates about an average speed of $v$ relative to the ground. The air drag force is $Av_{\text{rel}}^2$, where $A$ is a constant and $v_{\text{rel}}$ is the relative speed between the car and the wind. What is the average rate $P$ of energy dissipation due to the air resistance?
 \begin{anum}
 \item $\overline P = Av0(v0 + v)^2$
 \item $\overline P > Av_0(v_0+v)^2$
 \item $\overline P < Av_0(v_0+v)^2$
 \item Both (B) and (C) are possible depending on how v fluctuates.
 \item Both (A) and (C) are possible depending on how v fluctuates.
 \end{anum}
\end{anum}
A: If the true velocity of the car is $v + \delta v$, we have $$\overline P = A(v_0 + v + \delta v)^2 v_0 = Av_0 ((v_0+v)^2 + \delta v^2 + 2\delta v (v_0 + v)).$$ Now if we average this over $\delta v$, since it can be positive or negative, the third term goes the 0, while the second term is positive, so $\overline P > Av_0 (v_0+v)^2$, which is <strong>B</strong>.

[6] Concept Checks
Q: Here's a preview of gravity. We'll learn later, that the potential energy for a mass $m$ a distance $r$ away from a mass $M$ is given by $$U(r) = -\frac{GMm}{r}.$$ Analyze the stable equilibria. You should find that there are none. If so, how are there stable orbits? The answer to this question will be covered in a later week.
A: The graph has no local minima. There can be stable orbits because when an object is in orbit, it doesn't exist within the 1-D potential as a function of radius $r$, it moves in a circle. It turns out, an effective potential with a local minima <em>can</em> be found, but this will be covered later.

[7] Exercises — $F=ma$
Q: A ball is launched straight toward the ground from height $h$. When it bounces off the ground, it loses half of its kinetic energy. It reaches a maximum height of $2h$ before falling back to the ground again. What was the initial speed of the ball?
A: We have that the initial energy is $E_0 = \frac 12 mv_0^2 + mgh$, which by conservation is equal to the kinetic energy when it hits the ground. Thus, after it loses half of its kinetic energy, the ball has energy $E_1 = \frac 12 E_0$. Then, by conservation of energy $E_1 = 2mgh$, so $2mgh = \frac 12 E_0 = \frac 14 mv_0^2 + \frac 12 mgh$. Solving for $v_0$, we get $v_0^2 = 6gh \implies \boxed{v_0 = \sqrt{6gh}}.$

[8] Exercises
Q: A car of mass $m$ has an engine that delivers a constant power $P$. It drives up a plane inclined at $\theta$. The coefficient of static friction is $\mu_s > \tan \theta$. What is the maximum velocity $v$ it can achieve?
A: At max velocity, acceleration is zero, so $F_{\text{net}} = 0.$ In the rotated frame where the plane is horizontal, the only forces on the car in the $x$ direction are friction and the horizontal component of gravity. Therefore, 
\[F_{\text{net}} = f_s - mg\sin \theta = 0.\]

Therefore, since $P=f_s v,$ we have $\boxed{v_{max} = \frac P{mg\sin\theta}}$

[9] Exercises — Kleppner-Kolenkow
Q: A commonly used potential energy function to describe the interaction between two atoms is the Lennard-Jones potential, given by
 $$U = \epsilon \left[\left(\frac{r_0}{r}\right)^{12} - \left(\frac{r_0}{r}\right)^{6}\right].$$
 \begin{anum}
 \item Find the position of the potential minimum and its value.
 \item Near the minimum the atoms execute simple harmonic motion. Find the frequency of oscillation.
 \end{anum}
A: \begin{anum}
 \item Taking the derivative, we have $$\frac{dU}{dr} = \epsilon \left[ -12\frac{r_0^{12}}{r^{13}} - 6 \frac{r_0^6}{r^7} \right] = 0.$$ Solving, we get $$\left(\frac{r}{r_0}\right)^6 = 2 \implies \boxed{r_{eq} = 2^{1/6}r_0}.$$ Plugging that in, $U_{eq} = \boxed{-\epsilon / 4}.$
 \item Sorry about this part, we haven't covered oscillations yet. Here's the solution for those interested:
 
 We know that $$F = -\frac{dU}{dr} = \epsilon \left [12 \frac{r_0^{12}}{r^{13}} + 6 \frac{r_0^6}{r^7} \right].$$ Then, we plug in $r = r_{eq} + \delta r$, and we get $$F = \epsilon \left[\frac{3}{2^{1/6}r_0}\left(\frac{1}{1 + \delta r/ r_{eq}}\right)^{13} - \frac{3}{2^{1/6}r_0}\left(\frac{1}{1 + \delta r/ r_{eq}}\right)^{7}\right].$$
 Then, using the binomial expansion, we get that $$F \approx \frac{3\epsilon}{2^{1/6}r_0} \left[(7-13)\frac{\delta r}{r_{eq}} \right] = -\frac{18\epsilon}{r_{eq}^2} \delta r.$$ So, the frequnecy of oscillations is $\omega = \sqrt{\frac{18\epsilon}{\mu r_{eq}^2}}.$
 \end{anum}

[10] Exercises
Q: You pull a block of mass $m$ along the ground with a rope, which is angled at $\theta$ to the horizontal, at constant velocity $v$. If the coefficient of friction between the mass and the ground is $\mu$, how much power are you exerting? How does this vary with $\theta$? <em>Hint:</em> this problem isn't the same as the Alice and Bob problem from earlier.
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[width=5cm]{FRIC_PULL.png}
 \end{center}
A: We have that the horizontal component of the force is equal to the friction, so $$F_x = \mu N = \mu (mg - F \sin \theta) = \mu (mg - F_x \tan \theta ).$$ So, solving for $F_x$, we get $$F_x = \frac{\mu mg}{1 + \mu \tan \theta}.$$ Thus, the power is $P = F_xv = \boxed{\frac{\mu mg v}{1 +\mu \tan \theta}}.$

[11] Exercises — $F=ma$
Q: A car has an engine which delivers a constant power. It accelerates from rest at time $t=0$, and at $t=t_0$ its acceleration is $a_0$. What is its acceleration at $t = 2t_0$? Ignore energy loss due to friction.
 \begin{enumerate}[label=(\Alph*)]
 \item $\frac{1}{2}a_0$
 \item $\frac{1}{\sqrt{2}}a_0$
 \item $a_0$
 \item $\sqrt{2}a_0$
 \item $2a_0$
 \end{enumerate}
A: We have from the work energy theorem that $\frac 12 mv^2 = Pt$, so $v =\sqrt{2Pt/m}$. And because power is constant, we must have that $va$ is constant, so $$a \propto 1/v \propto 1/\sqrt{t},$$ from which we get $a(2t_0) = a_0/\sqrt{2}$, \textbf B.

[12] Exercises
Q: The classic roller-coaster. Suppose you have a roller-coaster loop of radius $R$ as shown below. What is the minimum starting height $h$ of the car such that it never loses contact on the loop? Neglect friction everywhere on the path.
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[width=5cm]{roller_bruh.png}
 \end{center}
A: On the verge of losing contact, the cart should experience 0 normal force at the top of the loop. Thus, at the top of the loop, by centripetal acceleration, we have $$\frac{mv^2}{R} = mg \implies mv^2 = mgR.$$ Then, by conservation of energy, we have $$\Delta K = - \Delta U \implies \frac 12 mv^2 = mgh - mg(2R).$$ Plugging in our centripetal acceleration equation, we get $mgh = 2mgR + \frac 12 mgR \implies \boxed{h=5R/2}.$

[13] Exercises
Q: A block of mass $m$ lies on the top of a fixed cylinder of radius $R$. It is given an infinitesimal kick to the right and begins sliding along the outside of the cylinder. At what angle $\theta$ from the top of the cylinder does the block lose contact?
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[width=4cm]{block_cylinder.png}
 \end{center}
A: Again, we use the idea that just before losing contact, the normal force is equal to $0$. So analyzing forces at the position $\theta$, with no normal force $$\frac{mv^2}{R} = mg \cos \theta.$$ Similarly, by conservation of energy, $$\frac 12 mv^2 = mg R - mg R \cos \theta.$$ Plugging in the first equation into the second, we get $$\frac 32 mg R \cos \theta = mgR \implies \cos \theta = \frac 23,$$ so we get $\boxed{\theta = \cos^{-1}(2/3)}.$

[14] Exercises — Kleppner-Kolenkow
Q: The block shown in the drawing is acted on by a spring with spring
constant $k$ and a weak friction force of constant magnitude $f$. The block is pulled distance $x_0$ from equilibrium and released. It oscillates many times and eventually comes to rest. Find the number of cycles $n$ the mass oscillates before coming to rest.
\begin{center}
 \includegraphics[width=5cm]{kk_58.png}
\end{center}
A: By the work-energy theorem, in one cycle, since the spring does no net work, we have $$\Delta K = -4Af,$$ where $A$ is the amplitude of oscillation. And because $U = \frac{1}{2} kA^2 = K$, we have $$\Delta U \approx 2 \cdot \frac 12 k A \Delta A = \Delta K = -4Af.$$ Thus, in one cycle, we have $\Delta A = -4f/k$, so the number of oscillations is just $n = x_0 / (-\Delta A) = \boxed{kx_0/4f}.$

[15] Challenge Problems — $F=ma$
Q: A cylindrical bucket of negligible mass has radius $R$ and height $h$, and is open at the top. It is submerged in water of density $\rho$, with its top a distance $H$ below the surface. How much work is needed to pull the bucket slowly up so that its bottom is just above the lake surface?
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[width=4cm]{fma_bucket.png}
 \end{center}
 <em>Hint:</em> this might look like you need to know fluid statics, but if you think about it it a bit, you can reduce the problem to a simple conservation of energy problem.
A: The solution to this problem isn't complicated, just tricky. The work needed is equal to the total change in energy of the system, which is just the change in gravitational potential energy here, as nothing is moving and no other forces are present. Since the bucket is massless, the change in potential energy just corresponds to the amount of additional water above the surface and how much it had to be raised. We can think of all the water in the bucket to be coming from the infinitesimally thin top layer of the lake as every other height in the lake has water everywhere. The bucket is an average height $h/2$ above the surface, so $$\Delta U = M_w \frac{gh}{2} = (\rho \pi R^2 h) \frac{h}{2} = \boxed{\rho \pi R^2 h^2/2}.$$

[16] Challenge Problems
Q: This time, a block of mass $m$ is dropped from a height $H$ above another block of mass $m$, which is connected to a spring of spring constant $k$. What is the maximum compression of the spring with respect to its original position? Note that the original position is slightly compressed from rest length already because of the mass $m$.
A: You can ignore this problem as it has momentum content, sorry!

[17] Challenge Problems — Kalda
Q: A light wire is bent into a right angle and a heavy ball is attached to the bend. The wire is placed onto supports with height difference h and horizontal distance a. Find the position of the wire in its equilibrium. Express the position as the angle between the bisector of the right angle and the vertical. Neglect any friction between the wire and the supports; the supports have little grooves keeping all motion in the plane of the wire and the figure.
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[width=5cm]{kalda_m_8.png}
 \end{center}
A: We know that the equilibrium point is a local maximum or minimum. And because of the setup above having the mass downward facing and the possible configurations, only a local minimum is possible. Now, the key trick here is that the wires make 90 degree angles, and so the possible locations of the mass form a circle, with a diameter between the two corners as shown below:
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[width=6cm]{kalda_8_sol.png}
 \end{center}
 Now, the minimum gravitational potential energy is just the bottom of the semicircle. All that's left to do now is angle chasing. The tilt of the semicircle is equal to the blue arc length highlighted above, and that's equal to the angle between the horizontal and the diameter. Thus, we compute $2\alpha = \arctan (h/a)$, so $\alpha = \boxed{\frac 12 \arctan(h/a)}$.

[18] Challenge Problems — USAPhO
Q: Here's an extension of problem Problem 12 that is a bit mathy, but good practice for higher olympiads.

 A uniform solid spherical ball starts from rest on a loop-the-loop track. It rolls without slipping along the track. However, it does not have enough speed to make it to the top of the loop. From what height h would the ball need to start in order to land at point $P$ directly underneath the top of the loop? Express your answer in terms of $R$, the radius of the loop. Assume that the radius of the ball is very small compared to the radius of the loop, and that there are no energy losses due to friction.
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[width=7cm]{usapho_2016_B1.png}
 \end{center}
A: See the official solutions <a href="https://www.aapt.org/physicsteam/2019/upload/USAPhO-2016-Solutions.pdf" target="_blank" rel="noopener">here</a>.

[19] Challenge Problems
Q: We discussed the hill analogy for analyzing potential energy graphs $U(x)$. However, note that $U(x)$ only considers one dimension. What if you had a 2D hill in real life? Would the motion of an object on it match the motion of an object on a 1-D potential $U(x)$ with the same shape?

 More formally, suppose you slide a bead along a wire which has shape $y(x) = U(x)/(mg)$. Calculate the horizontal acceleration $\ddot x$ and show that it is <em>not</em> equal to $-U'(x)/m$.
A: We can write down forces in the x and y directions. We have
 \begin{align*}
 m\ddot y &= N_x/y' - mg,\\
 m \ddot x &= -N_x.
 \end{align*}
 Thus, isolating $\ddot x$, we get $\ddot x = -N_x / m = \frac{y'(x)}{m}(-mg - \ddot y) = -\frac{U'(x)}{m} - \frac{U'(x) \ddot y}{m^2g}.$ Thus, at any point where there is non-zero y acceleration, the horizontal force is not equal to the value predicted by the analogy.

[20] Challenge Problems
Q: Consider a <em>block</em> (cannot be treated as a point mass) of mass $m$ which is pulled a distance $d$, at constant velocity $v$, on a floor with coefficient of kinetic friction $\mu$.
 \begin{anum}
 \item How much work does the rope do?
 \item How much work does friction do? <em>Hint:</em> this part is <em>extremely</em> subtle. You won't be able to come up with an exact quantity, but you should be able to come up with an inequality.
 \item Can you explain the inequality you found in part (b) by considering the microscopic workings of friction?
 \end{anum}
A: \begin{anum}
 \item This is simply $W = Fd = \boxed{\mu mg d}.$
 \item We have that the net work done on the block is $W_r + W_f$. And the net change in energy of the block (since it is not a point mass) is $$\frac{1}{2} m(v^2 - v^2) + \Delta K_{int},$$ where $K_{int}$ represents internal vibrations and any other kinetic energies associated with the inner parts of a non-point mass. Furthermore, because friction releases heat, the block will heat up a non-zero amount, so $\Delta K_{int} \ge 0$. Thus, we have $$W_{net} = \mu mg d + W_f = \Delta K = \Delta K_{int} \ge 0.$$ Thus, $\boxed{W_f \ge - \mu mg d}$,
 \item The basic idea is that the point of contact of the force of friction does not move the full distance $d$ because it's these microscopic pieces of the ground and block rubbing, which means the ground and the block aren't necessarily in contact for the full distance $d$. It's explained in further detail in <a href="https://brucesherwood.net/wp-content/uploads/2017/06/Friction1984.pdf" target="_blank" rel="noopener">this paper</a>.
 \end{anum}

------------------------------------------------------------------------------
LESSON: fma-07
------------------------------------------------------------------------------

[1] Concept checks
Q: A collection of masses are in space with some collection of velocities. Initially, their total momentum is $P$. After some number of collisions, the masses eventually move away from one another to infinity. Find their final momentum.
A: Momentum is conserved, since there are no external forces. So the final momentum is $P'=\boxed{P}.$

[2] Concept checks
Q: A bowling ball moving at speed $v$ collides with a stationary ping-pong ball. If the collision is elastic, what is the final speed of the ping-pong ball?
A: In the frame of the bowling ball, the ping-pong ball moves toward the bowling ball at speed $v$ and bounces right off at speed $v$, since the bowling ball is much heavier than the ping-pong ball (so it acts like a wall). Moving back into the lab frame, the final speed of the ping-pong ball is $v_f=v+v=\boxed{2v}.$

[3] Concept checks
Q: A car $m$ and a truck $M$ have the same kinetic energy. What is the ratio of their momenta, $p_m/p_M$?
A: Since $p=mv$, we can write the kinetic energy as $$K=\frac12mv^2=\frac12m\left(\frac{p}{m}\right)^2=\frac{p^2}{2m}$$and so we have $$\frac{p_m^2}{m}=\frac{p_M^2}{M}\Rightarrow \frac{p_m}{p_M}=\boxed{\sqrt{\frac{m}{M}}}$$

[4] Concept checks
Q: A box of mass $m_1$ initially moving at speed $v_1$ collides perfectly inelastically with a box of mass $m_2$ moving at speed $v_2$ (i.e. they stick).
 \begin{anum}
 \item If one box initially moves in the negative $x$-direction and the other moves in the positive $x$-direction, find their final speed. 
 \item If one box initially moves in the $y$-direction and the other moves in the $x$-direction, find their final speed.
 \end{anum}
A: \begin{anum}
 \item Let box 1 move in the positive direction and box 2 move in the negative direction. If the boxes stick together, they have the same final velocity $v$ after the collision. By conservation of momentum, $$m_1v_1-m_2v_2=(m_1+m_2)v\Rightarrow v=\boxed{\frac{m_1v_1-m_2v_2}{m_1+m_2}}$$
 \item If the boxes move perpendicularly, the total momentum will be found by adding their vectors via the Pythagorean theorem: $$p=\sqrt{p_x^2+p_y^2}=\sqrt{(m_1v_1)^2+(m_2v_2)^2}$$Again, since the boxes stick together, they have the same velocity after the collision. So, $$v=\frac{p}{m_1+m_2}=\boxed{\frac{\sqrt{(m_1v_1)^2+(m_2v_2)^2}}{m_1+m_2}}$$
 \end{anum}

[5] Concept checks
Q: A ball of mass $m$ is dropped from a height $h_i$ above the ground and makes an inelastic collision with the ground such that it rebounds to a height $h_f$. Find the impulse exerted by the ground on the ball. Also, what's the impulse exerted by the ball on the ground?
A: Setting the downward direction to be positive, the velocity just before the ball hits the ground is equal to $v_1 = \sqrt{2gh_1}$ by either kinematics or energy. Right after, the velocity is $v_2 = -\sqrt{2gh_2}$ (negative because it now goes up). The change in momentum is equal to the impulse, i.e. $$I=\Delta p = m(v_1 - v_2) = \boxed{m(\sqrt{2gh_1} +\sqrt{2gh_2})}.$$By Newton's third law, the impulse exerted by the ball on the ground is just the negative of this.

[6] Concept checks — Morin
Q: A pool ball with initial speed $v$ is aimed right between two other pool balls as shown. If the two right balls leave the (elastic) collision with equal speeds, find the final velocities of all three balls.
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[scale=0.2]{pool}
 \end{center}
A: Since the impulse to the two right balls comes from the normal force, their final velocities must be directed in the direction of the normal force at the moment of contact. Thus, the two right balls scatter each at an angle $30^\circ$ to the horizontal. Let their speeds be $w$ and the speed of the left ball to be $u$ after the collision (let the rightward direction be positive). By conservation of momentum in the $x$-direction, $$mv=mu+2mw\cos(30^\circ)$$and by conservation of energy, $$\frac 12 mv^2=\frac12 mu^2+2\cdot\frac12mw^2$$Solving the system yields \begin{gather*}
 u=\boxed{-\frac 15 v}\\
 w=\boxed{\frac{2\sqrt 3}{5}v}
 \end{gather*}

[7] Concept checks — $F=ma$
Q: A mass $3M$ moving at a speed $v$ collides with a mass $M$ moving directly toward it, also with a speed $v$. If the collision is elastic, the total kinetic energy after the collision is $K_e$. If the two masses stick together, the total kinetic energy after the collision is $K_s$. What is the ratio $K_e/K_s$?
 \begin{enumerate}[label = (\Alph*)]
\item $1/2$
\item $1$
\item $\sqrt 2$
\item $2$
\item $4$
\end{enumerate}
A: If the collision is elastic, the total kinetic energy is still the same as before, so $$K_e=\frac 12 (3M)v^2+\frac12 Mv^2=2Mv^2$$If the collision is inelastic, the final speed of the masses is found via conservation of momentum, i.e. $$3Mv-Mv=(3M+M)v'\Rightarrow v'=\frac v2$$And so $$K_s=\frac 12(4M)v'^2=Mv^2$$and $K_e/K_2=2$, answer choice <strong>(D)</strong>.

[8] Concept checks — $F=ma$
Q: Three boxes $A$, $B$, and $C$ lie along a straight line on a horizontal frictionless surface, as shown. Box $A$ is initially moving to the right with speed $v$ while the other two boxes are initially at rest. If all collisions are elastic, and the masses of the boxes can be chosen freely, which of the following is closest to the maximum possible final speed of box $C$?
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[scale=0.2]{fma1}
 \end{center}
 \begin{enumerate}[label = (\Alph*)]
\item $v$
\item $2v$
\item $3v$
\item $4v$
\item $5v$
\end{enumerate}
A: We found in lecture that the speed of $B$ after the collision with $A$ is $$v_B=\frac{2m_A}{m_A+m_B}v$$In order to maximize the fraction, assume $m_A$ is fixed. Then, we want to minimize the denominator, so $m_B\approx 0$ (i.e. $B$ is much lighter) and thus $v_B=2v$. Doing the same thing with $B$ and $C$, we can double the speed again. Thus, we can double the speed twice, leading to answer choice <strong>(D)</strong>.

[9] Concept checks — $F=ma$
Q: A ball of radius $R$ and mass $m$ is magically put inside a thin shell of the same mass and radius $2R$. The system is at rest on a horizontal frictionless surface initially. When the ball is, again magically, released inside the shell, it sloshes around in the shell and eventually stops at the bottom of the shell. How far does the shell move from its initial contact point with the surface?
\begin{center}
 \includegraphics[scale=0.2]{gravy}
 \end{center}
 \begin{enumerate}[label = (\Alph*)]
\item $R$
\item $R/2$
\item $R/4$
\item $3R/8$
\item $R/8$
\end{enumerate}
A: The center of mass of the system experiences only a downward gravitational force and not a horizontal force. Thus, its $x$ coordinate stays fixed. At the final position, the ball will be at the bottom of the shell and aligned with the vertical axis of the shell. Thus, the shell must be at the $x$ coordinate of the center of mass when everything comes to rest. At the position shown, the center of mass is a distance $mR/2m=R/2$ from the vertical axis of the shell. Thus, the answer is <strong>(B)</strong>.

[10] Exercises
Q: A wooden block of mass $M$ rests on a frictionless surface. A bullet of mass $m$ enters the block horizontally at a speed $v_i$, travels through, and leaves the block at a speed $v_f$. \begin{anum}\item Find the speed of the block after the collision. 
\item Find the energy lost in the collision.
\end{anum}
A: \begin{anum}
 \item Let the final speed of the block be $v$. Momentum is conserved, so $$mv_i=Mv+mv_f\Rightarrow v=\boxed{\frac{m}{M}(v_i-v_f)}.$$
 \item The initial kinetic energy is $$K_i=\frac 12mv_i^2.$$The final kinetic energy is $$K_f=\frac12mv_f^2+\frac12 Mv^2=\frac12mv_f^2+\frac12\frac{m^2}{M}(v_i-v_f)^2.$$The energy lost is the difference $$K_i-K_f=\boxed{\frac12 m\left(v_i^2-v_f^2-\frac mM(v_i-v_f)^2\right)}$$
 \end{anum}

[11] Exercises
Q: In the situation shown, a small cube $m$ is in the space between a wall and a large block of mass $M \gg m$. The cube is given an initial velocity $v$ and begins to bounce elastically between the wall and the block, making the block move to the right. After a long time, what is the speed of the block?
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[scale=0.2]{collisions}
 \end{center}
A: After a long time, both objects will have stable velocities that do not change, meaning that no more collisions occur. For this to happen, the cube and block will have the same ending velocity $v'$. Since the collisions are elastic, we use conservation of energy:$$\frac12mv^2=\frac12(M+m)v'^2\Rightarrow v'=\boxed{v\sqrt{\frac{m}{M+m}}}$$

[12] Exercises
Q: A string of mass $m$ and length $L$ is initially pulled taught right above a scale as shown. \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[scale=0.2]{string}
 \end{center}
 At time $t=0$, the string is released. The string piles up in a heap on the scale. Find the mass reading on the scale as a function of time $t$.
A: A point on the string at a height $h$ will fall onto the scale in a time $(1/2)gt^2=h$ by kinematics. Thus, at time $t$, a mass $mh/l=mgt^2/2l$ of the string will be on the scale. However, we must also consider the impulse the string exerts on the scale when an element of the string of length $\Delta x$ hits the scale and comes to rest. At time $t$, the pieces of the string hitting the scale would have gained a speed $v=gt$. In time $\Delta t$, a piece of the string with length $v\Delta t$ and mass $\Delta m=mv\Delta t/l$ will experience a change of momentum given by $$\Delta p=\Delta mv=\frac{mv^2\Delta t}{l}=\frac{mg^2t^2\Delta t}{l}$$Rearranging, we can reproduce Newton's second law. $$F=\frac{\Delta p}{\Delta t}=\frac{mg^2t^2}{l}$$The additional mass the scale detects is then $F/g=mgt^2/l$. Adding this to the mass sitting on the scale we found earlier, the reading on the scale is $$m_{\text{meas}}=\frac{mgt^2}{2l}+\frac{mgt^2}{l}=\boxed{\frac{3mgt^2}{2l}}$$

[13] Exercises — $F=ma$
Q: A block of mass $m$ is shot horizontally onto a curved wedge of mass $M$ at velocity $v$. What is the maximum height $h$ reached by the block after it shoots off the vertical segment of the wedge? Assume all surfaces are frictionless and that the wedge does not topple. 
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[scale=0.2]{fma2}
 \end{center}
A: Essentially, the difference between the initial kinetic energy and the final kinetic energy of the system is accounted for in the height of the block. The initial kinetic energy of the system is $\frac12mv^2$. The final horizontal momentum of the system is again $mv$, and the block and wedge have the same horizontal velocity. The final kinetic energy is thus $$\frac{p^2}{2m_{\text{tot}}}=\frac{m^2v^2}{2(M+m)}$$Then, the leftover energy is $$\Delta E=\frac{Mmv^2}{2(M+m)}$$so the final height of the block is $$h=\frac{\Delta E}{mg}=\boxed{\frac{Mv^2}{2(M+m)g}}$$

[14] Exercises
Q: A person of mass $m_p$ and a dog of mass $m_d$ are standing on a raft of mass $M$ and length $L$ in a lake. The person and dog initially stand on opposite ends of the raft as shown, but both move to the center of the raft. 
 \begin{anum}
 \item How far does the raft move during this process?
 \item If the person and the dog both run towards the center at a speed $v$ relative to the raft, how fast does the raft move while the individuals are moving on top of it?
 \end{anum}
A: \begin{anum}
 \item Because no external force acts on the system, the velocity of the center of mass must be constant. Well, the initial velocity is 0, so the center of mass should remain in the same place! Setting the center of the raft as the origin, the center of mass is located at$$x_{\text{cm}}=\frac{m_d(-L/2)+M(0)+m_p(L/2)}{m_d+M+m_p}=\frac{L}{2}\frac{m_p-m_d}{m_p+M+m_d}.$$Note that at the end, all the masses are at the same location, so they must all be at the center of mass. Thus the raft moves from its original location at the origin to $x_{\text{cm}}$ at a distance$$D=x_{\text{cm}}-0=\boxed{\frac{L}{2}\frac{m_p-m_d}{m_p+M+m_d}}$$
 \item It takes the person and dog a time $$t=\frac{L/2}{v}=\frac{L}{2v}$$to meet at the center of the raft. From above, the raft moves a distance $D$ in this time. Thus, the raft velocity is $$v_r=\frac{D}{t}=\boxed{\frac{m_p-m_d}{m_p+M+m_d}v}.$$
 \end{anum}

[15] Exercises
Q: A plank of mass $M$ and length $L$ is placed on a frictionless table, and a small block of mass $m$ is placed on the plank. The coefficient of friction between the block and the plank is $\mu$. Initially, the block is at the left end of the plank as shown but it suddenly gains a velocity $v_0$ to the right. What must $v_0$ be so that after a long time, the block will be at the right end of the plank?
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[scale=0.2]{blockmove}
 \end{center}
A: By conservation of momentum, the blocks must end up moving together at a speed $v = mv_0/(M + m)$. Their combined energy is therefore less, and the lost energy is equal to the work done by friction, $W_f = mg\mu L$. Hence,\begin{align*}
 mg\mu L&=\frac12mv_0^2-\frac12(M+m)\left(\frac{mv_0}{M+m}\right)^2\\
 mg\mu L&=\frac12mv_0^2-\frac12\frac{m^2}{M+m}v_0^2\\
 mg\mu L&=\frac12mv_0^2\left(1-\frac{m}{M+m}\right)\\
 L&=\frac{v_0^2}{2g\mu}\frac{M}{M+m}\\
 \Rightarrow v_0&=\boxed{\sqrt{2g\mu L\frac{M+m}{M}}}
 \end{align*}

[16] Exercises
Q: In the diagram shown, a block of mass $M$ is attached to a string of length $l$ and raised to an angle $\theta$ to the vertical. The block is released and swings down, colliding elastically with a stationary bead of mass $m$. If the table is a height h above the ground, what is the distance $D$ from the foot of the table to the landing point of the bead?
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[scale=0.25]{pendulum}
 \end{center}
A: By conservation of energy, the speed of the block right before the collision is $v_0=\sqrt{2gl(1-\cos\theta)}$. Since the speed of the bead is initially at rest, after an elastic collision it gains a speed $$v=\frac{2M}{M+m}v_0=\frac{2M}{M+m}\sqrt{2gl(1-\cos\theta)}.$$The rest of it is kinematics. The bead is in the air for a time $t=\sqrt{2h/g},$ and so it travels a distance $$D=vt=\boxed{\frac{4M}{M+m}\sqrt{hl(1-\cos\theta)}}.$$

[17] Exercises
Q: A tennis ball of mass $m$ is placed on top of a basketball of mass $M$ and dropped from a height $h$ as shown. All collisions are elastic.
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[scale=0.25]{ballfig.png}
 \end{center}
 If $m\ll M$, find the maximum height reached by the tennis ball $m$.
A: After falling the height $h$, both masses are moving downward at a speed $v=\sqrt{2gh}$. First, the mass $M$ collides with the ground, so for a very small instant $M$ is moving upward at $v$ and $m$ is moving down at $v$. Since $m\ll M$, we shift into the frame of $M$. In this frame, $M$ is stationary while $m$ moves toward it at speed $2v$. It then bounces up with speed $2v$, since we can imagine $M$ as a heavy wall. Moving back into the lab frame, $m$ ends up moving upward at $3v$. Thus, the maximum height is $$h'=\frac{(3v)^2}{2g}=\frac{(3\sqrt{2gh})^2}{2g}=\boxed{9h}$$

[18] Exercises
Q: In the situation shown, a block $m_1$ is traveling toward block $m_2$ at a speed $v$. A spring with constant $k$ is attached to the second block. The block $m_1$ sticks to the spring and the combined system moves to the right. \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[scale=0.15]{boxessprings}
 \end{center}\begin{anum}
 \item Explain why the spring must have its maximum compression when the blocks are stationary relative to one another.
 \item Find the maximum compression of the spring. 
 \item In the subsequent motion, find the maximum and minimum speeds of $m_2$.
 \end{anum}
A: \begin{anum}
 \item If the blocks have any relative motion, the spring is changing in length, either increasing or decreasing. Therefore, the blocks must be stationary relative to one another, i.e. having the same velocity, at maximum compression of the spring.
 \item The state we are looking for is that in which the blocks have the same velocity. This is exactly the state of an inelastic collision. However, in this case, the energy ``lost" is not to friction, but is stored in the spring. The lost energy is found by subtracting the kinetic energies $$\Delta K=\frac12m_1v^2-\frac12(m_1+m_2)\left(\frac{m_1v}{m_1+m_2}\right)^2=\frac12\frac{m_1m_2v^2}{m_1+m_2}$$Setting this equal to $U = (1/2)kx^2$, we have$$x=\boxed{v\sqrt{\frac{m_1m_2}{k(m_1+m_2)}}}$$
 \item The two blocks will begin oscillating on the spring, but the center of mass will always move at a constant velocity. In the frame of the center of mass, the situation right at the beginning of the motion is as shown:
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[scale=0.14]{sol1}
 \end{center}
 The center of mass in this frame is stationary, and the blocks oscillate at maximum speeds $v_{1\text{cm}}$ and $v_{2\text{cm}}$, respectively. For the $m_2$ block, therefore, the minimum speed it attains is when it has the maximum speed to the left in the COM frame ($v_{\text{min}} = v_{\text{cm}} - v_{2\text{cm}}$) and the maximum speed it attains is when it has the maximum speed to the right in the COM frame ($v_{\text{max}} = v_{\text{cm}} + v_{2\text{cm}}$). The velocity of the center of mass is given by the traditional formula,
 $$v_{\text{cm}}=\frac{m_1v}{m_1+m_2}.$$This means that $$v_{\text{2cm}}=|0-v_{\text{cm}}|=v_{\text{cm}}$$where we have taken the magnitude. Hence, \begin{gather*}
 v_{\text{min}} = v_{\text{cm}} - v_{2\text{cm}} = v_{\text{cm}} - v_{\text{cm}} = \boxed{0}\\
 v_{\text{max}}=v_{\text{cm}} + v_{2\text{cm}}=2v_{\text{cm}}=\boxed{\frac{2m_1v}{m_1+m_2}}
 \end{gather*}
 \end{anum}

[19] Challenge Problems
Q: A bowl of mass $M$ and a block of mass $m$ are positioned against a wall as shown. The bowl has a radius $R$, and all surfaces are frictionless. The system is released. Find the maximum speed of the block $m$ in the subsequent motion.
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[scale=0.2]{bowl}
 \end{center}
A: Let’s analyze the motion step-by-step. First, the block $m$ slides down to the bottom of the bowl, while the bowl stays put next to the wall (the normal force between the block and bowl is what pushes the bowl forward). At the bottom, by conservation of energy, the block as a speed $v =\sqrt{2gR}$. As the block starts rising up, the bowl starts moving to the right. After this point, the system loses contact with the wall and both momentum $p = m$v and energy $E = (1/2)mv^2$ are conserved.\\
 
 It is clear that the maximum speed of the block must be attained when it is moving directly to the left as to oppose the momentum of the bowl (the block and bowl “share” the momentum, so to maximize their individual momenta, we want them to move in opposite directions). If the block ends up moving to the left at speed $v_m$ (a negative value) and the bowl moves to the right at speed $v_M$ at the same time, the conservation equations yield \begin{gather*}Mv_M - mv_m = mv\\
\frac12Mv_M^2+\frac12mv_m^2=\frac12mv^2\end{gather*}where $v =\sqrt{2gR}$ as found earlier. Solving this system gives $$v_m =\boxed{\frac{2m}{M + m}\sqrt{2gR}}.$$

[20] Challenge Problems
Q: A moving ball of mass $M$ initially moving along the $x$-axis collides elastically with a stationary ball of mass $m$. The mass $M$ scatters at an angle $\theta$ to the $x$-axis. Find the maximum possible value of $\theta$.
A: Moving to the center of mass frame of reference, the center of mass has velocity $$v_{\text{cm}}=\frac{Mv}{M+m}$$In this frame, the velocity of $M$ is $$v_M=v-v_{\text{cm}}=\frac{mv}{M+m}$$ We know that when $M$ rebounds, it maintains the same velocity $v_M$ but could move at any angle $\theta$ after the collision. Thus, in this frame, the possible velocity vectors trace out a circle of radius $v_M$. Adding $v_{\text{cm}}$ back in as a vector, we can see that the possible final velocity vectors is as shown: \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[scale=0.2]{sol2}
 \end{center}The maximum deflection is at the tangent as shown, which has an angle $$\theta=\boxed{\sin^{-1}\frac{m}{M}}.$$

[21] Challenge Problems — USAPhO
Q: Suppose you drop a block of mass $m$ vertically onto a fixed ramp with angle $\theta$ with coefficient of static and kinetic friction $\mu$. The block is dropped in such a way that it does not rotate after colliding with the ramp. Assume the time of the collision is negligible.
 \begin{anum}
 \item Suppose the block’s speed just before it hits the ramp is $v$ and the block slides down the ramp immediately after impact. What is the speed of the block right after the collision?
 \item What is the minimum $\mu$ such that the speed of the block right after the collision is 0?
 \end{anum}
A: \begin{anum}
 \item During the collision, the block receives impulses from the normal force, friction, and gravity. Since the collision is very short, the impulse due to gravity is negligible. Let $p_N$ and $p_F$ be the magnitudes of the impulses from the normal force and friction force. Since the block stays on the ramp after the collision, its final momentum is parallel to the ramp. Then the normal force must completely eliminate the block’s initial momentum perpendicular to the ramp, so $$p_N=mv\cos\theta.$$Since the block still moves after the collision, $$p_F=\mu p_N.$$The block’s initial momentum parallel to the ramp is $mv \sin \theta$, so$$mv\sin\theta-p_F=mu,$$where $u$ is the final speed of the block. Solving for $u$ gives$$u = \boxed{v(\sin \theta - \mu \cos \theta)}.$$
 \item We set $u = 0$ to obtain $\mu = \boxed{\tan \theta}$.
 \end{anum}

[22] Challenge Problems
Q: A bead of mass $m$ is at the top of a hoop of mass $M$ and radius $R$, which is at rest on a frictionless surface. The bead is given a small kick and it starts sliding along the hoop. What is the maximum value for the normal force $N$ acting from the ground during the subsequent motion?
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[scale=0.15]{beadring}
 \end{center}
A: The normal force is greatest when the bead is at the bottom of the hoop. Here, the normal force must account for the weights of the two objects in addition to the centripetal acceleration of the bead. The most difficult part of this problem is finding the right value for the centripetal acceleration. Assuming the bead has a speed $v$ to the right at the bottom and the hoop has a speed $V$ to the left (it doesn’t really matter which direction we choose in the end, as long as they are opposite in direction), by conservation of momentum we have$$mv=MV.$$By conservation of energy, the kinetic energies of the bead and hoop equal the potential energy lost by the bead. Hence,$$2mgR=\frac12mv^2+\frac12MV^2.$$ Solving these two equations, \begin{gather*}
 v=\sqrt{\frac{4gRM}{M+m}}\\
 V=\sqrt{\frac{4gRm^2}{M(M+m)}}
 \end{gather*}The velocity we must use in the centripetal acceleration equation is the relative speed of the two masses, $v + V$. The normal force is \begin{align*}
 N&=mg+Mg+ma_{\text{cent}}\\
 &=mg+Mg+m\frac{(v+V)^2}{R}\\
 &=mg+Mg+\frac{m}{R}\frac{4gR}{M+m}\left(M+\frac{m^2}{M}+2m\right)\\
 &=mg+Mg+4mg\frac{M+m}{M}\\
 &=\boxed{\frac{(4m+M)(m+M)}{M}g}
 \end{align*}

LESSON: fma-08
