[1] 
Q: A heavy disk of radius $R$ is rolling downwards, unwinding two strings in the process. The strings are attached to
the ceiling and always remain under tension during the motion.
What was the magnitude of velocity of the disk’s centre when
its angular velocity was $\omega$ and the angle between the strings
was $\alpha$?
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[width=5.5cm]{kalda_k44.png}
 \end{center}
A: First we use idea 1.2 -- that the velocity of the string unrolling is $\omega R$. Thus, the velocity of the cylinder projected onto the two strings must be $\omega R$. Thus, we can draw the following diagram:
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[width=4cm]{rolllyyy].png}
 \end{center}
 Thus, we can see that $v = \frac{\omega R}{\cos \alpha / 2}$.

[2]  — EuPhO, extra challenge
Q: A massless thread makes N turns around statically fixed cylinder, as shown in the figure. Initially, the free (unwound) ends of the thread are parallel to the axis $X$. Then, a heavy point-like object P is attached to one end of the thread while the other end is pulled with a constant velocity $u$ along $X$. Find the maximum velocity attained by the heavy object.
\begin{center}
 \includegraphics[width=5.5cm]{disk_eupho.png}
\end{center}
The thread is inextendable and flexible. Suppose that
the turns of the thread are wound tightly to one another
and are placed practically in the same plane, perpendicular to the cylinder axis. Neglect any friction in the system. Do not consider the force of gravity.
A: Suppose the bead $P$ has some tangential velocity $v$ and some velocity perpendicular to the string $w$. Then, between two instances of time, the bead $P$ will swing out and also slide along the string, while the string gets pulled at a rate $u$ on the other side.

 Now, to relate $u$ to $v$ and $w$, we consider some edge cases. Suppose $w = 0$. Then, since the bead just slides tangentially, we must clearly have $u = v$. Now, with that idea in mind, suppose there is a non-zero $w$ but zero $v$. Then, that means the bead $P$ is just spiraling out on its own, so $u = 0 = v$. So, we could guess $u = v$. More concretely, notice that $u$ is equal to the speed of the <em>string</em> at the contact point on the $P$ side, and since $w$ doesn't affect the speed of the <em>string</em> at the contact point (it affects the speed of the contact point itself), we have that $u = v$.

 Now, to finish, we have to use some ideas from dynamics. Consider two instances of time as the bead $P$ moves:

 Since the string points perpendicular to the initial $w$, we must have that the velocity in that axis is unchanged between these two time stamps by Newton's second law. Thus, we can write
 $$w = (w+dw)\cos (d \theta) - v d \theta = w + dw- v d\theta,$$
 where $d\theta$ is the change in angle of the contact point, and dropping second order terms. So, we get that $dw = v d\theta$. And since the contact point rotates up to $2\pi N$ radians as it unwinds, we get
 $$w_f = \int_0^{w_f}dw = \int_0^{2\pi N} v d\theta = 2\pi Nv = 2\pi N u.$$
 So, the final speed is $\sqrt{w_f^2 + v^2} = u\sqrt{4\pi^2 N^2 + 1}.$

[3]  — Kalda
Q: Light wheels with radius $R$ are attached to a heavy axle. The system rolls along a horizontal surface which suddenly turns into a slope with angle $\alpha$. For which angles $\alpha$ will the wheels move without lifting off, i.e. touch the surface at all times? Mass of the wheels can be neglected. The axle is parallel to the boundary between horizontal and sloped surfaces and has velocity $v$.
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[width=5cm]{wheel_on_hill.png}
 \end{center}
A: The axle is essentially rotating about the corner through a total angle of $\alpha$. By conservation of energy through that period,
 $$\frac 12 mv^2 = \frac 12 mv_\alpha^2 - mgR(1 - \cos \alpha).$$
 And at the limit, we'll have $v_\alpha^2/R = mg \cos \alpha$. So, plugging that in, we get
 $$\frac 12 mv^2 = \frac 32 mg R \cos \alpha - mgR,$$
 so the maximum $\alpha$ is given by
 $$\frac 32 mg R \cos \alpha = \frac 12 mv^2 + mgR \implies \alpha \le \arccos\left[\frac 23 + \frac 13 \frac{v^2}{gR}\right].$$

[4]  — Kleppner-Kolenkow
Q: A mass $m$ is attached to a post of radius $R$ by a string. Initially it is distance $r$ from the center of the post and is moving tangentially with speed $v_0$.
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[width=4cm]{pulling thread.png}
 \end{center}
 \begin{anum}
 \item The string passes through a hole in the center of the
post at the top. The string is gradually shortened by drawing it
through the hole.
\item The string wraps around the outside of the post.
What quantities are conserved in each case? Find the final speed
of the mass when it hits the post for each case
 \end{anum}
A: \begin{anum}
 \item In this case, there is no net torque on the bead, so by conservation of angular momentum
 $$mv_0R = mv_fr \implies v_f = \frac{v_0R}{r}.$$
 \item This time, conservation of energy holds as there is no energy loss when the string sticks onto the cylinder--all the forces are perpendicular to the string as it rolls onto the rod. Thus, the final speed is simply $v_0$ as there are no other energies involved.
 \end{anum}

[5]  — Morin
Q: A board lies on top of two uniform cylinders that lie on a fixed plane inclined at an angle $\theta$, as shown below. The board has mass $m$, and each of the cylinders has mass $m/2$. The system is released from rest. If there is no slipping between any of the surfaces, what is the acceleration of the board? Use conservation of energy.
 \begin{center} \includegraphics[width=5cm]{cylinder_plank.png}
 \end{center}
A: Suppose that the board moves down a distance $d$ along the inclined plane. Then, by rolling without slipping, each of the cylinders move by a distance $d/2$. Lastly, if the board has velocity $v$, then by roling without slipping again, the cylinders have velocity $v/2$ and angular speed $v/2r$. So, we can write down conservation of energy:
 $$mgd\sin \theta + mg\frac{d}{2}\sin \theta = \frac{1}{2}mv^2 +\frac 12 m (v/2)^2 + \frac 12 (mr^2/2)(v/2r)^2.$$
 Using kinematics, we can write $v^2 = 2ad$, so
 $$\frac 32 mg d \sin \theta = mad + \frac14 mad + \frac 18 mad = \frac{11}8mad.$$
 So, dividing, we get $a = \frac{12}{11} g \sin \theta.$

[6]  — PPP
Q: A long slipway, inclined at an angle $\alpha$ to the horizontal is fitted with many identical rollers, consecutive ones being a distance $d$ apart. The rollers have horizontal axles and consist of rubber-covered solid steel cylinders each of mass $m$ and radius $r$. Planks of mass $M$, and length much greater than $d$, are released at the top of the slipway. Find the terminal speed $v_{\text{max}}$ of the planks. Ignore air resistance and friction at the pivots of the rollers.
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[width=6cm]{rollers_ppp.png}
 \end{center}
A: To find the terminal speed, we suppose the mass has a speed $v$ right as it crosses a roller and we analyze the motion until it finishes colliding with the next roller. Now we consider forces on this mass as it rolls down. There is the force of gravity and the forces from the friction on each of the rollers. Now, the tricky part here, is that we have to use momentum instead of energy. Energy is hard to use because there is energy lost to the rollers. Since the block is at terminal speed, the net impulse applied to it between collisions with rolelrs must be equal to $0$. Now, assuming that the block moves at speed $v$, the impulse due to the gravitational force is:
 $$J_g = Ft = F(d/v) = Mg\frac{d}{v}\sin \theta.$$
 Now, we calculate the impulse due to the rollers. From the initial state to the final state, all the rollers it was already sitting on are still rotating at angular speed $v/r$, so their angular momentum has no net change, which means there couldn't have been a net linear impulse on the block. Now, the nearest roller reached now has picked up angular speed $v/r$. so the angular impulse applied was
 $$\Delta L = \frac 12 mr^2 (v/r) = \frac 12 mvr,$$
 and so using idea 1.1, the net linear impulse applied on the block is $\Delta L /r = \frac 12 mv$. So, the total impulse on the block is
 $$mv - mv = 0 = \Delta p = Mg \frac dv \sin \theta - \frac 12 mv,$$
 which gives us $v = \sqrt{\frac{2Mgd \sin \theta}{m}}.$

[7]  — Kalda
Q: A cart has two cylindrical wheels connected by a
weightless horizontal rod using weightless spokes and frictionless axis as shown in the figure. Each of the wheels is made
of a homogeneous disc of radius $R$, and has a cylidrical hole
of radius $R/2$ drilled coaxially at the distance $R/3$ from the
centre of the wheel. The wheels are turned so that the holes
point towards each other, and the cart is put into motion on
a horizontal floor. What is the critical speed $v$ by which the
wheels start jumping?
\begin{center}
 \includegraphics[width=6cm]{holy_wheels.png}
\end{center}
A: We focus on one wheel. The wheel will start jumping when the normal force between it and the ground is equal to 0. Because the center of mass of the wheels is shifted off, as they rotate the forces must add to a proper centripetal force. When the hole is at the bottom, the centripetal force must be downward, and if it is required to be larger than $mg$, it will jump, as the normal force on the ground has to be $0$. So, we look for when the centripetal acceleration is greater than or equal to $g$. 
 
 First, we find the center of mass of the wheel. Suppose the original wheel had mass $M$. Then, the piece that was cut out has mass $M/4$. So, the center of mass (using negative mass idea),
 $$d = \frac{-(M/4) R/3}{M + (-M/4)} = - \frac{R}{9}.$$
 So, the centripetal acceleration is $\omega^2 d$, where $\omega = v/R$. Thus, for the critical $v$, we set
 $$\omega^2 d = (v/R)^2 d = g \implies v = R\sqrt{g/d} = \boxed{3 \sqrt{g/R}}.$$

[8]  — Morin
Q: A solid cylinder of mass $m$ and radius $r$ lies flat on a frictionless horizontal table, with a massless string running halfway around it, as shown below. A mass also of mass m is attached to one end of the string, and you pull on the other end with a force $T$. The circumference of the cylinder is sufficiently rough so that the string does not slip with respect to it. What is the acceleration of the mass $m$ attached to the end of the string?
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[width=5.5cm]{morin850.png}
 \end{center}
A: We can write down
 \begin{align*}
 ma_c &= T +T_1,\\
 \frac{mr^2}{2} \alpha &= (T-T_1)r,\\
 ma_m &= T_1,
 \end{align*}
 for forces and torque. And then for rolling without slipping, transform into the frame of the cylinder, and we get
 $$(a_m + a_c) = \alpha r.$$
 Now, plugging that relation in and then doubling the middle equation and adding all three, we get
 $$m(a_c + a_m) + m\alpha r = 2m\alpha r = 3T + T_1 - 2 T_1 + T_1 = 3 T.$$
 Thus, we get $\alpha = 3T/2mr$. Then, plugging this in and combining a couple equations:
 \begin{align*}
 a_m - a_c &= -\frac{T}{m},\\
 a_m + a_c &= \frac{3T}{2m}.
 \end{align*}
 Thus, adding the two, we finally get $\boxed{a_m = T/4m}$.

[9]  — Morin
Q: A massless string of negligible thickness is wrapped around a uniform cylinder of mass $m$ and radius $r$. The string passes up over a massless pulley and is tied to a block of mass m at its other end, as shown below. The system is released from rest. What are the accelerations of the block and the cylinder? Assume that the string does not slip with respect to the cylinder.
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[width=4cm]{block_cylinder_atwood.png}
 \end{center}
A: We write the force and torque equations:
 \begin{align*}
 ma &= T - mg,\\
 ma_c &= mg - T,\\
 \frac{mr^2}{2}\alpha_c &= Tr.
 \end{align*}
 We can also relate by rolling without slipping:
 $$a = a_c - \alpha_c r.$$
 Now, we just need to solve this system of four equations. First, the first two give us $a = -a_c$. Then, plugging that into the last one, we get $\alpha_c r = 2a_c$, which plugging into the 3rd equation leaves us with:
 \begin{align*}
 ma_c &= mg - T,\\
 ma_c &= T,
 \end{align*}
 so $a_c = g/2$ downward and $a = -g/2$ upward, so $g/2$ downward.

[10] 
Q: Let's resolve a paradox from the Rotation I lecture. A massless stick of length $b$ has one end pivoted on a support and the other end glued perpendicular to the middle of a stick of mass $m$ and length $\ell$. The two sticks are held in a vertical plane and then released.
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[width=5cm]{paradox_twigs.png}
 \end{center}
 \begin{anum}
 \item Calculate the upwards normal force on the left side of the massless rod.
 \item Calculate the downwards normal force on the right side of the massless rod.
 \item It appears that there is a net clockwise torque on the massless rod! However, the rod is massless, so this would imply infinite angular acceleration. What's going on here? <em>Hint:</em> consider treating the rod as having non-negligible thickness and analyze the forces on the top and bottom of the rod. 
 \end{anum}
A: \begin{anum}
 \item We did this in lecture, $N = mg\frac{l^2/12b^2}{1+(l^2/12b^2)}$.
 \item Because the acceleration of the right massive rod is the same as the whole setup, the normal force is $N = mg\frac{l^2/12b^2}{1+(l^2/12b^2)}$.
 \item Here's what the rod looks like at a microscopic level:
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[width=4cm]{connection_pece.png}
 \end{center}
 It must have at least 2 attachment points! This is because it is not a hinge and the whole system rotates together -- otherwise, the 90 degree angle would not remain 90 degrees. So, there is compression in the top (blue) and tension in the bottom (red), cancelling out the net torque from the vertical forces.
 \end{anum}

[11]  — BAUPC
Q: A small ball is attached to a massless string of length $L$, the other end of which is attached to a very thin pole. The ball is thrown so that it initially travels in a horizontal circle, with the string making an angle $\theta_0$ with the vertical. As time goes on, the string wraps itself around the pole. Assume that (1) the pole is thin enough so that the length of string in the air decre9ases very slowly, and (2) the pole has enough friction so that the string does not slide on the pole, once it touches it. Show that the ratio of the ball’s final speed (right before it hits the pole) to initial speed is $\sin \theta_0$.
A: If the string changes by $\mathrm d \ell$, then the "pivot" of the string goes down by $-\mathrm d \ell \cos \theta$, and then any change in height after than is $\mathrm d (\ell \cos \theta) = \cos \theta \mathrm d\ell - \ell \sin \theta \mathrm d\theta.$ So then adding the two changes, the conservation of energy equation is
\begin{equation}-mg\ell \sin \theta \mathrm d\theta = mv \mathrm d v.\end{equation}
And writing $F=ma$ radially, we also have
$$mg \tan \theta = \frac{mv^2}{\ell \sin\theta}.$$
So then substituting this into (1), 
$$-mg \frac{mv^2}{mg\tan \theta} \mathrm d\theta = mv \mathrm d v.$$
So now we just integrate, with $\theta \to \pi / 2$ at the end because $\ell \to 0$, so
$$\int_{\theta_0}^{\pi / 2}-\cot \theta \mathrm d \theta = \int_{v_i}^{v_f} \frac 1 v \mathrm d v \implies \ln\left(\frac{\sin \theta_0}{\sin \pi / 2}\right) = \ln \left(\frac{v_f}{v_i}\right).$$
So then we get the desired answer of $v_f / v_i = \sin \theta_0.$

[12] 
Q: The classic movable inclined plane. Consider a uniform cylinder of mass $m$ and radius $r$. It is released from rest from the top of an inclined plane of mass $M$ and inclination $\theta$. Assuming that the cylinder rotates without slipping, find the acceleration of the inclined plane.
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[width=5cm]{moving_inclined.png}
 \end{center}
A: By conservation of angular momentum, we have 
 $$mR^2\theta - m(R-h)^2 \theta = (MR^2/2)\alpha.$$
 Using the binomial approximation,
 $$2mRh = \frac{MR^2}{2}\alpha \implies \alpha = \theta\frac{4mh}{MR}.$$

[13]  — USAPhO
Q: A disk of uniform mass density, mass $M$, and radius $R$ sits at rest on a frictionless floor. The disk is attached to the floor by a frictionless pivot at its center, which keeps the center of the disk in place, but allows the disk to rotate freely. An ant of mass $m \ll M$ is initially standing on the edge of the disk; you may give your answers to leading order in $m/M$. . The ant walks an angular displacement $\theta$ along the edge of the disk. Then it walks radially inward by a distance $h \ll R$, tangentially through an angular displacement $-\theta$, then back to its starting point on the disk. Assume the ant walks with constant speed $v$.
\begin{center}
 \includegraphics[width=6cm]{21usaphoB1a.png}
 \end{center}
 Through what net angle does the disk rotate throughout this process, to leading order in $h/R$?
A: By conservation of angular momentum, we have 
 $$mR^2\theta - m(R-h)^2 \theta = (MR^2/2)\alpha.$$
 Using the binomial approximation,
 $$2mRh = \frac{MR^2}{2}\alpha \implies \alpha = \theta\frac{4mh}{MR}.$$

[14]  — USAPhO
Q: Consider the same setup as in the previous problem, but now the ant walks with speed $v$ along a circle of radius $r$, tangent to its starting point.
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[width=6cm]{21usaphoB1b.png}
 \end{center}
 Through what net angle does the disk rotate?
A: Let $C$ be the curve that the ant moves on. Then the integrated moment of inertia weighted angular displacement is
 $$\int_C md^2 d\theta = \frac{MR^2}{2} \alpha,$$
 where the curve is some function $d(\theta)$. But notice that $d^2d\theta$ is simply twice the polar area <span class="footnote">[noticing this is pretty tricky!]</span>, and so
 $$\int_C md^2 d\theta = 2m(\text{Area Circle} = 2m\pi r^2 = \frac{MR^2}{2}\alpha.$$
 Thus, $\alpha = \boxed{\theta\frac{4\pi mr^2}{MR^2}}.$

------------------------------------------------------------------------------
LESSON: fma-11
------------------------------------------------------------------------------

[1] Concept Checks
Q: A uniform rectangular block is in static equilibrium on an inclined plane, and experiences gravity, static friction, and the normal force from the plane. Though gravity acts on the entire volume of the block, for the purposes of torque balance, it is equivalent to a single resultant force acting at the block's center of mass. Similarly, while the normal force acts on the entire bottom surface of the block, its torque is equivalent to a resultant force acting at a single point. Which of the following best shows this point?
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[width=0.4\textwidth]{fma_normal_bot.jpg}
 \end{center}
A: Consider taking torque about the point where the normal force is concentrated. Since it is on the plane, the torque due to both the normal force and the friction force is equal to 0. The only remaining force is the gravitational force. Thus, the moment arm of the gravitational force should be equal to 0, which means the point should lie vertically below the center of mass, and so the answer is <strong>B</strong>.

[2] Concept Checks
Q: You pull on a block until it tips over (without the corner slipping). Just before it tips over, where is the normal force concentrated? Explain why. What is the magnitude of the force you're pulling with?
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[width=0.4\textwidth]{tipping.png}
 \end{center}
A: The normal force will always provide a clockwise torque, so at maximum force, we want to minimize other clockwise-contributing torques, so the normal force will shift to the right. This is also intuitive as it pivots around the right point when it starts to tip.

[3] Concept Checks
Q: You place a square block of side length $s$ on a ramp with an adjustable incline. The coefficient of static friction between the ramp and the block is $\mu$. Then, you slowly increase the incline from $0$ degrees to $90$. Under what condition does the block slip before it rolls?
A: If it doesn't tip, then it slips at angle $\theta = \arctan \mu$. For it to tip, we need the center of mass to be past the bottom corner of the square, so we need
 $$\arctan \mu < 45^\circ \implies \boxed{\mu < 1}.$$

[4] Concept Checks
Q: Consider the following inverted scissor lift contraption with a mass $mg$ hanging at the bottom. What is the magnitude of the tension in the string connected between the 3rd and 4th intersections?
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[width=5cm]{scissor.png}
 \end{center}
A: We use virtual work. If the mass in the system shifts down by a distance $dh$, then the work done by the gravity is
 $$dW_{\text{grav}} = mg dh,$$
 and since the string stretches by distance $dh/4$ by symmetry, the work done by the string is $-T (dh/4)$, so
 $$mg dh - \frac T4 dh = 0 \implies \boxed{T = 4mg}.$$

[5] Concept Checks — $F=ma$
Q: In the mobile below, the two cross beams and the seven supporting strings are all massless. The hanging
 objects are $M_1$ = 400 g, $M_2$ = 200 g, and $M_4$ = 500 g. What is the value of $M_3$ for the system to be in
 static equilibrium?
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[width = 0.6\textwidth]{fma_mobile.png}
 \end{center}
A: Consider the net torque about the point of attachment of the mobile. This is straightforward to calculate:
 $$M_2g(0.5) + M_4g(0.4) - M_1g(0.4) - M_3g(0.2) = 0.$$
 Plugging in the values for $M_1,M_2,M_4$, we get
 $$M_3 = \frac{140}{0.2} = \boxed{700 g}.$$

[6] Exercises — OPhO
Q: A solid cube of uniform density, mass $m$, and sidelength $\ell$ rests on the ground. A horizontal force $F$ is exerted on the top edge of one
vertical face of the cube while a rope is strung from pulley to another mass of mass
$M$. Assuming that the tension in the rope stays roughly the same, how far will the
normal force move from the central axis of the block to stop the block from toppling
over? Assume that there is sufficient friction such that the block does not slip and
that the mass on the pulley does not accelerate. Note that $m > M$
\begin{center}
 \includegraphics[width=5cm]{opho_tip.png}
\end{center}
Under what condition is it impossible for this setup to be static?
A: We calculate the net torque about the bottom right corner:
 $$Mg\frac{\ell}{2} + F \ell - mg \frac \ell 2 + Nd = 0,$$
 where $d$ is the distance of the point of normal force exertion from the bottom right corner. Also note that by vertical force balalnce, $N = mg$, so solving for $d$, we get
 $$d = \frac{\ell}2 - \frac{F\ell}{mg} - \frac{M\ell}{2m}.$$
 Thus, the shift is $\ell/2 - d = \boxed{\ell(F/mg + M/2m)}$.

[7] Exercises — $F=ma$
Q: The object shown below is made of three rigidly connected, identical rods with uniform density.
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[width=3cm]{back_lambda.png}
 \end{center}
 When it stands upright on a horizontal table, what fraction of its weight rests on the left leg?
A: Let the horizontal distance between the top and bottom of any rod be $x$, and let each rod have mass $m$. then, the torque about the bottom right point is
 $$N(2x) - mg\frac{3x}{2} - 2mg\frac{x}{2} = 0.$$
 Solving for $x$, we get
 $$N = \frac{5mgx}{4x} = \frac{5mg}{4}.$$
 Since the total weight is $3mg$, the fraction is $N/3mg = \boxed{5/12}.$

[8] Exercises — Morin
Q: Two sticks are connected, with hinges, to each other and to a wall. The bottom stick is horizontal and has length $L$, and the sticks make an angle of $\theta$ with each other, as shown below. If both sticks have the same mass per unit length, $\rho$, find the horizontal and vertical components
of the force that the wall exerts on the top hinge
\begin{center}
 \includegraphics[width=3cm]{triangle_wall.png}
\end{center}
A: First, consider torque about the bottom left corner. This gives us
 $$F_xL\tan \theta = \rho L(1+\sec \theta)g \frac{L}{2} \implies F_x = \frac{\rho g L}{2}(\cot \theta + \csc \theta).$$
 Then, for vertical, balance torques on just the top stick (only part of the system), and we get
 $$F_xL\tan \theta + \rho L\sec \theta g \frac L2 = F_y L \implies F_y = \frac{\rho g L}{2}(1 + 2\sec \theta).$$

[9] Exercises
Q: Four identical bricks are placed on top of each other at the edge of a table. Is it possible to slide them horizontally across each other in such a way that the projection of the topmost one is completely outside the table? What is the theoretical limit to the displacement of the topmost brick if the number of bricks is arbitrarily increased?
A: Let $L$ be the length of a brick. Observe for the first few cases, by calculating the location of the center of mass of each composite system:
 $$d_1 = L/2, d_2 = \frac{L}{2} + \frac L4, d_3 = \frac L2 + \frac L4 + \frac L6.$$
 It looks like the general case will be
 $$d_n = \frac L2 \left(1 + \frac 12 + \cdots + \frac 1n \right).$$
 We now show that this is true. If we have a bunch of bricks stacked on top of each other, sticking out as much as possible as shown below,
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[width=5cm]{bricks.png}
 \end{center}
 Then the center of mass of the top $k$ blocks must still lie on the surface of the $k+1$st block. Thus, in the maximally extended case, the center of mass will like on the edge of the $k+1$st block. So, the center of mass of the $k+1$ blocks will be a distance
 $$\frac{km(L/2) + 0\cdot m}{(k+1)m} = \frac{k}{k+1}\frac{L}{2}$$
 from the center of mass of the $k+1$st block. This means the edge of the $k+1$st block can be a distance $1 - \frac k{k+1} = \frac{1}{k+1}$ extended from the block below it, so
 $$d_{k+1} = d_k + \frac{1}{k+1},$$
 showing that our conjecture is true by induction.

 Thus, for 4 blocks we can get over the table as $(1+1/2+1/3+1/4)/2 \approx 1.04 > 1$. And in the general case, we can extend arbitrarily far as the harmonic series does not converge.

[10] Exercises
Q: Prove that for an object in static equilibrium, torque around any origin being zero implies that there's zero torque about any other origin.
A: Suppose that we have a collection of forces $\mathbf F_i$ being applied at radii $\mathbf r_i$ from some origin. Then, we have that
 $$\tau = \sum_i \mathbf r_i \times \mathbf F_i = 0.$$
 Then, about some other origin, $\mathbf r_0$, we have
 $$\tau_{r_0} = \sum_i (\mathbf r_i -\mathbf r_0) \times \mathbf F_i = \sum_i \mathbf r_i \times \mathbf F_i -\mathf r_0\sum_i \mathbf F_i.$$
 The first term is just $\tau = 0$ and since we are in static equilibrium the net force is 0, so $\sum_i \mathbf F_i = 0$, so
 $$\tau_{r_0} = 0 + 0 = 0,$$
 as desired.

[11] Exercises — Morin, modified
Q: A frictionless tube lies in the vertical plane and is in the shape of a function that has its endpoints at the same height but is otherwise arbitrary.
A chain with uniform mass per unit length lies in the tube from end to
end, as shown below. Use the principle virtual work to show that the setup is static. That is, show that if you move the chain by $dx$, the change in potential energy is order $dx^2$.
\begin{center}
 \includegraphics[width=3cm]{static_chain.png}
\end{center}
A: If we move the chain clockwise by $dx$, then it is equivalent to moving a bit of the chain from the left side and attaching it on the right side. The mass of such a piece is $\rho dx$. Then, the change in height is $dx \sin \theta_l + dx \sin \theta_r$ where $\theta_l$ and $\theta_r$ are the angles the left and right sides make with the horizontal. Thus, the change in energy is
 $$dU = (\rho dx)dx(\sin \theta_l + \sin \theta_r)g \sim (dx)^2,$$
 so the change in energy is order $(dx)^2$, and it is in static equilibrium.

[12] Exercises
Q: % A rope of mass density $\lambda$ is fixed by its ends on two supports of height $h$, such that the middle of the rope <em>almost</em> touches the ground. Find the tension at the following points on the rope:
% \begin{anum}
% \item the ends of the rope
% \item the middle of the rope
% \item a point on the rope that is $\frac{h}{2}$ above the ground.
% \end{anum}
% \begin{center}
% \includegraphics[width = 0.6\textwidth]{catenary.png}
% \end{center}
%
A: \begin{anum}
 \item We use the trick of taking torque about any point, and choose the contact point on the ground which cancels both gravity and friction. Thus, we get that the string's line must intersect the contact point, so $\theta = \arccos (r/R)$ by tangent line geometry.
 \item The horizontal component of the tension force is
 $$T \cos \theta,$$
 so we need $T\cos \theta \le \mu N $. The normal force is
 $$N = Mg - T\sin \theta ,$$
 so our inequality is
 $$T\cos \theta \le \mu Mg - \mu T \sin \theta \implies T \le \frac{\mu Mg}{\cos \theta + \mu \sin \theta}.$$
 \item Using part (b), we want to maximize $\cos \theta + \mu \sin \theta$. But this is actually the same maximization as in idea 1.4! The angle $\theta = \arctan \mu$ to minimize $T$, pulling perpendicular to the combined force. Thus,
 $$r/R = \cos(\arctan \mu) = \frac{1}{\sqrt{1 + \mu^2}},$$
 giving us $\boxed{r = R/\sqrt{1+\mu^2}}.$
 \end{anum}

[13] Exercises
Q: Here's an extension of the spool problem that we introduced a couple weeks ago. A spool consists of an axle of radius $r$ and an outside circle of radius $R$ which rolls on the ground. A thread is wrapped around the axle and is pulled with tension $T$ at an angle $\theta$ with the horizontal.
 \begin{anum}
 \item Given $R$ and $r$, what should $\theta$ be so that the spool doesn’t move? Assume that the friction between the spool and the ground is large enough so that the spool doesn’t slip.
 \item Given $R$, $r$, and the coefficient of friction $\mu$ between the spool and the ground, what is the largest value of $T$ for which the spool remains at rest?
 \item Given and $R$ and $\mu$, what should $r$ be so that you can make the spool slip from the static position with as small a $T$ as possible?
 \end{anum}
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[width = 0.3\textwidth]{morin_spool.png}
 \end{center}
A: \begin{anum}
 \item We use the trick of taking torque about any point, and choose the contact point on the ground which cancels both gravity and friction. Thus, we get that the string's line must intersect the contact point, so $\theta = \arccos (r/R)$ by tangent line geometry.
 \item The horizontal component of the tension force is
 $$T \cos \theta,$$
 so we need $T\cos \theta \le \mu N $. The normal force is
 $$N = Mg - T\sin \theta ,$$
 so our inequality is
 $$T\cos \theta \le \mu Mg - \mu T \sin \theta \implies T \le \frac{\mu Mg}{\cos \theta + \mu \sin \theta}.$$
 \item Using part (b), we want to maximize $\cos \theta + \mu \sin \theta$. But this is actually the same maximization as in idea 1.4! The angle $\theta = \arctan \mu$ to minimize $T$, pulling perpendicular to the combined force. Thus,
 $$r/R = \cos(\arctan \mu) = \frac{1}{\sqrt{1 + \mu^2}},$$
 giving us $\boxed{r = R/\sqrt{1+\mu^2}}.$
 \end{anum}

[14] Exercises
Q: The sign shown below consists of two uniform legs attached by a frictionless hinge. The coefficient of friction
between the ground and the legs is $\mu$. What is the maximum value of $\theta$ such that the sign will
not collapse?
\begin{center}
 \includegraphics[height=4cm]{hinged_sing.png}
\end{center}
A: First, note that by symmetry that the normal force at each of the legs should be $mg/2$. Now, take torque about the top corner on just the right stick. We have
 $$\frac{mg}{2}\frac L2 \sin (\theta/2) + \frac{\mu mg}{2}L\cos (\theta/2) = \frac{mg}{2}L \sin (\theta/2).$$
 So, isolating for $\mu$ and $\theta$, we get
 $$2\mu = \tan (\theta / 2) \implies \theta = \boxed{2\arctan(2\mu)}.$$

[15] Exercises — Kalda
Q: A rod with length $l$ is hinged to a ceiling with height
$h < l$. Underneath, a board is being dragged on the floor. The
rod is meant to block the movement the board in one direction
while allowing it move in the opposite direction. What condition should be fulfilled for it to do its job? The coefficient of
friction is $\mu_1$ between the board and the rod, and $\mu_2$ between
the board and the floor.
\begin{center}
 \includegraphics[width=4cm]{doorstop.png}
\end{center}
A: When we pull the board right, the force on the rod should be rightward so that it rotates and keeps the interaction tight and unmovable. From idea 1.4, the interaction force points at an angle $\arctan \mu_1$ to the vertical. So, for the force to be rightward, we need
 $$\arctan \mu_1 > \alpha \implies \mu_1 > \tan \alpha.$$

[16] Challenge Problems — Kalda
Q: On an incline with slope angle $\alpha$ there lies a cylinder with mass $M$, its axis being horizontal. A small block with mass $m$ is placed inside it. The coefficient of friction between the block and the cylinder is $\mu$; the incline is nonslippery. What is the maximum slope angle $\alpha$ for the cylinder to stay at rest? The block is much smaller than the radius of the cylinder.
\begin{center}
 \includegraphics[width = 0.4\textwidth]{block_in_block_kalda.jpg}
 \end{center}
A: First, note that for maximum slope, we want the mass $m$ to be as leftward as possible. The most this can be is when the mass $m$ is lying at a point on the circle that is $\theta = \arctan \mu$ from the vertical. Now, with that in mind, we take torque about the contact point of the cylinder and the surface.
 $$MgR\sin \alpha = mg(R\sin \theta - R\sin \alpha).$$
 So, rearranging, we get
 $$(M+m) \sin \alpha = \sin \theta = m\frac{\mu}{\sqrt{1+\mu^2}}.$$
 So, we get $\alpha = \boxed{\arcsin\left[\frac{m}{M+m} \frac{\mu}{\sqrt{1+\mu^2}}\right]}$.

[17] Challenge Problems
Q: A uniform rod of length $2R$ is placed inside a fixed, frictionless hemispherical bowl of radius $R$. In equilibrium, the rod makes an angle $\theta$ with the horizontal. Assume that the rod and bowl are ideally rigid, but that the lip of the bowl and the end of the rod are both slightly rounded, so that there is a well-defined normal direction at the points they touch. Find $\theta$.
A: There are only 3 forces on this rod. Thus, if we consider torque about the intersection point of two of them, the third must intersect as well, for there to be 0 net torque. Now, we have reduced it to a geometry problem. See the following diagram:
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[width=6cm]{rod_bowl.png}
 \end{center}
 We can calculate the length $BD$ in two ways.
 \begin{align*}
 BD &= BC - DC = 2R\cos \theta - R,\\
 BD &= AB \tan \theta = 2R \sin \theta \tan \theta.
 \end{align*}
 Thus, equating the two and simplifying, we get
 $$2\cos \theta - 1 = 2\frac{\sin^2\theta}{\cos \theta}.$$
 This gives us the quadratic
 $$4\cos^2 \theta - \cos \theta - 2 = 0,$$
 so solving we get $\theta \approx 32.5^\circ$.

[18] Challenge Problems
Q: Consider a massive string of mass density $\lambda$ fixed at two points a horizontal displacement $L$ from each other. What shape does the string make? <em>Hint:</em> consider forces on small pieces of the string.
A: Consider a small segment of the string which spans a distance $dx$ horizontally and makes an angle $\theta$ with the horizontal. Horizontal forces on this piece give us,
 \begin{align*}
 dT_x &= 0,\\
 dT_y &= gdm = \lambda \frac{dx}{\cos \theta} g.
 \end{align*}
 So, we can say there is some constant horizontal tension $T_x$. Then,
 $$T_y = T_x \frac{dy}{dx}.$$
 Also, we can write
 $$\cos \theta = \frac{1}{\sqrt{1 + (dy/dx)^2}}.$$
 Plugging these into the second equation, we get
 $$T_x \frac{d^2y}{dx^2} = \lambda g \sqrt{1 + \left(\frac{dy}{dx}\right)^2}.$$
 Solving this differential equation, we find that $y(x)$ is a hyperbolic cosine, a caternary.

[19] Challenge Problems
Q: This time, instead of a massive string, consider a massive, zero length spring of mass $m$ and total spring constant $k$. The two ends are fixed at points a horizontal displacement $L$ from each other. What shape does the spring make?
A: Divide the hanging spring by $x$ coordinate into $N$ pieces of horizontal length $dx$, where $N$ is very large. Then, consider the $i$th piece which has spring constant $k_i$. If the $i$th piece has mass $m_i$, then it makes up $m_i/m$ of the spring, and thus has spring constant
 $$k_i = \frac{m}{m_i}k.$$
 But since the horizontal tension throughout must be the same, we have that for all $i$, the expression $$T_x = k_idx = \frac{mkdx}{m_i}$$ must be the same, so the $N$ pieces must all have the same mass $m_i = m/N$. Thus, we've shown that infinitesmial slices horizontally all have the same mass and same spring constant. So now, if we consider a point $x$ from the center, all the spring segments from the center to that point have total mass
 $$m_x = \frac{mx}{L}.$$
 Thus, at coordinate $x$ we must have $T_y = m_xg = \frac{mx}{L}g$. So, we can write
 $$T_y = \frac{kL}{dx}dy = \frac{mx}{L}g \implies \frac{dy}{dx} = \frac{mg}{kL^2}x.$$This is the differential equation for a $\boxed{\text{parabola!}}$

[20] Challenge Problems — Kleppner \& Kolenkow
Q: A device called a capstan is used aboard ships in order to control
a rope which is under great tension. The rope is wrapped around
a fixed drum, usually for several turns (the drawing shows about a
three-quarter turn). The load on the rope pulls it with a force $T_A$,
and the sailor holds it with a much smaller force $T_B$. Show that
$T_B = T_Ae^{-\mu \theta}$, where $\mu$ is the coefficient of friction and $\theta$ is the total angle subtended by the rope on the drum.
\begin{center}
 \includegraphics[width=3cm]{capstan.png}
\end{center}
A: Consider a segment $d\theta$ of the string. Then, considering radial forces on it, we get
 $$2T(d\theta/2) = N \implies N = Td\theta.$$
 Thus, the maximal frictional force at that point is $df = \mu Td \theta$, and the maximal frictional force is equal to the maximal change in tension, so we can write
 $$dT = \mu T d\theta \implies \frac{dT}{T} = \mu d\theta.$$
 Solving the differential equation, we get
 $$T(\theta) = T_0 e^{\mu \theta},$$
 and since $T_B < T_A$, we can write $T_A = T_Be^{\mu \theta}$, the desired result.
