LESSON: fma-12
------------------------------------------------------------------------------

[1] Concept checks
Q: Imagine you're drinking water out of a cup through a straw. What is the maximum theoretical length of the straw that allows you to suck the water up, even if you had a vacuum pump?
A: The largest possible pressure differential you can make is $P_0$, so this equals $\rho_wgh$. Thus $h=P_0/\rho_wg=\boxed{10.3 m}$.

[2] Concept checks
Q: A cup is filled on one half with a fluid of density $\rho_1$, and on the other half with a fluid of density $\rho_2>\rho_1$ as shown. Is the fluid stable in this position, or will it move? If it moves, how should it move? Assume the fluids do not mix.
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[scale=0.17]{cup}
 \end{center}
A: The fluid is <span style="border:1px solid #555;padding:.1em .4em;font-weight:600;">not stable</span>, because the greater density on the right means a greater pressure existing on the right, leading fluid parcels along the interface to move toward the left. After a long time, all the heavier fluid will end up on the bottom as to minimize the gravitational potential energy on the system.

[3] Concept checks
Q: Imagine enclosing the surface $S$ in Theorem (another problem) with a portion of area $A$ along the bottom to make a closed shell. Now, fill the shell with a fluid with pressure $P$. By using Newton's laws in the $z$ direction, explain why the total force in the $z$ direction on the surface $S$ has magnitude $PA$.
A: By Newton's third law, the total force exerted by the gas on the shell must be 0, so the shell cannot move. We can easily see that the force the gas exerts on the bottom of area $A$ is $PA.$ As a result, for the total force to be 0 on the entire shell, the force the gas exerts on the surface $S$ must also be $\boxed{PA}$.

[4] Concept checks
Q: A meniscus, which results from surface tension, is when water rises slightly at the contact point to a container as shown along a curved surface. If the atmospheric pressure is $P_0$, what is the pressure in the water at points $A$, $B$, and $C$? Hint: At an interface between two mediums, the pressure is only the same on both sides of the interface if the interface has no curvature. 
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[scale=0.15]{men}
 \end{center}
A: Using the hint, we know that since $B$ is on the interface of the water and air on a flat surface, the pressure is $\boxed{P_B=P_0}$ there. The pressures at $A$ and $C$ follow easily with $\boxed{P_A=P_0-\rho gh}$ and $\boxed{P_C=P_0+\rho gH}$.

[5] Concept checks
Q: As an extension of the previous problem, let a sphere of water have radius $r$ and surface tension $\gamma$. The force of surface tension is found as $\gamma \ell$, where $\ell$ is the length of the interface between two mediums. Find the pressure in the sphere as a function of $r$, $\gamma$, and $P_0$. Hint: Split the sphere in half and use force balance.
A: If we split the sphere in half, we consider the forces along the symmetry axis of the sphere. The net force due to pressure on the sphere is due to that acting on its hemispherical surface. There are two components to this -- the outward pressure from the water and the inward pressure from the air. Using Theorem (another problem), the net force is simply the pressure differential times the area of the projection of the hemisphere onto a flat surface, which is just a circle of radius $r$. Hence, the net force due to pressure is $(P-P_0)\pi r^2$. This net force is balanced by the force of surface tension, which acts along the circumference of the circular base of the sphere: $2\pi r\gamma$. Equating these two forces, we get $$(P-P_0)\pi r^2=2\pi r\gamma\Rightarrow P=\boxed{P_0+\frac{2\gamma}{r}}$$

[6] Concept checks
Q: Let's say you have a scale with some fixed relative uncertainty $\sigma$. Approximate the value of $\sigma$ such that if you measure your weight in the atmosphere versus in a vacuum, you'll be able to resolve the difference in weight using the scale. In other words, what is the minimum $\sigma$ to be able to say the difference in your measurements is statistically significant?
A: Since the human body is made mostly of water, we approximate your body density to be about 1000 times greater than air. Then, if your mass is $m$, your weighted as measured in a vacuum differs from your weight in air by an amount $(\rho_a/\rho_w)m\approx 0.001m$. The relative error must then be half of the factor $\rho_a/\rho_w$ for us to resolve the two measurements, so $\sigma\approx \boxed{0.0005}.$

[7] Concept checks — $F=ma$
Q: A U-tube manometer consists of a uniform diameter cylindrical tube that is bent into a U shape. It is originally filled with water. The total length of the column of water is $L$. Ignore surface tension and viscosity. Oil with a density half that of water is added to one side of the tube until the total length of oil is equal to the total length of water. Determine the equilibrium height difference between the two sides.
 \begin{enumerate}[label = (\Alph*)]
\item $L$
\item $L/2$
\item $L/3$
\item $3L/4$
\item $L/4$
\end{enumerate}
A: Let the length of water on the side of the tube with pure water be $h$ after the oil is added. The pressures at the bottom of each section of the tube must be the same so that the fluid parcels feel no force acting on them. This means that $$\rho gh=\frac\rho 2gL+\rho g(L-h)$$which solves to yield $h=3L/4$. The height of fluid in the other side is then $L+L-h=5L/4$, so the difference in height is $<strong>(B)</strong>$ $L/2$.

[8] Concept checks
Q: An aluminum ball of density $\rho$ and volume $V$ sinks in water of density $\rho_w$. \begin{anum}
 \item If the aluminum ball falls a distance $h$, what is the change in gravitational potential energy of the system?
 \item Imagine you don't know anything about buoyancy. Using part (a), find the net force acting on the aluminum ball as it sinks. 
 \item Use your result from (b) to derive the buoyancy force $F_B$ on the aluminum ball. 
 \end{anum}
A: \begin{anum}
 \item As the ball moves down, it moves into the location of a sphere of water at its final position. The sphere of water then moves up to fill in the ball's initial position. The movement of the ball downward results in a decrease $-\rho Vgh$ of potential energy, and the movement of the water upward results in an increase $\rho_w Vgh$ of potential energy. The net change in potential energy is $$\Delta U=\boxed{\rho_wVgh-\rho Vgh}$$
 \item The work done by the net force on the ball when it sinks a distance $h$ is equal to $Fh$, which equals the negative of the change in potential energy. Setting $-Fh=\Delta U$ yields $$F=\boxed{\rho Vg-\rho_wVg}$$
 \item The gravitational force is $\rho Vg$, so the buoyancy force is the other component of the force equation, $F_B=\boxed{\rho_wVg}$.
 \end{anum}

[9] Concept checks
Q: We define the <em>momentum density</em> $\rho_p$ of a fluid to be the momentum per unit volume of a fluid. For a fluid of density $\rho$ and a speed $v$ at some point, find $\rho_p$.
A: We have $$\rho_p=\frac{mv}{V}=\boxed{\rho v}$$

[10] Concept checks
Q: Water with pressure $P_1$ is flowing through a pipe of cross-sectional area $A_1$ at a speed $v_1$ before the pipe narrows into a cross-sectional area $A_2$ without loss of energy. Find the new pressure of the water $P_2$.
A: By Bernoulli's law, $$P_1+\frac12\rho v_1^2=P_2+\frac12\rho v_2^2$$By continuity, $v_2=(A_1/A_2)v_1$. Combining both equations, we get $$P_2=\boxed{P_1+\frac12\rho v_1^2\left(1-\frac{A_1^2}{A_2^2}\right)}$$

[11] Concept checks
Q: A hose with gauge pressure $P$, cross sectional area $A$, and water velocity $v$ is fed into a sprinkler that narrows the stream into a cross-sectional area $S$ as it spits out the water into the air. \begin{anum}\item Find the change in the kinetic energy per unit volume of the water between when it is in the hose and when it is just released into the air by the sprinkler.
 \item Find the work done by external forces per unit volume of the water between the same two points discussed in part (a).
 \item Find the energy lost to frictional forces per unit volume of water. 
 \item What must $P$ be so that the energy lost in part (c) equals 0? You'll see that this matches Bernoulli's law.
 \end{anum}
A: \begin{anum}
 \item By mass continuity, the speed $u$ of the water when it exits the sprinkler is $u=(A/S)v$. Per unit volume, the change in energy is thus $$\Delta K=\frac12\rho u^2-\frac12\rho v^2=\boxed{\frac12\rho v^2\left(\frac{A^2}{S^2}-1\right)}$$
 \item The work comes from the gauge pressure $P$. The force produced by any pressure is $PA$, so the work produced by the pressure is $W=PA\Delta x=P\Delta V$. Thus, per unit volume the work done is simply $$W=\boxed{P}$$
 \item The work from part (b) contributes to both energy loss and the increase in kinetic energy of the fluid, so $$E_{\text{lost}}=W-\Delta K=\boxed{P-\frac12\rho v^2\left(\frac{A^2}{S^2}-1\right)}$$
 \item For teh energy lost to equal 0, we require $$P=\boxed{\frac12\rho v^2\left(\frac{A^2}{S^2}-1\right)}$$which upon some rearranging matches the form of Bernoulli's law: $$P+\frac12 \rho v^2=\frac12\rho u^2$$
\end{anum}

[12] Exercises
Q: A classic paradox in fluid statics is descrbibed as follows. I fill three cups with the same base area $A$ with the same volume $V$ of water. The water has density $\rho$. The walls of the three cups are slanted at different angles, however, so that the water levels in each cup end up being different.
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[scale=0.2]{wearofi}
 \end{center}
 The three cups are weighed on scales. Alice says they should have the same weight, since they contain the same amount of water. However, Bob argues that since the heights of the water levels in each cup are different, their pressures at the base are different. This should result in a larger force on the bottom of the middle cup than the one on the right, leading to different scale readings. Who is right, and what is wrong with the other person's argument?
A: <span style="border:1px solid #555;padding:.1em .4em;font-weight:600;">Alice is correct</span>. The issue with Bob's logic is that he forgot the force that the water exerts on the walls of the cup. If the walls are not slanted, the force exerted on the walls is perfectly horizontal. If the walls are slanted inward like in the middle cup, the water exerts an upward force on the cup that perfectly balances out the increased downward force on the base of the cup. If the walls are slanted outward like in the right cup, the water exerts an additional downward force along with the force on the base. As a result, the total force exerted on the scale by the cup is the same in all cases.

[13] Exercises — $F=ma$
Q: A car is turning left along a circular track of radius $r$ at a constant speed $v$. A cylindrical beaker is placed vertically inside the car. The beaker has a small hole on its right side. If the water's highest point in the beaker is a height $h$ above the hole, at what instantaneous speed does the water escape the hole, from a passenger's perspective?
A: As the car accelerates, we consider it as an accelerated frame of reference, where gravity now gains a component $a=v^2/r$ to the right as shown: \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[scale=0.2]{water}
 \end{center}
 Now, $g'=\sqrt{g^2+a^2}$ by the Pythagorean theorem. Everything we know about fluids still holds in this new frame with the adjusted $g'$, so we simply need to find the new height $h'$ of the opening under the water surface. By similar triangles, it holds that $h'=(g/g')h$. Then, the speed of the water coming out is just $$u=\sqrt{2g'h'}=\boxed{\sqrt{2gh}}$$

[14] Exercises — $F=ma$
Q: Water with total mass $M$ is poured into a cup of cross-sectional area $A$. A block of mass $m$, whose density is half that of water, is tied to a thin string. The string is attached to the bottom of the cup, and the block floats in the water as shown. The atmospheric pressure is $P_{\text{atm}}$. What is the total pressure force that the water exerts on the bottom of the cup?
\begin{center}
 \includegraphics[scale=0.2]{string}
\end{center}
\begin{enumerate}[label = (\Alph*)]
\item $Mg$
\item $(M+m)g$
\item $P_{\text{atm}}A+Mg$
\item $P_{\text{atm}}A+(M+m)g$
\item $P_{\text{atm}}A+(M+2m)g$
\end{enumerate}
A: The pressure force only depends on the height of the water, so it would equal the weight of the water in the container if we replaced the block with water. The block is half as dense as water, so the total mass of water would be $(M+2m)g$. We still need to include the force from the atmosphere, so we get <strong>(E)</strong> $P_{\text{atm}}A+(M+2m)g$. \\

 We can also find the answer from force balance. The net force acting on the bottom of the container is $P_{\text{atm}}A+(M+m)g$. The tension in the string keeps the block in equilibrium, and exerts a force $mg$ downward on the block to balance the buoyant force of $2mg$. This tension thus creates an upward force of $mg$ ont he base of the container. Since the total force equals the tension plus the pressure force, we again get <strong>(E)</strong> $P_{\text{atm}}A+(M+2m)g$.

[15] Exercises — $F=ma$
Q: We've seen this problem before, but now solve it using your knowledge of fluids. A cylindrical bucket of negligible mass has radius $R$ and height $h$, and is open at the top. It is submerged in water of density $\rho$, with its top a distance $H$ below the surface. How much work is needed to pull the bucket slowly up so that its bottom is just above the lake surface?
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[scale=0.2]{bucket}
 \end{center}
 \begin{enumerate}[label = (\Alph*)]
\item $\rho g\pi R^2h(H-h)$
\item $\rho g\pi R^2h^2$
\item $\rho g\pi R^2(H-h)^2$
\item $\rho g\pi R^2h^2/2$
\item $\rho g\pi R^2h(H-h)/2$
\end{enumerate}
A: Because of buoyancy, the net force on the bucket while it's fully submerged is 0. As the bucket begins to rise out of the water, the force linearly increases until the entire bucket is out of the water, at which point the force is $F=\rho g\pi R^2h$. The average force exerted on the bucket during this process is thus $\bar F=\rho g\pi R^2h/2$, and the total work is then $\bar Fh=\rho g\pi R^2h^2/2$, answer choice <strong>(D)</strong>.

[16] Exercises
Q: A triangular trough has faces that are equilateral triangles of side length $a$ and a length $L$. A farmer cuts the trough in half and tapes the halves together as shown. If the trough is now filled with fluid of density $\rho$, find the total horizontal force $F$ exerted by the tape between the two halves. 
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[scale=0.17]{tough2}
 \end{center}
A: The total weight of the water is $$F_g=\rho La^2g\frac{\sqrt 3}{4}$$This weight is due to the vertical component of pressure acting on each half of the trough, so we conclude that the vertical component of force acting on each half of the trough is $F_y=F_g/2$. Since the pressure force is directed perpendicular to the faces of the trough, the horizontal component force acting on each half of the trough is related to the vertical force by $F_y/F_x=\tan (30^\circ)$. As a result, $$F_x=\frac{F_y}{\tan(30^\circ)}=\frac{F_g\sqrt3}{2}=\boxed{\frac{3}{8}\rho La^2g}$$By force balance, this force is exactly equal to the force exerted by the tape.

[17] Exercises
Q: A boat of mass $M$, rim height $H$, base area $A$, and negligible volume is placed in a lake with water of density $\rho$ as shown. 
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[scale=0.15]{boat}
 \end{center}
 A hole is poked in the bottom of the boat with cross sectional area $S$ and water starts flowing in. Assume the boat sinks very slowly.
 \begin{anum}
 \item Find the initial speed $v_0$ of the water flowing into the boat.
 \item What volume of water is in the boat when it is about to capsize?
 \item Find the amount of time it takes the boat to sink.
 \end{anum}
A: \begin{anum}
 \item By force balance the depth of the boat's base $h$ underwater must displace a mass of water equal to $M$, so we have $M=\rho Ah$. By Bernoulli's law, $$v_0=\sqrt{2gh}=\boxed{\sqrt{\frac{2gM}{\rho A}}}$$
 \item The weight of water displaced by the ``air" portion of the boat must always stay the same to balance the force of the boat (note that the water in the boat feels no effective force, since its weight is equal to the water it displaces), so when the boat is about to capsize it's filled with a height $H-h$ of water. Thus, $$V=A(H-h)=\boxed{AH-\frac M\rho}.$$
 \item Notice that the difference in pressure between just outside the hole and just inside will always be $\rho g(H-h)$, so by Bernoulli's law the velocity of water flow into the boat is always $v=\sqrt{2g(H-h)}$. The rate of volume increase of water in the boat is thus $vS$, so using the result from (b) the time it takes for the boat to sink is $$t=\frac{V}{vS}=\frac{A(H-h)}{S\sqrt{2g(H-h)}}=\boxed{\frac{A}{S}\sqrt{\frac{H-M/A\rho}{2g}}}$$
 \end{anum}

[18] Exercises — NBPhO
Q: A box of volume $V$ contains gas molecules with mass $m$ and number density $n$ (i.e. there are $n$ molecules per unit volume) at a very low temperature (this means the gas particles are nearly completely still relative to the box). The box originally moves with a constant speed $v$, and suddenly stops. Find the pressure $P$ on the front face of the box as soon as the box stops moving.
A: Let $A$ be the area of the front face of the box. When the box stops moving, the gas molecules keep moving with speed $v$. In a small period of time $\Delta t$, there will be $N=nAv\Delta t$ molecules colliding with the wall. Each of these molecules will exert an impulse $\Delta p=2mv$ as they bounce off elastically. The total impulse is thus $N\Delta p$, and the force on the front face will be $$F=\frac{N\Delta p}{\Delta t}=\frac{2nmAv^2\Delta t}{\Delta t}=2nmAv^2$$The pressure is then $$P=\frac{F}{A}=\boxed{2nmv^2}$$

[19] Exercises — Kalda
Q: A hemispherical container is placed upside down on a smooth horizontal surface. Through a small hole at the bottom of the container, water is then poured in. Exactly when the container gets full, water starts leaking from between the table and the edge of the container. Find the mass $M$ of the container if water has density $\rho$ and radius of the hemisphere is $R$.
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[scale=0.17]{kalda}
 \end{center}
A: When the container is full, the water level is at a height $R$ so the pressure along the bottom of the container is $P=\rho gR$. The total force exerted by the water on the ground is thus $F=P\pi R^2=\pi \rho gR^3$. By Newton's third law, this must be the total normal force exerted upward on the system by the ground. For the container to just begin lifting up at this point, the normal force must equal the combined mass of the water and the container. As a result, $$\pi \rho gR^3=\frac23\pi\rho gR^3+M\Rightarrow M=\boxed{\frac13\pi\rho gR^3}$$

[20] Exercises
Q: A cylindrical cup of cross-section area $A$ is filled up with water to a height $h$. A cylindrical weight of mass $m$ with cross-section area $A$ that fits exactly in the cup is placed horizontally on top of the water. No water can leak out, so the weight stays on top of the water regardless of its mass. \begin{anum}
 \item Find the increase in pressure $\Delta P$ at all points in the water. 
 \item For most of our discussion on fluids, we've assumed the fluids are incompressible. However, water of an initial volume $V_0$ does compress by a small amount when experiencing an external pressure $\Delta P$, by an amount $\Delta V$ given by a constant known as the bulk modulus $B$: $$\Delta V=-\frac{B}{V_0}\Delta P$$
 When the weight $m$ is placed on top of the water, the water level will not decrease if we assume the water is incompressible. However, taking compressibility into account, find the decrease $\Delta h$ in the water level as a function of $A$, $h$, $m$, and $B$. 
 \item Now assume the weight is dropped from a height $H$ directly above the water level. Assume still that the water is compressible. When the weight lands on the water, it is slowed to a stop by the water. What is the largest decrease in water level $\Delta h$ over the course of the motion? Neglect frictional forces.
 \end{anum}
A: \begin{anum}
 \item By force balance, the new pressure at the top of the water level should support the weight of the weight. Let this new pressure be $P_0+\Delta P$. By force balance on the weight, $$(P_0+\Delta P-P_0)A=mg\Rightarrow \Delta P=\boxed{\frac{mg}{A}}$$
 \item Because of the increase in pressure $\Delta P$, the water's volume decreases and so the water level decreases. We use the formula stated in the problem to find the change in the volume: $$\Delta V=-\frac{B}{V_0}\Delta P=-\frac{Bmg}{A^2h}$$Then, since $\Delta V=A\Delta h$, we conclude that $$\Delta h=\boxed{-\frac{Bmg}{A^3h}}$$
 \item Notice that $\Delta h$ is linearly proportional to $mg$, which is identical to what would happen if the mass were suspended from a spring. We thus consider the water as a spring, with effective spring constant equal to $k_{eq}=-mg/\Delta h=A^3h/B$. Then, when the weight is dropped, the initial gravitational potential energy eventually becomes the ``spring energy" of the water. The initial potential energy is about equal to $mgH$, since the compression of the water is very small. We therefore set $$\frac12 k_{eq}(\Delta h)^2=mgH\Rightarrow \Delta h=\boxed{\sqrt{\frac{2BmgH}{A^3h}}}$$
 \end{anum}

[21] Challenge Problems — PPP
Q: An open-topped vertical tube of diameter $D$ is filled with water up to a height $h$. The narrow bottom-end of the tube, of diameter $d$, is closed by a stop as shown in the figure.
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[scale=0.2]{spill}
 \end{center}
 When the stop is removed, the water starts flowing out through the bottom orifice with approximate speed $v= \sqrt{2gh}$, by Bernoulli's law. However, this speed is reached by the liquid only after a certain time $\tau$. Ignore viscous effects. \begin{anum}
 \item What is the acceleration $a$ of the lowest layer of water at the moment when the stop is removed? 
 \item Obtain an estimate of the order of magnitude of $\tau$.
 \end{anum}
A: \begin{anum}
 \item After the stop is removed, in a small time $\Delta t$ let the lowest layer of water fall a distance $\Delta x$ to obtain a speed $\Delta v$. Then, $a$ can be found via the kinematic equation $a=(\Delta v)^2/2\Delta x$. If the lowest layer falls a distance $\Delta x$, we've essentially removed a thin layer of water of volume $\Delta V=\pi d^2\Delta x/4$ from the top and moved it to the bottom. The change in potential energy of the system because of this movement is thus $$\Delta U=\rho \Delta Vgh=\frac{\pi}{4}\rho ghd^2\Delta x$$This potential energy equals the increase in kinetic energy of the water. By mass continuity, most of the water will be moving at a speed $\Delta v(d^2/D^2)$, and so we consider this as the bulk of the kinetic energy: $$\Delta K=\frac12 \frac\pi 4\rho D^2h(\Delta v)^2\frac{d^4}{D^4}=\frac12 \frac\pi 4\rho h(\Delta v)^2\frac{d^4}{D^2}$$Equating this to $\Delta U$, we get $$\frac{\pi}{4}\rho ghd^2\Delta x=\frac12 \frac\pi 4\rho h(\Delta v)^2\frac{d^4}{D^2}\Rightarrow a=\frac{(\Delta v)^2}{2\Delta x}=\boxed{g\frac{D^2}{d^2}}$$
 \item This acceleration is large if $D\gg d$, so we approximate $$\tau=\frac{\sqrt{2gh}}{a}=\boxed{\frac{d^2\sqrt{2gh}}{gD^2}}$$
 \end{anum}

[22] Challenge Problems — Kalda
Q: If a beam with square cross-section and very low density is placed in water, it will sit in the water with one pair of its long opposite faces horizontal. This orientation, however, becomes unstable as we increase its density. Find the critical density when this transition occurs, in terms of the density of water $\rho$.
A: See solution 15 at <a href="https://physoly.tech/static/files/KaldaMech-121.pdf" target="_blank" rel="noopener">this link</a>.

------------------------------------------------------------------------------
LESSON: fma-13
------------------------------------------------------------------------------

[1] Concept checks
Q: Space explorers have found a planet covered with loose rock on its surface. They want to create a space station in a geostationary orbit around this planet. Is it possible for this to be done, regardless of the planet's rotation speed?
A: <span style="border:1px solid #555;padding:.1em .4em;font-weight:600;">Yes</span>. Notice that a planet can only hold itself together if particles on its surface are below the first cosmic speed, i.e. otherwise they will be in orbit and be flung off of the planet. Hence, the maximum possible spin speed of a planet is such that particles at its surface are barely in orbit, thereby making it possible for a satellite to be in geostationary orbit right on the surface.

[2] Concept checks — $F=ma$
Q: Kepler’s Laws state that\begin{enumerate}[label=(\alph*)]
 \item the orbits of planets are elliptical with one focus at the sun
 \item a line connecting the sun and a planet sweeps out equal areas in equal times
 \item the square the period of a planet’s orbit is proportional to the cube of its semimajor
axis
\end{enumerate}
Which of these three laws would remain true if the force of gravity were proportional to $1/r^3$ rather than $1/r^2$? More than one can be true.
A: <span style="border:1px solid #555;padding:.1em .4em;font-weight:600;">Only condition (b)</span>. Note that if the force of gravity were stronger or weaker depending on a given distance, the path of a planet would deviate from an ellipse, so (a) would not hold true. Condition (b) results from conservation of angular momentum, so it will still hold true. Condition (c) is false along the same logic as condition (a).

[3] Concept checks — $F=ma$
Q: Five astronauts are orbiting Earth in a low circular orbit. They decided to go around the planet following
the same orbit, but much faster, to set a new record for orbiting Earth. Four of them thought that they
could quickly increase the velocity, then use a jet engine to maintain an orbit of the same shape; in the
first four choices below, the red arrow denotes the direction that fuel will flow out of the jet nozzle. The
fifth astronaut was skeptical about such a possibility. Who is right? \\
\includegraphics[width=\linewidth]{fma1.png}
A: Answer <strong>(B)</strong>. If we travel at a faster speed, we will need additional centripetal acceleration provided by thrust directed out toward space.

[4] Concept checks
Q: Every so often, the ISS must fire its thrusters to avoid falling into the atmosphere. In which way should the thrusters on the ISS point (i.e. direction in which exhaust is fired) as to most effectively increase the maximum height of its orbit?\begin{enumerate}[label=(\Alph*)]
 \item Toward the Earth
 \item Opposite to the Earth
 \item In the direction of motion
 \item Opposite the direction of motion
 \item It depends on the dimensions of the orbit
 \end{enumerate}
A: Answer <strong>(D)</strong>. Since a higher energy correlates with a larger semi-major axis length, we need to maximize our increase in kinetic energy from a given impulse. Thus, our gain in speed should point in the direction of motion, i.e. our thrust should point in the opposite direction.

[5] Concept checks — $F=ma$
Q: A satellite is following an elliptical orbit around the Earth. Its engines are capable of providing a one-time
impulse of a fixed magnitude. In order to maximize the energy of the satellite, the impulse should be
\begin{center}
\includegraphics[width = 0.5\textwidth]{oweinw}
\end{center}
\begin{enumerate}[label=(\Alph*)]
 \item directed along the satellite’s velocity and applied when the satellite is in its perigee.
 \item directed along the satellite’s velocity and applied when the satellite is in apogee.
 \item directed toward the Earth and applied when the satellite is in perigee.
 \item directed toward the Earth and applied when the satellite is in apogee.
 \item directed away from the Earth and applied when the satellite is in apogee.
 \end{enumerate}
A: Answer <strong>(A)</strong>. From the solution to the previous problem, the thrust should be in the direction of motion, leaving only options (A) and (B) left. Let's say the satellite gains a velocity of $\Delta v$ when its current velocity is $v$. Then, its gain in kinetic energy is $$\Delta K =\frac12 m(v+\Delta v)^2-\frac12 mv^2=mv\Delta v +\frac12 m\Delta v^2.$$The gain in kinetic energy is larger with a greater current velocity $v$, so the impulse should be applied when the satellite is moving fastest, i.e. at perigee.

[6] Concept checks
Q: A satellite in an orbit of radius $r$ about a planet of mass $M$ experiences a drag force equal to $F_d=-cv$, where $c$ is a constant and $v$ is the speed of the satellite. Assume $c$ is small. After one full rotation around the planet, by how much has the satellite's orbit radius changed?
A: The drag force is relatively constant in one orbit, since $c$ is small so that $v$ doesn't change significantly. Using the first cosmic speed, we conclude $F_d=-c\sqrt{GM/r}$. The work done by this force is equal to the force times the circumference of the orbit, so the decrease in energy of the satellite is $$\Delta E=-2\pi c\sqrt{GMr}$$Notice that the total energy is related to the orbital radius by $E=-GMm/r$. Let $\delta r$ be the small difference in orbital radius. Then, we conclude that $$\Delta E=-\frac{GMm}{2(r-\delta r)}+\frac{GMm}{2r}=GMm\left(\frac{r-\delta r - r}{2r(r-\delta r)}\right)\approx -\frac{GMm}{2r^2}\delta r$$An equivalent analysis can be done via taking the derivative. Relating this to the $\Delta E$ found earlier, we conclude $$\delta r=\boxed{\frac{4\pi c}{m}\sqrt{\frac{r^5}{GM}}}$$

[7] Exercises
Q: The supermassive black hole at the center of the Milky Way is called Sagittarius A* with a mass of $8.26\times 10^{36}$ kg. It takes 230 million years for the solar system to orbit once around the galaxy. \begin{enumerate}[label=(\alph*)] \item Assuming the only other mass in the Milky Way is Sagittarius A*, find the distance of the solar system from the galactic center. \item Compare your value to the current accepted distance of $2.44\times 10^{20}$ m. Given the approximation we made, does your value make sense?\end{enumerate}
A: \begin{enumerate}[label=(\alph*)] \item The distance is $$R=\sqrt[3]{\frac{GMT^2}{4\pi^2}}=\boxed{9.02\times 10^{18} \text{m}}$$ \item <span style="border:1px solid #555;padding:.1em .4em;font-weight:600;">Yes</span>. In the solution above, $R$ increases with $M$. Since we drastically underestimated $M$, we should expect to find a significantly smaller value than reality.
\end{enumerate}

[8] Exercises — $F=ma$
Q: A spherical cloud of dust in space has a uniform density $\rho_0$ and a radius $R_0$. The gravitational
acceleration of free fall at the surface of the cloud due to the mass of the cloud is $g_0$.
A process occurs (heat expansion) that causes the cloud to suddenly grow to a radius $2R_0$, while
maintaining a uniform (but not constant) density. What is the new gravitational acceleration of free fall at a
point $R_0$ away from the center of the cloud?
A: The gravity at a given point is only due to the dust contained within a sphere enclosed below that point. Thus, for gas of density $\rho$ at a distance $R$, the gravitational acceleration is $a=(4/3)\pi G\rho R $, i.e. proportional to $\rho$. If the gas cloud expands to twice the radius, its density must have reduced by a factor of 8. Hence, the new gravitational acceleration is $g=\boxed{g_0/8}$.

[9] Exercises
Q: The $\Delta v$ in spaceflight is defined as the magnitude of the change in velocity needed during a particular maneuver in space. \begin{enumerate}[label=(\alph*)] \item What is the $\Delta v$ needed to escape Earth's gravitational pull from low-Earth orbit? \item What is the $\Delta v$ needed to reach Mars from low-Earth orbit? \end{enumerate}
A: \begin{enumerate}[label=(\alph*)] 
\item Using the first and second cosmic speeds, $$\Delta v=\sqrt{\frac{2GM_E}{R_E}}-\sqrt{\frac{GM_E}{R_E}}=\boxed{3.3 \times 10^3 \text{m}/\text{s}}$$
\item In Earth's reference frame, we first need to escape Earth's gravitational pull, i.e. the first portion requires a $\Delta v$ as calculated above. Then, in this frame, we will be at rest far away from the Earth. Now we need to consider the trans-Mars $\Delta v$. In the solar system's frame, we're cruising at the Earth's orbital speed around the Sun, which we can find by $v_1=\sqrt{GM_s/r_E}=2.97 \times 10^4 \text{m}/\text{s}$. To reach the orbit of Mars, we need to extend our orbit so that its apogee is tangent to Mars' orbit. This gives us a semi-major axis length of $a=(r_E+r_M)/2=1.89 \times 10^{11} \text{m}.$ Using energy, the velocity $v_2$ we must have when leaving the Earth's orbit is $$\frac12 v_2^2=-\frac{GM_s}{2a}+\frac{GM_s}{r_E} \Rightarrow v_2=3.27\times 10^4 \text{m}/\text{s}.$$ This leaves a $\Delta v$ for the second maneuver to be $v_2-v_1=3.0 \times 10^{3} \text{m}/\text{s}.$ We add this to the first $\Delta v$ we calculated to get a total $\Delta v = \boxed{6.3\times 10^3 \text{m}/\text{s}}.$
\end{enumerate}

[10] Exercises
Q: Imagine that at a given point in time, the Earth, Mars, and Sun lie on the same line (collinear). How many days will it be before they are all collinear again?
A: Let the Earth rotate counterclockwise around the Sun at an angular velocity $\omega_E$ and Mars counterclockwise at $\omega_M$. In the frame rotating around with the Earth, Mars moves clockwise at an angular velocity of magnitude $\omega=\omega_E-\omega_M$. The time we desire is $t=\pi/\omega.$ By Kepler's Third Law, we can find $\omega_M=\omega_E(r_E/r_M)^{3/2}=0.534\omega_E.$ Plugging this in, $t=6.736/\omega_E.$ Note one year is $T=2\pi/\omega_E=6.283/\omega_E,$ so $t=1.072 \text{years} =\boxed{391 days}$.

[11] Exercises
Q: If the Sun suddenly doubled in mass, what would be the new perigee and apogee of the Earth's orbit?
A: The stronger gravitaitonal pull from the Sun will reduce the semi-major axis of the orbit, which means the perigee will be lowered. The <span style="border:1px solid #555;padding:.1em .4em;font-weight:600;">apogee</span> will remain at $\boxed{r_E}$. To find the perigee, we can find the total energy and thus smei-major axis length \begin{align*}\frac{1}{2}v_0^2-\frac{G(2M_S)}{r_E}&=-\frac{G(2M_S)}{2a}\\
\frac{GM_S}{2r_E}-\frac{2GM_S}{r_E}&=-\frac{GM_S}{a}\\
-\frac{3}{2r_E}&=-\frac{1}{a}\\
\frac{2r_E}{3}&=a\end{align*}The sum of the perigee and apogee distance must equal $2a=4r_E/3,$ so the <span style="border:1px solid #555;padding:.1em .4em;font-weight:600;">perigee</span> distance is $\boxed{r_E/3}.$

[12] Exercises
Q: Imagine a well is dug down to the center of the Earth. A mass $m$ is placed a depth $d$ into the well, or a distance $R=R_E-d$ from the center of the Earth. What is the weight of the object at this point?
A: Assuming the Earth is spherical, a fraction $R^3/R_E^3$ of the mass of the Earth is contained below the point in question. So, the weight must be $$F=\frac{GMm(R^3/R_E^3)}{R^2}=\boxed{\frac{GMm}{R_E^3}R}$$

[13] Exercises
Q: A spherical planet has a mass density $\rho(r)=c r^\alpha$ that only varies on the radius from the planet's center $r$. What should $\alpha$ be so that the gravitational acceleration due to the planet is constant at all points in a tunnel dug to the planet's center?
A: I'll introduce two methods: one that doesn't need calculus and another that uses calculus to get the answer quickly. First, without calculus. If $g$ is the same at all points, then when we compare $g$ at a point at distance $r$ and a point at distance $r+\delta r$ from the center, we have $$\frac{M}{r^2}=\frac{M+4\pi \rho r^2}{(r+\delta r)^2}$$where $M$ is the mass contained in the distance $r$. Cross-multiplying, expanding, and neglecting the $\delta r^2$ term, we then get $$M(r^2+2r\delta r)=(M+4\pi \rho r^2)r^2\Rightarrow M=2\pi \rho r^3$$Notice that $M/r^2$ is a constant, so $\rho \propto 1/r$, and $\boxed{\alpha=-1}$. \\

 With calculus, notice that $M\propto r^2$. By integration, $$M=\int 4\pi\rho r^2 dr$$ For $M\propto r^2$, we need the integrand $\propto r$, so $\rho\propto r^{-1}$.

[14] Exercises
Q: You stand on a planet with radius $R$ made entirely of iron with density $\rho_1$. The acceleration due to gravity at the surface is $g$. Because of collisions from asteroids made of an unknown metal, the planet's surface is eventually covered a uniform metal layer of density $\rho_2$ and thickness $\Delta R \ll R$. What must $\rho_2$ be so that the acceleration due to gravity is still $g$?
A: Because the distribution of mass is spherically symmetric, we simply consider the total mass underneath the surface in each case. Because $\Delta R \ll R$, we can approximate the volume of the metallic layer as $4\pi R^2 \Delta R$ and neglect values of order $\Delta R^2.$ Setting the accelerations due to gravity equal, \begin{align*}
\frac{G}{R^2}\left(\frac{4}{3}\pi R^3 \rho_1 \right)&=\frac{G}{(R+\Delta R)^2}\left(\frac{4}{3}\pi R^3 \rho_1 + 4\pi R^2 \Delta R \rho_2 \right)\\
R\rho_1 &= \frac{1}{R^2+2R\Delta R}(R^3\rho_1 + 3R^2\Delta R \rho_2)\\
R^3\rho_1 +2R^2 \Delta R \rho_1 &=R^3\rho_1 + 3R^2\Delta R \rho_2\\
2\rho_1&=3\rho_2\\
\boxed{\frac{2}{3}\rho_1}&=\rho_2
\end{align*}

[15] Exercises
Q: On Planet Bong of mass $M$ and radius $R$, the creatures have dug out a spherical cavern of radius $R/2$ as shown. $M$ was the mass of the planet before the cavern was dug. \begin{center}
\includegraphics[width=0.2\linewidth]{sphere.png}
 \end{center}\begin{enumerate}[label=(\alph*)] \item What is the acceleration due to gravity at the top of the cavern? \item What is the acceleration at the bottom? \item Someone lost their car keys, which are currently sitting at the bottom of the cavern. How fast must a miner at the bottom of the cavern need to throw the keys upward so that the car owner at the top can catch them?\end{enumerate}
A: \begin{enumerate}[label=(\alph*)] \item We can imagine the cavern to be made of negative mass superimposed onto the rest of the planet. The acceleration at the top is therefore $$g_1=\frac{GM}{R^2}-\frac{G(M/8)}{(R/2)^2}=\boxed{\frac{GM}{2R^2}}$$
\item At the bottom, we are at the center of the planet so there is no gravitational force from the positive mass sphere. The only acceleration is from the negative mass. $$g_2=\boxed{\frac{GM}{2R^2}}$$
\item From energy considerations, the change in gravitational potential energy results in the necessary kinetic energy. Note that relative to the negative mass sphere, the keys are at the same point between the top and the bottom of the cavern. Hence, we can ignore its presence. There are many ways to find the change in gravitational potential energy from the top to the bottom, such as integrating the force from the top to bottom. Here we will introduce a trickier way. Notice that at a radius $r$ from the center, the force on an object is proportional to $r^3/r^2=r$ -- the numerator coming from the $r^3$ dependence of mass underneath the point in question, and $r^2$ coming from the gravitational force equation. Thus, the motion is simple harmonic with $\omega=\sqrt{GM/R^3}$. Since the necessary amplitude of the motion described here is $A=R$, we find that $$v=A\omega =R\omega = \boxed{\sqrt{\frac{GM}{R}}}.$$
\end{enumerate}

[16] Exercises — PPP
Q: Imagine the gravitational force is repulsive in a certain universe. Let square $ABCD$ in this universe have side length $a$. Two masses of mass $M$ are held in place at points $A$ and $C$, while two masses of mass $m \ll M$ are held in place at points $B$ and $D$. All masses are released. What are the final speeds of all the masses?
A: By symmetry, the final speeds of the smaller masses will equal the final speeds of the larger ones. Note that since the smaller masses are so light, they shoot off infinitely far away before the larger masses have significant time to move. As a result, we can assume that when the smaller masses are infinitely far away, the larger masses are roughly where they started. The change in gravitational potential energy between this arrangement and the initial arrangement is $$\Delta U_1=4\frac{GMm}{a^2}+\frac{Gm^2}{2a^2}\approx 4\frac{GMm}{a^2}.$$This energy goes into the final kinetic energies of the two smaller masses, so each has a velocity $$2\cdot \frac{1}{2}mv_\text{small}^2=\frac{4GMm}{a^2}\Rightarrow \boxed{v_\text{small}=2\sqrt{\frac{GM}{a^2}}}.$$Then, as the larger masses begin to move apart, their change in gravitational potential energy is simply $\Delta U_2=GM^2/2a^2$, so each has a final velocity $$2\cdot \frac{1}{2}Mv_\text{large}^2=\frac{GM^2}{2a^2}\Rightarrow \boxed{v_\text{large}=\sqrt{\frac{GM}{2a^2}}}.$$

[17] Exercises
Q: Revisit the situation in Problem Problem 6, but now let the drag force be $F_d=-cv^\alpha$, where $\alpha$ is some number. Assume $c$ stays the same value for the entire atmosphere. \begin{anum}
 \item For what $\alpha$ does the radius of the orbit decrease by the same amount for every completed orbit?
 \item Given $\alpha$, how many orbits will the satellite complete before it crashes into the Earth's surface? Let the radius of the earth be $R$. 
 \end{anum}
A: \begin{anum}
 \item Via the same analysis as Problem Problem 6, $F_d\propto r^{-\alpha/2}$ so that $\Delta E\propto r^{-\alpha/2+1}$. Then, $\delta r\propto r^{-\alpha/2 +3}$. For $\delta r$ to have no dependence on $r$, we thus require $\boxed{\alpha=6}$. 
 \item Given $\alpha$, it follows that $F_d=-c(GM/r)^3$, so that using the same analysis as before, $\delta r=4\pi cG^2M^2/m$, so that the number of orbits $N$ is $$N=\frac{r-R}{\delta r}=\boxed{\frac{m(r-R)}{4\pi cG^2M^2}}$$
 \end{anum}

[18] Challenge Problems
Q: A projectile is launched vertically off of a planet of radius $R$. The object reaches a maximum height $R$ above the surface. Using Kepler's second law, find the time it takes for the object to land back on the ground. Hint: the area of an ellipse with axes $a$ and $b$ is $\pi ab$.
A: We can imagine the orbit to be a stretched ellipse with semimajor axis length $R$, so that by Kepler's Third Law the period is $$T=2\pi \sqrt{\frac{R^3}{GM}}$$ By the Second Law, the projectile sweeps out an area of the orbit proportional to time. Thus, the fraction of the ``orbit" we are considering with the projectile above the Earth is the same fraction of the area to which it sweeps out. Upon examination, the area it sweeps out is a quarter of the ellipse with a triangle of height $R$ and base area $b$ (we are assuming $b$ to be very small). Thus, the fraction of the area of the whole ellipse is thus $$f=\frac{Rb/2+\pi Rb/4}{\pi Rb}=\frac{2+\pi}{4\pi}$$and so the time in the air is $$t=\frac{2+\pi}{4\pi}T=\boxed{\left(1+\frac{\pi}{2}\right)\sqrt{\frac{R^3}{GM}}}$$

[19] Challenge Problems — GPhO
Q: An astronaut in geostationary orbit around the Earth wants to hit his own spaceship with a bullet fired from a rifle equipped onto the spaceship. \begin{enumerate}[label=(\alph*)]\item Find the speed of the astronaut in geostationary orbit. \item The speed of the bullet leaving the rifle is $u_0$ = 1200 m/s and the bullet’s velocity lies on the orbital plane. Under which angle with respect to the vector pointing towards the center of the Earth does he needs to aim the rifle if he wants to hit the spaceship within the next 40 hours? You don’t need to prove that there is only one suitable shooting angle. \item The astronaut also tries out another rifle the bullet speed of which can be freely adjusted from zero to the maximal speed $u_m$ = 300 m/s. With this rifle, he aims strictly along the motion of the spaceship. What is the bullet’s smallest possible travel time until hitting the spaceship?\end{enumerate}
A: \begin{enumerate}[label=(\alph*)]\item The geostationary orbit has radius $R=4.21 \times 10^7$ m, so the geostationary speed is $v=\sqrt{GM/R}=\boxed{3100 m/s}$. \item We can hit the spaceship in an orbital period of 24 hours if we fire the bullet so it has the same speed (in the Earth's frame). Hence, we have the following figure of the necessary velocity vectors:
\begin{center}
\includegraphics[width = 0.25\linewidth]{hi}
\end{center}From geometry, the angle is $\theta = \sin^{-1}(600/3100)=\boxed{11.2^\circ}.$
\item Essentially, we can freely adjust the orbital period from $T_0=24$ hours up to the maximal given by $u_m$. If we fire it at this speed, our equation for energy is (without the mass of the bullet) $$-\frac{GM}{2a}=\frac{1}{2}(v+u_m)^2-\frac{GM}{R^2}$$Plugging our result for $a$ into $T=2\pi \sqrt{a^3/GM} =1.41 T_0$. By inspection, firing so that our period is $T=(4/3)T_0$ leads us to the shortest travel time. In this case, the spaceship will revolve around the Earth 4 times (and the bullet 3 times) for a total time of $t=\boxed{96 hours}$.
\end{enumerate}

[20] Challenge Problems — NBPhO
Q: A comet’s orbit intersects the Earth’s orbit at an angle $\alpha = 45^\circ$. The comet’s and the Earth’s orbits lie on the same plane. \begin{enumerate}[label=(\alph*)] \item Find the distance $r_\text{min}$ of the comet’s perihelion $P$ from the Sun (i.e. its shortest distance). The distance $r_\text{max}$ of the comet’s aphelion $A$ from the Sun (i.e. its longest distance) can be assumed to be much larger than $r_E$. \item (Calculus required) For how many days $t$ will the comet’s distance to the Sun be less than $r_E$? \end{enumerate}
A: See solution 2 <a href="https://www.ioc.ee/~kalda/ipho/es/NBPhO17_sol.pdf" target="_blank" rel="noopener">here.</a>
