LESSON: fma-14
------------------------------------------------------------------------------

[1] Concept Checks
Q: Suppose you are on a circular spaceship of radius $R$ isolated in space. At what angular velocity should the spaceship spin in order to simulate gravity on Earth?
A: This is simply applying the definition of centrifugal acceleration,
 $$\omega^2 R = g,$$
 so we choose $\omega = \boxed{\sqrt{g/R}}.$

[2] Concept Checks
Q: You hang a mass $m$ from the ceiling of your car, and you find that it sits at an angle $\theta$ from the vertical as your car accelerates at $a$. Find $\theta$ in terms of $a$ and the gravitational acceleration $g$.
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[width=4cm]{car_accelerate.png}
 \end{center}
A: Working in the frame of the car, because of the translational fictitious force, the effective gravity is the sum of $\vec a$ and $\vec g$, so the angle is given by
 $$\tan \theta = \frac ag,$$
 so $\theta = \boxed{\tan^{-1} a/g}$.

[3] Concept Checks — $F=ma$
Q: A cylindrical space station produces ‘artificial gravity’ by rotating with angular frequency $\omega$. Consider working in the reference frame rotating with the space station. In this frame, an astronaut is initially at rest standing on the floor, facing in the direction that the space station is rotating. The astronaut jumps up vertically relative to the floor of the space station, with an initial speed less than that of the speed of the floor. Just after leaving the floor, the motion of the astronaut, relative to the space station floor,
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[width=5cm]{fma_spin_spaceship.png}
 \end{center}
 \begin{anum}
 \item always has a component of acceleration directed toward the floor, and they land at the same point they jumped from.
 \item always has a component of acceleration directed toward the floor, and they land in front of the point they jumped from.
\item always has a component of acceleration directed toward the floor, and they land behind the
point they jumped from.
\item has a component of acceleration directed away from the floor, and they land behind the point
they jumped from.
\item has a zero acceleration relative to the floor, and the astronaut never reaches the floor again.
 \end{anum}
A: The answer is $\mathbf B$. Working in the rotating frame, there are two fictitious forces, the coriolis force and the centrifugal force. The centrifugal force is responsible for a component of acceleration towards the floor. As for coriolis, if we do the cross product, using the right hand rule we see that the acceleration is toward the direction of rotation, and thus they land in front.

[4] Concept Checks
Q: Assume that the Earth is a perfect sphere. Find the effective force of gravity, including the error due to the centrifugal force at:
 \begin{anum}
 \item the Equator.
 \item the North Pole.
 \item at latitude $45^\circ$.
 \end{anum}
A: \begin{anum}
 \item To start, the angular velocity of the earth is $\omega = \frac{2\pi}{24 hr} \approx 7.29 \times 10^{-5} rad/s$. At the equator, the centrifugal force points parallel to the gravitational acceleration, so the effective force of gravity is
 $$g - \omega^2 R_{\oplus} \approx 9.78 m/s^2.$$
 \item The centrifugal force has magnitude 0 here, so there is no deviation! $g_{\text{eff}} = g$!
 \item Here we have to make a triangle because the centrifugal force makes a $45^\circ$ angle with gravity and has magnitude $\omega^2 R_{\oplus} /\sqrt 2$. So, using the law of cosines,
 $$g_{\text{eff}}^2 = g^2 + (\omega^2 R_{\oplus}/\sqrt 2)^2 - 2g(\omega^2 R_{\oplus}/\sqrt 2)\cos 45^\circ.$$
 Plugging in the numbers and calculating, we get $g_{\text{eff}} = 9.79 m/s^2$.
 \end{anum}

[5] Exercises
Q: You might've heard that toilet bowls flush in different directions in different hemispheres because of the coriolis force. Is this realistic? Perform an order of magnitude estimate for the coriolis force on a droplet of mass $m$ that's being flushed. Then, estimate the amount of deflection on the mass as it travels down a typical-size toilet bowl.
A: The magnitude of the coriolis force is of order $2m\omega v$, where $\omega$ is the angular velocity of the earth. Thus, the acceleration is of magnitude
 $$ma = 2m\omega v \implies a \sim \omega v.$$
 Say the toilet bowl is of radius $r$ and that it takes a time $t$ for a water droplet to traverse it. Then, the coriolis acceleration is of magnitude $a \sim \omega r/t$ and the deflection is approximately
 $$\Delta x \sim at^2 \sim \omega r t.$$
 The following are estimates for the three terms:
 $$\omega \approx 7 \times 10^{-5} rad/s, r \approx 0.3 m, t \approx 0.5 s.$$
 So, the deflection is around $\Delta x = \omega r t = 10^{-5} \unit {m} \ll r$.

[6] Exercises — $F=ma$
Q: Alice and Bethany stand side by side on the Earth’s equator. If Alice jumps directly upward, in her frame of reference, to a small height $h$ much less than the radius of the Earth, she will land a distance $D$ to the west of Bethany. If Alice had instead jumped to a height $2h$, how far to the west of Bethany would she land? Neglect air resistance.
A: First, the vertical velocity dominates, so we have
 $$\frac 12 gt^2 \approx h.$$
 Thus, the time to fall $t$ is proportional to $\sqrt h$. Thus, the vertical velocity is proportional to $gt \propto \sqrt h$. The horizontal acceleration is due to coriolis, so
 $$a_{h} \propto v \omega \propto \sqrt{h}.$$
 And so the horizontal displacement is
 $$\Delta x \propto a_h t^2 \propto \sqrt{h}\times \sqrt{h}^2 \propto h^{3/2}.$$
 So, the new distance is $\boxed{2^{3/2}D}$.

[7] Exercises
Q: Find the angular frequency of a conical pendulum of length $\ell$, mass $m$, and angle to vertical $\theta$ by using the rotating frame.
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[width=4cm]{conical_penddd.png}
 \end{center}
A: Working in the rotating frame, we see that there is an outward centrifugal force of magnitude $\omega^2 l\sin \theta$ and gravity. This is exactly the same as problem 4, the string must point along the effective gravity, so
 $$\tan \theta = \frac{\omega^2 l \sin \theta}{g} \implies \omega^2 = \frac{g\tan \theta}{l\sin \theta} \implies \omega = \boxed{\sqrt{\frac{g}{l\cos \theta}}}.$$

[8] Exercises — $F=ma$
Q: A child in a circular, rotating space station tosses a ball in such a way so that once the station has rotated
through one half rotation, the child catches the ball. From the child’s point of view, which plot shows
the trajectory of the ball? The child is at the bottom of the space station in the diagrams below, but
only the initial location of the ball is shown.
 \includegraphics[width=8cm]{ball_spaceship_toss.png}
A: First, it must be C, D or B because the child catches the ball. Now, it is actually easier to think about the situation from the lab frame. In the lab frame, the ball simply travels straight across the circle, which means it has some upward velocity. So, in the child's frame who is rotating, the ball has both a horizontal and vertical component of velocity when it leaves the child, and the only choice that satisfies this is $\mathbf C$.

[9] Exercises — Kleppner-Kolenkow, modified
Q: A truck at rest has one door fully open, as shown. The truck accelerates forward at constant rate $A$, and the door begins to swing shut. The door is uniform and solid, has total mass $M$, height $h$, and width $w$. Neglect air resistance.
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[width=6cm]{car_door_closing.png}
 \end{center}
 \begin{anum}
 \item Find the instantaneous angular velocity of the door about its hinges when it has swung through $90^\circ$.
 \item Now this time instead of the door starting fully open, assume that the door is only slightly ajar when it starts accelerating, making an angle $\theta \ll 1$ with the car. How long does it take for the door to close?
 \end{anum}
A: \begin{anum}
 \item In the accelerating frame, we have an effective gravity which points left with magnitude $A$. Thus, we can use conservation of energy to solve this first part. We have
 $$MA\frac{w}{2} = \frac 12 I \omega^2.$$
 And $I$ for the rectangle here is $I = \frac 13 M w^2$, so
 $$MA\frac w2 = \frac 16 Mw^2 \omega^2 \implies \omega = \sqrt{\frac{3A}{w}}.$$
 \item This time, we still use the idea of effective gravity. In that view, this is just a pendulum! It's a physical pendulum with moment of inertia $I$ found in part (a) with its center of mass a distance $w/2$ from the pivot point. The period of such a pendulum is
 $$T = 2\pi \sqrt{\frac{I}{mgd}}.$$
 The door swinging shut is a quarter of the period, so our answer is $\boxed{\frac{\pi}{2}\sqrt{\frac{I}{mgd}}}.$
 \end{anum}

[10] Exercises
Q: Here's a problem from forces! Solve it with fictitious forces this time. A particle slides down a smooth inclined plane of elevation $\theta$, fixed in an elevator going up with an acceleration $a_0$. The base of the incline has a length $L$. Find the time taken by the particle to reach the bottom.
 \begin{center}
 
\end{center}
A: In the accelerating frame, there is an effective gravity of $g + a_0$, and the problem is just a normal inclined plane problem. So, time is
 $$\frac 12 (g+a_0)\sin \theta t^2 = \frac{L}{\cos \theta} \implies t = \sqrt{\frac{2L}{(g+a_0)\sin \theta \cos \theta}}.$$

[11] Exercises
Q: A classic test of a good driver is whether or not a passenger's mug of water spills. Collin drives in a circle of radius $R$ while Evan holds a mug of radius $r$ and height $h$ filled up $f = 3/4$ of the way up with water.
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[width=4cm]{mug_spin.png}
 \end{center}
 \begin{anum}
 \item What is the maximum speed $v_{\text{max}}$ that Collin can drive at before the water spills?
 \item At this maximum speed, what is the pressure at the "outer-bottom corner" (the part of the mug that is furthest from the center of the circle that Collin is driving on)? 
 \item What is the pressure at the "inner-bottom corner"?
 \item Does $P = \rho g h$ from our fluids lecture still hold?
 
 \end{anum}
A: \begin{anum}
 \item We want to find the maximum angle that the water can be at before it spills. Let the height of the triangle air section when the water is tilted be $h_a$. Then, the total volume of water in the cup is
 $$V = \pi r^2 h - \frac 12 \pi r^2 h_a = \pi r^2 (h - h_a/2).$$
 And since $V = f\pi r^2 h$, we have $$fh = h - \frac{h_a}{2} \implies h_a = 2(1-f)h.$$
 So, with this we can figure out the angle of effective gravity -- it'll be perpendicular to the surface of the water. The angle that the water makes with the horizontal is
 $$\tan \theta = \frac{h_a}{2r} .$$
 And the angle comes from the ratio of the centrifugal force to the gravitational force, so
 $$\frac{v^2/R}{g} = \frac{h_a}{2r} \implies \frac{v^2}{Rg} = \frac{(1-f)h}{r}.$$
 Thus, we see that $v = \boxed{\sqrt{(1-f)Rgh/r}}$.
 \item In the rotating frame, consider a point that is a distance $d$ vertically from the surface of the water. Then, its orthogonal distance from the surface of the water is given by
 $$d' = d\cos \theta .$$
 Thus, by hydrostatic equilibrium, the pressure at this point would be
 $$P = \rho g_{\text{eff}} d' = \rho \frac{g}{\cos \theta} d \cos \theta = \rho g d,$$
 so it turns out that we can just use our typical $\rho gh$! Thus, the answer is just $\boxed{P_0 + \rho gh}$.
 \item Using the result from the previous part, it is $P = P_0 + \rho g (h - h_a) = \boxed{P_0+\rho g (2f - 1)h}$.
 \item Yes, as we said in part (b).
 \end{anum}

[12] Exercises — $F=ma$
Q: Consider the set-up from the previous problem. Suppose now that we puncture a hole in the "outer-bottom corner" of the mug. With what speed does the water exit the hole?
A: We found that the pressure was $\rho g h$ in the previous problem. Thus, using our result from the fluid's lecture, the speed is just $v = \boxed{\sqrt{2gh}}.$

[13] Exercises — Morin
Q: A hoop of radius $R$ is made to rotate at constant angular speed $\omega$ around
a diameter, as shown below. A small bug of mass $m$ walks at
constant angular speed $\Omega$ around the hoop. Let $N$ be the total force that the hoop applies to the bug when the bug is at the angle $\theta$ shown, and
let $N_\perp$ be the component of $N$ that is perpendicular to the plane of the
hoop. Find $N_\perp$ in two ways (ignore gravity in this problem):
\begin{center}
 \includegraphics[width=5cm]{bug_walk.png}
\end{center}
\begin{anum}
 \item Work in the lab frame: At the angle $\theta$, find the rate of change of the bug’s angular momentum around the rotation axis, and then consider the torque on the bug.
 \item Work in the rotating frame of the hoop: At the angle $\theta$, find the
relevant fictitious force, and then take it from there.
\end{anum}
A: \begin{anum}
 \item If $r$ is the bug's distance from the axis, the angular momentum is
 $$L = m(\omega r)r = m\omega r^2.$$
 The bug's distance from the axis in terms of $\theta$ is $r = R \sin \theta$, so
 $$\frac{dL}{dt} = 2m\omega r \frac{dr}{dt} = 2m\omega (R\sin \theta) (R \cos \theta)\Omega.$$
 And in terms of forces, the torque is
 $$\tau = N_\perp R\sin \theta = \frac{dL}{dt} = 2m\omega (R\sin \theta) (R \cos \theta)\Omega.$$
 Thus, we see that $N_\perp = \boxed{2m\omega \Omega R\cos \theta}$.
 \item In the rotating frame, the only other force that can act perpendicular to the plane of the hoop is the coriolis force. The coriolis force has magnitude
 $$F_{\text{cor}} = |2m\omega \times v| = 2m\omega v \cos \theta = 2m \omega \Omega R\cos \theta,$$
 which the normal force must balance, so we find $N_\perp = \boxed{2m\omega \Omega R \cos \theta}$ again.
 \end{anum}

[14] Exercises
Q: In this problem we analyze the Roche limit. Consider a small mass of density $\rho_o$, and radius $r$ orbiting a distance $d$ from the center of a planet of density $\rho_p$ and radius $R \gg r$. Assume that the mass is tidally locked. Working in the rotating frame with the mass, find the distance $d$ at which the mass breaks apart.
A: In the rotating frame, consider some small point mass $m$ at the outer radius of the rock/small mass that's orbiting the planet. There are three forces on it: the gravitational force from the planet, the gravitational force from the rock, and the centrifugal force. We can balance those three forces:
 $$\left(\frac{GM_pm}{d^2} - \frac{2GM_pm}{d^3}r\right) + \frac{GM_rm}{r^2} = m\omega^2 (d+r).$$
 We have from circular motion that
 $$m\omega^2d = \frac{GM_pm}{d^2},$$
 so plugging that in, we get
 $$-\frac{2GM_pm}{d^3}r + \frac{GM_rm}{r^2} = \frac{GM_pm}{d^3}r.$$
 Thus, solving for $d$, we get
 $$d^3 = \frac{3M_p}{M_r/r^3} = R^3 \frac{3M_p/R^3}{M_r/r^3} = R^3\frac{3\rho_p}{\rho_o},$$
 giving us the roche limit, $d = \boxed{R\left(\frac{3\rho_p}{\rho_o}\right)^{1/3}}$.

[15] Challenge Problems — Morin
Q: For what angle $\theta$ (down from the north pole) is the angle between $g_{\text{eff}}$ and $g$ maximum? <em>Hint:</em> there is a way to do this with little calculation by geometry.
A: Notice that at a latitude $\theta$, the centrifugal acceleration has magnitude
 $$a_c= \omega^2 R_{\oplus} \cos \theta.$$
 Furthermore, at a latitude $\theta$, the centrifugal acceleration makes an angle $\theta$ with the gravitational acceleration. Thus, the set of possible $g_{\text{eff}}$ look like this:

 The maximum possible angle is when these two are tangent. The circle has radius $\omega^2 R_{\oplus} /2$, while the distance to the center is $g - \omega^2 R_{\oplus} / 2$, so the maximum angle is when it is tangent, which makes an angle
 $$\phi = \arcsin \left(\frac{\omega^2 R_\oplus / 2}{g - \omega^2 R_\oplus /2}\right).$$
 Since it is a right triangle here, the latitude is
 $$\theta = 90 - \phi = \boxed{\arccos \left(\frac{\omega^2 R_\oplus / 2}{g - \omega^2 R_\oplus /2}\right)}.$$

[16] Challenge Problems — HRK
Q: A vertical cylindrical vessel with radius $R$ is rotating
around its axis with the angular velocity $\omega$. Show that the shape of the surface of the water is a paraboloid, $y = kr^2$, and determine the constant $k$ in terms of $\omega, R,$ and $g$. Some calculus may be required.
\begin{center}
 \includegraphics[width=5cm]{water_paraboloid.png}
\end{center}
A: In the rotating frame, the gravitational field is an effective $$\mathbf g_{\text{eff}} = -g\hat y + \omega^2 r \hat r.$$
 In the static case, the surface of the water is an equipotential, so if we start from the center and traverse along $r$ and then traverse up along $y$ to get to the surface, our net change in pressure should be $0$. We have
 $$\frac{dP}{dr} = \rho \omega^2 r, \frac{dP}{dy} = -\rho g,$$
 off of the field. Thus, the pressure at the surface a distance $r$ away is
 $$P_0 + \int_0^r \rho \omega^2 r dr - \rho g y = P_0 + \frac 12 \rho \omega^2 r^2 - \rho g y = P_0.$$
 Thus, we get that $\frac 12 \rho \omega^2 r^2 = \rho g y$. Thus, we have the form
 $$y = \frac{\omega^2}{2g} r^2,$$
 as desired, with $k = \omega^2/2g$.

[17] Challenge Problems — Kalda
Q: A cylinder with radius $R$ spins around its axis with an
angular speed $\omega$. On its inner surface there lies a small block;
the coefficient of friction between the block and the inner surface of the cylinder is $\mu$. Find the values of $\omega$ for which the
block does not slip (stays still with respect to the cylinder).
Consider the cases where
\begin{center}
 \includegraphics[width=6cm]{spinning_block.png}
\end{center}
\begin{anum}
 \item the axis of the cylinder is horizontal
 \item the axis is inclined by angle $\alpha$ with respect to the horizon.
\end{anum}
A: \begin{anum}
 \item Consider the rotating frame. Below we have three effective forces: the gravitational force, the centrifugal force, and the combined friction and normal force.
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[width=6cm]{spinning_a.jpeg}
 \end{center}
 We see that as long as the circle lies within the black lines the block can be in static equilibrium. Thus, the condition is that
 $$\sin^{-1} \frac{\omega^2 R}{g} < \tan^{-1} \mu,$$
 so we see that $\omega < \sqrt{\frac{g}{R} \frac{\mu}{\sqrt{1+\mu^2}}}.$
 \item This is the same question, except the range of reachable angles on the green circle stops at $\alpha$ to the vertical, so if the $\arctan \mu$ angle is large enough, it will not be the limiting case. The limit is where
 $$\arctan \mu + \alpha = 90,$$
 so if $\mu < \cot \alpha$ <u>we get the answer in part (a)</u>. Otherwise, if $\mu > \cot \alpha$, then the first contact point is when the contact point is at the border of the arc, at $\alpha$. Thus, using law of sines, we get
 $$\frac{m\omega^2R}{\sin(\arctan \mu + \alpha)} = \frac{mg}{\sin(\arctan \mu)} \implies \omega < \sqrt{\frac{g}{R}\frac{\sin(\arctan \mu + \alpha)}{\sin \arctan \mu}}.$$
 \end{anum}

[18] Challenge Problems — USAPhO, Classical
Q: Consider a simple pendulum of length $\ell$.
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[width=4cm]{focault_pendulum.png}
 \end{center}
 \begin{anum}
 \item What is the period of the pendulum $\omega$?
 \item Now, view the pendulum in a frame rotating at $\Omega \ll \omega$. How long does it take for the pendulum to begin oscillating in its original plane? <em>Hint:</em> don't use fictitious forces
 \item Now, can you explain the result in part (b) using fictitious forces?
 \end{anum}
A: \begin{anum}
 \item This is simply $\sqrt{g/l}$.
 \item In the lab frame, the pendulum only oscillates in a single plane. Thus, in the rotating frame, this plane simply rotates at a rate of $\omega$, and it only needs to rotate by $\pi$ to return to the original plane, so we get $\boxed{\pi/\Omega}$.
 \item Solving this exactly is a little tough, but we can make some estimations. In the rotating frame, as the mass swings, it experiences a perpendicular coriolis force which rotates its plane of oscillation. The coriolis force has magnitude
 $$2m\Omega v(t),$$
 so the angular acceleration of the mass is approximately
 $$\alpha (t) = 2\Omega v(t) / r(t) = \frac{2\Omega(\omega x_0 \sin \omega t)}{x_0 \cos \omega t} = 2\Omega \omega \tan \omega t.$$
 So, over a period, it pick up an average velocity of around
 $$\omega' \sim \Omega \omega \times \frac{1}{\omega} \sim \Omega.$$
 Thus, it'll take order $1/\omega' \sim 1/\Omega$ to rotate back to the original plane.
 \end{anum}

[19] Challenge Problems — Bonus
Q: Try the first long question on <a href="https://usaaao.files.wordpress.com/2022/03/usaaao_second_exam_2022_problems.pdf#page=4" target="_blank" rel="noopener">this exam</a>, titled "The Curious Orbit of James Webb". This is a USAPhO level and style problem.
A: See the official solutions <a href="https://usaaao.files.wordpress.com/2022/03/usaaao_second_exam_2022_solutions.pdf#page=11" target="_blank" rel="noopener">here</a>.

------------------------------------------------------------------------------
LESSON: fma-15
------------------------------------------------------------------------------

[1] Concept checks
Q: A spring of constant $k$ and relaxed length $\ell$ is suspended from the ceiling. A mass $m$ is attached to the spring and released from rest at the spring's relaxed position. Find $z(t)$, the distance between the mass and the ceiling as a function of time.
A: The equilibrium position is at a distance $z_0=\ell+mg/k$ from the ceiling, and the mass oscillates (starting from a local minimum) with amplitude $A=mg/k$. As a result, $$z(t)=\boxed{\ell+\frac{mg}{k}\left(1-\cos\sqrt{\frac km}t\right)}$$

[2] Concept checks — $F=ma$
Q: A pendulum of length $L$ oscillates inside a box. A person picks up the box and gently shakes it horizontally with frequency $\omega$ and a fixed amplitude for a fixed time. The final amplitude can be maximized if $\omega$ satisfies \begin{enumerate}[label = (\Alph*)]
\item $\omega=\sqrt{g/L}$
\item $\omega=2\sqrt{g/L}$
\item $\omega=(1/2)\sqrt{g/L}$
\item There will be no effect on the amplitude for any value of $\omega$.
\item None of the above.
\end{enumerate}
A: We want to rock the box so that when the mass is moving to the left inside the box, we pull the box to the right, and when the mass is moving to the left inside the box, we pull the box to the left. Thus, we want to set $\omega$ equal to the natural oscillation frequency of the pendulum, which is <strong>(A)</strong> $\omega=\sqrt{g/L}.$

[3] Concept checks — $F=ma$
Q: A point mass $m$ is glued inside a massless hollow rod of length $L$ at an unknown location. When the rod is pivoted at one end, the period of small oscillations is $T$. When the rod is pivoted at the other end, the period of small oscillations is $2T$. How far is the mass from the center?
\begin{enumerate}[label = (\Alph*)]
\item $L/8$
\item $L/6$
\item $L/4$
\item $3L/10$
\item $2L/5$
\end{enumerate}
A: We have $T\propto\sqrt{\Delta L}$, where $\Delta L$ is the distance from the pivot point to the mass. Then the ratio of
distances is 4, so the mass is $L/5$ from one of the ends, or <strong>(D)</strong> $3L/10$ from the center.

[4] Concept checks
Q: A block of mass $m$ attached to a horizontal spring $k$ is oscillating on a frictionless floor with amplitude $A$. 
 \begin{anum}
 \item Find the period $T$ of one oscillation.
 \item Let the equilibrium position be $x=0$. How long does it take the block to travel from $x=A$ to $x=A/2$? (The technique with this type of problem is to model the position of the mass as the projection of a point moving around a circle with angular velocity $\omega$)
 \item How long does it take for the block to travel from equilibrium to a general position $0<x_0<A$ if it's initially moving to the right? 
 \end{anum}
A: \begin{anum}
 \item The period is $$T=\frac{2\pi}{\omega}=\boxed{2\pi\sqrt{\frac mk}}$$
 \item Using the hint, the situation is like so: 
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[scale=0.2]{sol1}
 \end{center}When the block travels from $A$ to $A/2$, we're effectively looking at when the point drawn travels an angle $60^\circ$ on the circle. Thus, the time it takes is $60^\circ/360^\circ=1/6$ of the period, or $$t=\boxed{\frac{\pi}{3}\sqrt{\frac mk}}$$
 \item In this case, the point on the circle starts out at the angle $270^\circ$ and travels until the $x$-projection is $x_0$. The angle appended on the circle during this time is $\sin^{-1}(x_0/A)$, so as a fraction of $2\pi$ the time is $$t=\frac{\sin^{-1}(x_0/A)}{2\pi}T=\boxed{\sin^{-1}(x_0/A)\sqrt{\frac mk}}$$
 \end{anum}

[5] Concept checks
Q: A uniform hula hoop is hung from a rod. Let $\omega_1$ be the frequency of oscillations in the hoop's own plane, and let $\omega_2$ be the frequency of oscillations in the plane perpendicular to the hoop as shown. Find $\omega_1/\omega_2.$
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[scale=0.2]{hula.png}
 \end{center}
A: The distance to the center of mass is the same in both cases, so $\omega_1/\omega_2=\sqrt{I_2/I_1}$. By the parallel axis theorem, $I_1=mr^2+mr^2=2mr^2$. By both the perpendicular and parallel axis theorems, $I_2=mr^2/2+mr^2=3mr^2/2$. Thus, $\omega_1/\omega_2=\boxed{\sqrt{3/4}}.$

[6] Concept checks — $F=ma$
Q: Three identical masses are connected with identical rigid rods and pivoted at point $A$. If the lowest mass receives a small horizontal push to the left, it oscillates with period $T_1$. If it instead receives a small push into the page, it oscillates with period $T_2$. The ratio $T_1/T_2$ is
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[scale=0.2]{osc1}
 \end{center}
 \begin{enumerate}[label = (\Alph*)]
\item $1/2$
\item $1$
\item $\sqrt3$
\item $2\sqrt2$
\item $2\sqrt5$
\end{enumerate}
A: By the same method as the previous question, $T_1/T_2=\sqrt{I_1/I_2}.$ Notice $I_1=3ml^2$ and $I_2=ml^2$ so the answer is <strong>(D)</strong> $\sqrt{3}$.

[7] Concept checks
Q: I have a box with springs of constant $k_1$ and another box with springs of constant $k_2>k_1$. I can choose two springs from one box and one spring from the other box. Then, I will attach the three springs to a block $m$ with two in series and one in parallel as shown. Let $\omega_1$ be the greatest frequency of oscillation possible under these circumstances, and $\omega_2$ be the smallest frequency possible. Find $\omega_1/\omega_2.$
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[scale=0.2]{springs}
 \end{center}
A: To make things clearer, let $k_2=\alpha k_1$ with $\alpha >1$. Clearly, we should choose two springs $k_2$ and one spring $k_1$ to maximize the frequency. If we have two springs $k_2$ on the left, then our total spring constant is $k_1+k_2/2=k_1(1+\alpha/2)$. If we have one of each on the left, then our total spring constant is $k_2+k_1k_2/(k_1+k_2)=k_1\alpha(\alpha+2)/(\alpha+1)$. To compare the two results, notice that $$\frac{\alpha(\alpha+2)}{\alpha+1}-\left(1+\frac \alpha 2\right)=\frac{(\alpha+2)(\alpha-1)}{2(\alpha+1)}>0$$ so the latter result is greater. By symmetry, the smallest frequency we can make is with two springs of constant $k_1$, with one of each on the left: $k_1+k_1k_2/(k_1+k_2)$. The ratio of the frequencies is proportional to the square root of the ratio of spring constants, so $$\frac{\omega_1}{\omega_2}=\boxed{\sqrt{\frac{k_2(2k_1+k_2)}{k_1(k_1+2k_2)}}}$$

[8] Exercises — $F=ma$
Q: A bead attached to a string of length $\ell=10 m$ is released from a very small angle $\theta_0$ to the vertical. A wall is placed in the path of the bead such that the bead collides elastically with the wall when the string is at an angle $\theta_0/2$ to the vertical, as shown.
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[scale=0.2]{water}
 \end{center}
 What is the time interval between the bead’s collisions with the wall?
 \begin{enumerate}[label = (\Alph*)]
\item $2\pi/3 s$
\item $3\pi/4 s$
\item $4\pi/3 s$
\item $3\pi/2 s$
\item $2\pi s$
\end{enumerate}
A: The period without the wall is $T_0=2\pi \sqrt{\ell/g}=2\pi s.$ Notice that the new period is just that of $T_0$ minus two times the time it takes for the ball to travel from $A$ to $A/2$, which we found already in Problem Problem 4. Thus, $T=T_0(1-2\cdot(1/6))=(2/3)T_0=4\pi/3 s$, answer <strong>(C)</strong>.

[9] Exercises
Q: A uniform ball of mass $m$ and radius $R$ is attached at its top to a spring $k$ to the wall. Find the frequency $\omega$ of oscillations if the ball rolls without slipping. 
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[scale=0.2]{ball}
 \end{center}
A: If the ball rolls an angle $\theta$, then the torque about the contact point to the ground is $\tau=-2kR^2\theta$. The moment of inertia of the ball about the contact point, by the parallel axis theorem, is $7mR^2/5$. Thus, the equation of motion is $$-2kR^2\theta=\frac{7}{5}mR^2\ddot\theta$$from which we have $$\omega=\boxed{\sqrt{\frac{10k}{7m}}}$$

[10] Exercises
Q: A uniform cylinder with mass $m$ and radius $R$ is placed circular sides up on a flat frictionless table. The edges of the cylinder are attached to the midpoints of two springs of constant $k$ as shown from the top view. The springs are initially at their relaxed length. \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[scale=0.2]{cylinder2}
 \end{center}\begin{anum}
 \item The cylinder is translationally displaced a small distance along the directions of the springs. Find the frequency of oscillation.
 \item The cylinder is now rotated by a small angle relative to its center of mass while keeping the center of mass fixed. Find the frequency of oscillation.
 \end{anum}
A: \begin{anum}
 \item Splitting each spring in half, we decompose each spring into two springs of constant $2k$. There are four of these halves connected in parallel, so the effective spring constant is $8k$. As a result, $$\omega=\boxed{\sqrt{\frac{8k}{m}}}$$
 \item Rotating the cylinder by a small angle $\theta$, each of the spring halves of constant $2k$ are stretched by $R\theta$, so the restoring torque is $\tau=-8kR^2\theta$. The moment of inertia of the cylinder is $mR^2/2$, so our equation of motion is $$-8kR^2\theta=\frac12mR^2\ddot\theta$$from which we have $$\omega=\boxed{\sqrt{\frac{16k}{m}}}$$
 \end{anum}

[11] Exercises
Q: A uniform rod of mass $m$ and length $l$ is put upright on a horizontal floor, which has coefficient of static friction $\mu$. The top of the rod is connected to a spring $k$ and set into small oscillations with amplitude $\theta_0$ (the angle between the rod and the vertical). 
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[scale=0.2]{rodman}
 \end{center}
 \begin{anum}
 \item What is the necessary relation between $m$, $l$, and $k$ for the rod to be able to oscillate about a stable equilibrium point?
 \item Assuming the condition in (a) is satisfied, what is the minimum $\mu$ needed to prevent the rod from slipping during the motion?
 \item Assuming the conditions in (a) and (b) are satisfied, what is the frequency of oscillations $\omega$?
 \end{anum}
A: \begin{anum}
 \item Displacing the rod by a small angle $\theta$, the torque due to gravity about the contact point to the ground is equal to $mgl\theta/2$ while the torque due to the spring is $kl^2\theta$. For the equilibrium to be stable, the spring torque must be greater than the gravitational torque, which implies that $$\boxed{kl>\frac{mg}{2}}$$
 \item We will balance forces in the horizontal direction to find the necessary frictional force. First, however, we must find the horizontal acceleration of the rod's center of mass. The maximal acceleration of the rod during the motion is that at the maximum amplitude. At this moment, the net torque on the rod is equal to $$\tau=kl^2\theta_0-\frac{mgl\theta_0}{2}$$ Since the moment of inertia about the contact point to the ground is $ml^2/3$, we have the angular acceleration $\alpha$ to be $$\alpha=\frac{3\tau}{ml^2}=3\theta_0\left(\frac{k}{m}-\frac{g}{2l}\right)$$which immediately leads to the acceleration of the center of mass as $$a=\alpha\frac{l}{2}=\frac{3\theta_0}{2}\left(\frac{kl}{m}-\frac{g}{2}\right)$$Taking a positive frictional force to counteract the force of the spring, the net force that generates this acceleration is $kl\theta_0-mg\mu$. Solving for $\mu$, we get $$\mu=\boxed{\theta_0\left(\frac34-\frac{kl}{2mg}\right)}$$This is positive if $kl<3mg/2$, and we must switch the sign if $kl>3mg/2$. 
 \item Using the torque from the previous part, we have $$\ddot\theta=-3\theta_0\left(\frac{k}{m}-\frac{g}{2l}\right)$$ so the frequency of oscillations is $$\omega=\boxed{\sqrt{3\left(\frac km-\frac{g}{2l}\right)}}$$
 \end{anum}

[12] Exercises — $F=ma$
Q: A U-tube manometer consists of a uniform diameter cylindrical tube that is bent into a U shape. It is originally filled with water that has a density $\rho_w$. The total length of the column of water is $L$. Ignore surface tension and viscosity. The water is displaced slightly so that one side moves up a distance $x$ and the other side lowers a distance $x$. Find the frequency of oscillation.
A: The most intuitive way to do this is with the energy method. Let the cross section of the tube be $A$. When the water is displaced as described, we basically move a parcel of water of volume $Ax$ a distance $x$ upward, so $U=\rho_w Agx^2=(1/2)2\rho_wAgx^2$. If the column moves at a speed $\dot x$, then the total kinetic energy is $K=(1/2)\rho_w LA\dot x^2$. As a result, we have $\kappa = 2\rho_wAg$ and $\mu=\rho_wLA$, so $$\omega=\sqrt{\frac\kappa \mu}=\boxed{\sqrt{\frac{2g}{L}}}$$

[13] Exercises
Q: Model an iceberg as a square pyramid of base length $a$ and height $h$ floating in water. The iceberg floats upside down so that it's in a stable equilibrium. Let the density of water be $\rho$, and the density of ice is $0.9\rho$. The iceberg is perturbed in the vertical direction and begins oscillating in the water. What is the frequency of oscillations? Ignore the inertia of the water and frictional forces.
A: Observe the following diagram:
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[scale=0.2]{pye.png}
 \end{center}Let $d$ be the depth of the iceberg under water, and $b=ad/h$ the length of the cross-section square at the water level. By scaling, the volume of the pyramid underwater is related to the whole volume by $V_d/V=d^3/h^3$. Balancing forces with buoyancy, $$\rho V_d=0.9\rho V\Rightarrow \frac dh=0.9^{1/3}$$As a result, we have $b=0.9^{1/3} a$. Now, if the iceberg is displaced by a distance $x$ downward, then the buoyancy force from the water will increase by $F=-\rho b^2gx=-0.9^{2/3}\rho a^2gx$. The equation of motion is thus $$\frac{0.9\rho a^2h}{3}\ddot x=-0.9^{2/3}\rho a^2gx$$ so that $$\omega=\boxed{\sqrt{\frac{3g}{0.9^{1/3}h}}}$$

[14] Exercises
Q: Let's revisit the tunnel-planet problem, but now we won't assume the tunnel passes through the planet's center. Given a planet of mass $M$ and radius $R$ of uniform density, dig a tunnel through the planet that runs a distance $r$ from the center of the planet as shown. Is the motion simple harmonic? If it is, find the time it takes for a mass released from rest at one end of the tunnel to reach the other end. 
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[scale=0.2]{planet.png}
 \end{center}
A: Observe the following diagram: 
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[scale=0.17]{planetsol.png}
 \end{center}
 The total force when the particle is a distance $d$ from the midpoint of the tunnel is equal to $$F=\frac{GM}{d^2+r^2}\frac{\sqrt{(d^2+r^2)^3}}{R^3}=\frac{GM}{R^3}\sqrt{d^2+r^2}$$Now, notice that the normal force from the wall of the tunnel will cancel out the vertical component of the force, so we are only concerned with the horizontal component, which can be found via the right triangle: $$F_x=F\frac{d}{\sqrt{d^2+r^2}}=\frac{GM}{R^3}d$$so indeed, the force is proportional to $d$, so the motion is simple harmonic. Here we have $\omega=\sqrt{GM/R^3}$, so the time it takes to reach from one end of the tunnel to the other is $$t=\frac{\pi}{\omega}=\boxed{\pi\sqrt{\frac{R^3}{GM}}}$$

[15] Exercises — $F=ma$
Q: Two identical carts $A$ and $B$ each with mass $m$ are connected via a spring with spring constant $k$. Two additional springs, identical to the first, connect the carts to two fixed points. The carts are free to oscillate under the effect of the springs in one dimensional frictionless motion.
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[scale=0.2]{fma1}
 \end{center}
 Under suitable initial conditions, the two carts will oscillate in phase according to $$x_A(t)=x_0\sin\omega_1t=x_B(t)$$ where $x_A$ and $x_B$ are the locations of carts $A$ and $B$ relative to their respective equilibrium positions. Under other suitable initial conditions, the two carts will oscillate exactly out of phase according to$$x_A(t)=x_0\sin\omega_2t=-x_B(t)$$Determine the ratio $\omega_2/\omega_1.$
 \begin{enumerate}[label = (\Alph*)]
\item $\sqrt3$
\item $2$
\item $2\sqrt2$
\item $3$
\item $5$
\end{enumerate}
A: If the carts oscillate in phase, then the middle spring will not compress, so the carts don't influence each other's motion. Thus, we have $\omega_1=\sqrt{k/m}$. If the carts oscillate out of phase, then the middle point of the middle spring will always stay fixed. Thus, if we imagine this point to be instead connected to a wall set in between the two masses, then each mass experiences force from a spring of constant $2k$ (from splitting the middle spring in half) and $k$ (from the other spring). Thus, $\omega_2=\sqrt{3k/m}$ so the answer is <strong>(A)</strong> $\sqrt 3$.

[16] Challenge Problems
Q: Two simple pendulums with mass $m$ and string length $l$ are attached by a spring $k$. 
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[scale=0.2]{coupled.png}
 \end{center}
 \begin{anum}
 \item Describe the two normal modes of the system and find their frequencies of oscillation.
 \item (Calculus required) The mass on the right is given a speed $v\ll \sqrt{gl}$ to the left at $t=0$. Let $\theta_1(t)$ and $\theta_2(t)$ describe the angles made by the left and right string, respectively, with respect to time. Find $\theta_1(t)$ and $\theta_2(t)$. 
 \end{anum}
A: \begin{anum}
 \item The two normal modes are when the masses oscillate perfectly in phase and when they oscillate perfectly out of phase, just like in the previous problem. When the masses oscillate perfectly in phase, we can neglect the influence of the spring so $$\omega_1=\boxed{\sqrt{\frac gl}}$$When the masses oscillate perfectly out of phase, the restoring torque on each mass (when its string makes an angle $\theta$ to the vertical) is given by $\tau=-(mgl\theta+2kl\theta)$, and so the equation of motion is $-(mgl\theta+2kl\theta)=ml^2\ddot\theta$, with $$\omega_2=\boxed{\sqrt{\frac gl+\frac{2k}{ml}}}$$
 \item The equations of motion of the masses are \begin{gather*}
 ml^2\ddot\theta_1=-kl(\theta_1-\theta_2)-mgl\theta_1\\
 ml^2\ddot\theta_2=-kl(\theta_2-\theta_1)-mgl\theta_2
 \end{gather*}Adding the two equations yields $$ml^2(\ddot\theta_1+\ddot\theta_2)=-mgl(\theta_1+\theta_2)$$and subtracting the two equations yields $$ml^2(\ddot\theta_1-\ddot\theta_2)=-2kl(\theta_1-\theta_2)-mgl(\theta_1-\theta_2)$$Thus $\theta_1+\theta_2$ will exhibit simple harmonic motion with $\omega_1$ found earlier, and $\theta_1-\theta_2$ will exhibit simple harmonic motion with $\omega_2$ found earlier. Thus, if we have \begin{gather*} \theta_1+\theta_2=A\cos\omega_1t+B\sin\omega_1t\\
 \theta_1-\theta_2=C\cos\omega_2t+D\sin\omega_2t
 \end{gather*}we can use the initial condition $\theta_1(0),\theta_2(0)=0$ to find that $A,C=0$, so that $\theta_1=(1/2)(B\sin\omega_1t+D\sin\omega_2t)$ and $\theta_2=(1/2)(B\sin\omega_1t-D\sin\omega_2t)$. Taking the derivatives and using $\dot\theta_1(0)=0$ and $\dot\theta_2(0)=v/l$, we have $\omega_1B+\omega_2D=0$ and $\omega_1B-\omega_2D=2v/l$, which solves as $B=v/l\omega_1$ and $D=-v/l\omega_2$. To conclude, \begin{equation*}\boxed{\begin{gathered}\theta_1(t)=\frac{v}{2l}\left(\frac{\sin\omega_1t}{\omega_1}-\frac{\sin\omega_2t}{\omega_2}\right)\\
 \theta_2(t)=\frac{v}{2l}\left(\frac{\sin\omega_1t}{\omega_1}+\frac{\sin\omega_2t}{\omega_2}\right)\end{gathered}}\end{equation*}
 \end{anum}

[17] Challenge Problems — USAPhO
Q: A large block of mass $m_b$ is located on a horizontal frictionless surface. A second block of mass $m_t$ is located on top of the first block; the coefficient of friction (both static and kinetic) between the two blocks is given by $\mu$. All surfaces are horizontal; all motion is effectively one dimensional. A spring with spring constant $k$ is connected to the top block only; the spring obeys Hooke’s Law equally in both extension and compression. Assume that the top block never falls off of the bottom block; you may assume that the bottom block is very, very long. The top block is moved a distance $A$ away from the equilibrium position and then released from rest.
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[scale=0.25]{osc2}
 \end{center}
 \begin{anum}
 \item Depending on the value of $A$, the motion can be divided into two types: motion that experiences no frictional energy losses and motion that does. Find the value $A_c$ that divides the two motion types. Write your answer in terms of any or all of $\mu$, the acceleration of gravity $g$, the masses $m_t$ and $m_b$, and the spring constant $k$.
 \item Consider now the scenario $A\gg A_c$. In this scenario the amplitude of the oscillation of the top block as measured against the original equilibrium position will change with time. Determine the magnitude of the change in amplitude, $\Delta A$, after one complete oscillation, as a function of any or all of $A$, $\mu$, $g$, and the angular frequency of oscillation of the top block $\omega_t$.
 \item Assume still that $A\gg A_c$. What is the maximum speed of the bottom block during the first complete oscillation cycle of the upper block?
 \end{anum}
A: See official solutions <a href="https://www.aapt.org/Common/upload/USAPhO-2015-Solutions_rev-4012021.pdf" target="_blank" rel="noopener">here</a>.

[18] Challenge Problems
Q: A block of mass $m$ sits on the ground and a relaxed spring of constant $k$ is attached to the right of it. A person will pull the other end of the spring at a constant speed $v$ to the right. The ground has coefficients of static and kinetic friction $\mu_s$ and $\mu_k$, respectively, with $\mu_s>\mu_k$. The person begins pulling the spring at time $t=0$. \begin{anum}
 \item At what time will the block first start to move?
 \item How long will the block slip for before it stops again? Hint: A change in reference frame will be helpful. 
 \item How far will the block move during this time? Hint: If $t_2$ is the answer to part (b), you can write your answer to this part using $t_2$ without having solved part (b).
 \item How much additional time will pass (after the block stops) before the block starts moving again? Hint: You don't need to have solved part (b) to solve this part.
 \end{anum}
A: \begin{anum}
 \item The spring force will overcome static friction when the stretch in the spring becomes $x=mg\mu_s/k$, or in time $$t_1=\frac{x}{v}=\boxed{\frac{mg\mu_s}{kv}}$$
 \item After the block starts slipping, the friction will become kinetic friction until the block stops again. Moving into a constant velocity frame of speed $v$ to the right, the block starts slipping when the spring is stretched $mg\mu_s/k$ from its relaxed state, at which point in this frame the block has a speed $v$ to the left. Notice that the kinetic friction force is directed to the left in this frame and is constant in time, so that we can relate the situation to a spring in a gravitational field with a shifted equilibrium point. This is illustrated by the diagram below:
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[scale=0.2]{block333}
 \end{center}
 The shifted equilibrium point is a distance $mg\mu_k/k$ from the relaxed state of the spring, and the block will slip until it reaches a speed $v$ to the left again in this frame (which corresponds to coming back to rest in the lab frame). As a result, it will move along the arrow shown. The amplitude of oscillations in this frame is found by finding the total energy. At $t_1$, the block has speed $v$, and the spring is stretched a distance $mg(\mu_s-\mu_k)/k$ from equilibrium. Thus the amplitude is found via $$\frac12 kA^2=\frac12 k\left(\frac{mg(\mu_s-\mu_k)}{k}\right)^2+\frac12 mv^2\Rightarrow A=\sqrt{\left(\frac{mg(\mu_s-\mu_k)}{k}\right)^2+\frac{mv^2}{k}}$$So in using the same strategy in Problem Problem 4, the time it takes the block to travel along the path shown by the arrow is a fraction $(2\pi-2\sin^{-1}(d/A))/2\pi$ of the period, where $d$ is the original distance from equilibrium. Thus the time it takes for the block to stop slipping is $$t_2=\boxed{\left(2\pi-2\sin^{-1}\frac1{\sqrt{1+kv^2/mg^2(\mu_s-\mu_k)^2}}\right)\sqrt{\frac mk}}$$
 \item The person's hand will move a distance $vt_2$ during this time, and the block will also move a distance $2mg(\mu_s-\mu_k)/k$ relative to the hand, so the total distance traveled is $$d=\boxed{vt_2+\frac{2mg(\mu_s-\mu_k)}{k}}$$
 \item The time is just $$t_3=\boxed{\frac{2mg(\mu_s-\mu_k)}{kv}}$$
 \end{anum}

Q: A solid, uniform cylinder of height $h$ and base area $s$ floats in a cylindrical beaker of height $H$ and inner bottom area $S$ filled with a liquid. The ratio between the density of the cylinder and that of the liquid is $\gamma$. The cylinder is oscillating vertically, so that its axis always coincides with that of the beaker. The amplitude of the liquid level oscillations is $A$. Find the period of the motion $T$. Neglect the viscosity of the liquid.
A: See official solutions <a href="https://eupho.ee/wp-content/uploads/2022/05/EuPhO_2022_Solutions.pdf" target="_blank" rel="noopener">here</a>.

[1] Exercises
Q: Practice with Coulomb and Gauss!
 \begin{anum}
 \item Find the electric field due to a ring of radius $R$ and total charge $Q$ a distance $z$ above its center along its axis.
 \item Find the electric field above and below an infinite flat sheet of charge of surface density $\sigma$ (charge per unit area). 
 \item Find the electric field a distance $r$ away from an infinitely long wire with charge per unit length $\lambda$. 
 \item Find the electric field a distance $r$ directly above a hole of radius $r$ within an infinite flat sheet of charge with surface density $\sigma$, as shown below.
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[scale=0.2]{ηολε}
 \end{center}
 \item Find the electric field above an infinite half-sheet of charge of surface density $\sigma$ (side view -- assume the sheet extends infinitely in and out of the page as well as to the right). 
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[scale=0.2]{ηαλω}
 \end{center}
 \item (Optional) Find the electric field at a point on the symmetry axis of a hemispherical shell of radius $R$ and charge density $\sigma$, at position $z$ relative to the center, for any $z$ value.
 \end{anum}
A: \begin{anum}
\item All the charges are a distance $\sqrt{R^2 + z^2}$ away, and we want to extract the vertical components of their electric fields. By trig, this is $\frac{z}{\sqrt{R^2 + z^2}}$, so the total electric field is
 $$E = \frac{kQ}{R^2 + z^2}\frac{z}{\sqrt{R^2+z^2}}.$$
 \item We use Gauss's law in a cylinder that intersects the sheet of charge. If the cylinder has cross-section area $A$, then it incloses charge $\sigma A$. The net flux is symmetric out both of the faces of the cylinder, so we have $$2EA=\frac{\sigma A}{\epsilon_0}\Longrightarrow E=\frac{\sigma}{2\epsilon_0}$$
 \item Here we use Gauss's law in a cylinder of radius $r$ and length $L$ coaxial with the wire. The charge enclosed is $\lambda L$, and the flux is through the lateral face of the cylinder across a surface area $2\pi rL$. Thus, $$E\cdot 2\pi rL = \frac{\lambda L}{\epsilon_0}\Longrightarrow E = \frac{\lambda}{2\pi \epsilon_0 r}$$
 \item Let us imagine the infinite sheet as composed of many thin circular rings of increasing radius. To make things easier, let us denote the location of each ring via the angle $\theta$ with respect to the vertical; for example, the closest ring, which has radius $r$, will have $\theta = \pi/4$. The furthest ring, which is infinitely far away, has $\theta = \pi/2$. 

 As a function of $\theta$ and $d\theta$, let us find the contribution of each ring. 
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[scale=0.3]{ringthing}
 \end{center}
 In the diagram below, we can see that the ring that corresponds to angles $\theta$ to $\theta + d\theta$ has a thickness $(r/\cos^2\theta)d\theta$, so it has a total charge $$dQ=\sigma \cdot 2\pi r\tan\theta \cdot \frac{r}{\cos^2\theta}d\theta=2\pi \sigma r^2 \frac{\sin\theta}{\cos^3\theta}d\theta$$
 Using the result from part (a), the contribution to the electric field can be integrated for all rings: \begin{align*}
 E = \int_{\pi/4}^{\pi/2} \frac{dQ\cos\theta}{4\pi\epsilon_0(r/\cos\theta)^2}= \int_{\pi/4}^{\pi/2}\frac{\sigma\sin\theta}{2\pi\epsilon_0}d\theta=\frac{\sigma}{2\sqrt 2\pi\epsilon_0}
 \end{align*}
 \item We can think of the plane as a bunch of wires parallel to each other. Again parameterizing with $\theta$, the linear charge density for the wire at angle $\theta$ can be thought to be $$\lambda = \sigma \cdot \frac{r}{\cos^2\theta}d\theta$$Clearly the total electric field will be at an angle, so let us integrate the vertical components and horizontal components separately. First, in the vertical direction, we have $$E_y=\int_0^{\pi/2} \frac{\lambda}{2\pi\epsilon_0(r/\cos\theta)}\cos\theta=\int_0^{\pi/2}\frac{\sigma}{2\pi\epsilon_0}d\theta = \frac{\sigma}{4\epsilon_0}$$ As expected, this is just half of $\sigma/2\epsilon_0$, which would be the vertical component if the other half of the infinite plane was also there. For the horizontal component, we have $$E_x=\int_0^{\pi/2}\frac{\lambda}{2\pi\epsilon_0(r/\cos\theta)}\sin\theta = \int_0^{\pi/2}\frac{\sigma}{2\pi\epsilon_0}\tan\theta d\theta = \infty$$ So the field diverges. This also makes sense because we're integrating many wires of charge, whose field tails off as $1/r$. The integral of $1/r$ diverges, of course.
 \item This one is extremely messy. We use polar coordinates. Consider a slice of the hemisphere that is $\theta$ down from the top. The total charge on it is $\sigma (2\pi R^2 \sin \theta d\theta)$. The distance away from the center of the ring is $R\cos \theta - z$. Thus, using our previous result, the z contribution to the electric field from this ring is
 $$dE_z = -\frac{\sigma (2\pi R^2 \sin \theta d\theta)}{4\pi \epsilon_0 r^2} \frac{R\cos \theta - z}{r},$$
 where $r = \sqrt{(R\cos \theta - z)^2 + (R\sin \theta)^2}.$ Integrating this (best way is to use an integration table or some computational help), we get
 $$E(z) = \begin{cases}
 \frac{\sigma R^2}{2\epsilon_0 z^2}\left(\frac{1}{\sqrt{1+z^2/R^2}}-1\right) & z < R\\ \frac{\sigma R^2}{2\epsilon_0 z^2}\left(\frac{1}{\sqrt{1+z^2/R^2}}+ 1\right) & z >R.
 \end{cases}$$
\end{anum}

[2] Exercises — Purcell
Q: $~$
 Suppose you have a point charge $q$, located a distance $r$ from the origin. Using a physical argument (not integration), show that the average electric field over a sphere centered at the origin and with radius $R$ is
 \begin{anum}
 \item Zero, when $r < R$,
 \item and $\frac{q}{4\pi \epsilon_0 r^2}$ when $r > R$.
 \end{anum}
A: \begin{anum}
\item The average electric field mathematically over the sphere is
 $$\mathbf E_{\text{avg}} = \frac{1}{SA}\int_{\text{sphere}} \mathbf E dA.$$
 Now, suppose we multiply by $\sigma / \sigma$, such that the expression is
 $$\mathbf E_{\text{avg}} = \frac{1}{\sigma SA} \int_{\text{sphere}} \mathbf E (\sigma dA).$$
 Notice that if we physically have a sphere of radius $R$ and charge density $\sigma$, then this expression is precisely the net force on this sphere divided by its total charge. Thus, if we have a charge $q$ inside the sphere, by shell theorem and Newton's third law, there is no force on the sphere, and thus the expression is ${0}$.
 \item Similarly this time, by the other shell theorem, the net force on the sphere, if it has total charge $Q$ is given by
 $$F = \frac{kQq}{r^2}.$$
 Then, plugging that into our expression from above,
 $$\mathbf E_{\text{avg}} = \frac{1}{Q} F = \frac 1Q \frac{kQq}{r^2} = \frac{q}{4\pi \epsilon_0 r^2},$$
 as desired.
\end{anum}

[3] Exercises
Q: In a sphere of uniformly distributed charge of radius $R$ and charge density $\rho$, we cut out a sphere of radius $a$ a distance $b$ from the center. What is the electric field inside this sphere?
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[width=5cm]{ball_ball_light.png}
 \end{center}
A: Consider the electric field as a superposition of that from a uniform sphere and a negatively charged sphere of radius $b$. As we derived in class, the field due to a sphere of charge density $\rho$ is given by
 $$\mathbf E = \frac{\rho \mathbf r}{3\epsilon_0}.$$
 So, if we have a superposition of spheres, the field is
 $$\mathbf E = \mathbf E_\rho + \mathbf E_{-\rho} = \frac{\rho \mathbf r}{3\epsilon_0} +\frac{(-\rho) \mathbf r_{\text{inside}}}{3\epsilon_0} = \frac{\rho}{3\epsilon_0}(\mathbf r - \mathbf r_{\text{inside}}).$$
 But for any point within the "$a$" sphere, the vector $\mathbf r - \mathbf r_{\text{inside}}$ is the vector $\mathbf b$ to the center of the smaller sphere. So, the electric field inside anywhere in the sphere is the constant $\mathbf E = \frac{\rho \mathbf b}{3\epsilon_0}.$

[4] Exercises
Q: Consider two spheres of radii $R$ and charge densities of $\pm \rho$. They are placed a distance $d < 2R$ apart from each other such that they have overlap of 0 charge density as shown below. What is the electric field in this region?
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[width=5cm]{two_spheres.png}
 \end{center}
A: Using the ideas from the previous problem, the electric field is
 $$\mathbf E = \mathbf E_{\rho} + \mathbf E_{-\rho} = \frac{\rho \mathbf r_+}{3\epsilon_0}-\frac{\rho \mathbf r_-}{3\epsilon_0}.$$
 But $\mathbf r_+ - \mathbf r_-$ is just the vector between the centers, $\mathbf d$. Thus, the field in that region is just $\mathbf E = \frac{\rho \mathbf d}{3\epsilon_0}.$

[5] Exercises
Q: In this problem we do some dipole calculations.
 \begin{anum}
 \item To begin, we find the field from a dipole of charges $\pm q$ separated by a distance $d$ at a point $P$ which is a distance $r$ along the axis. Write your answer in terms of $r$, $p = qd$, and fundamental constants.
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[width=7cm]{dipole.png}
 \end{center}
 \item Now, suppose we take two of those dipoles and stack them as shown below. What is the field now at point $P$? This configuration is known as a quadrupole, whose field goes as $\sim 1/r^4$. <em>Hint:</em> Be careful to consider all necessary higher order terms when approximating.
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[width=8.5cm]{quadrupole.png}
 \end{center}
 To be clear, this setup is a two $-q$ charges on the outside and then a charge of $2q$ in the center.
 \item If you stack two opposing quadrupoles, you get an octopole. Can you make a conjecture about how the field from an octopole scales as $r$? This series of expansions motivates the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Multipole_expansion" target="_blank" rel="noopener">multipole expansion</a>.
 \end{anum}
A: \begin{anum}
\item We simply use Coulomb's law to write down
 $$E = \frac{kq}{(r-d/2)^2} - \frac{kq}{(r+d/2)^2}.$$
 Using the binomial approximation,
 $$E = \frac{kq}{r^2}\left[\left(1+ \frac d r\right) - \left(1 - \frac d r\right)\right] = \frac{kq}{r^2} \frac{2d}{r} = \frac{2kp}{r^3}.$$
 \item If you directly calculate coulomb's law from each of the four charges, you should find that the first order terms also cancel. However, if we use our result from the previous problem, we can write
 $$E = \frac{2kp}{(r+d/2)^3} - \frac{2kp}{(r-d/2)^3} = \frac{2kp}{r^3} \left[\left(1 +\frac{3d}{2r}\right) - \left(1 -\frac{3d}{2r}\right)\right] = \frac{6kpd}{r^4}.$$
 \item We can guess that it goes as $\sim 1/r^5$ based on the trend from the previous two problems.
\end{anum}

[6] Exercises — Purcell
Q: A half-infinite line has linear charge density $\lambda$. Find the electric
field at a point that is “even” with the end, a distance $\ell$ from it, as
shown below. You should find that the field always points up
at a $45^\circ$ angle, independent of $\ell$.
\begin{center}
 \includegraphics[width=5cm]{purcell_half_line.png}
\end{center}
A: Consider superposing the configuration in the problem with an infinite line of charge of charge density $-\lambda$. The field due to this infinite line would point straight down from the point, with a magnitude of $E = \frac{\lambda}{2\pi \epsilon_0 \ell}$ from Gauss's law. But the superposition of the two produces a half-infinite line of charge density $-\lambda$, so the field should just be the horizontal reflection of the field due to the current line. Thus, by vector geometry,
 $$2E_y = E_{\text{full line}} = \frac{\lambda}{2\pi\epsilon_0 \ell}.$$
 So, now we just need to find the horizontal component of the electric field. There is no neat trick to do this, we simply integrate along the half infinite line. Consider paramaterizing by $\theta$ from the vertical axis as we did in problem 1. Then, for each $d\theta$, the amount of charge is
 $$dq = \lambda d(\ell \tan \theta) = \lambda \ell \sec^2 \theta d\theta.$$
 And the distance away is $\ell /\cos \theta$, so the horizontal component is
 $$d E_x = \frac{kdq}{(\ell / \cos \theta)^2}\sin \theta = \frac{k\lambda}{\ell}\sin \theta d\theta.$$
 So, integrating over the line,
 $$E_x = \int_0^{\pi /2} \frac{k\lambda}{\ell}\sin \theta d\theta = \frac{k\lambda}{\ell} = \frac{\lambda}{4\pi \epsilon_0 \ell}.$$
 Thus,
 $$E_x = E_y = \frac{\lambda}{4\pi \epsilon_0 \ell}.$$
 So, the electric field is of total magnitude $\frac{\sqrt{2}\lambda}{4\pi \epsilon_0 \ell}$, and points $45^\circ$ up from the horizontal.

[7] Exercises
Q: Prove that if you have a configuration of static charges, none of the charges can be in stable equilibrium. This is known as <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earnshaw%27s_theorem" target="_blank" rel="noopener">Earnshaw's Theorem</a>. <em>Hint:</em> consider one charge and what the field lines must look like around it if it was in a stable equilibrium.
A: Suppose for the sake of contradiction that charge $q_i$ was in a stable equilibrium. Without loss of generality, assume that it is positive. Then, because it is in stable equilibrium, the field created with the absence of this charge $q_i$ (the field from all the other charges) must point inward in all directions. However, consider a gaussian surface at the location of where $q_i$ is in the configuration without $q_i$. Because all the field lines point inward here, there is a nonzero flux. However, because $q_i$ is gone, there should be 0 flux, which is a contradiction. Thus, none of the charges can be in a stable equilibrium.

[8] Exercises — Purcell
Q: Find the flux through the circle shown below.
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[width=6cm]{circle_disk.png}
 \end{center}
A: We push out the disk into the a sphere with radius $\ell / \cos \theta$. Now it is clear that the flux through this disk is equal to the fraction of the sphere that its projected spherical cap takes up. This is a cap with angle $\theta$, so we can find the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solid_angle#Cone,_spherical_cap,_hemisphere" target="_blank" rel="noopener">solid angle of the spherical cap</a>,
 $$\Omega = \int_0^{2\pi} \int_0^\theta \sin \theta d\theta d\phi = 2\pi (1-\cos \theta).$$
 So, the fraction of the total sphere is $\frac{\Omega}{4\pi}$, and since the total flux is $q/\epsilon_0$ by Gauss's law, the flux through this disks is
 $$\Phi = \frac{\Omega}{4\pi} \frac{q}{\epsilon_0} = \frac{q(1-\cos \theta)}{2\epsilon_0}.$$

[9] Exercises
Q: You have two disks of charge density $\pm \sigma$ attached by a rod at the center. A point charge $q$ is placed as shown below, a distance $\ell$ away from the smaller disk. What is the net force on the two-disk contraption? How does it depend on the distance between the two plates, $d$?
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[width=6cm]{two_disk.png}
 \end{center}
A: Similar to the problem that was covered in lecture, note that the force on a plane of charge is given by
 $$F = \int_S \mathbf E \cdot (\sigma d\mathbf A) = \sigma \int_S \mathbf E \cdot d\mathbf A = \sigma \Phi_E.$$
 Now, notice that the frustum created by the two disks has 0 total flux through it as it contains no charges. Furthermore, because the field lines due to the $+q$ charge point parallel to the lateral faces of the frustum, the only flux is through the disks, so
 $$\Phi_\sigma = \Phi_{-\sigma},$$
 which means that the net force is
 $$F = \sigma \Phi_\sigma - \sigma \Phi_{-\sigma} = 0.$$

[10] Exercises — Purcell
Q: The figure below shows a spherical shell of charge, of radius $a$ and surface density $\sigma$, from which a small circular piece of radius $b \ll a$
has been removed. What is the direction and magnitude of the field
at the midpoint of the aperture?

\begin{center}
 \includegraphics[width=6cm]{hole_shell.png}
\end{center}
A: Suppose that the electric field is $E$ at that point without the circular piece. Then, if we place the circular piece back on to that same place, the magnitude of the force will be
 $$F = Eq = \sigma E \pi b^2.$$
 But we also know from force on a layer of charge that
 $$F = \sigma \pi b^2 \frac 12 (E_1 + E_2).$$
 When the piece is back in the sphere it is a normal spherical shell. Thus, the field inside is $0$, and by Gauss's law with a pillbox at the surface, the field just outside is $\sigma /\epsilon_0$. So,
 $$\sigma E \pi B^2 = \sigma \pi b^2 \frac 12 (0 + \sigma / \epsilon_0).$$
 Thus, ${E = \sigma / 2\epsilon_0}.$

[11] Exercises — Purcell
Q: The two strips of charge shown below have width $b$,
infinite height, and negligible thickness (in the direction perpendicular to the page). Their charge densities per unit area are
$\pm \sigma$. 
\begin{center}
 \includegraphics[width=5cm]{two_strips.png}
\end{center}
\begin{anum}
 \item Find the magnitude of the electric field due to one of the
strips, a distance $x$ away from it (in the plane of the page).
 \item Show that the force (per unit height) between the two strips
equals $\sigma^2b(\ln 2)/\pi \epsilon_0$. Note that this result is finite, even though
you will find that the field due to a strip diverges as you get
close to it.
\end{anum}
A: \begin{anum}
\item The infinite strips are made up of infinite lines of charge, so we can integrate the field due to an infinite line of charge,
 $$E = \int_x^{x+b} \frac{(\sigma dx)}{2\pi \epsilon_0 x} = \frac{\sigma}{2\pi \epsilon_0} \ln \left(\frac{x+b}{x}\right).$$
 \item This time we integrate our known expression for the electric field to add up the forces on the other strip of charge. We have that
 $$F = \int_0^b E(x)\sigma dx = \int_0^b \frac{\sigma^2}{2\pi \epsilon_0} \ln \left(\frac{x+b}{x}\right) dx = \frac{\sigma^2}{2\pi \epsilon_0} \int_0^b \ln (x+b) - \ln (x) dx.$$
 Now, using the integral for $\ln x$, which is $x \ln x - x$, we compute
 $$F = \frac{\sigma^2}{2\pi \epsilon_0} \left(2b \ln 2b - b \ln b - b \ln b \right) = \frac{\sigma^2 b}{2\pi \epsilon_0} \ln 2.$$
\end{anum}

[12] Exercises
Q: Consider a dipole in space. \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[width=0.5\linewidth]{dipolecirc.png}
 \end{center}
 Find the radial component of the dipole field along a small circle of radius $\delta\ll r$ around $P$, in two ways:
 \begin{anum}
 \item Use Coulomb's law to find the electric field from each charge in the dipole.
 \item Create a thin cylinder of radius $\delta$ along the circle. Find the flux through each circular face of the circle, and use Gauss's law to find the radial field.
 \end{anum}
A: \begin{anum}
 \item To good approximation, the distance from the charges to the circle is $r\pm d/2$, meaning we neglect $\delta$. Applying Coulomb's law in the radial direction gives \begin{align*}E_r&=\frac{q}{4\pi \epsilon_0(r-d/2)^2}\frac{\delta}{r-d/2}-\frac{q}{4\pi\epsilon_0(r+d/2)^2}\frac{\delta}{r+d/2}\\
 &=\frac{q\delta}{4\pi\epsilon_0}\left[\frac{1}{(r-d/2)^3}-\frac 1{(r+d/2)^3}\right]=\frac{3p\delta}{4\pi\epsilon_0 r^4} \end{align*}We can evaluate the part in the brackets via the first-order Taylor expansion or by treating the quantity as a derivative, i.e. $\frac d{dr}r^{-3}=-3dr/r^4$ where in this case $dr\equiv d$. 
 \item Using the solution to the previous problem on dipoles, the field at $P$ is $$E=\frac{p}{2\pi\epsilon_0 r^3}$$Let us have a cylinder of small width $dr$. Then the difference in flux through the circular faces is $\pi \delta ^2 dE$ where $dE$ is the difference in field strength at distance $r$ and $r+dr$. By a Taylor expansion or alternatively taking the derivative of the electric field, we have $$\pi \delta^2 dE=\pi\delta^2 \frac d{dr}\frac{p}{2\pi\epsilon_0r^3}=\frac{3p\delta^2}{2\epsilon_0r^4}$$This must be precisely the flux through the sides of the cylinder, which is equal to $E_r\cdot 2\pi \delta dr$. Equating the two quantities yields $$E_r\cdot 2\pi \delta dr=\frac{3p\delta^2}{2\epsilon_0r^4}\Longrightarrow E_r=\frac{3p\delta}{4\pi\epsilon_0 r^4}$$
 \end{anum}

[13] Exercises — Kalda
Q: A thin planar electrode has a small circular opening of radius $a$. There is a uniform electric field $E_1$ one one side of the electrode and a uniform electric field $E_2$ on the other side.
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[width=0.5\linewidth]{kalda1.png}
\end{center}A beam of electrons is passed through the circular opening at a distance $z_1$ from the electrode and refocuses on the other side at a distance $z_2$ from the electrode. Assume that particle energy $eU_0 \gg eE_1z_1, eE_2z_2$ and $a\ll z_1, z_2$. Show that under these conditions the ``lens formula" $$\frac{1}{z_1} + \frac 1{z_2} = \frac{E_1-E_2}{4U_0}$$ holds. <em>Hint:</em> use Gauss's law to approximate the radial electric field in the circular opening.
A: Let there exist a thin Gaussian cylinder of width $\delta$ and radius $r<a$ at the opening. If we assume the electric field through the faces are $E_1$ and $E_2$, we can assume the radial electric field is only significant when electrons pass along this cylinder. From the analysis before, we can find the radial electric field as a function of $r$ by arguing that the total flux through the cylinder surfaces is zero: $$2\pi r\delta E_r+\pi r^2 E_2=\pi r^2E_1\Longrightarrow E_r=\frac{r(E_1-E_2)}{2\delta}$$While the electrons (which we assign charge $e$ and mass $m$) travel through the cylinder, we can approximate the total impulse they experience with $$\Delta p = F\Delta t = eE_r\frac{\delta}{v}=\frac{er(E_1-E_2)}{2v}$$ The electrons that intersect the plane at $r$ have radial velocity $(r/z_1)v$ outward before going through the plane, and radial velocity $(r/z_2)v$ inward after going through the plane (this is required for the beam of electrons to converge to a single point). This change in velocity must have been supplied by the impulse. With this information, we have $$\Delta p =\frac{er(E_1-E_2)}{2v}= m\left(\frac{rv}{z_1}+\frac{rv}{z_2}\right)\Longrightarrow \frac{1}{z_1}+\frac{1}{z_2}=\frac{e(E_1-E_2)}{2mv^2}$$If the particle energy is $eU_0=mv^2/2$, we can make a quick substitution to arrive at the desired answer.

[14] Challenge Problems
Q: Consider the setup from Problem 1Problem 1. Let us place a particle of mass $m$ and charge $q$ a small distance right above the center of the hole. What is the period of small oscillations of the particle? Assume that the particle is charged oppositely as $\sigma$.
A: Let us take the setup from 1Problem 1, and instead of being placed $r$ above the plane we place the particle a distance $z\ll r$ above the plane. This means we replace $\pi/4$ in the integral from that problem with a general $\phi=\cos^{-1}(z/r)$ that is very close to $\pi/2$. The force on the particle will be $$F=qE=q\int_\phi^{\pi/2}\frac{\sigma\sin\theta}{2\pi\epsilon_0}d\theta=\frac{q\sigma}{2\pi\epsilon_0}\cos\phi = \frac{q\sigma z}{2\pi\epsilon_0r}$$It follows immediately from $F=m\ddot z$ that we have the frequency of oscillation $\omega = \sqrt{q\sigma / 2\pi\epsilon_0 mr}$ and so $$T=\frac{2\pi}{\omega}=(2\pi)^{3/2}\sqrt{\frac{\epsilon_0mr}{q\sigma}}$$

[15] Challenge Problems — IdPhO
Q: A narrow straight channel passes through the center of a solid, fixed cube with side length $a$. The cube is uniformly charged and the charge density is $\rho$. The total length of the channel is $L$. In the channel, we place a small particle of mass $m$ and charge $q$. Find the period of small oscillations of the particle near the center. The gravitational interaction of the particle and the cube can be neglected. The cube and the particle are oppositely charged. \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[scale=0.2]{idpho}
 \end{center}
A: Let us move the particle a small distance $(x,y,z)$ in the channel. Once this move happens, the particle can be thought of being in the exact center of a smaller rectangular prism of side lengths $a-2x,a-2y,a-2z$, with three slabs of width $2x,2y,2z$ on the sides. 
\begin{center}
 \includegraphics[width=0.5\linewidth]{idphosol.png}
\end{center}The particle experiences no force from the prism, since it is in the exact center. In class we found the force that is exerted by a square of side length $a$, surface charge density $\sigma$, on a particle $q$ a distance $a/2$ away is $q\sigma/6\epsilon_0$. Since $(x,y,z)$ are small, we can think of the slabs of width $2x,2y,2z$ as being effective thin sheets with surface charge densities $2\rho x,2\rho y,2\rho z$. We can neglect the small pieces of overlap between the slabs because they have charge of higher orders of $(x,y,z)$, which we can neglect. Thus the force on the particle is $$F=\left(\frac{q\rho x}{3\epsilon_0}, \frac{q\rho y}{3\epsilon_0}, \frac{q\rho z}{3\epsilon_0}\right)$$ and using $F=m\ddot r$ this leads us to a frequency of oscillation $$\omega =\sqrt{ \frac{q\rho}{3\epsilon_0 m}}\Longrightarrow T=\frac{2\pi}{\omega}=2\pi\sqrt{ \frac{3\epsilon_0 m}{q\rho}}$$

[16] Challenge Problems — MPPP
Q: Two insulating hemispherical shells (e.g. the two halves of a pingpong ball) are placed very close to each other with their centres almost coincident, as shown in figure a). Both of them are uniformly charged, one with total electric
charge $Q$, and the other with total charge $q$. 
\begin{center}
 \includegraphics[width=8cm]{two_hemispheres.png}
\end{center}

\begin{anum}
 \item What is the magnitude of the net electric force exerted by the two bodies on each other?
 \item What about if the radius of one of the hemispherical shells is only one-half that of the other, as shown in figure b)?
\end{anum}
A: \begin{anum}
\item First, consider the case where $Q = q$. Using the force on a layer of charge idea, we have
 $$F/A = \frac{Q}{2\pi R^2}\cdot \frac 12 \left(0 + \frac{k(2Q)}{R^2}\right).$$
 Then, using the pressure idea, the net force on the hemisphere is
 $$F = (\pi R^2)\frac{2kQ^2}{4\pi R^4}.$$
 Thus, the average electric field due to the left hemisphere of charge $Q$ is
 $$E_{\text{avg}} = \frac{F}{Q} = \frac{kQ}{2R^2}.$$
 So, the force on the hemisphere of chrage $q$ is $q E_{\text{avg}} = {\frac{kQq}{2R^2}}.$
 \item This one requires quite a bit of ingenuity!
 \begin{center}
 \includegraphics[width=8.5cm]{mppp_trickyy.png}
 \end{center}
 We follow the transformations in the figure above. First, the force on the small shell is unchanged by adding a large sphere of $-2Q$. This then transforms to the figure in (c). We can change the sign of the charges which only changes the direction. Lastly, flipping and superposing this with part (a), we get (f), which shows that the force on having a full shell of $2q$ is in fact just double that of one. By shell theorem, we can scale this down to part (g), from where we have the exact problem from lecture,
 $$F = \frac{kq}{R^2}\frac{Q}{2\pi R^2} \times (\pi R^2) = \frac{kQq}{2R^2}.$$
\end{anum}
